ANNAPLE\'S AMBITION FALLEN.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I.\r\n\r\nST. AMBROSE\'S CHOIR.\r\n\r\n  \'For be it known\r\n  That their saint\'s honour is their own.\'--SCOTT.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe town of Micklethwayte was rising and thriving.  There were\r\nsalubrious springs which an enterprising doctor had lately brought into\r\nnotice.  The firm of Greenleaf and Dutton manufactured umbrellas in\r\nlarge quantities, from the stout weather-proof family roof down to the\r\ndaintiest fringed toy of a parasol.  There were a Guild Hall and a\r\nhandsome Corn Market.  There was a Modern School for the boys, and a\r\nHigh School for the girls, and a School of Art, and a School of\r\nCookery, and National Schools, and a British School, and a Board\r\nSchool, also churches of every height, chapels of every denomination,\r\nand iron mission rooms budding out in hopes to be replaced by churches.\r\n\r\nLike one of the animals which zoologists call radiated, the town was\r\nconstantly stretching out fresh arms along country roads, all living\r\nand working, and gradually absorbing the open spaces between.  One of\r\nthese arms was known as St. Ambrose\'s Road, in right of the church, an\r\nincomplete structure in yellow brick, consisting of a handsome chancel,\r\nthe stump of a tower, and one aisle just weather-tight and usable, but,\r\nby its very aspect, begging for the completion of the beautiful design\r\nthat was suspended above the alms-box.\r\n\r\nIt was the evening of a summer day which had been very hot.  The choir\r\npractice was just over, and the boys came out trooping and chattering;\r\nvery small ones they were; for as soon as they began to sing tolerably\r\nthey were sure to try to get into the choir of the old church, which\r\nhad a foundation that fed, clothed, taught, and finally apprenticed\r\nthem.  So, though the little fellows were clad in surplices and\r\ncassocks, and sat in the chancel for correctness sake, there was a\r\nspace round the harmonium reserved for the more trustworthy band of\r\ngirls and young women who came forth next, followed by four or five\r\nmechanics.\r\n\r\nBehind came the nucleus of the choir--a slim, fair-haired youth of\r\ntwenty; a neat, precise, well-trimmed man, closely shaven, with\r\nstooping shoulders, at least fifteen years older, with a black poodle\r\nat his heels, as well shorn as his master, newly risen from lying\r\noutside the church door; a gentle, somewhat drooping lady in black, not\r\nyet middle-aged and very pretty; a small eager, unformed, black-eyed\r\ngirl, who could hardly keep back her words for the outside of the\r\nchurch door; a tall self-possessed handsome woman, with a fine\r\nclassical cast of features; and lastly, a brown-faced, wiry hardworking\r\nclergyman, without an atom of superfluous flesh, but with an air of\r\ngreat energy.\r\n\r\n\'Oh! vicar, where are we to go?\' was the question so eager to break\r\nforth.\r\n\r\n\'Not to the Crystal Palace, Nuttie.  The funds won\'t bear it.  Mr.\r\nDutton says we must spend as little as possible on locomotion.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sure I don\'t care for the Crystal Palace.  A trumpery tinsel\r\nplace, all shams.\'\r\n\r\n\'Hush, hush, my dear, not so loud,\' said the quiet lady; but Nuttie\r\nonly wriggled her shoulders, though her voice was a trifle lowered. \'If\r\nit were the British Museum now, or Westminster Abbey.\'\r\n\r\n\'Or the Alps,\' chimed in a quieter voice, \'or the Ufizzi.\'\r\n\r\n\'Now, Mr. Dutton, that\'s not what I want.  Our people aren\'t ready for\r\nthat, but what they have let it be real.  Miss Mary, don\'t you see what\r\nI mean?\'\r\n\r\n\'Rather better than Miss Egremont herself,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Well,\' said the vicar, interposing in the wordy war, \'Mrs. Greenleaf\'s\r\nchildren have scarlatina, so we can\'t go to Horton Bishop.  The choice\r\nseems to be between South Beach and Monks Horton.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s no harm,\' cried Nuttie; \'Mrs. Greenleaf is so patronising!\'\r\n\r\n\'And both that and South Beach are so stale,\' said the youth.\r\n\r\n\'As if the dear sea could ever be stale,\' cried the young girl.\r\n\r\n\'I thought Monks Horton was forbidden ground,\' said Miss Mary.\r\n\r\n\'So it was with the last regime\', said the vicar; \'but now the new\r\npeople are come I expect great things from them.  I hear they are very\r\nfriendly.\'\r\n\r\n\'I expect nothing from them,\' said Nuttie so sententiously that all her\r\nhearers laughed and asked \'her exquisite reason,\' as Mr. Dutton put it.\r\n\r\n\'Lady Kirkaldy and a whole lot of them came into the School of Art.\'\r\n\r\n\'And didn\'t appreciate "Head of Antinous by Miss Ursula Egremont,"\' was\r\nthe cry that interrupted her, but she went on with dignity\r\nunruffled--\'Anything so foolish and inane as their whole talk and all\r\ntheir observations I never heard.  "I don\'t like this style," one of\r\nthem said.  "Such ugly useless things!  I never see anything pretty and\r\nneatly finished such as we used to do."\'  The girl gave it in a tone of\r\nmimicry of the nonchalant voice, adding, with fresh imitation, "\'And\r\nanother did not approve of drawing from the life--models might be such\r\nstrange people."\'\r\n\r\n\'My ears were not equally open to their profanities,\' said Miss Mary.\r\n\'I confess that I was struck by the good breeding and courtesy of the\r\nleader of the party, who, I think, was Lady Kirkaldy herself.\'\r\n\r\n\'I saw!  I thought she was patronising you, and my blood boiled!\' cried\r\nNuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Will boiling blood endure a picnic in the park of so much ignorance,\r\nfolly, and patronage?\' asked Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, indeed, Mr. Dutton, Nuttie never said that,\' exclaimed gentle Mrs.\r\nEgremont.\r\n\r\n\'Whether it is fully worth the doing is the question,\' said the vicar.\r\n\r\n\'Grass and shade do not despise,\' said Miss Mary.\r\n\r\n\'There surely must be some ecclesiastical remains,\' said the young man.\r\n\r\n\'And there is a river,\' added the vicar.\r\n\r\n\'I shall get a stickleback for my aquarium,\' cried Nuttie.  \'We shall\r\nmake some discoveries for the Scientific Society.  I shall note down\r\nevery individual creature I see!  I say! you are sure it is not a sham\r\nwaterfall or Temple of Tivoli?\'\r\n\r\n\'It would please the choir boys and G. F. S. girls quite as much, if\r\nnot more, in that case,\' said Miss Mary; \'but you need not expect that,\r\nNuttie.  Landscape-gardening is gone by.\'\r\n\r\n\'Even with the county people?\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'By at least half a century,\' said Mr. Dutton, \'with all deference to\r\nthis young lady\'s experience.\'\r\n\r\n\'It was out of their own mouths,\' cried the girl defiantly.  \'That\'s\r\nall I know about county people, and so I hope it will be.\'\r\n\r\n\'Come in, my dear, you are talking very fast,\' interposed Mrs.\r\nEgremont, with some pain in the soft sweet voice, which, if it had been\r\na little stronger, would have been the best in the choir.\r\n\r\nThese houses in St. Ambrose\'s Road were semi-detached.  The pair which\r\nthe party had reached had their entrances at the angles, with a narrow\r\ngravel path leading by a tiny grass plat to each.  One, which was\r\ncovered with a rich pall of purple clematis, was the home of Mrs.\r\nEgremont, her aunt, and Nuttie; the other, adorned with a Gloire de\r\nDijon rose in second bloom, was the abode of Mary Nugent, with her\r\nmother, the widow of a naval captain.  Farther on, with adjoining\r\ngardens, was another couple of houses, in one of which lived Mr.\r\nDutton; in the other lodged the youth, Gerard Godfrey, together with\r\nthe partner of the principal medical man.  The opposite neighbours were\r\na master of the Modern School and a scholar.  Indeed, the saying of the\r\nvicar, the Rev. Francis Spyers, was, and St. Ambrose\'s Road was proud\r\nof it, that it was a professional place.  Every one had something to do\r\neither with schools or umbrellas, scarcely excepting the doctor and the\r\nsolicitor, for the former attended the pupils and the latter supplied\r\nthem.  Mr. Dutton was a partner in the umbrella factory, and lived, as\r\nthe younger folk said, as the old bachelor of the Road.  Had he not a\r\nhousekeeper, a poodle, and a cat; and was not his house, with lovely\r\nsill boxes full of flowers in the windows, the neatest of the neat; and\r\ndid not the tiny conservatory over his dining-room window always\r\nproduce the flowers most needed for the altar vases, and likewise\r\nbouquets for the tables of favoured ladies. Why, the very daisies never\r\ndurst lift their heads on his little lawn, which even bore a French\r\nlooking-glass globe in the centre. Miss Nugent, or Miss Mary as every\r\none still called her, as her elder sister\'s marriage was recent, was\r\nassistant teacher at the School of Art, and gave private drawing\r\nlessons, so as to supplement the pension on which her mother lived.\r\nThey also received girls as boarders attending the High School.\r\n\r\nSo did Miss Headworth, who had all her life been one of those people\r\nwho seem condemned to toil to make up for the errors or disasters of\r\nothers.  First she helped to educate a brother, and soon he had died to\r\nleave an orphan daughter to be bred up at her cost.  The girl had\r\nmarried from her first situation; but had almost immediately lost her\r\nhusband at sea, and on this her aunt had settled at Micklethwayte to\r\nmake a home for her and her child, at first taking pupils, but when the\r\nHigh School was set up, changing these into boarders; while Mrs.\r\nEgremont went as daily governess to the children of a family of\r\nsomewhat higher pretensions.  Little Ursula, or Nuttie, as she was\r\ncalled, according to the local contraction, was like the child of all\r\nthe party, and after climbing up through the High School to the last\r\nform, hoped, after passing the Cambridge examination, to become a\r\nteacher there in another year.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\n\r\nMONKS HORTON.\r\n\r\n  \'And we will all the pleasures prove,\r\n  By shallow rivers, by whose falls\r\n  Melodious birds sing madrigals.\'--Old Ballad.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was holiday-time, and liberties were taken such as were not\r\npermissible, when they might have afforded a bad precedent to the\r\nboarders.  Therefore, when two afternoons later Mary Nugent, returning\r\nfrom district visiting, came out into her garden behind the house, she\r\nwas not scandalised to see a pair of little black feet under a holland\r\nskirt resting on a laurel branch, and going a few steps more she beheld\r\na big shady hat, and a pair of little hands busy with a pencil and a\r\nblank book; as Ursula sat on the low wall between the gardens, shaded\r\nby the laburnum which facilitated the ascent on her own side.\r\n\r\n\'Oh Miss Mary!  Delicious!  Come up here!  You don\'t know how charming\r\nthis is.\'\r\n\r\nShe moved aside so as to leave the ascent--by an inverted flower-pot\r\nand a laurel branch--open to her friend, thus knocking down one of the\r\npile of books which she had taken to the top of the wall.  Miss Nugent\r\npicked it up, \'Marie Stuart!  Is this your way of studying her?\'\r\n\r\n\'Now, you know \'tis holiday time, and volunteer work; besides, she was\r\nwaiting for you, and I could not help doing this.\'  She held out a\r\nhand, which was scarcely needed, and Mary sprang lightly to share her\r\nperch upon the wall.  \'Look here!\'\r\n\r\n\'Am I to guess the subject as in the game of historic outlines,\' said\r\nMiss Nugent, as the book was laid on her lap.  \'It looks like a\r\nmodern--no, a mediaeval--edition of Marcus Curtius about to leap into\r\nthe capital opening for a young man, only with his dogs instead of his\r\nhorse.  That hound seems very rationally to object.\'\r\n\r\n\'Now don\'t!  Guess in earnest.\'\r\n\r\n\'A compliment to your name.  The Boy of Egremont, poor fellow, just\r\nabout to bound across the strid.\'\r\n\r\n\'Exactly!  I always feel sure that my father must have done something\r\nlike this.\'\r\n\r\n\'Was it so heroic?\' said Miss Mary. \'You know it was for the hundredth\r\ntime, and he had no reason to expect any special danger.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, but his mother was waiting, and he had to go.  Now, I\'ll tell you\r\nhow it must have been with my father.  You know he sailed away in a\r\nyacht before I was born, and poor mother never saw him again; but I\r\nknow what happened.  There was a ship on fire like the Birkenhead, and\r\nthe little yacht went near to pick up the people, and my father called\r\nout, like Sir Humphrey Gilbert--\r\n\r\n          "Do not fear, Heaven is as near\r\n           By water as by land."\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd the little yacht was so close when the great ship blew up that it\r\ngot sucked down in the whirlpool, and rescuers and all died a noble\r\ndeath together!\'\r\n\r\n\'Has your mother been telling you?\' asked Miss Mary.\r\n\r\n\'Oh no! she never mentions him.  She does not know.  No one does; but I\r\nam quite sure he died nobly, with no one to tell the tale, only the\r\nangels to look on, and that makes it all the finer.  Or just suppose he\r\nwas on a desert island all the time, and came back again to find us!  I\r\nsometimes think he is.\'\r\n\r\n\'What?  When you are _quite sure_ of the other theory?\'\r\n\r\n\'I mean I am quite sure while I am thinking about it, or reading\r\nRobinson Crusoe, or the Swiss Family.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!\'\r\n\r\n\'Miss Mary, has no one ever told you anything about my father?\'\r\n\r\n\'No one.\'\r\n\r\n\'They never tell me.  Mother cries, and aunt Ursula puts on her\r\n"there\'s-an-end-of-it look."  Do you think there is anything they are\r\nwaiting to tell me till I am older?\'\r\n\r\n\'If there were, I am sure you had better not try to find it out\r\nbeforehand.\'\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t think I would do anything of _that sort?_  But I thought you\r\nmight know.  Do you remember their first settling here?\'\r\n\r\n\'Scarcely.  I was a very small child then.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Nugent had a few vague recollections which she did not think it\r\nexpedient to mention.  A dim remembrance rose before her of mysterious\r\nwhisperings about that beautiful young widow, and that it had been said\r\nthat the rector of the Old Church had declared himself to know the\r\nladies well, and had heartily recommended them.  She thought it wiser\r\nonly to speak of having been one of their first scholars, telling of\r\nthe awe Miss Headworth inspired; but the pleasure it was to bring a\r\nlesson to pretty Mrs. Egremont, who always rewarded a good one with a\r\nkiss, \'and she was so nice to kiss--yes, and is.\'\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Ursel and mother both were governesses,\' continued the girl, \'and\r\nyet they don\'t want me to go out.  They had rather I was a teacher at\r\nthe High School.\'\r\n\r\n\'They don\'t want to trust their Little Bear out in the world.\'\r\n\r\n\'I think it is more than that,\' said the girl.  \'I can\'t help thinking\r\nthat he--my father--must have been some one rather grand, with such a\r\nbeautiful name as Alwyn Piercefield Egremont.  Yes; I know it was that,\r\nfor I saw my baptismal certificate when I stood for the scholarship; it\r\nwas Dieppe,--Ursula Alice, daughter of Alwyn Piercefield and Alice\r\nElizabeth Egremont, May 15, 1860.  James Everett--I think he was the\r\nchaplain at Dieppe.\'\r\n\r\nMary Nugent thought it the wisest way to laugh and say: \'You, of all\r\npeople in the world, to want to make out a connection with the\r\naristocracy!\'\r\n\r\n\'True love is different,\' said Ursula.  \'He must have been cast off by\r\nhis family for her sake, and have chosen poverty--\r\n\r\n     "To make the croon a pund, my Alwyn gaed to sea,\r\n      And the croon and the pund, they were baith for me."\'\r\n\r\n\r\nMiss Mary did not think a yacht a likely place for the conversion of a\r\ncroon into a pound, and the utter silence of mother and aunt did not\r\nseem to her satisfactory; but she feared either to damp the youthful\r\nenthusiasm for the lost father, or to foster curiosity that might lead\r\nto some painful discovery, so she took refuge in an inarticulate sound.\r\n\r\n\'I think Mr. Dutton knows,\' proceeded Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t mean to ask him?\'\r\n\r\n\'Catch me!  I know how he would look at me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Slang!  A forfeit!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, it\'s holiday time, and the boarders can\'t hear.  There\'s Mr.\r\nDutton\'s door!\'\r\n\r\nThis might in one way be a relief to Miss Nugent, but she did not like\r\nbeing caught upon the wall, and therefore made a rapid descent, though\r\nnot without a moment\'s entanglement of skirt, which delayed her long\r\nenough to show where she had been, as Mr. Dutton was at the same moment\r\nadvancing to his own wall on the opposite side of the Nugent garden.\r\nPerhaps he would have pretended to see nothing but for Nuttie\'s cry of\r\nglee.\r\n\r\n\'You wicked elf,\' said Miss Mary, \'to inveigle people into\r\npredicaments, and then go shouting ho! ho! ho! like Robin Goodfellow\r\nhimself.\'\r\n\r\n\'You should have kept your elevation and dignity like me,\' retorted\r\nUrsula; \'and then you would have had the pleasure of seeing Mr. Dutton\r\nclimbing his wall and coming to our feet.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mischievous elves deserve no good news,\' said Mr. Dutton, who was by\r\nno means so venerable that the crossing the wall was any effort or\r\ncompromise of dignity, and who had by this time joined Mary on her\r\ngrass plat.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, what is it!  Are we to go to Monks Horton?\' cried Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Here is a gracious permission from Lord Kirkaldy, the only\r\nstipulations being that no vestiges of the meal, such as sandwich\r\npapers or gooseberry skins, be left on the grass; and that nobody does\r\nany mischief,\' he added in an awful tone of personality.  \'So if I see\r\nanybody rooting up holly trees I shall be bound to interfere.\'\r\n\r\n\'Now, Mr. Dutton, it was only a baby holly in a chink.\'\r\n\r\n\'Only a holly tree!  Just like the giant\'s daughter when she only\r\ncarried off waggon, peasant, oxen, and all in her pinafore.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is not longer than my finger now!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, remember, mischief either wanton or scientific is forbidden. You\r\nare to set an example to the choir-boys.\'\r\n\r\n\'Scientific mischief is a fatal thing to rare plants,\' said Mary.\r\n\r\n\'If I\'m not to touch anything, I may as well stay at home,\' pouted\r\nNuttie.\r\n\r\n\'You may gather as many buttercups and daisies as the sweet child\r\npleases,\' said Mr. Dutton; whereupon she threatened to throw her books\r\nat his head.\r\n\r\nMiss Nugent asked how they were to go, and Mr. Dutton explained that\r\nthere was only a quarter of a mile\'s walk from the station; that return\r\ntickets would be furnished at a tariff of fourpence a head; and that\r\nthere would be trains at 1.15 and 7.30.\r\n\r\n\'How hungry the children will be.\'\r\n\r\n\'They will eat all the way.  That\'s the worst of this sort of outing.\r\nThey eat to live and live to eat.\'\r\n\r\n\'At least they don\'t eat at church,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Not since the peppermint day, when Mr. Spyers suspended Dickie Drake,\'\r\nput in Mary.\r\n\r\nAnd the Spa Terrace Church people said it was incense.\'\r\n\r\n\'No.  Nuttie!\'\r\n\r\n\'Indeed they did.  Louisa Barnet attacked us about it at school, and I\r\nsaid I wished it had been.  Only they mustn\'t eat peppermint in the\r\ntrain, for it makes mother quite ill.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you mean that Mrs. Egremont will come?\' exclaimed Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, she shall.  It is not too far, and it will be very good for\r\nher.  I shall make her.\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s young England\'s filial duty!\' said Mary.\r\n\r\n\'Why, I know what is good for her, and she always does as "I wish."\'\r\n\r\n\'Beneficent despotism!\' said Mr. Dutton.  \'May I ask if Miss Headworth\r\nis an equally obedient subject.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! Aunt Ursel is very seldom tiresome.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie! Nuttie! my dear,\' and a head with the snows of more than half\r\na century appeared on the other side of the wall, under a cap and\r\nparasol.  \'I am sorry to interrupt you, but it is cool enough for your\r\nmother to go into the town, and I wish you to go with her.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\n\r\nHEIR HUNTING.\r\n\r\n  \'And she put on her gown of green,\r\n  And left her mother at sixteen,\r\n       To marry Peter Bell!\'--WORDSWORTH.\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the shrubberies of Monks Horton were walking a lady somewhat past\r\nmiddle age, but full of activity and vigour, with one of those bright\r\nfaces that never grow old, and with her a young man, a few years over\r\ntwenty, with a grave and almost careworn countenance.\r\n\r\nMore and more confidential waxed the conversation, for the lady was\r\nmaking fresh acquaintance with a nephew seldom seen since he had been\r\nher pet and darling as almost a baby, and he was experiencing the\r\ninexpressible charm of tone and manner that recalled the young mother\r\nhe had lost in early boyhood.\r\n\r\n\'Then your mind is made up,\' she said; \'you are quite right to decide\r\non having a profession; but how does your father take it?\'\r\n\r\n\'He is quite convinced that to repeat my uncle\'s life, dangling on as\r\nheir, would be the most fatal mistake.\'\r\n\r\n\'Assuredly, and all the legal knowledge you acquire is so much in\r\nfavour of your usefulness as the squire.\'\r\n\r\n\'If I ever am the squire, of which I have my doubts.\'\r\n\r\n\'You expect Mr. Egremont to marry?\'\r\n\r\n\'Not a future marriage, but one in the past.\'\r\n\r\n\'A private marriage!  Do you suspect it?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t suspect it--I know it.  I have been hoping to talk the matter\r\nover with you.  Do you remember our first governess, Miss Headworth?\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear Mark, did I not lose at Pera the charms of your infancy?\'\r\n\r\n\'Then neither my mother nor my grandmother ever wrote to you about her?\'\r\n\r\n\'I do remember that it struck me that immunity from governesses was a\r\ncompensation for the lack of daughters.\'\r\n\r\n\'Can you tell me no details,\' said Mark anxiously.  \'Have you no\r\nletters?  It was about the time when Blanche was born, when we were\r\nliving at Raxley.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am sorry to say that our roving life prevented my keeping old\r\nletters.  I have often regretted it.  Let me see, there was one who\r\nboxed May\'s ears.\'\r\n\r\n\'That was long after.  I think it was that woman\'s barbarity that made\r\nmy father marry again, and a very good thing that was.  It was wretched\r\nbefore.  Miss Headworth was in my own mother\'s time.\'\r\n\r\n\'I begin to remember something happening that your mother seemed unable\r\nto write about, and your grandmother said that she had been greatly\r\nupset by "that miserable affair," but I was never exactly told what it\r\nhad been.\'\r\n\r\n\'Miss Headworth came when I was four or five years old.  Edda, as we\r\nused to call her in May\'s language, was the first person who gave me a\r\nsense of beauty.  She had dark eyes and a lovely complexion.  I\r\nremember in after times being silenced for saying, "not so pretty as my\r\nEdda."  I was extremely fond of her, enough to have my small jealousy\r\nexcited when my uncle joined us in our walks, and monopolised her,\r\nturning May and me over to play with his dog!\'\r\n\r\n\'But, Mark, Mr. Egremont is some years older than your father.  He\r\ncould not have been a young man at that time.\'\r\n\r\n\'So much the worse.  Most likely he seemed to her quite paternal. The\r\nnext thing I recollect was our being in the Isle of Wight, we two\r\nchildren, with Miss Headworth and the German nurse, and our being told\r\nof our new sister.  Uncle Alwyn and his yacht were there, and we went\r\non board once or twice.  Then matters became confused with me, I\r\nrecollect a confusion, papa and grandmamma suddenly arriving, everybody\r\nseeming to us to have become very cross, our dear Miss Headworth\r\nnowhere to be found, our attendants being changed, and our being\r\nforbidden to speak of her again.  I certainly never thought of the\r\nmatter till a month ago.  You know my uncle\'s eyes have been much\r\naffected by his illness, and he has made a good deal of use of me. He\r\nhas got a valet, a fellow of no particular country, more Savoyard than\r\nanything else, I fancy.  He is a legacy, like other evils, from the old\r\nGeneral, and seems a sort of necessity to my uncle\'s existence.\r\nGregorio they call him.  He was plainly used to absolute government,\r\nand viewed the coming down amongst us as an assertion of liberty much\r\nagainst his will.  We could see that he was awfully jealous of my\r\nfather and me, and would do anything to keep us out; but providentially\r\nhe can\'t write English decently, though he can speak any language you\r\nplease.  Well, the man and I came into collision about a scamp of a\r\ngroom who was doing intolerable mischief in the village, and whom they\r\nput it on me to get discharged.  On that occasion Mr. Gregorio grew\r\ninsolent, and intimated to me that I need not make so sure of the\r\nsuccession.  He knew that which might make the Chanoine and me change\r\nour note.  Well, my father is always for avoiding rows; he said it was\r\nan unmeaning threat, it was of no use to complain of Gregorio, and we\r\nmust digest his insolence.  But just after, Uncle Alwyn sent me to hunt\r\nup a paper that was missing, and in searching a writing-case I came\r\nupon an unmistakable marriage certificate between Alwyn Piercefield\r\nEgremont and Alice Headworth, and then the dim recollections I told you\r\nof began to return.\'\r\n\r\n\'What did you do?\'\r\n\r\n\'I thought I had better consult my father, expecting to hear that she\r\nwas dead, and that no further notice need be taken of the matter. But\r\nhe was greatly disturbed to hear of the certificate, and would hardly\r\nbelieve me.  He said that some friend of my grandmother had written her\r\nword of goings on at Freshwater between his brother and the young\r\ngoverness, and that they went off at once to put a stop to it, but\r\nfound us left with the German maid, who declared that Miss Headworth\r\nhad gone off with Mr. Egremont in the yacht.  No more was heard of my\r\nuncle for six weeks, and when he came back there was a great row with\r\nthe old General, but he absolutely denied being married.  I am afraid\r\nthat was all the old sinner wished, and they went off together in the\r\nyacht to the West Indies, where it was burnt; but they, as you know,\r\nnever came to England again, going straight off to the Mediterranean,\r\nhaving their headquarters at Sorrento, and cruising about till the\r\nGeneral\'s death ten years ago.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I once met them at Florence, and thought them two weary pitiable\r\nmen.  One looked at the General as a curious relic of the old buck of\r\nthe Regency days, and compassionated his nephew for having had his life\r\nspoilt by dangling after the old man.  It was a warning indeed, and I\r\nam glad you have profited by it, Mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'He came back, after the old man died, to club life in London, and\r\nseldom has been near the old place; indeed, it has been let till\r\nrecently, and he wants to let it again, but it is altogether too\r\ndilapidated for that without repairs.  So he came down to see about it,\r\nand was taken ill there.  But to return to what my father told me. He\r\nwas shocked to hear of the certificate, for he had implicitly believed\r\nhis brother\'s denial of the marriage, and he said Miss Headworth was so\r\nchildish and simple that she might easily have been taken in by a sham\r\nceremony.  He said that he now saw he had done very wrong in letting\r\nhis mother-in-law take all the letters about "that unhappy business"\r\noff his hands without looking at them, but he was much engrossed by my\r\nmother\'s illness, and, as he said, it never occurred to him as a duty\r\nto trace out what became of the poor thing, and see that she was\r\nprovided for safely.  You know Mrs. Egremont says laissez faire is our\r\nfamily failing, and that our first thought is how _not_ to do it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, utter repudiation of such cases was the line taken by the last\r\ngeneration; and I am afraid my mother would be very severe.\'\r\n\r\n\'Another thing that actuated my father was the fear of getting his\r\nbrother into trouble with General Egremont, as he himself would have\r\nbeen the one to profit by it.  So I do not wonder so much at his\r\nletting the whole drop without inquiry, and never even looking at the\r\nletters, which there certainly were.  I could not get him to begin upon\r\nit with my uncle, but Mrs. Egremont was strongly on my side in thinking\r\nthat such a thing ought to be looked into, and as I had found the paper\r\nit would be best that I should speak.  Besides that there was no\r\nenduring that Gregorio should be pretending to hold us in terror by\r\nsuch hints.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, and has there been a wife and family in a cottage all this time?\'\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Margaret, he has never seen or heard of her since he left her at\r\nDieppe!  Would you believe it, he thinks himself a victim?  He never\r\nmeant more than to amuse himself with the pretty little governess; and\r\nhe took on board a Mr. and Mrs. Houghton to do propriety, shady sort of\r\npeople I imagine, but that she did not know.\'\r\n\r\n\'I have heard of them,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, significantly.\r\n\r\n\'She must have been a kind friend to the poor girl,\' said Mark.  \'On\r\nsome report that Lady de Lyonnais was coming down on her, wrathful and\r\nterrible, the poor foolish girl let herself be persuaded to be carried\r\noff in the yacht, but there Mrs. Houghton watched over her like a\r\ndragon.  She made them put in at some little place in Jersey, put in\r\nthe banns, all unknown to my uncle, and got them married. Each was\r\ntrying to outwit the other, while Miss Headworth herself was quite\r\ninnocent and unconscious, and, I don\'t know whether to call it an\r\nexcuse for Uncle Alwyn or not, but to this hour he is not sure whether\r\nit was a legal marriage, and my father believes it was not, looking on\r\nit as a youthful indiscretion.  He put her in lodgings at Dieppe, under\r\nMrs. Houghton\'s protection, while he returned home on a peremptory\r\nsummons from the General.  He found the old man in such a state of body\r\nand mind as he tries to persuade me was an excuse for denying the whole\r\nthing, and from that time he represents himself as bound hand and foot\r\nby the General\'s tyranny.  He meant to have kept the secret, given her\r\nan allowance, and run over from time to time to see her, but he only\r\ncould get there once before the voyage to the West Indies.  The whole\r\naffair was, as he said, complicated by his debts, those debts that the\r\nestate has never paid off.  The General probably distrusted him, for he\r\ncurtailed his allowance, and scarcely let him out of sight; and he--he\r\nsubmitted for the sake of his prospects, and thinking the old man much\r\nnearer his end than he proved to be.  I declare as I listened, it came\r\nnear to hearing him say he had sold his soul to Satan!  From the day he\r\nsailed in the Ninon he has never written, never attempted any\r\ncommunication with the woman whose life he had wrecked, except one\r\ninquiry at Dieppe, and that was through Gregorio.\'\r\n\r\n\'What! the valet?\'\r\n\r\nYes.  I believe I seemed surprised at such a medium being employed, for\r\nUncle Alwyn explained that the man had got hold of the secret\r\nsomehow--servants always know everything--and being a foreigner he was\r\nlikely to be able to trace her out.\r\n\r\n\'I daresay he profited by the knowledge to keep Alwyn in bondage during\r\nthe old man\'s lifetime.\'\r\n\r\n\'I have no doubt of it, and he expected to play the same game with me.\r\nThe fellow reminds me, whenever I look at him, of a sort of incarnate\r\nfamiliar demon.  When I asked my uncle whether he could guess what had\r\nbecome of her, he held up his hands with a hideous French grimace.  I\r\ncould have taken him by the throat.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nay, one must pity him.  The morals of George IV.\'s set had been\r\nhanded on to him by the General,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, rejoicing in the\r\ngenuine indignation of the young face, free from all taint of vice, if\r\nsomewhat rigid.  \'And what now?\'\r\n\r\n\'He assured me that he could make all secure to my father and me, as if\r\nthat were the important point; but finally he perceived that we had no\r\nright to stand still without endeavouring to discover whether there be\r\na nearer heir, and my father made him consent to my making the search,\r\ngrinning at its Quixotism all the time.\'\r\n\r\n\'Have you done anything?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes.  I have been to Jersey, seen the register--July 20, 1859--and an\r\nold French-speaking clerk, who perfectly recollected the party coming\r\nfrom the yacht, and spoke of her as tres belle.  I have also\r\nascertained that there is no doubt of the validity of the marriage.\r\nThen, deeply mistrusting Master Gregorio, I went on to Dieppe, where I\r\nentirely failed to find any one who knew or remembered anything about\r\nthem--there is such a shifting population of English visitors and\r\nresidents, and it was so long ago.  I elicited from my uncle that she\r\nhad an aunt, he thought, of the same name as herself; but my father\r\ncannot remember who recommended her, or anything that can be a clue.\r\nHas any one looked over my grandmother\'s letters?\'\r\n\r\n\'I think not.  My brother spoke of keeping them till I came to London.\r\nThat might give a chance, or the Houghtons might know about her.  I\r\nthink my husband could get them hunted up.  They are sure to be at some\r\ncontinental resort.\'\r\n\r\n\'What\'s that?\' as a sound of singing was heard.\r\n\r\n\'"Auld Langsyne."  The natives are picnicking in the ravine below\r\nthere.  They used to be rigidly excluded, but we can\'t stand that; and\r\nthis is the first experiment of admitting them on condition that they\r\ndon\'t make themselves obnoxious.\'\r\n\r\n\'Which they can\'t help.\'\r\n\r\n\'We have yet to see if this is worse than an Austrian or Italian\r\nfestival.  See, we can look down from behind this yew tree.  It really\r\nis a pretty sight from this distance.\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s the cleric heading his little boys and their cricket, and\r\nthere are the tuneful party in the fern on the opposite side.  They\r\nhave rather good voices, unless they gain by distance.\'\r\n\r\n\'And there\'s a girl botanising by the river.\'\r\n\r\n\'Sentimentalising over forget-me-nots, more likely.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear Mark, for a specimen of young England, you are greatly\r\nbehindhand in perception of progress!\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! you are used to foreigners, Aunt Margaret.  You have never\r\nfathomed English vulgarity.\'\r\n\r\n\'It would serve you right to send you to carry the invitation to go\r\nround the gardens and houses.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you mean it, aunt?\'\r\n\r\n\'Mean it?  Don\'t you see your uncle advancing down the\r\nroad--there--accosting the clergyman--what\'s his name--either Towers or\r\nSpires--something ecclesiastical I know.  We only waited to reconnoitre\r\nand see whether the numbers were unmanageable.\'\r\n\r\n\'And yet he does not want to sit for Micklethwayte?\'\r\n\r\n\'So you think no one can be neighbourly except for electioneering! O\r\nMark, I must take you in hand.\'\r\n\r\n\'Meantime the host is collecting.  I abscond.  Which is the least showy\r\npart of the establishment?\'\r\n\r\n\'I recommend the coal cellar--\'and, as he went off--\'poor boy, he is a\r\ndear good fellow, but how little he knows how to be laughed at!\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\r\n\r\nA NAME.\r\n\r\n  \'Sigh no more lady, lady sigh no more,\r\n    Men were deceivers ever,\r\n  One foot on sea and one on land--\r\n    To one thing constant never.\'--Old Ballad.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'So you have ventured out again,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, as her nephew\r\nstrolled up to her afternoon tea-table under a great cedar tree:\r\n\r\n\'The coast being clear, and only distant shouts being heard in the\r\nravine--\r\n\r\n          \'"Like an army defeated\r\n            The choir retreated;\r\n            And now doth fare well\r\n            In the valley\'s soft swell,"\'\r\n\r\nsaid the aunt.\r\n\r\n\'At least you have survived; or is this the reaction,\' said the nephew,\r\nputting on a languid air.\r\n\r\n\'There were some very nice people among them, on whom the pictures were\r\nby no means thrown away.  What would you say, Mark, if I told you that\r\nI strongly suspect that I have seen your lost aunt?\'\r\n\r\n\'Nonsense!\' cried Mark, as emphatically as disrespectfully.\r\n\r\n\'I am not joking in the least,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, looking up at him.\r\n\'I heard the name of Egremont, and made out that it belonged to a very\r\nlady-like pretty-looking woman in gray and white; she seemed to be\r\ntrying to check and tame a bright girl of eighteen or so, who was in a\r\nperfect state of rapture over the Vandykes.  I managed to ask the\r\nclergyman who the lady was, and he told me she was a Mrs. Egremont, who\r\nlives with her aunt, a Miss Headworth, who boards girls for the High\r\nSchool; very worthy people, he added.\'\r\n\r\n\'Headworth?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes.\'\r\n\r\n\'But if it were, she would have known your name.\'\r\n\r\n\'Hardly.  The title had not come in those days; and if she heard of us\r\nat all it would be as Kerrs.  I ventured further to put out a feeler by\r\nasking whether he knew what her husband had been, and he said he\r\nbelieved he had been lost at sea, but he, Mr. Spyers I mean, had only\r\nbeen at Micklethwayte three or four years, and had merely known her as\r\na widow.\'\r\n\r\n\'I suppose it is worth following up,\' said Mark, rather reluctantly. \'I\r\nwish I had seen her.  I think I should know Miss Headworth again, and\r\nshe would hardly know me.\'\r\n\r\n\'You see what comes of absconding.\'\r\n\r\n\'After all, it was best,\' said Mark.  \'Supposing her to be the real\r\nwoman, which I don\'t expect, it might have been awkward if she had\r\nheard my name!  How can we ascertain the history of this person without\r\ncommitting ourselves?\'\r\n\r\nLord Kirkaldy, an able man, who had been for many years a diplomatist,\r\nhere joined the party, and the whole story was laid before him.  He was\r\nnew to Micklethwayte, having succeeded a somewhat distant kinsman, and\r\ndid not know enough of the place to be able to fix on any one to whom\r\nto apply for information; but the result of the consultation was that\r\nLady Kirkaldy should go alone to call on Miss Headworth, and explain\r\nthat she was come to inquire about a young lady of the same name, who\r\nhad once been governess to the children of her sister, Lady Adelaide\r\nEgremont.  Mark was rather a study to his uncle and aunt all the\r\nevening.  He was as upright and honourable as the day, and not only\r\nacted on high principle, but had a tender feeling to the beautiful\r\nplayfellow governess, no doubt enhanced by painful experiences of\r\nsuccessors chosen for their utter dissimilarity to her.  Still it was\r\nevidently rather flat to find himself probably so near the tangible\r\ngoal of his romantic search; and the existence of a first cousin had\r\nbeen startling to him, though his distaste was more to the taking her\r\nfrom second-rate folk in a country town than to the overthrow of his\r\nown heirship.  At least so he manifestly and honestly believed, and\r\nknowing it to be one of those faiths that make themselves facts, the\r\nKirkaldys did not disturb him in it, nor commiserate him for a loss\r\nwhich they thought the best thing possible for him.\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth was accustomed to receive visitors anent boarders, so\r\nwhen Lady Kirkaldy\'s card was brought to her, the first impression was\r\nthat some such arrangement was to be made.  She was sitting in her\r\npretty little drawing-room alone, for Nuttie and her mother had gone\r\nout for a walk with Miss Nugent.\r\n\r\nThe room, opening on the garden, and cool with blinds, had a certain\r\nhomely grace about the faded furniture.  The drawings on the walls were\r\ngood, the work quaint and tasteful.  There was a grand vase of\r\nfoxgloves before the empty grate, and some Marshal Nial roses in a\r\nglass on the table.  The old lady herself--with alert black eyes and a\r\nsweet expression--rose from her chair in the window to receive her\r\nguest.\r\n\r\nLady Kirkaldy felt reassured as to the refinement of the surroundings,\r\nand liked the gentle but self-possessed tones of the old lady.  She\r\nnoticed the foxgloves.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Miss Headworth, \'they are the fruits of yesterday\'s\r\nexpedition.  My two children, as I call them, brought them home in\r\ntriumph.  I cannot tell you what pleasure Lord Kirkaldy\'s kindness gave\r\nthem--and many more.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am glad,\' said the lady, while she said to herself, \'now for it,\'\r\nand sat forward.  \'It struck me,\' she said, \'on hearing your name that\r\nyou might be related to--to a young lady who lived a good while ago in\r\nthe family of my sister, Lady Adelaide Egremont.\'\r\n\r\nA strange look came into Miss Headworth\'s eyes, her lips trembled, she\r\nclutched tightly the arm of her chair, but then cast a puzzled glance\r\nat her visitor.\r\n\r\n\'Perhaps if you heard of me then,\' said the latter, \'it was as Lady\r\nMargaret Kerr.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Miss Headworth, then pausing, she collected herself and\r\nsaid in an anxious voice,  \'Do I understand that your ladyship is come\r\nto inquire for my niece, being aware of the circumstances.\'\r\n\r\n\'I only became aware of them yesterday,\' said Lady Kirkaldy.  \'I was in\r\nTurkey at the time, and no particulars were given to me; but my nephew,\r\nMark Egremont, your niece\'s old pupil, came to consult us, having just\r\ndiscovered among his uncle\'s papers evidence of the marriage, of which\r\nof course he had been ignorant.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then,\' exclaimed Miss Headworth, holding her hands tightly clasped,\r\n\'Shall I really see justice done at last to my poor child?\'\r\n\r\n\'It is young Mark\'s most earnest wish and his father--\'Lady Kirkaldy\r\nhesitated for a word, and Miss Headworth put in:\r\n\r\n\'His father!  Why would he never even acknowledge either Alice\'s\r\nletters or mine?  We wrote several times both to him and Lady Adelaide,\r\nand never received any reply, except one short one, desiring he might\r\nnot be troubled on such a subject.  It was cruel! Alice said it was not\r\nin his writing.  She had done very wrong, and the family might well be\r\noffended, but a poor child like her, just eighteen, might have been\r\ntreated with some pity.\'\r\n\r\n\'My sister was in declining health.  He was very much engrossed.  He\r\nleft the matter to--to others,\' said Lady Kirkaldy.  \'He is very sorry\r\nnow that he acquiesced in what was then thought right.  He did not then\r\nknow that there had been a marriage.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should have thought in that case a clergyman would have been bound\r\nto show the more compassion.\'\r\n\r\nLady Kirkaldy knew that the cruel silence had been chiefly the work of\r\nthe stem Puritan pitilessness of her mother, so she passed this over,\r\nsaying, \'We are all very anxious to atone, as far as possible, for what\r\nis past, but we know little or nothing, only what my nephew Mark has\r\nbeen able to gather.\'\r\n\r\n\'Little Mark!  Alice always talked of him with great affection.  How\r\npleased she will be to hear of his remembering her.\'\r\n\r\n\'Would you object to telling me what you know of this history?\' said\r\nLady Kirkaldy.  \'I am afraid it is very painful to you, but I think we\r\nshould understand it clearly.  Please speak to me as a friend, as woman\r\nto woman.\'\r\n\r\n\'Your ladyship is very kind,\' said the poor old lady.  \'I have only\r\nmentioned the subject once since we came to settle here, seventeen\r\nyears ago, but such things one cannot forget.  If you will excuse me, I\r\nhave some dates that will assist my accuracy.\'\r\n\r\nShe hurried away, and came back in a few moments, having evidently\r\ndried some tears, perhaps of thankfulness, but she paused as if\r\nreluctant to begin.\r\n\r\n\'I think your niece had no nearer relation than yourself,\' said Lady\r\nKirkaldy, anxious to set her off and at ease.\r\n\r\n\'Oh no, or she never would have been so treated.  She was an orphan. My\r\npoor brother was a curate.  He married--as young men will--on\r\ninsufficient means, his strength gave way, and he died of diphtheria\r\nwhen this poor child was only two years old.  Indeed, two little ones\r\ndied at the same time, and the mother married again and went to\r\nShanghai.  She did not long live there, poor thing, and little Alice\r\nwas sent home to me.  I thought I did my best for her by keeping her at\r\na good school.  I have often wished that I had given up my situation,\r\nand become an assistant there, so as to have her more under my own\r\neyes; but I fancied it important to receive a salary out of which I\r\ncould save.  I am wearying your ladyship, but I can\'t but dwell on the\r\nexcuses for my poor child.\'\r\n\r\n\'Indeed I wish to hear all the details,\' was the sincere and gentle\r\nanswer.\r\n\r\n\'I had her with me generally in the holidays, and I confess I was\r\nabsolutely alarmed to see how pretty the child was growing, knowing how\r\ngreat a disadvantage it often is.  She was always a good girl, not\r\nnaturally so studious as could be wished, but docile, merry, gentle, a\r\nfavourite with every one, and peculiarly innocent and childish.  I\r\nwished her to remain a few years longer as teacher, but it so happened\r\nthat Lady Adelaide Egremont, coming to consult the head of the\r\nestablishment about a nursery-governess, saw Alice, and was so much\r\nstruck with her sweet face, which was all sunshine then, as to insist\r\non engaging her.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! my dear sister, I remember her enthusiastic letter about her\r\npretty governess, and her boy\'s affection for her, an affection that\r\nhas lasted--\'\r\n\r\n\'It seemed so safe.  A clergyman\'s family in the country, and so kind a\r\nlady at the head, that, though Alice had been educated for a superior\r\ngoverness, it appeared the best beginning she could have. And she was\r\nvery happy, and met with great kindness.  Only, unfortunately, Lady\r\nAdelaide was delicate, and for many weeks entirely confined to the\r\nsofa.  Mr. Egremont\'s elder brother was much there.  He seemed to my\r\npoor inexperienced child quite elderly, and his attentions like those\r\nof--of an old uncle--she told me afterwards--\'\r\n\r\n\'He must really have been over forty--\'\r\n\r\n\'No doubt my poor Alice was unguarded.  We know what a merry, happy,\r\nchildish girl may be, but I never heard that her conduct was even\r\ncensured while she remained at Baxley, though I find that Captain\r\nEgremont used to join them in their walks, under pretext of playing\r\nwith the children.  Then she was sent to Freshwater with the two eldest\r\nchildren during Lady Adelaide\'s confinement, and there, most\r\nunjustifiably, Captain Egremont continually visited them from his\r\nyacht, and offered to take them out in it.  Alice knew she ought not to\r\ngo without a married lady on board, and he brought a Mr. and Mrs.\r\nHoughton to call, who were very kind and caressing to her and the\r\nchildren, so that she thought all was right.  Oh! Lady Kirkaldy, I\r\ndon\'t mean to defend her, I daresay she was very giddy and silly, she\r\nreproaches herself, poor dear, but I do say that a wicked advantage was\r\ntaken of her innocence and ignorance.  She says that she had begun to\r\ngrow a little uneasy at the way people looked when Captain Egremont\r\njoined them on the beach; and the nurse, a German, said something that\r\nshe could not understand.  On the 1st of July--yes--but I have the date\r\nhere--came a telegram to the hotel to have rooms for Lady de Lyonnais\r\nand Mr. Egremont ready by the evening.  The whole place knew it, and\r\nsome meddling person burst on Alice with the news, roughly and coarsely\r\ngiven, that they were coming to call her to account for her goings on.\r\nCaptain Egremont found her crying in the utmost terror, and--she really\r\nhardly knew what he said to her--she thinks he offered to shelter her\r\non board the Ninon, from Lady de Lyonnais\' first wrath while he and\r\nMrs. Houghton explained matters; but she cannot tell, for she lost her\r\nsenses with fright, only knew that he was kind and sweet to her in her\r\ndistress, and thought only of escaping.  Well, I don\'t excuse her.  Of\r\ncourse it was the most terrible and fatal thing she could have done,\r\nand--\' The good old lady was quite overcome, and Lady Kirkaldy had\r\ntears in her eyes as she said,\r\n\r\n\'It was frightful folly--but she was guarded.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, her innocence was guarded, thank God,\' said Miss Headworth\r\nfervently.  \'You see she did know that Mr. and Mrs. Houghton were on\r\nboard, and Mrs. Houghton was a truly kind protector who deserved her\r\nconfidence, though, poor lady, she admitted to me that her own conduct\r\nhad not been-strictly correct.\'\r\n\r\n\'How long was it before you heard of her?\'\r\n\r\n\'There was a dreadful letter from Mr. Egremont enclosing what was due\r\nof her salary, and then I heard no more for seven months.  I went to\r\nthe Isle of Wight and made all inquiries, but the nurse and children\r\nhad gone away immediately, and I could obtain no trace of them.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then she--your niece, never wrote.\'\r\n\r\n\'She was afraid, poor dear.  She had never been at her ease with me.\r\nHer mother had taught her to think me strict and harsh, and she had\r\nnever opened to me in those days.  Besides, he had forbidden her.  At\r\nlast, however, in January, came a letter from this Mrs. Houghton,\r\ntelling me that my Alice was very unwell at Dieppe, that nothing had\r\nbeen heard of her husband, Captain Egremont, to whom she had been\r\nmarried on the 20th of July at St. Philippe, in Jersey, and that she\r\nherself was obliged to leave the place almost immediately; but she\r\nwould, if possible, wait till my arrival, as Mrs. Egremont was not in a\r\ncondition to be left alone.  My dear friends, with whom I was then\r\nliving, were as kind as possible, and set me free to go.  I was there\r\nin three days, and truly the dear, beautiful, merry girl I had parted\r\nwith only a year before was a sad piteous sight.  Mrs. Houghton seemed\r\nbroken-hearted at leaving her, thinking there was little chance of her\r\nliving; but Mr. Houghton, who, I am afraid, was a professed gambler,\r\nhad got into some scrape, and was gone to Paris, where she had to\r\nfollow him.  She told me all about it, and how, when Captain Egremont\r\nfancied that a marriage in the Channel Islands was one he could play\r\nfast and loose with, she had taken care that the formalities should be\r\nsuch as to make all secure.  Foolish and wrong as poor Alice had been,\r\nshe had awakened all the best side of that poor woman\'s nature, and no\r\nmother could have been more careful and tender.  She gave me the\r\ncertificate--here it is--and assured me that it would hold good.  I\r\nhave shown it to a lawyer, and he said the same; but when I sent a copy\r\nto Mr. Egremont, my letter was returned unopened.\'\r\n\r\n\'Captain Egremont had denied the marriage, and they believed him,\' said\r\nLady Kirkaldy.  \'It is hard to believe that he could be so heartless,\r\nbut he was in bondage to the old General Egremont, and dreaded losing\r\nhis inheritance.\'\r\n\r\n\'So he told them in his one visit to Dieppe.  He said he must keep his\r\nmarriage secret, but promised an allowance, on condition that Alice\r\nwould live quietly at Dieppe, and not communicate with any one of her\r\nown family or his.  He had left \xa3100 with her, but that was nearly\r\ngone, and she had never heard from him.  It had preyed on her, and she\r\nwas so ill that I never expected, any more than Mrs. Houghton, to see\r\nher recover.  I stayed there with her; she could not be moved, even if\r\nshe would have consented, when she was continually expecting him; but\r\nat last--four days after her little girl was born--came the news of the\r\nNinon having been burnt, with all on board, three months before.  Do\r\nyou know, strange to say, though I had feared so much to tell her, she\r\nbegan to revive from that time.  The suspense and watching were over.\r\nShe saw that he had not deserted her, and believed that he had loved\r\nher to the last.  She cried a great deal, but it was in a peaceful,\r\nnatural way.  I wrote then, as I had already written, to Lady Adelaide\r\nand to Mr. Egremont, but was not answered.\'\r\n\r\n\'I can account for that,\' said Lady Kirkaldy.  \'My sister had been\r\nordered to Madeira in the autumn, and there they remained till her\r\ndeath in May.  All the letters were sent to my mother, and she did not\r\nthink fit to forward, or open, any bearing on the subject.  In the\r\nmeantime Mr. Egremont was presented to the family living, and on his\r\nreturn moved to Bridgefield Egremont.  And you came here?\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course I could not part with my poor Alice again.  Mr. and Mrs.\r\nFordyce, whose daughter I had long ago educated, had always kept up a\r\ncorrespondence with me, and, knowing all the story, proposed to me to\r\ncome here.  He was then rector of the old church, and by their help and\r\nrecommendation, with such capital as I had, we were able to begin a\r\nlittle school; and though that has had to give way to the High School,\r\nwhat with boarders, and with Alice\'s employment as daily governess, we\r\nhave, I am thankful to say, gone on very well and comfortably, and my\r\ndear child has recovered her cheerfulness, though she can never be\r\nquite what--I think she was meant to be,\' said the old lady, with a sad\r\nsmile, \'though perhaps she is something better.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you think she was absolutely convinced of his death?\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you mean that he is alive?\' exclaimed Miss Headworth in dismay.\r\n\'Oh! he is a wickeder man than even I supposed, to have forsaken her\r\nall these years.  Is my poor child in his power?  Must her peace, now\r\nshe has attained it, be disturbed?\'\r\n\r\n\'There is a great deal to take into consideration,\' said Lady Kirkaldy.\r\n\'I had better tell you how this visit of mine came about, and explain\r\nsome matters about the Egremont family.\'\r\n\r\nShe then told how Captain Egremont, after a brief service in the Life\r\nGuards, had been made to retire, that the old General, whose heir he\r\nwas, might keep him in attendance on him.  Already self-indulgent and\r\nextravagant, the idleness of the life he led with the worn-out old roue\r\nhad deadened his better feelings, and habituated him to dissipation,\r\nwhile his debts, his expensive habits, and his dread of losing the\r\ninheritance, had bound him over to the General.  Both had been saved\r\nfrom the fire in the Ninon, whence they were picked up by a Chilian\r\nvessel, and they had been long in communicating with home. The General\r\nhated England, and was in broken health.  He had spent the remaining\r\nyears of his life at various continental resorts, where he could enjoy\r\na warm climate, combined with facilities for high play.\r\n\r\nWhen at length, he died, Captain Egremont had continued the life to\r\nwhich he had become accustomed, and had of late manifested an\r\nexpectation that his nephew Mark should play the same part by him as he\r\nhad done by the General, but the youth, bred in a very different tone,\r\nwould on no account thus surrender himself to an evil bondage. Indeed\r\nhe felt all the severity of youthful virtue, and had little toleration\r\nfor his uncle\'s ways of thinking; though, when the old man had come\r\nhome ill, dejected, and half blind, he had allowed himself to be made\r\nuseful on business matters.  And thus he had discovered the marriage,\r\nand had taken up the cause with the ardour stimulated by a chivalrous\r\nfeeling for the beautiful vision of his childhood, whose sudden\r\ndisappearance had ended his brightest days.\r\n\r\n\'I suppose it is right and generous of the young man,\' said Miss\r\nHeadworth.  \'But since the--the man is alive, I wish my poor Alice\r\ncould have been left at peace!\'\r\n\r\n\'You forget that her daughter has rights which must be taken into\r\nconsideration.\'\r\n\r\n\'Little Nuttie!  Dear child!  I should so far like her to be provided\r\nfor, so far as that she need not go out in the world to earn her own\r\nlivelihood.  But no! better be as we are than accept anything from that\r\nman!\'\r\n\r\n\'I quite understand and respect your feeling, Miss Headworth,\' returned\r\nthe lady; \'but may I return to my question whether you think your niece\r\nhas any doubt of her husband being dead.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth considered.  \'Since you ask me, I think she has kept the\r\npossibility of the life before her.  We have never mentioned the\r\nsubject, and, as I said, the belief in his death ended a great suspense\r\nand sense of wounded affection.  She began soon and vigorously to turn\r\nher attention to the support of her child, and has found a fair measure\r\nof happiness; but at the same time she has shrunk from all notice and\r\nsociety, more than would be natural in so very young a widow and so\r\nattractive, more than I should have expected from her original\r\ncharacter.  And once, when she did apprehend symptoms of admiration,\r\nshe insisted that I should tell the history, enough, as she said, to\r\nmake it plain that it was impossible.  There was one night too, when\r\nshe had scarlatina, and was a little lightheaded, only four years ago,\r\nwhen she talked a good deal about his coming back; but that might have\r\nbeen only the old impression on her brain, of that long watching at\r\nDieppe.  He--Captain Egremont, does not yet know where she is?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, certainly not.  But I fear he must.\'\r\n\r\n\'I suppose he ought,\' sighed Miss Headworth; \'but in the meantime, till\r\nwe know what line he takes, surely she need not be unsettled by the\r\nknowledge of his existence.\'\r\n\r\n\'By no means.  You had better act as you think best about that.  But\r\nyou will not object to my nephew, her old pupil, Mark, coming to see\r\nher?  I will make him promise not to enter upon the subject.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth had only time to make a sign of reluctant acquiescence\r\nwhen the door opened and mother and daughter came in.  Nuttie first,\r\neager as usual and open-mouthed, unaware that any one was there, for\r\nLady Kirkaldy, wishing to avoid talk and observation, had left her\r\ncarriage at the livery stables, and walked to St. Ambrose Road.  The\r\ngirl, whom in a moment she classed as small, dark, and oddly like May\r\nEgremont, stopped short at sight of a stranger; the mother would have\r\nretreated but for Miss Headworth\'s nervous call \'Alice, my dear, here\r\nis Lady Kirkaldy.\'\r\n\r\nVery lovely was Lady Kirkaldy\'s impression as she saw a slender figure\r\nin a dark gray linen dress, and a face of refined, though not\r\nintellectual, beauty and sweetness, under a large straw hat with a good\r\ndeal of white gauziness about it, and the curtsey was full of natural\r\ngrace.\r\n\r\n\'You do not know me,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, taking her hand, \'but I am\r\naunt to some former pupils of yours, one of whom, Mark Egremont, is\r\nvery anxious to come and see you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mark!  My dear little Mark,\' and her face lighted up.  \'How very kind\r\nof him.  But he is not little Mark now.\'\r\n\r\n\'He is not a very big Mark either.  Most of the Egremonts are small. I\r\nsee your daughter takes after them,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, shaking hands\r\nwith Ursula, who looked at her in unmitigated amazement.\r\n\r\nAlice faltered something about Lady Adelaide.\r\n\r\n\'My dear sister fell into a decline, and died while the three children\r\nwere still babies.  Poor things, I believe they had a sad time till\r\ntheir father married a Miss Condamine, who has been an excellent\r\nstepmother to them.  I have been to see them, but Mark was not then at\r\nhome, so he has come to me at Monks Horton.  When will he find you at\r\nhome?  Or may I bring him in at once.  He was to meet me at\r\nMicklethwayte.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should like very much to see him,\' was the answer.  And Miss\r\nHeadworth was obliged to say something about her ladyship taking a cup\r\nof tea.  Lady Kirkaldy, knowing that Mark was on the watch, set off in\r\nsearch of him, and found him, as she expected, pacing the pavement in\r\nfront of the church.  There was no great distance in which to utter her\r\nexplanations and cautions, warning him of her promise that the\r\nintelligence of the husband\'s being alive was to be withheld for a\r\nfitter time, but he promised dutifully, and his aunt then took him in\r\nwith her.\r\n\r\nThe recognition of her claims was a less stunning shock to Alice\r\nEgremont than to her aunt.  Shielded by her illness, as well as by her\r\nsimplicity and ignorance, she had never been aware of her aunt\'s\r\nattempted correspondence with the Egremonts, nor of their deafness to\r\nappeals made on her behalf.  Far less had it ever occurred to her that\r\nthe validity of her marriage could be denied, and the heinous error of\r\nher elopement seemed to her quite sufficient to account for her having\r\nbeen so entirely cast off by the family.  The idea that as wife or\r\nwidow she had any claims on them, or that Ursula might have rights\r\nabove those of Mark, had not come into her mind, which, indeed, at the\r\nmoment was chiefly occupied by the doubt whether the milk was come in,\r\nand by ordering in the best teacups, presented by the boarders.\r\n\r\nThus she was in the passage when Mark entered, and his exclamation\r\ninstantly was \'Oh, Edda, dear old Edda!  You aren\'t a bit altered!\' and\r\nhe put his head under her hat and kissed her, adding, as she seemed\r\nrather startled, \'You are my aunt, you know; and where\'s my cousin?\r\nYou are Ursula?\'\r\n\r\nHe advanced upon Nuttie, took her by the hand and kissed her forehead\r\nbefore she was aware, but she flashed at him with her black eyes, and\r\nlooked stiff and defiant.  She had no notion of kisses to herself,\r\nstill less to her pretty mother whom she protected with a half proud,\r\nhalf jealous fondness.  How could the man presume to call her by that\r\nfoolish name?  However, that single effusion had exhausted Mark\'s\r\npowers of cordiality, or else Nuttie\'s stiffness froze him.  They were\r\nall embarrassed, and had reason to be grateful to Lady Kirkaldy\'s\r\npractised powers as a diplomate\'s wife.  She made the most of Mrs.\r\nEgremont\'s shy spasmodic inquiries, and Mark\'s jerks of information,\r\nsuch as that they were all living at Bridgefield Egremont, now, that\r\nhis sister May was very like his new cousin, that Blanche was come out\r\nand was very like his mother, etc. etc.  Every one was more at ease\r\nwhen Lady Kirkaldy carried the conversation off to yesterday\'s\r\nentertainment, hoping no one had been overtired, and the like.  Mrs.\r\nEgremont lighted up a little and began telling some of the expressions\r\nof delight she had heard, and in the midst, Nuttie, waking from her\r\ntrance of stiff displeasure, came plump in with \'Oh! and there\'s a\r\nwater-soldier, a real Stratiotes aculeatus in your lake.  May we get\r\nit?  Mr. Dutton didn\'t think we ought, but it would be such a prize!\'\r\n\r\n\'Ursula means a rare water-plant,\' said Mrs. Egremont gently, seeing\r\nthat Lady Kirkaldy had no notion of the treasure she possessed.  \'She\r\nand some of her friends are very eager botanists.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am sure you may,\' said the lady, amused.\r\n\r\n\'Thank you!  Then, O mother!  Miss Mary and I will go.  And we\'ll wait\r\ntill after office hours, and then Gerard Godfrey can come and fish it\r\nout for us!  Oh, thank you.  He wants the pattern of the Abbot\'s cross\r\nfor an illumination, and he can get some ferns for the church.\'\r\n\r\nSoon after this ebullition, Lady Kirkaldy carried off her nephew, and\r\nhis first utterance outside the door was \'A woman like that will be the\r\nsalvation of my uncle.\'\r\n\r\n\'Firstly, if you can bring them together,\' said his aunt; \'and\r\nsecondly, if there is stuff enough in that pretty creature.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\n\r\nSUSPENSE.\r\n\r\n  \'Where shall the traitor rest\r\n  He, the deceiver?\'--SCOTT.\r\n\r\n\r\nPoor Miss Headworth\'s peace of mind was utterly destroyed.  That the\r\nniece whom she had nursed back to life and happiness, and brought to\r\nlove her as a mother, should be at the mercy of a man whom she looked\r\non as a heartless profligate, was dreadful to her beyond measure. And\r\nit involved Ursula\'s young life likewise?  Could it be a duty, after\r\nthese eighteen years, to return to him?  What legal rights had he to\r\nenforce the resumption of the wife he had deserted.  \'I will consult\r\nMr. Dutton,\' said the old lady to herself; \'Mr. Dutton is the only\r\nperson who knows the particulars.  He will give me the best advice.\'\r\n\r\nAnd while Miss Headworth, over her evening toilette, was coming to this\r\nresolution in one bedroom, Nuttie, in another, was standing aghast at\r\nher mother\'s agitation, and receiving a confession which filled her\r\nwith astonishment.\r\n\r\n\'I can\'t think why that gentleman should go and be so affectionate all\r\non a sudden, \'quoth Nuttie;\' if he is my cousin, and so fond of you,\r\nwhy couldn\'t he have come to see us before?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Nuttie, dear, you don\'t understand why it is so good of him!  My\r\ndear, now this has come, I must tell you--you must hear--the sad thing\r\nyour mother did.  Yes, my dear, I was their governess--and--and I did\r\nnot--In short, my dear, I eloped.\'\r\n\r\n\'You, mother!  Oh what fun!\' cried the girl in the utter extremity of\r\nwonder.\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie!\' exclaimed Mrs. Egremont, in a tone of horror and\r\nindignation--nay, of apprehension.\r\n\r\n\'O mother--I didn\'t mean that!  But I can\'t get to believe it.  You,\r\nlittle mother mine, you that are so timid and bashful and quiet. That\r\nyou--you should have done such a thing.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie, my dear, can\'t you understand that such a thing would make me\r\nquiet?  I am always feeling when I see people, or they bring their\r\ndaughters here.  "If they only knew--"\'\r\n\r\n\'No, no, no!  They would still see you were the sweetest dear.  But\r\ntell me all about it.  How very much in love you must have been!\' said\r\nNuttie, a magnificent vision of a young sailor with curly hair and open\r\nthroat rising before her.\r\n\r\n\'I think I was more frightened than in love,\' faintly said Mrs.\r\nEgremont.  \'At least I didn\'t know it was love, I thought he was only\r\nkind to me.\'\r\n\r\n\'But you liked it?\' said Ursula magisterially.\r\n\r\n\'I liked it, oh, I liked it!  It gave me a feeling such as nothing else\r\never did, but I never thought of its being love, he was so much older.\'\r\n\r\n\'Older!\' exclaimed Nuttie, much taken aback.  \'Oh! as old as Mr.\r\nDutton?\'\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton is thirty-six, I think.  Yes, he was older than that.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mother, how could you?\'  For to be older than Mr. Dutton seemed to the\r\nyouthful fancy to be near decrepitude; but she added, \'I suppose he was\r\nvery noble, and had done great things.\'\r\n\r\n\'He was the grandest gentleman I ever saw, and had such, a manner,\'\r\nsaid the mother, passing over the latter suggestion.  \'Anyway, I never\r\nthought what it all meant--all alone with the children as I was--till I\r\nfound people looking at me, and laughing at me, and then I heard Lady\r\nde Lyonnais and Mr. Egremont were coming down, very angry, to send me\r\naway.  I ought, I know it now, to have waited, for they would have\r\nwritten to my aunt.  But I was horribly frightened, and I couldn\'t bear\r\nto think of never seeing him again, and he came and comforted me, and\r\nsaid he would take me to Mrs. Houghton, the kind lady who was staying\r\nin the Ninon, and they would make it all square for me--and then--oh!\r\nit was very sweet--but I never knew that we were sailing away to Jersey\r\nto be married!  I knew it was very dreadful without any one\'s leave,\r\nbut it was so noble of him to take the poor little governess and defend\r\nher, and it wasn\'t as if my mother had been alive.  I didn\'t know Aunt\r\nUrsel then as I did afterwards.  And Mrs. Houghton said there was\r\nnothing else to be done.\'\r\n\r\n\'O don\'t leave off, mother.  Do tell me.  How long did you have him?\'\r\n\r\n\'Six weeks then--and afterwards one fortnight at Dieppe.  He was not\r\nfree.  He had an old uncle, General Egremont, who was sick and\r\nhot-tempered, and he was obliged to keep everything secret from him,\r\nand therefore from everybody else.  And so I was to live at Dieppe,\r\nwhile he went out to take care of his uncle, and you know--you know--\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I know, dear mother.  But I am sure he was saving somebody else,\r\nand it was a noble death!  And I know how Aunt Ursel came to Dieppe,\r\nand how I--your own little Frenchwoman--came to take care of you.  And\r\nhaven\'t we been jolly without any of these fine relations that never\r\nlooked after you all this time?  Besides, you know he is very likely to\r\nbe on a lonely coral island, and will come home yet. I often think he\r\nis.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear child, I have been happier than I deserved,\' said Alice\r\nEgremont, drying her eyes.  \'But oh! Nuttie, I hope you will be a wiser\r\nwoman than your mother.\'\r\n\r\n\'Come, don\'t go on in that way!  Why, I\'ve such advantages!  I\'ve Miss\r\nMary, and Aunt Ursel, and Mr. Spyers, and Mr. Dutton, and you, you poor\r\nlittle thing, had nobody!  One good thing is, we shall get the\r\nwater-soldier.  Mr. Dutton needn\'t come, for he\'s like a cat, and won\'t\r\nsoil his boots, but Gerard is dying to get another look at the old\r\nruin.  He can\'t make up his mind about the cross on one of the\r\nstone-coffin lids, so he\'ll be delighted to come, and he\'ll get it out\r\nof the pond for us.  I wonder when we can go.  To-night is choir\r\npractice, and to-morrow is cutting-out day.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth was not sorry that the small sociabilities of the\r\nfriends did not leave her alone with her niece all that evening, or the\r\nnext day, when there was a grand cutting-out for the working party,--an\r\noperation always performed in the holidays.  Miss Headworth had of late\r\nyears been excused from it, and it gave her the opportunity she wanted\r\nof a consultation with Mr. Dutton.  He was her prime adviser in\r\neverything, from her investments (such as they were) to the\r\neccentricities of her timepieces; and as the cuckoo-clock had that\r\nnight cuckooed all the hours round in succession, no one thought it\r\nwonderful that she should send a twisted note entreating him to call as\r\nearly as he could in the afternoon.  Of course Nuttie\'s chatter had\r\nproclaimed the extraordinary visitors, and it needed not the old lady\'s\r\ndash under "on an anxious affair" to bring him to her little\r\ndrawing-room as soon as he could quit his desk.  Perhaps he hastened\r\nhis work with a hope in his heart which he durst not express, but the\r\nagitation on the usually placid face forbade him to entertain it for an\r\ninstant, and he only said, \'So our expedition has led to unforeseen\r\nconsequences, Miss Headworth.\'  And then she answered under her breath,\r\nas if afraid of being overheard: \'Mr. Dutton, my poor child does not\r\nknow it yet, but the man is alive!\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton compressed his lips.  It was the greater shock, for he had\r\nactually made inquiries at the Yacht Club, but the officials there\r\neither had not been made aware of the reappearance of the two\r\nEgremonts, or they did not think it worth while to look beyond the\r\nrecord which declared that all hands had perished, and the connection\r\nof the uncle and nephew with the Yacht Club had not been renewed.\r\nPresently he said, \'Then hers was a right instinct.  There is reason to\r\nbe thankful.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth was too full of her own anxieties to heed his causes for\r\nthankfulness.  She told what she had heard from Lady Kirkaldy and from\r\nMark Egremont, and asked counsel whether it could be Alice\'s duty to\r\nreturn to the man who had deserted her, or even to accept anything from\r\nhim.  There was an impetuous and indignant spirit at the bottom of the\r\nold lady\'s heart, in spite of the subdued life she had led for so many\r\nyears, and she hardly brooked the measured considerate manner in which\r\nher adviser declared that all depended on circumstances, and the manner\r\nin which Captain Egremont made the first move.  At present no one was\r\nacting but young Mark, and, as Mr. Dutton observed, it was not a matter\r\nin which a man was very likely to submit to a nephew\'s dictation.\r\n\r\nThere was certainly no need for Mrs. Egremont to _force_ her presence\r\non him.  But Mr. Dutton did think that for her own sake and her child\'s\r\nthere ought to be full recognition of their rights, and that this\r\nshould be proved by their maintenance.\r\n\r\n\'I imagine that Ursula may probably be a considerable heiress, and her\r\nlights must not be sacrificed.\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor little girl!  Will it be for her happiness?  I doubt it greatly!\'\r\n\r\n\'Of that I suppose we have no right to judge,\' said Mr. Dutton,\r\nsomewhat tremulously. \'Justice is what we have to look to, and to allow\r\nNuttie to be passed over would be permitting a slur to be cast on her\r\nand her mother.\'\r\n\r\n\'I see that,\' said Miss Headworth, with an effort.  \'I suppose I am\r\nafter all a selfish, faithless old woman, and it is not in my hands\r\nafter all.  But I must prepare my poor Alice for what may be coming.\'\r\n\r\n\'If any terms are offered to her, she had better put the matter into a\r\nlawyer\'s hands.  Dobson would be a safe man to deal with.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth was amazed that he--who had helped her in many a little\r\nquestion bordering on law--should not proffer his aid now in this\r\ngreatest stress.  He was a resolute, self-controlled man, and she never\r\nguessed at the feeling that made him judge himself to be no fitting\r\nchampion for Alice Egremont against her husband.  Ever since, ten years\r\nago, he had learnt that his beautiful neighbour did not regard herself\r\nso certainly a widow as to venture to open her heart to any other love,\r\nhe had lived patiently on, content to serve her as a trustworthy\r\nfriend, and never betraying the secret hope so long cherished and now\r\nentirely crushed.\r\n\r\nHe was relieved to escape from the interview, and the poor old lady\r\nremained a little more certain as to her duty perhaps, but with a\r\ncertainty that only made her more unhappy, and she was so restless and\r\nnervous that, in the middle of the evening\'s reading of Archbishop\r\nTrench\'s Lectures on History, Alice suddenly broke off in the very\r\nmiddle of a sentence and exclaimed, \'Aunt Ursel! you are keeping\r\nsomething from me.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth made a faint attempt by saying something about\r\npresently, and glancing with her eyes to indicate that it was to be\r\nreserved till after Nuttie\'s bedtime, but the young lady comprehended\r\nthe signs and exclaimed, \'Never mind me, Aunt Ursel,--I know all about\r\nmother; she told me last night.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is!\' broke in Mrs. Egremont, who had been watching her aunt\'s face.\r\n\'You have heard of _him_.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, my father!  You really have!\' cried Nuttie.  \'Then he really was\r\non the desert island all this time; I was quite sure of it.  How\r\ndelightful!\'  She jumped up and looked at the door, as if she expected\r\nto see him appear that instant, clad in skins like Robinson Crusoe, but\r\nher aunt\'s nervous agitation found vent in a sharp reproof: \'Nuttie,\r\nhold your tongue, and don\'t be such a foolish child, or I shall send\r\nyou out of the room this instant!\'\r\n\r\n\'But aunt?\' gasped Alice, unable to bear the suspense.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, my poor dear child.  Captain Egremont with the General got off\r\nwith some of the crew in a boat when the Ninon was burnt.  He spent a\r\ngood many years abroad with the old man, but he has now inherited the\r\nfamily place, and is living there.\'  Miss Headworth felt as if she had\r\nfired a cannon and looked to see the effect.\r\n\r\n\'Ah, if we could have stayed at Dieppe!\' said Mrs. Egremont.  \'But we\r\ndid write back to say where we could be heard of.\'\r\n\r\n\'That was of no use.  Mark found no traces of us when he went thither.\'\r\n\r\n\'Did he send Mark?\'\r\n\r\n\'No.  My dear Alice, I must not conceal from you that this is all Mr.\r\nMark Egremont\'s doing.  He seems to have been helping his uncle with\r\nhis papers when he came on the evidence of your marriage, and,\r\nremembering you as he does, he forced the confession of it from the\r\ncaptain, and of his own accord set forth to discover what had become of\r\nyou and to see justice done to you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Dear little Mark!\' said she; \'he always was such an affectionate\r\nlittle boy.\'\r\n\r\n\'And now, my dear, you must consider how you will receive any advances\r\non his part.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Aunt Ursel, don\'t!  I can\'t talk now.  Please let me go to bed.\r\nNuttie, dear, you need not come yet.\'\r\n\r\nThe desire for solitude, in which to realise what she had heard, was\r\noverpowering, and she fled away in the summer twilight, leaving Nuttie\r\nwith wide open eyes, looking after her vanished hero and desert island.\r\n\r\n\'My poor Alice!\' sighed the old lady.\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Ursel!\' exclaimed Nuttie, \'was--I mean--is my father a good or a\r\nbad man?\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, should a daughter ask such a question?\'\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Ursel, I can\'t help it.  I think I ought to know all about it,\'\r\nsaid Nuttie gravely, putting away her childishness and sitting down by\r\nher aunt.  \'I did not think so much of it when mother told me they\r\neloped, because, though I know it was very wrong, people do do odd\r\nthings sometimes when they are very much in love (she said it in a\r\nsuperior patronising tone that would have amused Miss Headworth very\r\nmuch at any other time); and it has not spoilt mother for being the\r\ndearest, sweetest, best thing in the world, and, besides, they had\r\nneither of them any fathers or mothers to disobey.  But, then, when I\r\nfound he was so old, and that he kept it a secret, and must have told\r\nstories only for the sake of money (uttered with extreme contempt), I\r\ndidn\'t like it.  And if he left her as Theseus left Ariadne, or Sir\r\nLancelot left Elaine, I--I don\'t think it is nice.  Do you think he\r\nonly pretended to be lost in the Ninon to get rid of her, or that he\r\ncould not find her?\'\r\n\r\n\'The Ninon was really reported lost with all on board,\' said Miss\r\nHeadworth.  \'That was ascertained.  He was saved by a Chilian ship, and\r\nseems to have been a good while making his way back to Europe.
