I had\r\ntaken care that our address should be known at Dieppe, but it is quite\r\npossible that he may not have applied to the right people, or that they\r\nmay not have preserved my letter, so that we cannot feel sore that he\r\nwas to blame.\'\r\n\r\n\'If he had been worth anything at all, he would have moved heaven and\r\nearth to find her!\' cried Nuttie; \'and you said yourself it was all\r\n_that_ Mark\'s doing!\'\r\n\r\n\'He seems to be a very upright and generous young man, that Mr. Mark\r\nEgremont,\' said Miss Headworth, a whole romance as to Nuttie\'s future\r\ndestiny sweeping across her mind in an instant, with a mental\r\ndispensation to first cousins in such a case.  \'I think you will find\r\nhim a staunch champion even against his own interests.\'\r\n\r\nPerceptions came across Nuttie.  \'Oh, then I am a sort of lost heiress,\r\nlike people in a story!  I see!  But, Aunt Ursel, what do you think\r\nwill happen?\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear child, I cannot guess in the least.  Perhaps the Egremont\r\nproperty will not concern you, and only go to male heirs.  That would\r\nbe the best thing, since in any case you must be sufficiently provided\r\nfor.  Your father must do that.\'\r\n\r\n\'But about mother?\'\r\n\r\n\'A proper provision must be insisted on for her,\' said Miss Headworth.\r\n\'It is no use, however, to speculate on the future.  We cannot guess\r\nhow Mr. Mark Egremont\'s communication will be received, or whether any\r\nwish will be expressed for your mother\'s rejoining your father.  In\r\nsuch a case the terms must be distinctly understood, and I have full\r\ntrust both in Mr. Mark and in Lady Kirkaldy as her champions to see\r\nthat justice is done to you both.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sure he doesn\'t deserve that mother should go to him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nor do I expect that he will wish it, or that it would be proper; but\r\nhe is bound to give her a handsome maintenance, and I think most\r\nprobably you will be asked to stay with your uncle and cousins,\' said\r\nMiss Headworth, figuring to herself a kind of Newstead Abbey or some\r\nsuch scene of constant orgies at Bridgefield Egremont.\r\n\r\n\'I shall accept nothing from the family that does not include mother,\'\r\nsaid Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Dear child, I foresee many trials, but you must be her protector.\'\r\n\r\n\'That I will,\' said Nuttie; and in the gallant purpose she went to bed,\r\nto find her mother either asleep or feigning slumber with tears on her\r\ncheek.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\n\r\nTHE WATER-SOLDIER.\r\n\r\n  \'Presumptuous maid, with looks intent,\r\n  Again she stretched, again she bent,\r\n  Nor knew the gulf between.\'--GRAY.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt all seemed like a dream to Ursula, perhaps likewise to her mother,\r\nwhen they rose to the routine of daily life with the ordinary interests\r\nof the day before them.  There was a latent unwillingness in Mrs.\r\nEgremont\'s mind to discuss the subject with either aunt or daughter;\r\nand when the post brought no letter, Ursula, after a moment\'s sense of\r\nflatness, was relieved, and returned to her eager desire to hurry after\r\nthe water-soldier.  It was feasible that very afternoon.  Mary Nugent\r\ncame in with the intelligence.\r\n\r\n\'And can Gerard come? or we shall only look at it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, Gerard can come, and so will Mr. Dutton,\' said Mary, who,\r\nstanding about half-way between Mrs. Egremont and her daughter, did not\r\nthink herself quite a sufficient chaperon.\r\n\r\n\'He will look on like a hen at her ducklings,\' said Nuttie.  \'It is\r\ncruel to take him, poor man!\'\r\n\r\n\'Meantime, Nuttie, do you like an hour of "Marie Stuart?"\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, thank you!\'  But she whispered, \'Aunt Ursel, may I tell her?\'\r\n\r\n\'Ask your mother, my dear.\'\r\n\r\nLeave was given, half reluctantly, and with a prohibition against\r\nmentioning the subject to any one else, but both mother and aunt had\r\nconfidence in Mary Nugent\'s wisdom and discretion, so the two friends\r\nsat on the wall together, and Ursula poured out her heart.  Poor little\r\ngirl! she was greatly discomfited at the vanishing of her noble vision\r\nof the heroic self-devoted father, and ready on the other hand to\r\nbelieve him a villain, like Bertram Risingham, or \'the Pirate,\' being\r\npossessed by this idea on account of his West Indian voyages.  At any\r\nrate, she was determined not to be accepted or acknowledged without her\r\nmother, and was already rehearsing magnanimous letters of refusal.\r\n\r\nMiss Mary listened and wondered, feeling sometimes as if this were as\r\nmuch a romance as the little yacht going down with the burning ship;\r\nand then came back the recollection that there was a real fact that\r\nNuttie had a father, and that it was entirely uncertain what part he\r\nmight take, or what the girl might be called on to do.  Considering\r\nanxiously these bearings of the question, she scarcely heard what she\r\nwas required to assent to, in one of Nuttie\'s eager, \'Don\'t you think\r\nso?\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear Nuttie,\' she said, rousing herself, \'what I do think is that\r\nit will all probably turn out exactly contrariwise to our imaginations,\r\nso I believe it would be wisest to build up as few fancies as possible,\r\nbut only to pray that you may have a right judgment in all things, and\r\nhave strength to do what is right, whatever you may see that to be.\'\r\n\r\n\'And of course that will be to stick by mother.\'\r\n\r\n\'There can be little doubt of that, but the how?  No, dear, do not let\r\nus devise all sorts of _hows_ when we have nothing to go upon. That\r\nwould be of no use, and only perplex you when the time comes. It would\r\nbe much better to "do the nexte thinge," and read our "Marie Stuart."\'\r\n\r\nNuttie pouted a little, but submitted, though she now and then broke\r\ninto a translation with \'You know mother will never stand up for\r\nherself,\' or \'They think I shall be asked to stay with the Egremonts,\r\nbut I must work up for the exam.\'\r\n\r\nHowever, the school habit of concentrating her attention prevailed, and\r\nthe study quieted Nuttie\'s excitement.  The expedition took place as\r\narranged.  There was a train which stopped so that the party could go\r\ndown by it, and the distance was not too great for walking back.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton met them on the platform, well armed with his neat silk\r\numbrella, and his black poodle, Monsieur, trotting solemnly after him.\r\nGerard Godfrey bore materials for an exact transcript of the Abbot\'s\r\nmonumental cross, his head being full of church architecture, while\r\nNuttie carried a long green tin case, or vasculum as she chose to call\r\nit, with her three vowels, U A E, and the stars of the Little Bear\r\nconspicuously painted on it in white.\r\n\r\n\'You did not venture on that the other day,\' said Mr. Dutton.  \'How\r\nmuch of the park do you mean to carry away in it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Let me take it,\' said Gerard politely.\r\n\r\n\'No, thank you.  You\'d leave it behind, while you were pottering over\r\nthe mouldings.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are much more likely to leave it behind yourself.\'\r\n\r\n\'What--with my soldier, my Stratiotes, in it?  I think I see myself.\'\r\n\r\n\'Give it to me,\' said Gerard.  \'Of course I can\'t see you carrying a\r\ngreat thing like that.\'\r\n\r\n\'Can\'t you, indeed?\'\r\n\r\n\'Gently, gently, my dear,\' said Miss Mary, as the young people seemed\r\nvery near a skirmish, and the train was sweeping up.  Then there was\r\nanother small scuffle, for Nuttie had set her heart on the third class;\r\nbut Mr. Dutton had taken second-class tickets, and was about to hand\r\nthem into a carriage whence there had just emerged a very supercilious\r\nblack-moustached valet, who was pulling out a leather-covered\r\ndressing-case, while Gerard was consoling Nuttie by telling her that\r\nMonsieur never deigned to go third class.\r\n\r\n\'It is a smoking carriage,\' said Miss Nugent, on the step.  \'Pah! how\r\nit smells,\' as she jumped back.\r\n\r\n\'Beautiful backy--a perfect nosegay,\' said Gerard.\r\n\r\n\'Trust that fellow for having the best.\'\r\n\r\n\'His master\'s, no doubt,\' suggested Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'You\'d better go in it, to enjoy his reversion,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'And where\'s my escort, then?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I\'m sure we don\'t want you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie, my dear,\' expostulated Miss Nugent, dragging her into the next\r\ncarriage.\r\n\r\n\'You may enjoy the fragrance still,\' said Nuttie when seated.  \'Do you\r\nsee--there\'s the man\'s master; he has stood him up against that post,\r\nwith his cigar, to wait while he gets out the luggage.  I daresay you\r\ncan get a whiff if you lean out far enough.\'\r\n\r\n\'I say! that figure is a study!\' said Gerard.  \'What is it that he is\r\nso like?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! I know,\' said Nuttie.  \'It is Lord Frederick Verisopht, and the\r\nbad gentlefolks in the pictures to the old numbers of Dickens that you\r\nhave got, Miss Mary.  Now, isn\'t he?  Look! only Lord Frederick wasn\'t\r\nfat.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was in a state of excitement that made her peculiarly\r\nunmanageable, and Miss Nugent was very grateful to Mr. Dutton for his\r\nsharp though general admonition against staring, while, under pretext\r\nof disposing of the umbrella and the vasculum, he stood up, so as to\r\nblock the window till they were starting.\r\n\r\nThere was no one else to observe them but a demure old lady, and in ten\r\nminutes\' time they were in open space, where high spirits might work\r\nthemselves off, though the battle over the botanical case was ended by\r\nMiss Nugent, who strongly held that ladies should carry their own extra\r\nencumbrances, and slung it with a scarf over Nuttie\'s shoulders in a\r\nknowing knapsack fashion.\r\n\r\nThe two young people had known one another all their lives, for Gerard\r\nwas the son of a medical man who had lived next door to Miss Headworth\r\nwhen the children were young.  The father was dead, and the family had\r\nleft the place, but this son had remained at school, and afterwards had\r\nbeen put into the office at the umbrella factory under charge of Mr.\r\nDutton, whose godson he was, and who treated him as a nephew.  He was a\r\ngood-hearted, steady young fellow, with his whole interest in\r\necclesiastical details, wearing a tie in accordance with \'the colours,\'\r\nand absorbed in church music and decorations, while his recreations\r\nwere almost all in accordance therewith.\r\n\r\nThere was plenty of merriment, as he drew and measured at the very\r\nscanty ruins, which were little more than a few fragments of wall,\r\novergrown luxuriantly with ivy and clematis, but enclosing some fine\r\nold coffin-lids with floriated crosses, interesting to those who cared\r\nfor architecture and church history, as Mr. Dutton tried to make the\r\nchildren do, so that their ecclesiastical feelings might be less\r\nnarrow, and stand on a surer foundation than present interest, a\r\nslightly aggressive feeling of contempt for all the other town\r\nchurches, and a pleasing sense of being persecuted.\r\n\r\nThey fought over the floriations and mouldings with great zest, and\r\neach maintained a date with youthful vigour--both being, as Mr. Dutton\r\nby and by showed them, long before the foundation.  The pond had been\r\nleft to the last with a view to the wellbeing of the water-soldier on\r\nthe return.  Here the difficulties of the capture were great, for the\r\nnearest plant flourished too far from the bank to be reached with\r\ncomfort, and besides, the sharp-pointed leaves to which it owes its\r\nname were not to be approached with casual grasps.\r\n\r\n\'Oh Monsieur, I wish you were a Beau,\' sighed Nuttie.  \'Why, are you\r\ntoo stupid to go and get it?\'\r\n\r\n\'It is a proof of his superior intelligence,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'But really it is too ridiculous--too provoking--to have come all this\r\nway and not get it,\' cried the tantalised Nuttie.  \'Oh, Gerard, are you\r\ntaking off your boots and stockings?  You duck!\'\r\n\r\n\'Just what I wish I was,\' said the youth, rolling up his trousers.\r\n\r\nBut even the paddling in did not answer.  Mr. Dutton called out\r\nanxiously, \'Take care, Gerard, the bottom may be soft,\' and came down\r\nto the very verge just in time to hold out his hand, and prevent an\r\nutterly disastrous fall, for Gerard, in spite of his bare feet, sank at\r\nonce into mud, and on the first attempt to take a step forward, found\r\nhis foot slipping away from under him, and would in another instant\r\nhave tumbled backwards into the slush and weeds.  He scrambled back,\r\nhis hat falling off into the reeds, and splashing Mr. Dutton all over,\r\nwhile Monsieur began to bark \'with astonishment at seeing his master in\r\nsuch a plight,\' declared the ladies, who stood convulsed with cruel\r\nlaughter.\r\n\r\n\'Isn\'t it dreadful?\' exclaimed Ursula.\r\n\r\n\'Well!  It might have been worse,\' gravely said Mr. Dutton, wiping off\r\nthe more obnoxious of his splashes with his pocket handkerchief.\r\n\r\n\'Oh I didn\'t mean you, but the water-soldier,\' said Nuttie.  \'To have\r\ncome five miles for it in vain!\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t know what to suggest,\' added Gerard.  \'Even if the ladies were\r\nto retire--\'\r\n\r\n\'No, no,\' interposed Mr. Dutton, \'\'tis no swimming ground, and I forbid\r\nthe expedient.  You would only be entangled in the weeds.\'\r\n\r\n\'Behold!\' exclaimed Mary, who had been prowling about the banks, and\r\nnow held up in triumph one of the poles with a bill-hook at the end\r\nused for cutting weed.\r\n\r\n\'Bravo, Miss Nugent!\' cried Gerard.\r\n\r\n\'Female wit has circumvented the water-soldier,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t cry out too soon,\' returned Mary; \'the soldier may float off and\r\nescape you yet.\'\r\n\r\nHowever, the capture was safely accomplished, without even a dip under\r\nwater to destroy the beauty of the white flowers.  With these, and a\r\nfew waterlilies secured by Gerard for the morrow\'s altar vases, the\r\nparty set out on their homeward walk, through plantations of whispering\r\nfirs, the low sun tingeing the trunks with ruddy light; across heathery\r\ncommons, where crimson heath abounded, and the delicate blush-coloured\r\nwax-belled species was a prize; by cornfields in ear hanging out their\r\ndainty stamens; along hedges full of exquisite plumes of feathering or\r\nnodding grass, of which Nuttie made bouquets and botanical studies, and\r\nGerard stored for harvest decorations.  They ran and danced on together\r\nwith Monsieur at their heels, while the elders watched them with some\r\nsadness and anxiety. Free-masonry had soon made both Mary and Mr.\r\nDutton aware of each other\'s initiation, and they had discussed the\r\nmatter in all its bearings, agreed that the man was a scoundrel, and\r\nthe woman an angel, even if she had once been weak, and that she ought\r\nto be very resolute with him if he came to terms.  And then they looked\r\nafter their young companions, and Mr. Dutton said, \'Poor children, what\r\nis before them?\'\r\n\r\n\'It is well they are both so young,\' answered Mary.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\n\r\nTHAT MAN.\r\n\r\n  \'It is the last time--\'tis the last!\'--SCOTT.\r\n\r\n\r\nSundays were the ever-recurring centres of work and interests to the\r\nlittle circle in St. Ambrose\'s Road.  To them the church services and\r\nthe various classes and schools were the great objects and excitements\r\nof the week.  A certain measure of hopeful effort and varying success\r\nis what gives zest to life, and the purer and higher the aim, and the\r\nmore unmixed the motives, the greater the happiness achieved by the\r\n\'something attempted, something done.\'\r\n\r\nSetting apart actual spiritual devotion, the altar vases, purchased by\r\na contribution of careful savings, and adorned with the Monks Horton\r\nlilies, backed by ferns from the same quarter; the surplices made by\r\nthe ladies themselves, the chants they had practised, the hymns they\r\nhad taught, could not but be much more interesting to them than if they\r\nhad been mere lookers on.  Every cross on the markers, every flower on\r\nthe altar cloth was the work of one or other of them; everything in the\r\nchurch was an achievement, and choir boys, school children, Bible\r\nclasses, every member of the regular congregation, had some special\r\ninterest; nay, every irregular member or visitor might be a convert in\r\ntime--if not a present sympathiser, and at the very least might swell\r\nthe offertory that was destined to so many needs of the struggling\r\ndistrict.\r\n\r\nThus it was with some curiosity mingled with self-reproach that Nuttie,\r\nwhile singing her Benedictus among the tuneful shop-girls, to whom she\r\nwas bound to set an example, became aware of yesterday\'s first-class\r\ntraveller lounging, as far as the rows of chairs would permit, in the\r\naisle, and, as she thought, staring hard at her mother.  It was well\r\nthat Mrs. Egremont\'s invariable custom was never to lift her eyes from\r\nher book or her harmonium, or she surely must have been disconcerted,\r\nher daughter thought, by the eyes that must have found her out, under\r\nher little black net bonnet and veil, as the most beautiful woman in\r\nchurch,--as she certainly was,--even that fine good-for-nothing\r\ngentleman thinking so.  Nuttie would add his glances to the glories of\r\nher lovely mother!\r\n\r\nAnd she did so, with triumph in her tone of reprobation, as she trotted\r\noff, after the early dinner, to her share of Sunday-school work as\r\nusual under Miss Nugent\'s wing.  It began with a children\'s service,\r\nand then ensued, in rooms at the factory, lent by Mr. Dutton, the\r\nteaching that was to supply the omissions of the Board School; the\r\nestablishment of a voluntary one being the next ambition of St.\r\nAmbrose\'s.\r\n\r\nComing home from their labours, in the fervent discussion of their\r\nscholars, and exchanging remarks and greetings with the other teachers\r\nof various calibres, the friends reached their own road, and there, to\r\ntheir amazement, beheld Miss Headworth.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, it really is!\' cried Nuttie.  \'We can\'t be too late?  No--there\'s\r\nno bell!  Aunt Ursel!  What has brought you out?  What\'s the matter?\r\nWhere\'s mother?\'\r\n\r\n\'In the house.  My dear,\' catching hold of her, and speaking\r\nbreathlessly, \'I came out to prepare you.  He is come--your father--\'\r\n\r\n\'Where?\' cried Nuttie, rather wildly.\r\n\r\n\'He is in the drawing-room with your mother.  I said I would send you.\'\r\nPoor Miss Headworth gasped with agitation.  \'Oh! where\'s Mr.\r\nDutton--not that anything can be done--\'\r\n\r\n\'Is it _that man_?\' asked Nuttie, and getting no answer, \'I know it is!\r\nOh Aunt Ursel, how could you leave her with him?  I must go and protect\r\nher.  Gerard--come.  No, go and fetch Mr. Dutton.\'\r\n\r\n\'Hush! hush, Nuttie,\' cried her aunt, grasping her.  \'You know nothing\r\nabout it.  Wait here till I can tell you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Come in here, dear Miss Headworth,\' said Mary, gently drawing her arm\r\ninto hers, for the poor old lady could hardly stand for trembling, and\r\nbidding Gerard open the door of her own house with the latch-key.\r\n\r\nShe took them into the dining-room, so as not to disturb her mother,\r\nsent Gerard off after Mr. Dutton in the very uttermost astonishment and\r\nbewilderment, and set Miss Headworth down in an easy-chair, where she\r\nrecovered herself, under Mary\'s soothing care, enough to tell her story\r\nin spite of Nuttie\'s exclamations.  \'Wait! wait, Nuttie!  You mustn\'t\r\nburst in on them so!  No, you need not be afraid.  Don\'t be a silly\r\nchild!  He won\'t hurt her!  Oh no!  They are quite delighted to meet.\'\r\n\r\n\'Delighted to meet?\' said Nuttie, as if transfixed.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said her aunt.  \'Oh yes, I always knew the poor child cared for\r\nhim and tried to believe in him all along.  He only had to say the\r\nword.\'\r\n\r\n\'I wouldn\'t,\' cried the girl, her eyes flashing.  \'Why didn\'t you ask\r\nhim how he could desert her and leave her?\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear! how can one come between husband and wife?  Oh, my poor\r\nAlice!\'\r\n\r\n\'How was it, how did they meet, dear Miss Headworth?\' asked Mary,\r\nadministering the wine she had been pouring out.\r\n\r\n\'You hadn\'t been gone half an hour, Alice was reading to me, and I was\r\njust dozing, when in came Louisa.  "A gentleman to see Mrs. Egremont,"\r\nshe said, and there he was just behind.  We rose up--she did not know\r\nhim at once, but he just said "Edda, my little Edda, sweeter than ever,\r\nI knew you at once," or something of that sort, and she gave one little\r\ncry of "I knew you would come," and sprang right into his arms.\r\nI--well, I meant to make him understand how he had treated her, but\r\njust as I began "Sir"--he came at me with his hand outstretched--\'\r\n\r\n\'You didn\'t take it, aunt, I hope?\' cried Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'My dear, when you see him, you will know how impossible it is.  He\r\n_has_ that high-bred manner it is as if he were conferring a favour.\r\n"Miss Headworth, I conclude," said he, "a lady to whom I owe more than\r\nI can express."  Just as if I had done it for his sake.\'  Miss Nugent\r\nfelt this open expression dangerous on account of the daughter, and she\r\nlooked her consternation at Mr. Dutton, who had quietly entered,\r\nruthlessly shutting Gerard Godfrey out with only such a word of\r\nexplanation as could be given on the way.\r\n\r\n\'Then he comes with--with favourable intentions,\' said Mary, putting as\r\nmuch admonition as she could into her voice.\r\n\r\n\'Oh! no doubt of that,\' said Miss Headworth, drawing herself together.\r\n\'He spoke of the long separation,--said he had never been able to find\r\nher, till the strange chance of his nephew stumbling on her at Abbots\r\nNorton.\'\r\n\r\n\'That is--possib--probably true,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'It can\'t be,\' broke in Nuttie.  \'He never troubled himself about it\r\ntill his nephew found the papers.  You said so, Aunt Ursel!  He is a\r\ndreadful traitor of a man, just like Marmion, or Theseus, or Lancelot,\r\nand now he is telling lies about it!  Don\'t look at me. Aunt Ursel,\r\nthey are lies, and I _will_ say it, and he took in poor dear mother\r\nonce, and now he is taking her in again, and I can\'t bear that he\r\nshould be my father!\'\r\n\r\nIt was so entirely true, yet so shocking to hear from her mouth, that\r\nall three stood aghast, as she stood with heaving chest, crimson\r\ncheeks, and big tears in her eyes.  Miss Headworth only muttered, \'Oh,\r\nmy poor child, you mustn\'t!\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton prevented another passionate outburst by his tone of grave,\r\ngentle authority.  \'Listen a moment, Ursula,\' he said.  \'It is\r\nunhappily true that this man has acted in an unjustifiable way towards\r\nyour mother and yourself.  But there are, no doubt, many more excuses\r\nfor him than you know of, and as I found a few years ago that the\r\npeople at Dieppe had lost the address that had been left with them, he\r\nmust have found no traces of your mother there.  You cannot understand\r\nthe difficulties that may have been in his way.  And there is no use,\r\nquite the contrary, in making the worst of him.  He has found your\r\nmother out, and it seems that he claims her affectionately, and she\r\nforgives and welcomes him--out of the sweet tenderness of her heart.\'\r\n\r\n\'She may--but I can\'t,\' murmured Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'That is not a fit thing for a daughter, nor a Christian, to say,\' Mr.\r\nDutton sternly said.\r\n\r\n\'\'Tis not for myself--\'tis for her,--\'objected Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'That\'s nonsense; a mere excuse,\' he returned.  \'You have nothing at\r\nall to forgive, since he did not know you were in existence.  And as to\r\nyour mother, whom you say you put first, what greater grief or pain can\r\nyou give her than by showing enmity and resentment against her husband,\r\nwhen she, the really injured person, loves and forgives?\'\r\n\r\n\'He\'s a bad man.  If she goes back to him, I know he will make her\r\nunhappy--\'\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t know any such thing, but you do know that your opposition\r\nwill make her unhappy.  Remember, there\'s no choice in the matter. He\r\nhas legal rights over you both, and since he shows himself ready (as I\r\nunderstand from Miss Headworth that he is) to give her and you your\r\nproper position, you have nothing to do but to be thankful.  I think\r\nmyself that it is a great subject of thankfulness that your mother can\r\nreturn so freely without any bitterness.  It is the blessing of such as\r\nshe--\'\r\n\r\nNuttie stood pouting, but more thoughtful and less violent, as she\r\nsaid, \'How can I be thankful?  I don\'t want position or anything.  I\r\nonly want him to let my--my own mother, and aunt, and me alone.\'\r\n\r\n\'Child, you are talking of what you do not understand.  You must not\r\nwaste any more time in argument.  Your mother has sent for you, and it\r\nis your duty to go and let her introduce you to your father.  I have\r\nlittle doubt that you will find him very unlike all your imagination\r\nrepresents him, but let that be as it may, the fifth Commandment does\r\nnot say, "Honour only thy good father," but, "Honour thy father."  Come\r\nnow, put on your gloves--get her hat right, if you please, Miss Mary.\r\nThere--now, come along, be a reasonable creature, and a good girl, and\r\ndo not give unnecessary pain and vexation to your mother.\'  He gave her\r\nhis arm, and led her away.\r\n\r\n\'Well done, Mr. Dutton!\' exclaimed Miss Nugent.\r\n\r\n\'Poor Mr. Dutton!\'  All Aunt Ursel\'s discretion could not suppress that\r\nsigh, but Mary prudently let it pass unnoticed, only honouring in her\r\nheart the unselfishness and self-restraint of the man whose long,\r\npatient, unspoken hopes had just received a death-blow.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Mary!  I never thought it would have been like this!\' cried the\r\npoor old lady.  \'I ought not to have spoken as I did before the child,\r\nbut I was so taken by surprise!  Alice turned to him just as if he had\r\nbeen the most faithful, loving husband in the world.  She is believing\r\nevery word he says.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is very happy for her that she can,\' pleaded Mary.\r\n\r\n\'So it is, yes, but--when one knows what he is, and what she is!  Oh,\r\nMr. Dutton, is the poor child gone in?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I saw her safe into the room.  She was very near running off up\r\nthe stairs,\' said Mr. Dutton.  \'But I daresay she is fascinated by this\r\ntime.  That sort of man has great power over women.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie is hardly a woman yet,\' said Miss Nugent.\r\n\r\n\'No, but there must be a strong reaction, when she sees something\r\nunlike her compound of Marmion and Theseus.\'\r\n\r\n\'I suppose there is no question but that they must go with him!\' said\r\nMiss Headworth wistfully.\r\n\r\n\'Assuredly.  You say he--this Egremont--was affectionate,\' said Mr.\r\nDutton quietly, but Mary saw his fingers white with his tight clenching\r\nof the bar of the chair.\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, warmly affectionate, delighted to find her prettier than ever,\r\npoor dear; I suppose he meant it.  Heaven forgive me, if I am judging\r\nhim too hardly, but I verily believe he went to church to reconnoitre,\r\nand see whether she pleased his fancy--\'\r\n\r\n\'And do you understand,\' added Mr. Dutton, \'that he is prepared to do\r\nher full justice, and introduce her to his family and friends as his\r\nwife, on equal terms?  Otherwise, even if she were unwilling to stand\r\nup for herself, it would be the duty of her friends to make some\r\nstipulations.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am pretty sure that he does,\' said the aunt; \'I did not stay long\r\nwhen I saw that I was not wanted, but I heard him say something about\r\nhis having a home for her now, and her cutting out the Redcastle\r\nladies.\'\r\n\r\n\'Besides, there is the nephew, Mr. Mark Egremont,\' said Mary.  \'He will\r\ntake care of her.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Mr. Dutton.  \'It appears to be all right.  At any rate,\r\nthere can be no grounds for interference on our part.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton took his leave with these words, wringing Miss Headworth\'s\r\nhand in mute sympathy, and she, poor old lady, when he was gone, fairly\r\ncollapsed into bitter weeping over the uncertain future of those whom\r\nshe had loved as her own children, and who now must leave her desolate.\r\nMary did her best with comfort and sympathy, and presently took her to\r\nshare her griefs and fears with gentle old Mrs. Nugent.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\n\r\nTHE FATHER.\r\n\r\n  \'I do think this lady\r\n  To be my child.\'--King Lear.\r\n\r\n\r\nNuttie, in her fresh holland Sunday dress, worked in crewels with wild\r\nstrawberries by her mother\'s hands, and with a white-trimmed straw hat,\r\nwas almost shoved into the little drawing-room by Mr. Dutton, though he\r\nwas himself invisible.\r\n\r\nHer eyes were in such a daze of tears that she hardly saw more at first\r\nthan that some one was there with her mother on the sofa.  \'Ah, there\r\nshe is!\' she heard her mother cry, and both rose.  Her mother\'s arm was\r\nround her waist, her hand was put into another, Mrs. Egremont\'s voice,\r\ntremulous with exceeding delight, said, \'Our child, our Ursula, our\r\nNuttie!  Oh, this is what I have longed for all these years!  Oh,\r\nthanks, thanks!\' and her hands left her daughter to be clasped and\r\nuplifted for a moment in fervent thanksgiving, while Nuttie\'s hand was\r\nheld, and a strange hairy kiss, redolent of tobacco-smoking, was on her\r\nforehead--a masculine one, such as she had never known, except her\r\ncousin Mark\'s, since the old rector died, and she had grown too big for\r\nMr. Dutton\'s embraces.  It was more strange than delightful, and yet\r\nshe felt the polish of the tone that said, \'We make acquaintance\r\nsomewhat late, Ursula, but better late than never.\'\r\n\r\nShe looked up at this new father, and understood instantly what she had\r\nheard of his being a grand gentleman.  There was a high-bred look about\r\nhim, an entire ease and perfect manner that made everything he did or\r\nsaid seem like gracious condescension, and took away the power of\r\nquestioning it at the moment.  He was not above the middle size, and\r\nwas becoming unwieldy; but there was something imposing and even\r\ngraceful in his deportment, and his bald narrow forehead looked\r\naristocratic, set off between side tufts of white hair, white whiskers,\r\nand moustaches waxed into sharp points, Victor Emmanuel fashion, and a\r\nround white curly beard.  His eyes were dark, and looked dull, with\r\nyellow unwholesome corners, and his skin was not of a pleasant colour,\r\nbut still, with all Nuttie\'s intentions of regarding him with horror,\r\nshe was subdued, partly by the grand breeding and air of distinction,\r\nand partly by the current of sympathy from her mother\'s look of perfect\r\nhappiness and exultation. She could not help feeling it a favour,\r\nalmost an undeserved favour, that so great a personage should say, \'A\r\ncomplete Egremont, I see. She has altogether the family face.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am so glad you think so,\' returned her mother.\r\n\r\n\'On the whole it is well, but she might have done better to resemble\r\nyou, Edda,\' he said caressingly; \'but perhaps that would have been too\r\nmuch for the Earlsforth natives.  William\'s girls will have enough to\r\nendure without a double eclipse!\' and he laughed.\r\n\r\n\'I--I don\'t want--\' faltered the mother.\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t want, no, but you can\'t help it,\' he said, evidently with a\r\nproud delight in her beauty.  \'Now that I have seen the child,\' he\r\nadded, \'I will make my way back to the hotel.\'\r\n\r\n\'Will you--won\'t you stay to tea or dinner?\' said his wife, beginning\r\nwith an imploring tone which hesitated as she reviewed possible chops\r\nand her aunt\'s dismay.\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, I have ordered dinner at the hotel,\' he answered, \'and\r\nGregorio is waiting for me with a cab.  No doubt you will wish to make\r\narrangements with Madame--the old lady--and I will not trouble her\r\nfurther to-night.  I will send down Gregorio to-morrow morning, to tell\r\nyou what I arrange.  An afternoon train, probably, as we shall go no\r\nfarther than London.  You say Lady Kirkaldy called on you.  We might\r\nreturn her visit before starting, but I will let you know when I have\r\nlooked at the trains.  My compliments to Miss Headworth.  Good evening,\r\nsweetest.\'  He held his wife in a fond embrace, kissing her brow and\r\ncheeks and letting her cling to him, then added, \'Good evening, little\r\none,\' with a good-natured careless gesture with which Nuttie was quite\r\ncontent, for she had a certain loathing of the caresses that so charmed\r\nher mother.  And yet the command to make ready had been given with such\r\neasy authority that the idea of resisting it had never even entered her\r\nmind, though she stood still while her mother went out to the door with\r\nhim and watched him to the last.\r\n\r\nComing back, she threw her arms round her daughter, kissed her again\r\nand again, and, with showers of the glad tears long repressed, cried,\r\n\'Oh, my Nuttie, my child, what joy!  How shall I be thankful enough!\r\nYour father, your dear father!  Now it is all right.\'  Little sentences\r\nof ecstasy such as these, interspersed with caresses, all in the\r\nincoherence of overpowering delight, full of an absolute faith that the\r\nlost husband had loved her and been pining for her all these years, but\r\nthat he had been unable to trace her, and was as happy as she was in\r\nthe reunion.\r\n\r\nThe girl was somewhat bewildered, but she was carried along by this\r\nflood of exceeding joy and gladness.  The Marmion and Theseus images\r\nhad been dispelled by the reality, and, with Mr. Dutton\'s sharp reproof\r\nfresh upon her, she felt herself to have been doing a great injustice\r\nto her father; believed all that her mother did, and found herself the\r\nobject of a romantic recognition--if not the beggar girl become a\r\nprincess, at any rate, the little school-teacher a county lady!  And\r\nshe had never seen her mother so wildly, overpoweringly happy with joy.\r\nThat made her, too, feel that something grand and glorious had happened.\r\n\r\n\'What are we going to do?\' she asked, as the vehemence of Mrs.\r\nEgremont\'s emotion began to work itself off.\r\n\r\n\'Home!  He takes us to his home!  _His_ home!\' repeated her mother, in\r\na trance of joy, as the yearnings of her widowed heart now were\r\nfulfilled.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, but Aunt Ursel!\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor Aunt Ursel!  Oh, Nuttie, Nuttie, I had almost forgotten!  How\r\ncould I?\' and there was a shower of tears of compunction.  \'But he said\r\nhe owed everything to her!  She will come with us!  Or if she doesn\'t\r\nlive with us, we will make her live close by in a dear little cottage.\r\nWhere is she?  When did she go?  I never saw her go.\'\r\n\r\nThe sound of the front door was heard, for the visitor had been watched\r\naway and Miss Headworth was returning to her own house to be there\r\nreceived with another fervent gush of happiness, much more trying to\r\nher, poor thing, than to Nuttie.\r\n\r\nThere was evensong imminent, and the most needful act at the moment was\r\nto compose the harmonium-player sufficiently for her to take her part.\r\nMiss Headworth was really glad of the necessity, since it put off the\r\ndiscussion, and made a reason for silencing Nuttie until all should be\r\nmore recovered from the first agitation.  Alice Egremont herself was\r\nglad to carry her gratitude and thankfulness to the Throne of Grace,\r\nand in her voluntary, and all her psalms, there was an exulting strain\r\nthat no one had thought the instrument capable of producing, and that\r\nwent to the heart of more than one of her hearers.  No one who knew her\r\ncould doubt that hers was simply innocent exultation in the recovery of\r\nhim whom she so entirely loved and confided in.  But there could not\r\nbut be terrible doubts whether he were worthy of that trust, and what\r\nthe new page in her life would be.\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth had said they would not talk till after church, but\r\nthere was no deferring the matter then.  She was prepared, however,\r\nwhen her niece came up to her in a tender deprecating manner, saying,\r\n\'Aunt Ursel, dear Aunt Ursel, it does seem very ungrateful, but--\'\r\n\r\n\'He is going to take you away?  Yes, I saw that.  And it ought to be,\r\nmy dear.  You know where?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; to London first, to be fitted out, and then to his own home. To\r\nBridgefield Egremont.  I shall have to see Mr. Egremont,\' and her voice\r\nsank with shame.  \'But Mark will be good to me, and why should I care\r\nwhen I have him.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is quite right. I am glad it should be so,\' firmly said the old\r\nlady.\r\n\r\n\'And yet to leave you so suddenly.\'\r\n\r\n\'That can\'t be helped.\'\r\n\r\n\'And it will only be for a little while,\' she added, \'till you can make\r\narrangements to come to us.  My dear husband says he owes you\r\neverything.  So you must be with us, or close to us.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, it\'s very dear and good of you to think of it, but I must be\r\nindependent.\'  She put it in those words, unwilling again to speak\r\nunguardedly before Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, dear auntie, indeed you must!  Think what you are to us, and what\r\nyou have done for us.  We can\'t go away to be happy and prosperous and\r\nleave you behind.  Can we, Nuttie?  Come and help me to get her to\r\npromise.  Do--do dearest auntie,\' and she began the coaxing and\r\ncaressing natural to her, but Nuttie did not join in it, and Miss\r\nHeadworth shook her head and said gravely--\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t, Alice.  It is of no use.  I tell you once for all that my mind\r\nis made up.\'\r\n\r\nAlice, knowing by long experience that, when her aunt spoke in that\r\ntone, persuasion was useless, desisted, but looked at her in\r\nconsternation, with eyes swimming in tears.  Nuttie understood her a\r\nlittle better, and felt the prickings of distrust again.\r\n\r\n\'But, aunt, dear aunt, how can we leave you?  What will you do with all\r\nthe boarders,\' went on Mrs. Egremont.\r\n\r\n\'I shall see my way, my dear.  Do not think about that.  It is a great\r\nthing to see you and this child receive justice.\'\r\n\r\n\'And only think, after all the hard things that have been said of him,\r\nthat we should meet first at church!  He would not wait and send\r\nletters and messages by Mark.  You see he came down himself the first\r\nmoment.  I always knew he would.  Only I am so sorry for him, that he\r\nshould have lost all those sweet years when Nuttie was a tiny child.\r\nShe must do all she can to make up to him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh dear!\' broke out Nuttie.  \'It is so strange!  It will be all so\r\nstrange!\'\r\n\r\n\'It will be a very new life,\' said her aunt, rather didactically; \'but\r\nyou must do your best to be a good daughter, and to fill your new\r\nposition, and I have no doubt you will enjoy it.\'\r\n\r\n\'If I could but take all with me!\' said Nuttie.  \'Oh dear! whatever\r\nwill you do, Aunt Ursel?  Oh mother, the choir!  Who will play the\r\nharmonium? and who will lead the girls? and whatever will Mr. Spyers\r\ndo? and who will take my class?  Mother, couldn\'t we stay a little\r\nlonger to set things going here?\'\r\n\r\n\'It is nice of you to have thought of it, my dear,\' said Mrs. Egremont,\r\n\'but your father would not like to stay on here.\'\r\n\r\n\'But mightn\'t I stay, just a few days, mother, to wish everybody\r\ngood-bye?  Mr. Dutton, and Miss Mary, and Gerard, and all the girls?\'\r\n\r\nThere was some consolation in this plan, and the three women rested on\r\nit that night, Mrs. Egremont recovering composure enough to write three\r\nor four needful notes, explaining her sudden departure.  The aunt could\r\nnot talk of a future she so much dreaded for her nieces, losing in it\r\nthe thought of her own loneliness; Alice kept back her own loving,\r\ntender, undoubting joy with a curious sense that it was hard and\r\nungrateful towards the aunt; but it was impossible to think of that,\r\nand Nuttie was in many moods.\r\n\r\nEager anticipation of the new unseen world beyond, exultation in\r\nfinding herself somebody, sympathy with her mother\'s happiness, all had\r\ntheir share, but they made her all the wilder, because they were far\r\nfrom unmixed.  The instinctive dislike of Mr. Egremont\'s countenance,\r\nand doubt of his plausible story, which had vanished before his\r\npresence, and her mother\'s faith, returned upon her from time to time,\r\ncaught perhaps from her aunt\'s tone and looks.  Then her aunt had been\r\nlike a mother to her--her own mother much more like a sister, and the\r\nquitting her was a wrench not compensated for as in Mrs. Egremont\'s\r\ncase by a more absorbing affection.  Moreover, Nuttie felt sure that\r\npoor Gerard Godfrey would break his heart.  As the mother and daughter\r\nfor the last time lay down together in the room that had been theirs\r\nthrough the seventeen years of the girl\'s life, Alice fell asleep with\r\na look of exquisite peace and content on her face, feeling her long\r\nterm of trial crowned by unlooked-for joy, while Ursula, though\r\nrespecting her slumbers too much to move, lay with wide-open eyes, now\r\nspeculating on the strange future, now grieving over those she\r\nleft--Aunt Ursel, Gerard, Mary, and all such; the schemes from which\r\nshe was snatched, and then again consoling herself with the hope that,\r\nsince she was going to be rich, she could at once give all that was\r\nwanted--the white altar cloth, the brass pitcher--nay, perhaps finish\r\nthe church and build the school!  For had not some one said something\r\nabout her position?  Oh yes, she had not thought of it before, but,\r\nsince she was the elder brother\'s daughter, she must be the heiress!\r\nThere was no doubt a grand beautiful story before her; she would\r\nwithstand all sorts of fascinations, wicked baronets and earls\r\ninnumerable, and come back and take Gerard by the hand, and say, \'Pride\r\nwas quelled and love was free.\'  Not that Gerard had ever uttered a\r\nword tending in that direction since he had been seven years old, but\r\nthat would make it all the prettier; they would both be silently\r\nconstant, till the time came, perhaps when she was of age.  Mother\r\nwould like it, though _that_ father would certainly be horrid.  And how\r\nnice it would be to give Gerard everything, and they would go all over\r\nthe Continent, and see pictures, and buy them, and see all the\r\ncathedrals and all the mountains.  But perhaps, since Mark Egremont had\r\nreally been so generous in hunting up the cousin who was displacing\r\nhim, she was bound in duty to marry him; perhaps he reckoned on her\r\ndoing so.  She would be generous in her turn, give up all the wealth to\r\nhim, and return to do and be everything to Micklethwayte.  How they\r\nwould admire and bless her.  And oh! she was going to London\r\nto-morrow--London, which she so much wished to see--Westminster Abbey,\r\nBritish Museum, All Saints, National Gallery, no end of new dresses.\r\n\r\nHalf-waking, half-dreaming, she spent the night which seemed long\r\nenough, and the light hours of the summer morning seemed still longer,\r\nbefore she could call it a reasonable time for getting up. Her\r\nsplashings  awoke her mother, who lay smiling for a few moments,\r\nrealising and giving thanks for her great joy, then bestirred herself\r\nwith the recollection of all that had to be done on this busy morning\r\nbefore any summons from her husband could arrive.\r\n\r\nCombining packing and dressing, like the essentially unmethodical\r\nlittle woman she was, Mrs. Egremont still had all her beautiful silky\r\nbrown hair about her shoulders when the bell of St. Ambrose\'s was heard\r\ngiving its thin tinkling summons to matins at half-past seven. She was\r\ndisappointed; she meant to have gone for this last time, but there was\r\nno help for it, and Nuttie set off by herself.\r\n\r\nGerard Godfrey was at his own door.  He was not one of the regular\r\nattendants at the short service, being of that modern species that\r\nholds itself superior to \'Cranmer\'s prayers,\' but on this morning he\r\nhastened up to her with outstretched hand.\r\n\r\n\'And you are going away!\' he said.\r\n\r\n\'I hope to get leave to stay a few days after mother,\' she said.\r\n\r\n\'To prolong the torment?\' he said.\r\n\r\n\'To wish everybody good-bye.  It is a great piece of my life that is\r\ncome to an end, and I can\'t bear to break it off so short.\'\r\n\r\n\'And if you feel so, who are going to wealth and pleasure, what must it\r\nbe to those who are left behind?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!\' said Nuttie, \'some one will be raised up.  That\'s what they\r\nalways say.\'\r\n\r\n\'I shall go into a brotherhood,\' observed Gerard desperately.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, don\'t,\' began Nuttie, much gratified, but at that moment Miss\r\nNugent came out at her door, and Mr. Spyers, who was some way in\r\nadvance, looked round and waited for them to come up.  He held out his\r\nhands to her and said, \'Well, Nuttie, my child, you are going to begin\r\na new life.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh dear!  I wish I could have both!\' cried Nuttie, not very relevantly\r\nas far as the words went.\r\n\r\n\'Scheiden und weiden thut weh!\' quoted Mary.\r\n\r\n\'If his place was only Monks Horton.  What will Aunt Ursel do?\'\r\n\r\n\'I think perhaps she may be induced to join us,\' said Mary.  \'We mean\r\nto do our best to persuade her.\'\r\n\r\n\'And there\'s the choir!  And my class, and the harmonium,\' went on\r\nNuttie, while Gerard walked on disconsolately.\r\n\r\n\'Micklethwayte has existed without you, Nuttie,\' said Mr. Spyers,\r\ntaking her on with him alone.  \'Perhaps it will be able to do so again.\r\nMy dear, you had better look on.  There will be plenty for you to learn\r\nand to do where you are going, and you will be sure to find much to\r\nenjoy, and also something to bear.  I should like to remind you that\r\nthe best means of going on well in this new world will be to keep self\r\ndown and to have the strong desire that only love can give to be\r\nsubmissive, and to do what is right both to God and your father and\r\nmother.  May I give you a text to take with you? "Children, obey your\r\nparents in the Lord, for this is right."\'\r\n\r\nThey were at the door and there was no time for an answer, but Nuttie,\r\nas she took her place, was partly touched and partly fretted at the\r\nadmonition.\r\n\r\nThe question as to her remaining a day or two after her mother was soon\r\ndisposed of.  Mrs. Egremont sent a pretty little note to make the\r\nrequest, but the elegant valet who appeared at ten o\'clock brought a\r\nverbal message that his master wished Mrs. and Miss Egremont to be\r\nready by two o\'clock to join him in calling on Lady Kirkaldy at Monks\r\nHorton, and that if their luggage was ready by four o\'clock, he\r\n(Gregorio) would take charge of it, as they were all to go up to town\r\nby the 4.40 train.\r\n\r\n\'Did he have my note?\' faltered Alice, stimulated by the imploring\r\nglances of aunt and daughter, but anticipating the answer.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, madame, but he wishes that Miss Egremont should accompany you\r\nimmediately.\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course,\' was Alice\'s comment, \'now that he has found his child, he\r\ncannot bear to part with her.\'\r\n\r\nAnd all through the farewells that almost rent the gentle Alice\'s heart\r\nin two, she was haunted by the terror that she or her daughter should\r\nhave red eyes to vex her husband.  As to Mr. Dutton, he had only come\r\nin with Gerard in a great hurry just after breakfast, said there was\r\nmuch to do to-day at the office, as they were going to take stock, and\r\nthey should neither of them have time to come home to luncheon.  He\r\nshook the hands of mother and daughter heartily, promised to \'look\r\nafter\' Miss Headworth, and bore off in his train young Gerard, looking\r\nthe picture of woe, and muttering \'I believe he has got it up on\r\npurpose;\' while mother and daughter thought it very odd, and rather\r\nunkind.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\n\r\nNEW PLUMES.\r\n\r\n  \'And ye sall walk in silk attire,\r\n  And siller hae to spare.\'--Old Ballad.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe very best open fly and pair of horses, being the equipage most like\r\na private carriage possessed by the Royal Hotel, came to the door with\r\nMr. Egremont seated in it, at a few minutes after two o\'clock, and\r\nfound Alice in her only black silk, with a rose in her bonnet, and a\r\ntie to match on her neck, hastily procured as signs of her wifehood.\r\n\r\nShe had swallowed her tears, and Nuttie was not a crying person, but\r\nwas perfectly scarlet on her usually brown cheeks.  Her father muttered\r\nsome civility about back seats, but it was plain that it was only in\r\nwords, and she never thought of anything but looking back, with her\r\nlast wave to her aunt and the two maids, one crying at the gate, the\r\nother at the door.\r\n\r\n\'There,\' said Mr. Egremont, as they drove away, \'that is over!\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear aunt,\' said his wife.  \'Who can express her goodness to me?\'\r\n\r\n\'Cela va sans dire,\' was the reply.  \'But these are connections that\r\nhappily Ursula is young enough to forget and leave behind.\'\r\n\r\n\'I shall never forget!\' began Nuttie, but she saw her father composing\r\nhimself in his corner without paying the slightest heed to what she was\r\nsaying, and she encountered a warning and alarmed glance from her\r\nmother, so she was forced to content herself with uttering silent vows\r\nof perpetual recollection as she passed each well-known object,--the\r\nunfinished church, with Mr. Spyers at the door talking to old Bellman;\r\nthe Town Hall, whose concerts, lectures, and S. P. G. meetings had been\r\nher chief gaiety and excitement; the School of Art, where Lady\r\nKirkaldy\'s appearance now seemed to her to have been like that of a\r\nbird of omen; past the shops in the High Street, with a little\r\nexultation at the thought of past desires which they had excited.  Long\r\ncould she have rattled away, her hopes contradicting her regrets, and\r\nher regrets qualifying her anticipations, but she saw that her mother\r\nwas nervous about every word and gesture, and fairly looked dismayed\r\nwhen she exclaimed, \'Oh, mother, there\'s Etta Smith; how surprised she\r\nwill be!\' bowing and smiling with all her might.\r\n\r\nThere was a look of bare toleration on Mr. Egremont\'s face, as if he\r\nendured because it would soon be over, as Nuttie bowed several times,\r\nand his wife, though less quick to catch people\'s eyes, sometimes also\r\nmade her recognition.  When the streets were past and Nuttie had aimed\r\nher last nods at the nursery parties out walking on the road, she\r\nbecame aware that those cold, lack-lustre, and yet sharply critical\r\neyes of her father were scanning her all over.\r\n\r\n\'She has been educated?\' he presently said to his wife.\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes,\' was the eager answer.  \'She is in the highest form at the\r\nHigh School, and has to go up for the Senior Local Examination.  Miss\r\nBelper makes sure that she will get a first class.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont gave a little wave of the hand, as dismissing something\r\nsuperfluous, and said, \'I hope she has some accomplishments.\'\r\n\r\n\'She has done very fairly in French and German--\'\r\n\r\n\'And Latin,\' put in Ursula.\r\n\r\n\'And she has had several prizes at the School of Art.\'\r\n\r\n\'And music?  That\'s the only thing of any value in society,\' he said\r\nimpatiently, and Mrs. Egremont said more timidly, \'She has learnt music\r\nregularly.\'\r\n\r\n\'But I don\'t care about it,\' broke in Nuttie.  \'I haven\'t mother\'s ear\r\nnor her voice.  I learnt the science in case I should have to teach,\r\nand they make me practise.  I don\'t mind classical music, but I can\'t\r\nstand rubbish, and I think it is waste of time.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont looked fairly amused, as at the outspoken folly of an\r\nenfant terrible, but he only said, either to his wife or to himself, \'A\r\nlittle polish, and then she may be fairly presentable.\'\r\n\r\n\'We have taken great pains with her,\' answered the gentle mother,\r\nevidently taking this as a great compliment, while the daughter was\r\ntingling with indignation.  She, bred up by mother, and aunt, and Mary\r\nNugent, to be barely presentable.  Was not their society at\r\nMicklethwayte equal in good manners to any, and superior, far superior,\r\nin goodness and intelligence to these stupid fashionable people, who\r\nundervalued all her real useful acquirements, and cared for nothing but\r\ntrumpery music.\r\n\r\nThe carriage entered the park, and Nuttie saw lake and woods from a\r\nfresh point of view.  The owners were both at home, and Nuttie found\r\nherself walking behind her parents into a cheerful apartment, half\r\nlibrary, half morning-room.  Mrs. Egremont was by far the most shy and\r\nshrinking of the party, but it was an occasion that showed her\r\nhusband\'s complete tact and savoir favre.  He knew perfectly well that\r\nthe Kirkaldys knew all about it, and he therefore took the initiative.\r\n\'You are surprised to see us,\' he said, as he gave his hand, \'but we\r\ncould not leave the country without coming to thank Lady Kirkaldy for\r\nher kindness in assisting in following up the clue to Mrs. Egremont\'s\r\nresidence.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am very happy,\' said Lady Kirkaldy, while all were being seated.\r\n\r\n\'I think it was here that my nephew Mark first met one whom, child as\r\nhe was, he could not but remember.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t think you met him here,\' said Lady Kirkaldy to Mrs. Egremont;\r\n\'but he heard the name and was struck by it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Dear Mark!\' was the response.  \'He was so kind.\'\r\n\r\n\'He is a dear good boy,\' chimed in my lady.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said her lord, \'an excellent good fellow with plenty of brains.\'\r\n\r\n\'As he well knows,\' said Mr. Egremont.  \'Oh yes; I quite agree with all\r\nyou say of him!  One ought to be thankful for the possession of a rare\r\nspecimen.\'\r\n\r\nIt was in the tone in which Falstaff discussed that sober boy, Lord\r\nJohn of Lancaster.  Lord Kirkaldy asked if the visitors were going to\r\nremain long in the neighbourhood.\r\n\r\n\'We are due in London to-night,\' replied Mr. Egremont.  \'We shall spend\r\na day or two there, and then go home.  Alice,\' he added, though his\r\nwife had never heard him call her so before, \'Lady Kirkaldy knows your\r\ninexperience.  Perhaps she would be good enough to give you some\r\naddresses that might be useful.\'\r\n\r\n\'I shall be delighted,\' said the lady, cordially looking at the\r\nblushing Mrs. Egremont.\r\n\r\n\'Dressmaker, and all the rest of it,\' said Mr. Egremont.  \'You know\r\nbetter than she does what she will require, and a little advice will be\r\ninvaluable.  Above all, if you could tell her how to pick up a maid.\'\r\n\r\nLady Kirkaldy proposed to take the mother and daughter up to her\r\ndressing-room, where she kept her book of addresses to London\r\ntradesmen; and Mr. Egremont only begged that they would remember the\r\n4.40 train.  Then Lord Kirkaldy was left to entertain him, while the\r\nladies went up the broad staircase to the pleasant room, which had a\r\nmingled look of refinement and usefulness which struck Nuttie at once.\r\nLady Kirkaldy, as soon as the door was shut, took her visitor by the\r\nhand, kissed her forehead, and said, \'You must let me tell you how glad\r\nI am.\'\r\n\r\nThe crystal veil at once spread over Alice\'s eyes.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, thank you. Lady Kirkaldy!  I am _so_ happy, and yet I am so\r\nafraid.  Please tell me what we shall _do_ so that we may not vex him,\r\nso high bred and fastidious as he is?\'\r\n\r\n\'Be yourself!  That\'s all, my dear,\' said Lady Kirkaldy tenderly.\r\n\'Don\'t be afraid.  You are quite incapable of doing anything that could\r\ndistress the most fastidious taste.\'\r\n\r\nIt was perfectly true of the mother, perhaps less so of the daughter;\r\nbut Lady Kirkaldy only thought of her as a mere girl, who could easily\r\nbe modelled by her surroundings.  The kind hostess applied herself to\r\ngiving the addresses of the people she thought likely to be most useful\r\nin the complete outfit which she saw would be necessary, explaining to\r\nwhich establishments she applied with confidence if she needed to\r\ncomplete her wardrobe in haste, feeling certain that nothing would be\r\nsent her that she disliked, and giving leave to use her name.  She soon\r\nsaw that the mother was a little dazed, while Ursula\'s eyes grew\r\nrounder at the unlimited vista of fine clothes, and she assented, and\r\nasked questions as to the details.  As to a maid, Lady Kirkaldy would\r\nwrite to a person who would call on Mrs. Egremont at the hotel in\r\nLondon, and who might be what was wanted; and in conclusion, Lady\r\nKirkaldy, with some diffidence, begged to be written to--\'if--if,\' she\r\nsaid, \'there happened to be any difficulty about which you might not\r\nlike to consult Mrs. William Egremont.\'  Nuttie hardly knew whether to\r\nbe grateful or not, for she did not believe in any standard above that\r\nof Micklethwayte, and she was almost angry at her mother\'s grateful\r\nanswer--\'Oh, thank you!  I should be so grateful!  I am so afraid of\r\nannoying him by what he may think small, ignorant, country-town ways!\r\nYou will understand--\'\r\n\r\nLady Kirkaldy did understand, and she dreaded what might be before the\r\nsweet little yielding woman, not from want of breeding so much as from\r\nthe long-indulged selfishness of her husband; but she encouraged her as\r\nmuch as possible, and promised all possible counsel, bringing her\r\ndownstairs again just in time.\r\n\r\n\'Pretty little soul!\' said Lord Kirkaldy, as the fly clattered away. \'I\r\nwonder whether Mark has done her a kindness!\'\r\n\r\n\'It is just what she is, a pretty, nay, a beautiful soul, full of\r\ntenderness and forgiveness and affection and humility, only I doubt\r\nwhether there is any force or resolution to hold her own.  You smile!\r\nWell, perhaps the less of that she has the better she may get on with\r\nhim.  Did he say anything about her?\'\r\n\r\n\'No; I think he wants to ignore that they have not spent the last\r\ntwenty years together.\'\r\n\r\n\'That may be the best way for all parties.  Do you think he will behave\r\nwell to her?\'\r\n\r\n\'No man could well do otherwise to such a sweet little thing,\' said\r\nLord Kirkaldy; \'especially as she will be his most obedient slave, and\r\nwill make herself necessary to him.  It is much better luck than he\r\ndeserves; but I pity her when she comes to make her way with yon\r\nladies!\'\r\n\r\n\'I wish I was there!  I know she will let herself be trodden on!\r\nHowever, there\'s Mark to stand up for her, and William Egremont will do\r\nwhatever he thinks right and just.  I wish I knew how his wife will\r\ntake it!\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\n\r\nBRIDGEFIELD EGREMONT.\r\n\r\n  \'Let us see these handsome houses\r\n  Where the wealthy nobles dwell.\'--TENNYSON.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Mother, mother!\' cried two young people, bursting open the door of the\r\npretty dining-room of Bridgefield Rectory, where the grown-up part of\r\nthe family were lingering over a late breakfast.\r\n\r\n\'Gently, gently, children,\' said the dignified lady at the head of the\r\ntable.  \'Don\'t disturb papa.\'\r\n\r\n\'But we really have something to say, mother!\' said the elder girl,\r\n\'and Fraulein said you ought to know.  Uncle Alwyn is come home, and\r\nMrs. Egremont.  And please, are we to call her Aunt Egremont, or Aunt\r\nAlwyn, or what?\'\r\n\r\nThe desired sensation was produced.  Canon Egremont put down his\r\nnewspaper.  The two elder sisters looked from one to the other in\r\nunmitigated astonishment.  Mark briefly made answer to the final\r\nquestion, \'Aunt Alice,\' and Mrs. Egremont said gravely, \'How did you\r\nhear this, Rosalind?  You know I always forbid you to gossip.\'\r\n\r\n\'We didn\'t gossip, mother.  We went up to the gardens to get some\r\nmulberries for our half-holiday feast; and Ronaldson came out and told\r\nus we must ask leave first, for the ladies were come.  The Squire came\r\nhome at nine o\'clock last night, and Mrs. Egremont and all, and only\r\nsent a telegram two hours before to have the rooms got ready.\'\r\n\r\n\'Has Uncle Alwyn gone and got himself married?\' exclaimed one of the\r\nyoung ladies, in utter amazement.\r\n\r\n\'Not just now, Blanche,\' said her father.  \'It is an old story now.\r\nYour uncle married this lady, who had been governess to May and Mark,\r\nmany years ago, and from--circumstances in which she was not at all to\r\nblame, he lost sight of her while he was abroad with old General\r\nEgremont.  Mark met her about a fortnight ago, and this has led to your\r\nuncle\'s going in quest of her, though he has certainly been more sudden\r\nin his proceedings than I expected.\'\r\n\r\nThe mother here succeeded in sending Rosalind and Adela, with their\r\nwondering eyes, off the scene, and she would much have liked to send\r\nher two stepdaughters after them, but one-and-twenty and eighteen could\r\nnot so readily be ordered off as twelve and ten; and Mark, who had been\r\nprohibited from uttering a word to his sisters, was eagerly examining\r\nMargaret whether she remembered their Edda; but she had been only three\r\nyears old at the time of the adventures in the Isle of Wight, and\r\nremembered nothing distinctly but the aspect of one of the sailors in\r\nthe yacht.\r\n\r\n\'Well,\' said Mrs. Egremont, \'this has come very suddenly upon us.  It\r\nwould have been more for her own dignity if she had held out a little\r\nbefore coming so easily to terms, after the way in which she has been\r\ntreated.\'\r\n\r\n\'When you see her, mother, you will understand,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\n\'Shall we have to be intimate with her?\' asked May.\r\n\r\n\'I desire that she should be treated as a relation,\' said the Canon\r\ndecidedly.  \'There is nothing against her character,\' and, as his wife\r\nwas about to interrupt,--\'nothing but an indiscretion to which she was\r\nalmost driven many years ago.  She was cruelly treated, and I for one\r\nam heartily sorry for having let myself be guided by others.\'\r\n\r\nMrs. William Egremont felt somewhat complacent, for she knew he meant\r\nLady de Lyonnais, and there certainly had been no love lost between her\r\nand her step-children\'s grandmother; but she was a sensible woman, and\r\nforbore to speak, though there was a mental reservation that intimacy\r\nwould a good deal depend upon circumstances.  Blanche cried out that it\r\nwas a perfect romance, and May gravely said, \'But is she a lady?\'\r\n\r\n\'A perfect lady,\' said Mark.  \'Aunt Margaret says so.\'\r\n\r\n\'One knows what a perfect lady means,\' returned May.\r\n\r\n\'Come, May,\' said Mrs. Egremont, \'do not let us begin with a prejudice.\r\nBy all accounts the poor thing has conducted herself with perfect\r\nrespectability all this time.  What did you tell me, Mark? She has been\r\nliving with an aunt, keeping a school at Micklethwayte.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not quite,\' said Mark.  \'She has been acting as a daily governess. She\r\nseemed to be on friendly terms with the clerical folk.  I came across\r\nthe name at a school feast, or something of the kind, which came off in\r\nthe Kirkaldys\' park.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, then, I know exactly the sort of person!\' returned May, pursing up\r\nher lips.\r\n\r\nMark laughed and said, \'I wonder whether it is too soon to go up and\r\nsee them.  I wonder what my uncle thinks of his daughter.\'\r\n\r\n\'What!  You don\'t mean to say there is a daughter?\' cried May.\r\n\r\n\'Even so.  And exactly like you too, Miss May.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then you are cut out, Mark!\'\r\n\r\n\'You are cut out, I think, May.  You\'ll have to give her all your Miss\r\nEgremont cards.\'\r\n\r\n\'No,\' said the young lady; \'mother made me have my Christian name\r\nprinted.  She said all but the daughters of the head of the family\r\nought to have it so.  I\'m glad of it.\'\r\n\r\n\'How old is she?\' asked Blanche.\r\n\r\n\'About a year younger than you.\'\r\n\r\n\'I think it is very interesting,\' said Blanche.  \'How wonderful it must\r\nall be to her!  I will go up with you, Mark, as soon as I can get\r\nready.\'\r\n\r\n\'You had better wait till later in the day, Blanche,\' said the mother.\r\nShe knew the meeting was inevitable, but she preferred having it under\r\nher own eye, if she could not reconnoitre.\r\n\r\nShe was a just and sensible woman, who felt reparation due to the\r\nnewly-discovered sister-in-law, and that harmony, or at least the\r\nappearance of it, must be preserved; but she was also exclusive and\r\nfastidious by nature, and did not look forward to the needful\r\nintercourse with much satisfaction either on her own account or that of\r\nher family.\r\n\r\nShe told Mark to say that she should come to see Mrs. Egremont after\r\nluncheon, since he was determined to go at once, and moreover to drag\r\nhis father with him.  Canon Egremont was a good and upright man,\r\naccording to his lights, which were rather those of a well-beneficed\r\nclergyman of the first than of the last half of the century,\r\nintensified perhaps that the passive voice was the strongest in him.\r\nAll the country knew that Canon Egremont could be relied on to give a\r\nprudent, scholarly judgment, and to be kind and liberal, when once\r\ninduced to stir mind or body--but how to do that was the problem.  He\r\nhad not been a young man at the time of his first marriage, and was\r\nonly a few years\' junior to his brother, though he had the fresh,\r\nwholesome look of a man who kept regular hours and lived much in the\r\nair.\r\n\r\nAlice knew him at once, and thought eighteen years had made little\r\nchange, as, at Nuttie\'s call to her, she looked from the window and saw\r\nthe handsome, dignified, gray-haired, close-shaven rosy face, and the\r\nclerical garb unchanged in favour of long coats and high waistcoats.\r\n\r\nThe mother and daughter were exploring the house together.  Mr.\r\nEgremont had made it known that he preferred having his breakfast\r\nalone, and not being disturbed in the forenoon.  So the two ladies had\r\nbreakfasted together at nine, the earliest hour at which they could\r\nprevail on the household to give them a meal.  Indeed Nuttie had slept\r\ntill nearly that time, for between excitement and noise, her London\r\nslumbers had been broken; and her endeavour to keep Micklethwayte hours\r\nhad resulted in a long, weary, hungry time in the sitting-room of the\r\nhotel, with nothing to do, when the gaze from the window palled on her,\r\nbut to write to her aunt and Mary Nugent.  The rest of the day had been\r\nspent in driving about in a brougham with her mother shopping, and this\r\nshe could not but enjoy exceedingly, more than did the timid Mrs.\r\nEgremont, who could not but feel herself weighted with responsibility;\r\nand never having had to spend at the utmost more than ten pounds at a\r\ntime, felt bewildered at the cheques put into her hands, and then was\r\nalarmed to find them melting away faster than she expected.\r\n\r\nThere was a very late dinner, after which Mr. Egremont, on the first\r\nday, made his wife play bezique with him.  She enjoyed it, as a tender\r\nreminiscence of the yachting days; but Nuttie found herself de trop,\r\nand was reduced to the book she had contrived to purchase on her\r\ntravels.  The second night Mr. Egremont had picked up two friends, not\r\nyet gone out of town, whose talk was of horses and of yachts, quite\r\nincomprehensible to the ladies.  They were very attentive to Mrs.\r\nEgremont, whom they evidently admired, one so visibly as to call up a\r\nblush; but they disregarded the daughter as a schoolgirl.  Happily they\r\nappeared no more after the dinner; but Nuttie\'s first exclamation of\r\nastonished disgust was silenced at once by her mother with unusual\r\ndetermination, \'You must not speak so of your father\'s friends.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not when--\'\r\n\r\n\'Not at all,\' interrupted Mrs. Egremont.\r\n\r\nThe only sense of promotion to greatness that Ursula had yet enjoyed\r\nwas in these fine clothes, and the maid whom Lady Kirkaldy had\r\nrecommended, a grave and severe-looking person, of whom both stood\r\nsomewhat in awe.  The arrival at Bridgefield had been too late for\r\nanything to be taken in but a general impression of space and\r\ndreariness, and the inevitable dinner of many courses, after which\r\nNuttie was so tired out that her mother sent her to bed.\r\n\r\nSince the waking she had made some acquaintance with the house. There\r\nwas no show of domestics, no curtseying housekeeper to parade the new\r\nmistress over the house; Mr. Egremont had told his wife that she must\r\nfill up the establishment as she pleased, but that there was an\r\nadmirable cook downstairs, and he would not have her interfered\r\nwith--she suited his tastes as no one else did, and she must be left to\r\ndeal with the provisions and her own underlings.  There was a stable\r\nestablishment, and a footman had been hired in town, but there was\r\nbesides only one untidy-looking housemaid, who began by giving warning;\r\nand Alice and Nuttie had roamed about without meeting any one from the\r\nbig wainscotted dining-room with faded crimson curtains and family\r\nportraits, the older grimy, the younger chalky, to the two\r\ndrawing-rooms, whose gilding and pale blue damask had been preserved by\r\npinafores of brown holland; the library, which looked and smelt as if\r\nMr. Egremont was in the habit of sitting there, and a big\r\nbilliard-room, all opening into a shivery-feeling hall, with Scagliola\r\ncolumns and a few dirty statues between them; then upstairs to a\r\npossible morning-room, looking out over a garden lawn, where mowing was\r\ngoing on in haste, and suites of dreary shut-up fusty bedrooms.\r\nNuttie, who had notions of choosing her own bower, could not make up\r\nher mind which looked the least inviting.  It did not seem as if girls\r\ncould ever have laughed together, or children clattered up and down the\r\nstairs.  Mrs. Egremont begged her to keep possession for the present at\r\nleast of the chamber where the grim housemaid had chosen to put her,\r\nand which had the advantage of being aired.\r\n\r\nThe two windows looked out over the park, and thence it was that while\r\nMorris, the maid, was unpacking and putting away the new purchases, and\r\nNuttie was standing, scarcely realising that such pretty hats and\r\nbonnets could be her very own, when her mother beheld the Canon and\r\nMark advancing up the drive.  It was with a great start that she called\r\nUrsula to come down directly with her, as no one would know where to\r\nfind them, hastily washing the hands that had picked up a sense of\r\ndustiness during the exploration, and taking a comprehensive glance in\r\nthe cheval glass, which showed her some one she felt entirely\r\nunfamiliar to her in a dainty summer costume of pale gray silk picked\r\nout with a mysterious shade of pink.  Ursula too thought Miss\r\nEgremont\'s outer woman more like a Chelsea shepherdess than Nuttie\'s\r\ntrue self, as she tripped along in her buckled shoes and the sea green\r\nstockings that had been sent home with her skirt.  With crimson cheeks\r\nand a throbbing heart, Alice was only just at the foot of the stairs\r\nwhen the newcomers had made their way in, and the kind Canon, ignoring\r\nall that was past, held out his hands saying, \'Well, my dear, I am glad\r\nto see you here,\' kissing Mrs. Egremont on each cheek.  \'And so this is\r\nyour daughter.  How do you do, my dear--Ursula?  Isn\'t that your name?\'\r\nAnd Ursula had again to submit to a kiss, much more savoury and kindly\r\nthan her father\'s, though very stubbly.  And oh! her uncle\'s dress was\r\nlike that of no one she had ever seen except the rector of the old\r\nchurch, the object of unlimited contempt to the adherents of St.\r\nAmbrose\'s.\r\n\r\nAs to Mark, he only kissed his aunt, and shook hands with her, while\r\nhis father ran on with an unusual loquacity that was a proof of\r\nnervousness in him.\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Egremont--Jane, I mean--will be here after luncheon.  She thought\r\nyou would like to get settled in first.  How is Alwyn?  Is he down yet?\'\r\n\r\n\'I will see,\' in a trembling voice.\r\n\r\n\'Oh no, never mind, Alwyn hates to be disturbed till he has made\r\nhimself up in the morning.  My call is on you, you know.  Where are you\r\nsitting?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t quite know.  In the drawing-room, I suppose.\'\r\n\r\nThe Canon, knowing the house much better than she did, opened a door\r\ninto a third drawing-room she had not yet seen, a pretty little room,\r\nfitted up with fluted silk, like a tent, somewhat faded but not much\r\nthe worse for that, and opening into a conservatory, which seemed to\r\nhave little in it but some veteran orange trees.  Nuttie, however,\r\nexclaimed with pleasure at the nicest room she had seen, and Mark began\r\nunfastening the glass door that led into it.  Meantime Alice, with\r\nburning cheeks and liquid eyes, nerved her voice to say, \'Oh, sir--Mr.\r\nEgremont--please forgive me!  I know now how wrong I was.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nonsense, my dear.  Bygones are bygones.  You were far more sinned\r\nagainst than sinning, and have much to forgive me.  There, my dear, we\r\nwill say no more about it, nor think of it either.  I am only too\r\nthankful that poor Alwyn should have some one to look after him.\'\r\n\r\nAlice, who had dreaded nothing more than the meeting with her former\r\nmaster, was infinitely relieved and grateful for this kindness.  She\r\nhad ejaculated, \'Oh, you are so good!\' in the midst, and now at the\r\nmention of her husband, she exclaimed, \'Oh! do you think he is ill? I\r\ncan\'t help being afraid he is, but he will not tell me, and does not\r\nlike to be asked.\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor fellow, he has damaged his health a good deal,\' was the answer.\r\n\'He had a sharp attack in the spring, but he has pretty well got over\r\nit, and Raikes told me there was no reason for uneasiness, provided he\r\nwould be careful; and that will be a much easier matter now.  I should\r\nnot wonder if we saw him with quite a renewed youth.\'\r\n\r\nSo the Canon and Mrs. Egremont were getting on pretty well together,\r\nbut there was much more stiffness and less cordiality between the two\r\ncousins, although Mark got the window open into the conservatory, and\r\nshowed Nuttie the way into the garden, advising her to ask Ronaldson,\r\nthe gardener, to fill the conservatory with flowers.  The pavilion, as\r\nthis little room was called, always seemed to have more capacities for\r\nbeing lived in than any other room in the house.  It had been fitted up\r\nwhen such things were the fashion for the shortlived bride of \'our\r\ngreat uncle.\'\r\n\r\n\'The colour must have been awful then,\' said Mark, looking up at it,\r\n\'enough to set one\'s teeth on edge; but it has faded into something\r\nquite orthodox--much better than could be manufactured for you.\'\r\n\r\nMark had evidently some ideas of art, and was besides inclined to do\r\nthe honours to the stranger; but Nuttie was not going to encourage him\r\nor anybody else to make up to her, while she had that look of Gerard\r\nGodfrey\'s in her mind\'s eye.  So she made small answer, and he felt\r\nrebuffed, but supposed her shy, and wondered when he could go back to\r\nher mother, who was so much more attractive.\r\n\r\nPresently his father went off to storm the den of the master of the\r\nhouse, and there was a pleasant quarter of an hour, during which the\r\nthree went out through the conservatory, and Mark showed the\r\nins-and-outs of the garden, found out Ronaldson, and congratulated him\r\non having some one at last to appreciate his flowers, begging him to\r\nmake the conservatory beautiful.  And Mrs. Egremont\'s smile was so\r\neffective that the Scot forthwith took out his knife and presented her\r\nwith the most precious of the roses within his reach.\r\n\r\nMoreover Mark told the names and ages of all his sisters, whole and\r\nhalf.  He was the only son, except a little fellow in the nursery. And\r\nhe exhorted his aunt not to be afraid of his step-mother, who was a\r\nmost excellent person, he declared, but who never liked to see any one\r\nafraid of her.\r\n\r\nThere was something a little alarming in this, but on the whole the\r\nvisit was very pleasant and encouraging to Mrs. Egremont; and she began\r\nrejoicing over the kindness as soon as the Canon had summoned his son,\r\nand they had gone away together.\r\n\r\n\'I am sure you must be delighted with your uncle and cousin, my dear,\'\r\nshe said.\r\n\r\n\'He\'s not a bit my notion of a priest,\' returned Nuttie.  \'And I don\'t\r\nbelieve he has any daily prayers!\'\r\n\r\n\'He is old-fashioned, my dear.\'\r\n\r\n\'One of the stodgey old clergymen in books,\' observed Nuttie.  \'I\r\ndidn\'t think there were any of that sort left.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, my dear, pray don\'t take fancies into your head!  He is a very,\r\nvery good man, and has been most kind to me, far more than I deserve,\r\nand he is your uncle, Nuttie.  I do so hope you will get on well with\r\nyour cousins.\'\r\n\r\nHere a gong, a perfectly unknown sound to Nuttie, made itself heard,\r\nand rather astonished her by the concluding roar.  The two ladies came\r\nout into the hall as Mr. Egremont was crossing it.  He made an\r\ninclination of the head, and uttered a sort of good morning to his\r\ndaughter, but she was perfectly content to have no closer salutation.\r\nHaving a healthy noonday appetite, her chief wish was at the moment\r\nthat those beautiful little cutlets, arranged in a crown form, were not\r\nso very tiny; or that, with two men-servants looking on, it were\r\npossible to attain to a second help, but she had already learnt that\r\nGregorio would not hear her, and that any attempt to obtain more food\r\nfrightened her mother.\r\n\r\n\'So his reverence has been to see you,\' observed Mr. Egremont.\r\n\'William, if you like it better.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, and he was kindness itself!\'\r\n\r\n\'And how did Master Mark look at finding I could dispense with his\r\nassistance?\'\r\n\r\n\'I think he is very glad.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont laughed.  \'You are a simple woman, Edda!  The pose of\r\nvirtuous hero was to have been full compensation for all that it might\r\ncost him!  And no doubt he looks for the reward of virtue likewise.\'\r\n\r\nWherewith he looked full at Ursula, who, to her extreme vexation, felt\r\nherself blushing up to the ears.  She fidgeted on her chair, and began\r\na most untrue \'I\'m sure--\' for, indeed, the poor girl was sure of\r\nnothing, but that her father\'s manner was most uncomfortable to her.\r\nHis laugh choked whatever she might have said, which perhaps was well,\r\nand her mother\'s cheeks glowed as much as hers did.\r\n\r\n\'Did the Canoness--Jane, I mean--come up?\' Mr. Egremont went on.\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Egremont?  No; she sent word that she is coming after luncheon.\'\r\n\r\n\'Hm!  Then I shall ride out and leave you to her majesty.  Now look\r\nyou, Alice, you are to be very careful with William\'s wife.
