She is a\r\nCondamine, you know, and thinks no end of herself, and your position\r\namong the women-folk of the county depends more on how she takes you up\r\nthan anything else.  But that doesn\'t mean that you are to let her give\r\nherself airs and domineer over you.  Remember you are the elder\r\nbrother\'s wife--Mrs. Egremont of Bridgefield Egremont--and she is\r\nnothing but a parson\'s wife, and I won\'t have her meddling in my house.\r\nOnly don\'t you be absurd and offend her, for she can do more for or\r\nagainst you in society than any one else--more\'s the pity!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! won\'t you stay and help me receive her?\' exclaimed the poor lady,\r\nutterly confused by these contrary directions.\r\n\r\n\'Not I!  I can\'t abide the woman! nor she me!\'  He added, after a\r\nmoment, \'You will do better without me.\'\r\n\r\nSo he went out for his ride, and Ursula asked, \'Oh, mother! what will\r\nyou do?\'\r\n\r\n\'The best I can, my dear.  They are good people, and are sure to be\r\nkinder than I deserve.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was learning that her mother would never so much as hear, far\r\nless answer, a remark on her husband.  It was beginning to make a sore\r\nin the young heart that a barrier was thus rising, where there once had\r\nbeen as perfect oneness and confidence as could exist between two\r\nnatures so dissimilar, though hitherto the unlikeness had never made\r\nitself felt.\r\n\r\nMrs. Egremont turned the conversation to the establishing themselves in\r\nthe pavilion, whither she proceeded to import some fancy-work that she\r\nhad bought in London, and sent Nuttie to Ronaldson, who was arranging\r\ncalceolarias, begonias, and geraniums in the conservatory, to beg for\r\nsome cut-flowers for a great dusty-looking vase in the centre of the\r\ntable.\r\n\r\nThese were being arranged when Mrs. William Egremont and Miss Blanche\r\nEgremont were ushered in, and there were the regular kindred embraces,\r\nafter which Alice and Nuttie were aware of a very handsome,\r\ndignified-looking lady, well though simply dressed in what was\r\nevidently her home costume, with a large shady hat and feather, her\r\nwhole air curiously fitting the imposing nickname of the Canoness.\r\nBlanche was a slight, delicate-looking, rather pretty girl in a\r\nlawn-tennis dress.  The visitor took the part of treating the newcomers\r\nas well-established relations.\r\n\r\n\'We would not inundate you all at once,\' she said, \'but the children\r\nare all very eager to see their cousin.  I wish you would come down to\r\nthe Rectory with me.  My ponies are at the door.  I would drive you,\r\nand Ursula might walk with Blanche.\'  And, as Alice hesitated for a\r\nmoment, considering how this might agree with the complicated\r\ninstructions that she had received, she added, \'Never mind Alwyn.  I\r\nsaw him going off just before I came up, and he told William he was\r\ngoing to look at some horses at Hale\'s, so he is disposed of for a good\r\nmany hours.\'\r\n\r\nAlice decided that her husband would probably wish her to comply, and\r\nshe rejoiced to turn her daughter in among the cousins, so hats,\r\ngloves, and parasols were fetched, and the two mothers drove away with\r\nthe two sleek little toy ponies.  By which it may be perceived that\r\nMrs. William Egremont\'s first impressions were favourable.\r\n\r\n\'It is the shortest way through the gardens,\' said Blanche.  \'Have you\r\nbeen through them yet?\'\r\n\r\n\'Mark walked about with us a little.\'\r\n\r\n\'You\'ll improve them ever so much.  There are great capabilities. Look,\r\nyou could have four tennis courts on this one lawn.  We wanted to have\r\na garden-party up here last year, and father said we might, but mother\r\nthought Uncle Alwyn might think it a liberty; but now you\'ll have some\r\ndelicious ones?  Of course you play lawn-tennis?\'\r\n\r\n\'I have seen it a very few times,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, we must teach you!  Fancy living without lawn-tennis!\' said\r\nBlanche.  \'I always wonder what people did without it.  Only\'--with an\r\neffort at antiquarianism--\'I believe they had croquet.\'\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Ursula says there weren\'t garden-parties before croquet came in.\'\r\n\r\n\'How dreadful, Ursula!  Your name\'s Ursula, isn\'t it?  Haven\'t you some\r\njolly little name to go by?\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie!  That\'s scrumptious!  I\'ll call you Nuttie, and you may call\r\nme Pussycat.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s not so nice as Blanche.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mother won\'t have me called so when strangers are there, but you\r\naren\'t a stranger, you know.  You must tell me all about yourself, and\r\nhow you came never to learn tennis!\'\r\n\r\n\'I had something else to do,\' said Nuttie, with dignity.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, you were in the schoolroom!  I forgot.  Poor little Nuts!\'\r\n\r\n\'At school,\' said Ursula.\r\n\r\n\'Ah, I remember!  But you\'re out now, aren\'t you?  I\'ve been out since\r\nthis spring.  Mother won\'t let us come out till we are eighteen, isn\'t\r\nit horrid?  And we were so worked there!  I can tell you a finishing\r\ngoverness is an awful institution!  Poor little Rosie and Adey will be\r\nin for one by and by.  At present they\'ve only got a jolly little\r\nFraulein that they can do anything they please with.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I wonder if she would tell me of some German books!\'\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t mean that you want to read German!\' and Blanche stood still,\r\nand looked at her cousin in astonishment.\r\n\r\n\'Why, what else is the use of learning it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I don\'t know.  Every one does.  If one went abroad or to court,\r\nyou know,\' said Blanche vaguely; but Ursula had now a fresh subject of\r\ninterest; for, on emerging from the shrubbery, they came in sight of a\r\npicturesque but not very architectural church, which had the smallest\r\nproportion of wall and the largest of roof, and a pretty oriel-windowed\r\nschoolhouse covered with clematis.  Nuttie rushed into inquiries about\r\nservices and schools, and was aghast at hearing of mere Sundays and\r\nsaints\' days.\r\n\r\n\'Oh no! father isn\'t a bit Ritualistic.  I wish he was, it would be so\r\nmuch prettier; and then he always advertises for curates of moderate\r\nviews, and they are so stupid.  You never saw such a stick as we have\r\ngot now, Mr. Edwards; and his wife isn\'t a lady, I\'m sure.\'\r\n\r\nThen as to schools, it was an absolute amazement to Nuttie to find that\r\nthe same plans were in force as had prevailed when her uncle had come\r\nto the living and built that pretty house--nay, were kept up at his\r\nsole expense, because he liked old-fashioned simplicity, and did not\r\nchoose to be worried with Government inspection.\r\n\r\n\'And,\' said Blanche, \'every one says our girls work ever so much\r\nbetter, and make nicer servants than those that are crammed with all\r\nsorts of nonsense not fit for them.\'\r\n\r\nAs to the Sunday school.  Mother and the curate take care of that. I\'m\r\nsure, if you like it, you can have my class, for I always have a\r\nheadache there, and very often I can\'t go.  Only May pegs away at it,\r\nand she won\'t let me have the boys, who are the only jolly ones,\r\nbecause she says I spoil them.  But you must be my friend--mind,\r\nNuttie, not May\'s, for we are nearer the same age.  When is your\r\nbirthday?  You must put it down in my book!\'\r\n\r\nNuttie, who had tolerable experience of making acquaintance with new\r\ngirls, was divided between a sense of Blanche\'s emptiness, and the\r\nwarmth excited by her friendliness, as well as of astonishment at all\r\nshe heard and saw.\r\n\r\nCrossing the straggling, meandering village street, the cousins entered\r\nthe grounds of the Rectory, an irregular but well-kept building of the\r\nsoft stone of the country, all the garden front of it a deep verandah\r\nthat was kept open in summer, but closed with glass frames in the\r\nwinter--flower-beds lying before it, and beyond a lawn where the young\r\nfolk were playing at the inevitable lawn-tennis.\r\n\r\nMargaret was not so pretty as Blanche, but had a more sensible face,\r\nand her welcome to Ursula was civil but reserved.  Rosalind and Adela\r\nwere bright little things, in quite a different style from their\r\nhalf-sisters, much lighter in complexion and promising to be handsomer\r\nwomen.  They looked full of eagerness and curiosity at the new cousin,\r\nwhom Blanche set down on a bank, and proceeded to instruct in the\r\nmysteries of the all-important game by comments and criticisms on the\r\nplayers.\r\n\r\nAs soon as Mark and Adela had come out conquerors, Ursula was called on\r\nto take her first lesson.  May resigned her racket, saying she had\r\nsomething to do, and walked off the field, and carrying off with her\r\nAdela, who, as Blanche said, \'had a spine,\' and was ordered to lie down\r\nfor an hour every afternoon.  The cheerfulness with which she went\r\nspoke well for the training of the family.\r\n\r\nNuttie was light-footed and dexterous handed, and accustomed to active\r\namusements, so that, under the tuition of her cousins, she became a\r\npromising pupil, and thawed rapidly, even towards Mark.\r\n\r\nShe was in the midst of her game when the two mothers came out, for the\r\ndrive had been extended all round the park, under pretext of showing it\r\nto its new mistress, but really to give the Canoness an opportunity of\r\njudging of her in a tete-a-tete.  Yet that sensible woman had asked no\r\nalarming questions on the past, still less had offered any advice that\r\ncould seem like interference.  She had only named localities, mentioned\r\nneighbours, and made little communications about the ways of the place\r\nsuch as might elicit remarks; and, as Alice\'s voice betrayed less and\r\nless constraint, she ventured on speaking of their daughters, so as to\r\ndraw forth some account of how Ursula might have been educated.\r\n\r\nAnd of this, Alice was ready and eager to talk, telling how clever and\r\nhow industrious Nuttie had always been, and how great an advantage Miss\r\nNugent\'s kindness was, and how she was hoping to go up for the\r\nCambridge examination; then, detecting some doubt in her companion\'s\r\nmanner, she said, \'It would be a great disappointment to her not to do\r\nso now.  Do you think she had better not?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t think she will find time to go on with the preparation! And,\r\nto tell the truth, I don\'t think we are quite ripe for such things in\r\nthis county.  We are rather backward, and Ursula, coming in fresh upon\r\nus, might find it a disadvantage to be thought much cleverer than other\r\npeople.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!  I was not quite sure whether her father would like it.\'\r\n\r\n\'I do not think he would.  I am sure that if my little Rose were to\r\ntake it into her head, I should have hard work to get her father\'s\r\nconsent, though no doubt the world will have progressed by the time she\r\nis old enough.\'\r\n\r\n\'That settles it,\' said Alice.  \'Thank you, Mrs. Egremont.  I own,\' she\r\nadded presently, \'that I do somewhat regret that it cannot be, for I\r\nthought that a motive for keeping up her studies would be helpful to my\r\nchild;--I do not mean for the sake of the studies, but of the--the\r\nbalance in all this change and novelty.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are quite right, I have felt it myself,\' said her sister-in-law.\r\n\'Perhaps something could be done by essay societies.  May belongs to\r\none, and if Ursula is an intellectual girl, perhaps you could keep her\r\nup to some regular employment in the morning.  I succeeded in doing so\r\nwhen May came out, but I can accomplish nothing regular but music with\r\nBlanche; and an hour\'s steady practice a day is better than nothing.\'\r\n\r\nThe drive was on the whole a success, and so was the tea-drinking in\r\nthe verandah, where Aunt Alice and little five-years old Basil became\r\nfast friends and mutual admirers; the Canon strolled out and was\r\ninstalled in the big, cushioned basket-chair that crackled under his\r\nweight; Blanche recounted Nuttie\'s successes, and her own tennis\r\nengagements for the week; Mark lay on a rug and teased her, and her\r\ndachshund; Nuttie listened to the family chatter as if it were a play,\r\nand May dispensed the cups, and looked grave and severe.\r\n\r\n\'Well?\' said the Canon anxiously, when Mark, Blanche, and little Basil\r\nhad insisted on escorting the guests home, and he and his wife were for\r\na few minutes tete-a-tete.\r\n\r\n\'It might have been much worse,\' said the lady.  \'She is a good little\r\ninnocent thing, and has more good sense than I expected. Governessy,\r\nthat\'s all, but she will shake out of that.\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course she will.  It\'s the best thing imaginable for Alwyn!\'\r\n\r\nHis wife kept back the words, \'A hundred times too good for Alwyn!\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\n\r\nLAWN-TENNIS.\r\n\r\n  \'Madam, the guests are come, supper served up,\r\n  My young lady asked for!\'--Romeo and Juliet.\r\n\r\n\r\nA garden-party, Mrs. William Egremont decided, would be the best mode\r\nof testifying her approbation of her sister-in-law, and introducing the\r\nnewcomers to the neighbourhood.  So the invitations were sent forth for\r\nan early day of the coming week.\r\n\r\nFrom how many points of view was Mrs. William Egremont\'s garden-party\r\nregarded, and how different!  There was Basil, to whom it meant wearing\r\nhis velvet suit and eating as many ices as mother would allow.  To\r\nBlanche, it was an occasion for triumph on the tennis ground for\r\nherself, and for hopes for her pupil; and Ursula herself looked forward\r\nto it and practised for it like a knight for his first encounter in the\r\nlists, her sole care being to distinguish herself with her racket.  To\r\nher mother, it was an ordeal, where she trusted not to be a\r\nmortification to her husband and his family; while to the hostess, it\r\nwas a not unwelcome occasion of exercising honest diplomacy and tact,\r\nnot without a sense of magnanimity.  To May, it was a bore to be\r\nendured with dutiful philosophy; to her good-natured father an occasion\r\nfor hospitality, where he trusted that his brother would appear, and\r\nappear to advantage, and was ready even to bribe him thereto with that\r\nwonderful claret that Alwyn had always envied, and declared to be\r\nwasted on a parson.  And Mark, perhaps he viewed the occasion with\r\ndifferent eyes from any one else.  At any rate, even the denizens of\r\nBridgefield mustered there with as many minds as Scott ascribes to the\r\ncombatants of Bannockburn, and there were probably as many other\r\ncircles of feeling more or less intersecting one another among the more\r\ndistant guests, most of them, however, with the same feeling of\r\ncuriosity as to what this newly-discovered wife and daughter of Alwyn\r\nEgremont might be like.\r\n\r\nExternally, in her rich black silk, trimmed with point lace, and her\r\nlittle straw-coloured bonnet with its tuft of feathery grass and blue\r\ncornflower, she was so charming that her daughter danced round her,\r\ncrying, \'O mammy, mammy, if they could but see you at home\'--then, at a\r\nlook: \'Well then--Aunt Ursel, and Miss Mary, and Mr. Dutton!\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was very much pleased with her own pretty tennis dress; but she\r\nhad no personal vanity for herself, only for her mother.  The knowledge\r\nthat she was no beauty was no grievance to her youthful spirits; but\r\nwhen her father surveyed them in the hall, she looked for his verdict\r\nfor her mother as if their relations were reversed.\r\n\r\n\'Ha!  Well, you certainly are a pretty creature, Edda,\' he said\r\ngraciously.  \'You\'ll pass muster!  You want nothing but style.  And,\r\nhang it! you\'ll do just as well without it, if the Canoness will only\r\ndo you justice.  Faces like that weren\'t given for nothing.\'\r\n\r\nShe blushed incarnadine and accepted one of his kisses with a pleasure,\r\nat which Nuttie wondered, her motherly affection prompting her to\r\nmurmur in his ear--\r\n\r\n\'And Ursula?\'\r\n\r\n\'She\'ll not cut you out; but she is Egremont enough to do very fairly.\r\nGoing already?\'\r\n\r\n\'If you would come with us,\' she said wistfully, to the horror of\r\nNuttie, who was burning to be at the beginning of all the matches.\r\n\r\n\'I? oh no!  I promised old Will to look in, but that won\'t be till late\r\nin the day, or I shall have to go handing all the dowagers into the\r\ndining-room to tea.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then I think we had better go on.  They asked us to come early, so as\r\nto see people arrive and know who they are.\'\r\n\r\n_They_ was a useful pronoun to Alice, who felt it a liberty to call her\r\ngrand-looking sister-in-law, Jane--was too well-bred to term her Mrs.\r\nWilliam.\r\n\r\nThe mother and daughter crossed the gardens, Nuttie chattering all the\r\nway about the tennis tactics she had picked up from Blanche, while her\r\nmother answered her somewhat mechanically, wondering, as her eye fell\r\non the square squat gray church tower, what had become of the earnest\r\ndevotion to church work and intellectual pursuits that used to\r\ncharacterise the girl.  True, always both mother and daughter had\r\nhitherto kept up their church-going, and even their Sunday-school\r\nhabits, nor had any hindrance come in their way, Mr. Egremont\r\napparently acquiescing in what he never shared.  But these things\r\nseemed, in Ursula\'s mind, to have sunk out of the proportion they held\r\nat Bridgefield, no longer to be the spirit of a life, but mere Sunday\r\nduties and occupations.\r\n\r\nWas this wicked world getting a hold of the poor child?  Which was\r\nduty? which was the world?  This was the thought that perplexed Alice,\r\ntoo simple as yet to perceive that Ursula\'s former absorption had been\r\nin the interests that surrounded her and her companions, exactly as\r\nthey were at present, and that the real being had yet to work itself\r\nout.\r\n\r\nFor herself, Alice did not think at all.  She was rejoicing in her\r\nrestored husband, and his evident affection.  Her duty towards him was\r\nin her eyes plain.  She saw, of course, that he had no religion, but\r\nshe accepted the fact like that of bad weather; she loved him, and she\r\nloved her daughter; she said her prayers with all her heart for them,\r\nshe hoped, and she did her best, without trying to go below the surface.\r\n\r\nThere was the Rectory gate wide open.  There was Basil rushing up to\r\ngreet his dear Aunt Alice, there were all the windows and doors of the\r\nRectory open, and the nearer slopes covered with chairs and seats of\r\nall dimensions, some under trees, some umbelliferous, and glowing\r\nAfghan rugs, or spotted skins spread for those who preferred the\r\nground.  There was Blanche flitting about wild with excitement, and\r\npouncing on Nuttie to admire her outfit, and reiterate instructions;\r\nthere were the two younger girls altering the position of chairs\r\naccording to their mother\'s directions; there were actually two\r\nguests--not very alarming ones, only the curate and his wife, both\r\nrather gaunt, bony people.  He was button-holing the Canon, and she was\r\ntrying to do the same by the Canoness about some parish casualty. The\r\nCanon hoped to escape in the welcome to his sister-in-law and niece,\r\nbut he was immediately secured again, while his wife found it requisite\r\nto hurry off else where, leaving Mrs. Edwards to tell her story to Mrs.\r\nEgremont.  In point of fact, Alice really liked the good lady, was\r\nquite at ease with her, and felt parish concerns a natural element, so\r\nthat she gave full heed and attention to the cruelty of Mrs. Parkins\'\r\ndepriving Betsy Butter (with an old father and mother to support) of\r\nher family washing, on the ground of a missing pocket handkerchief, the\r\nwhich Mrs. Edwards believed to have been abstracted by the favourite\r\npickle of Miss Blanche\'s class, if only a confession could be elicited\r\nfrom him when undefended by his furious mother.  Mrs. Egremont was\r\nlistening with actual interest and sympathy to the history of Betsy\r\nButter\'s struggles, and was inquiring the way to her cottage, when she\r\nwas called off to be introduced to the arrivals who were beginning to\r\nflood the lawn.  She presently saw May, who had just come down, walking\r\nup and down with Mrs. Edwards, evidently hearing the story of the\r\nhandkerchief.  She thought it had been Nuttie for a moment.  There was\r\na general resemblance between the cousins that made them be mistaken\r\nfor one another several times in the course of the day, since their\r\ndresses, though not alike, were of the same make and style.\r\n\r\nThus it was that as Nuttie was sitting on the grass in earnest\r\ncontemplation of Blanche\'s play, a hand was familiarly laid on her\r\nshoulder, and a voice said,  \'I haven\'t seen that horrid girl yet!\'\r\n\r\nAfter so many introductions, Nuttie had little idea whom she knew, or\r\nwhom she did not know.  She looked up and saw a small person in light\r\nblue, with the delicate features, transparent skin, and blue eyes that\r\naccompany yellow hair, with an indescribable glitter of mirth and\r\njoyousness about the whole creature, as if she were part and parcel of\r\nthe sunbeam in which she stood.\r\n\r\n\'What horrid girl?\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'The interloper, the newly-discovered savage, come to upset--Ah!\'--with\r\na little shriek--\'It isn\'t May!  I beg your pardon.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m May\'s cousin,\' said Nuttie, \'Ursula Egremont.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, oh!\' and therewith the fact burst on both girls at once.  They\r\nstood still a moment in dismay, then the stranger went into a fit of\r\nlaughter.  \'Oh, I beg your pardon!  I can\'t help it!  It is so funny!\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was almost infected, though somewhat hurt.  \'Who said I was\r\nhorrid?\' she asked.\r\n\r\n\'Nobody!  Nobody but me--Annaple Ruthven--and they\'ll all tell you, May\r\nand all, that I\'m always putting my foot in it.  And I never meant that\r\nyou were horrid--you yourself--you know--only--\'\r\n\r\n\'Only nobody wanted us here,\' said Nuttie; \'but we could not help it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course not.  It was shocking, just my way.  Please forgive me!\' and\r\nshe looked most pleading.  Nuttie held out her hand with something\r\nabout \'No one could mind;\' and therewith Annaple cried, \'Oh, if you\r\ndon\'t mind, we can have our laugh out!\' and the rippling laughter did\r\nset Nuttie off at once.  The peal was not over when May herself was\r\nupon them demanding what was the joke.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, there she is!  The real May!  Why,\' said Annaple, kissing her,\r\n\'only think here I\'ve been and gone and thought this was you, and\r\ninquired about--What was it?--the awful monster--the chimera dire--that\r\nMark had routed up--\'\r\n\r\n\'No; you didn\'t say that,\' said Nuttie, half provoked.\r\n\r\n\'Never mind what I said.  Don\'t repeat it.  I only wish myself and\r\nevery one else to forget it.  Now it is swept to the winds by a good\r\nwholesome giggling.  But what business have you two to be so\r\ninconveniently alike?  You are as bad as the twin Leslies!\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s an old foremother on the staircase in white satin who left her\r\nlooks to us both,\' said May.\r\n\r\n\'You\'ll have to wear badges,\' said Annaple.  \'You know the Leslies were\r\nso troublesome that one had to be shipped off to the East Indies and\r\nthe other to the West.\'\r\n\r\n\'They married, that\'s all,\' said May, seeing Nuttie looking mystified;\r\nand at that moment, Blanche\'s side coming out victorious, Nuttie\r\ndescended into the arena to congratulate and be asked to form part of\r\nthe next set.\r\n\r\n\'Well, that was a scrape!\' said Annaple; \'but she wasn\'t bad about it!\r\nI must do something to make up for it somehow--get Janet to invite her,\r\nbut really Janet is in such a state of mind that I am mildness itself\r\ncompared with her.  She would not have come, only John was curious, and\r\ndeclared he should go whether we did or not.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!\' said May, \'I saw him, like the rest of mankind, at madame\'s feet.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! is she of that sort?\'\r\n\r\n\'No,\' said May, \'not at all.  Mother and father too both think she is\r\ngood to the backbone; but she is very pretty, with just the inane soft\r\nsweetness that men rave about--innocent really.  All accounts of her\r\nare excellent, and she has nice parish ways, and will be as helpful as\r\nUncle Alwyn will let her.\'\r\n\r\n\'But she couldn\'t always have been nice?\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I verily believe it was all Uncle Alwyn\'s and grandmamma\'s\r\nfault.  I know Mark thinks so.\'\r\n\r\n\'When the women of a family acquit a woman it goes for something,\' said\r\nAnnaple.  \'That\'s not original, my dear, I heard old Lady Grosmede say\r\nso to Janet when she was deliberating over the invitation, "For a good\r\ndeal more than Mr. Mark\'s, at any rate.\'"\r\n\r\n\'Mark is very fond of her--the mother, I mean.  He says when he was a\r\nlittle fellow her loss was worse to him than even our mother\'s.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you remember the catastrophe?\'\r\n\r\n\'Not a bit.  Only when she is petting Basil it strikes me that I have\r\nheard the tones before.  I only remember the time of misery under the\r\ncrosspatches grandmamma got for us.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, it was a splendid cutting of his own throat in Mark,\' said\r\nAnnaple, \'so it ought to turn out well.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t know how it is to turn out for Mark,\' answered May.  \'Oh, here\r\nhe comes!\'\r\n\r\n\'Will you come into this set, Annaple?\' he asked.  \'They want another\r\ncouple,\' and, as she accepted,  \'How do you get on with May\'s double?\'\r\n\r\n\'I pity May for having such a double.\'\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t encourage her by misplaced pity.\'\r\n\r\n\'It\'s abominable altogether!  I want to fly at somebody!\'\r\n\r\n\'Exhaust your feelings on your racket, and reflect that you see a man\r\nreleased from bondage.\'\r\n\r\n\'Is that philosophy or high-faluting?\' she said in a teasing tone as\r\nthe game began.\r\n\r\nThe Ruthvens had very blue blood in their veins, but as there were nine\r\nof the present generation, they possessed little beyond their long\r\npedigree; even the head of the family, Lord Ronnisglen, being forced to\r\nlive as a soldier, leaving his castle to grouse shooters. His seven\r\nbrothers had fared mostly in distant lands as they could, and his\r\nmother had found a home, together with her youngest child, at Lescombe,\r\nwhere her eldest was the wife of Sir John Delmar.  Lady Ronnisglen was\r\nan invalid, confined to the house, and Lady Delmar had daughters fast\r\ntreading on the heels of Annabella, so christened, but always called\r\nAnnaple after the old Scottish queens, her ancestors. She had been May\r\nEgremont\'s chief friend ever since her importation at twelve years old,\r\nand the intimacy had been promoted by her mother and sister.  Indeed,\r\nthe neighbourhood had looked on with some amusement at the competition\r\nascribed to Lady Delmar and to the wealthy parvenu, Mrs. West, for the\r\nheir-presumptive of Bridgefield Egremont.\r\n\r\nAnnaple\'s lightness and dexterity rendered her the best of the lady\r\ntennis-players, and the less practised Ursula found herself defeated in\r\nthe match, in spite of a partner whose play was superior to Mark\'s, and\r\nwith whom she shyly walked off to eat ices.\r\n\r\n\'I see,\' said Annaple, \'it is a country-town edition of May.  I shan\'t\r\nblunder between them again.\'\r\n\r\n\'She will polish,\' said Mark, \'but she is not equal to her mother.\'\r\n\r\n\'Whom I have not seen yet.  Ah, there\'s Mr. Egremont!  Why, he looks\r\nquite renovated!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well he may be!\'\r\n\r\n\'But Mark, not to hurt your feelings, he must have behaved atrociously.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m not going to deny it,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\n\'I always did think he looked like it,\' said Annaple.\r\n\r\n\'When have you seen him before?\'\r\n\r\n\'Only once, but it was my admirable sagacity, you understand?  I always\r\nsee all the villains in books just on his model.  Oh, but who\'s that?\r\nHow very pretty!  You don\'t mean it is she!  Well, she might be the\r\nheroine of anything!\'\r\n\r\n\'Isn\'t she lovely?\'\r\n\r\n\'And has she been keeping school like Patience on a monument all these\r\nyears?  It doesn\'t seem to have much damaged her damask cheek!\'\r\n\r\n\'It was only daily governessing.  She looks much better than when I\r\nfirst saw her; and as to the damask--why, that\'s deepened by the\r\nintroduction to old Lady Grosmede that is impending.\'\r\n\r\n\'She is being walked up to the old Spanish duck with the red rag round\r\nher leg to receive her fiat.  What a thing it is to be a bearded\r\nDowager, and rule one\'s neighbourhood!\'\r\n\r\n\'I think she approves.  She has made room for her by her side.  Is she\r\ngoing to catechise her?\'\r\n\r\nAnnaple made an absurd sound of mingled pity and disgust.\r\n\r\n\'Not that she--my aunt, I mean--need be afraid.  The shame is all on\r\nthe other side.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I think Lady Grosmede has too much sense to think the worse of her\r\nfor having worked for herself,\' added Annaple.  \'If it was not for\r\nmother I should long to begin!\'\r\n\r\n\'You?  It\'s a longing well known to me!--but you!\'\r\n\r\n\'Exactly!  As the Irishman felt blue moulded for want of a bating, so\r\ndo I feel fagged out for want of an honest day\'s work.\'\r\n\r\n\'If one only knew what to turn to,\' said Mark so wearily that Annaple\r\nexclaimed,\r\n\r\n\'We seem to be in the frozen-out state of mind, and might walk up and\r\ndown singing "I\'ve got no work to do,"\'--to which she gave the well\r\nknown intonation.\r\n\r\n\'Too true,\' said he, joining in the hum.\r\n\r\n\'But I thought you were by way of reading law.\'\r\n\r\n\'One must see more than only "by way of" in these days to do any good.\'\r\n\r\nAt that moment Basil ran up with a message that Lady Delmar was ready\r\nto go home.\r\n\r\nThey walked slowly up the terrace and Mark paused as they came near\r\nMrs. Egremont to say, \'Aunt Alice, here is Miss Ruthven, May\'s great\r\nfriend.\'\r\n\r\nAnnaple met a pleasant smile, and they shook hands, exchanging an\r\nobservation or two, while a little way off Lady Grosmede was nodding\r\nher strong old face at Lady Delmar, and saying, \'Tell your mother I\'ll\r\nsoon come and see her, my dear.  That\'s a nice little innocent body,\r\nlady-like, and thoroughly presentable.  Alwyn Egremont might have done\r\nworse.\'\r\n\r\n\'The only wonder is he did not!\' returned Lady Delmar.  \'They make the\r\nbest of it here.\'\r\n\r\n\'Very good taste of them.  But, now I\'ve seen her, I don\'t believe\r\nthere\'s anything behind.  Very hard upon the poor young man, though it\r\nwas all his doing, his mother says.  I congratulate you that it had not\r\ngone any farther in that quarter.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, dear no!  Never dreamt of it.  She is May\'s friend, that\'s all.\'\r\n\r\nNevertheless Lady Delmar made a second descent in person to hurry\r\nAnnaple away.\r\n\r\n\'Isn\'t it disgusting?\' said May, catching her stepmother\'s smile.\r\n\r\n\'You will see a good deal more of the same kind,\' said the Canoness; \'I\r\nam afraid more mortification is in store for Mark than he guesses. I\r\nwish that girl were more like her mother.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mamma! a girl brought up among umbrella-makers!  Just fancy!  Why, she\r\nhas just nothing in her!\'\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t set Mark against her, May; he might do worse.\'\r\n\r\n\'Her head is a mere tennis ball,\' said May, drawing her own higher than\r\never, \'and no one would know her from a shop girl.\'\r\n\r\n\'She is young enough,\' said the Canoness.  \'Don\'t class me with Lady\r\nDelmar, May--I only say--if--and that I don\'t think you realise the\r\nchange Mark will feel.\'\r\n\r\n\'Better so than sell himself,\' muttered May.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\n\r\nOUT OF WORK.\r\n\r\n  \'I\'m seeking the fruit that\'s nae growing.\'--Ballad.\r\n\r\n\r\nSociety recognised the newcomers.  Lady Grosmede\'s card appeared the\r\nnext day, and was followed by showers of others, and everybody asked\r\neverybody \'Have you seen Mrs. Egremont?\'\r\n\r\nIt was well for Alice\'s happiness even at home that she was a success.\r\nWhen Alwyn Egremont had been lashed by his nephew\'s indignant integrity\r\ninto tardy recognition of the wife of his youth, it had been as if he\r\nhad been forced to pick up a flower which he had thrown away.  He had\r\nconsiderable doubts whether it would answer. First, he reconnoitred,\r\nintending, if he found a homely or faded being, to pension her off; but\r\nthis had been prevented by her undeniable beauty and grace, bringing up\r\na rush of such tender associations as he was capable of.  Yet even\r\nthen, her position depended on the impression she might make on those\r\nabout him, on her own power of self-assertion, and on her contributing\r\nto his comfort or pleasure.\r\n\r\nOf self-assertion Alice had none, only a gentle dignity in her\r\nsimplicity, and she was so absolutely devoted to him that he found his\r\nhouse far more pleasant and agreeable for her presence and unfailing\r\nattention, though still his estimation of her was influenced more than\r\nhe owned to himself by that of the world in general, and the Rectory in\r\nparticular.\r\n\r\nAnd the Rectory did its part well.  The Canon was not only charmed with\r\nthe gentle lady, but felt an atonement due to her; and his wife,\r\nwithout ever breathing into any ears, save his, the mysterious\r\nadjective \'governessy,\' praised her right and left, confiding to all\r\ninquirers the romance of the burnt yacht, the lost bride, and the happy\r\nmeeting under Lady Kirkaldy\'s auspices, with the perfect respectability\r\nof the intermediate career, while such was the universal esteem for,\r\nand trust in herself and the Canon, that she was fully believed; and\r\npeople only whispered that probably Alwyn Egremont had been excused for\r\nthe desertion more than he deserved.\r\n\r\nThe subject of all this gossip troubled herself about it infinitely\r\nless than did the good Canoness.  In effect she did not know enough of\r\nthe world to think about it at all.  Her cares were of a different\r\norder, chiefly caused by tenderness of conscience, and solicitude to\r\nkeep the peace between the two beings whom she best loved.\r\n\r\nTwo things were in her favour in this latter respect, one that they saw\r\nvery little of each other, since Mr. Egremont seldom emerged from his\r\nown rooms till after luncheon; and the other that Ursula\'s brains ran\r\nto little but lawn-tennis for the ensuing weeks.  To hold a champion\'s\r\nplace at the tournaments, neck and neck with her cousin Blanche, and\r\ndefeat Miss Ruthven, and that veteran player, Miss Basset, was her\r\nforemost ambition, and the two cousins would have practised morning,\r\nnoon, and night if their mothers would have let them.  There need have\r\nbeen no fear of Ursula\'s rebellion about the Cambridge honours, she\r\nnever seemed even to think of them, and would have had no time in the\r\nmore important competition of rackets. Indeed, it was almost treated as\r\na hardship that the pair were forbidden to rush together before twelve\r\no\'clock, and that Ursula\'s mother insisted on rational home occupation\r\nuntil that time, setting the example herself by letter-writing,\r\nneedlework, and sharing in the music which was a penance to the girl,\r\nonly enforced by that strong sense of protecting affection which\r\nforbade rebellion.  But Alice could hope that their performances were\r\npleasant to her husband in the evening, if only to sleep by, and so she\r\npersisted in preparing for them.\r\n\r\nNuttie\'s rage for tennis, and apparent forgetfulness of her old life\r\nand aspirations, might be disappointing, but it conduced to make her\r\nmother\'s task easier than if she had been her original, critical, and\r\nprotesting self.  In the new and brilliant surroundings she troubled\r\nherself much less than could have been expected at the failure of her\r\nfather, his house, nay, and of the parish itself, in coming up to the\r\nSt. Ambrose standard.  How much was owing to mere novelty and\r\nintoxication, how much to a yet unanalysed disappointment, how much to\r\nMay\'s having thrown her upon the more frivolous Blanche, could not be\r\nguessed.  The effect was unsatisfactory to her mother, but a certain\r\nrelief, for Nuttie\'s aid would have been only mischievous in the\r\nhousehold difficulties that weighed on the anxious conscience. Good\r\nservants would not stay at Bridgefield Hall for unexplained causes,\r\nwhich their mistress believed to be connected with Gregorio, or with\r\nthe treasure of a cook-housekeeper over whom she was forbidden to\r\nexercise any authority, and who therefore entirely neglected all meals\r\nwhich the master did not share with the ladies. Fortunately, Mr.\r\nEgremont came in one day at their luncheon and found nothing there but\r\nsemi-raw beef, upon which there was an explosion; and being by this\r\ntime convinced that his wife both would and could minister to his\r\ncomfort, her dominion was established in the female department, though,\r\nas long as Gregorio continued paramount with his master, and the\r\nstables remained in their former state, it was impossible to bring\r\nmatters up to the decorous standard of the Rectory, and if ever his\r\nmistress gave an order he did not approve, Gregorio overruled it as her\r\nignorance.  In fact, he treated both the ladies with a contemptuous\r\nsort of civility.  Meantime Mr. Egremont was generally caressing and\r\nadmiring in his ways towards his wife, with only occasional bursts of\r\ntemper when anything annoyed him.  He was proud of her, gave her a\r\nliberal allowance, and only refused to be troubled; and she was really\r\nhappy in his affection, for which she felt a gratitude only too humble\r\nin the eyes of her daughter.\r\n\r\nThey had parties.  Blanche\'s ambition of tennis courts all over the\r\nlawn was fulfilled, and sundry dinners, which were crosses to Alice,\r\nwho had neither faculty nor training for a leader and hostess, suffered\r\nmuch from the menu, more from the pairing of her guests, more again in\r\ncatching her chief lady\'s eye after, and most of all from her husband\'s\r\nscowls and subsequent growls and their consequence, for Ursula broke\r\nout, \'It is not fair to blame my mother.  How should she have all the\r\nsavoir-faire, or what you may call it, of Aunt Jane, when she has had\r\nno practice?\'\r\n\r\n\'Perhaps, Mrs. Egremont,\' he retorted with extreme suavity, \'you will\r\nalso attend to your daughter\'s manners.\'  Otherwise he took little\r\nnotice of Ursula, viewing her perhaps, as did the neighbourhood, as a\r\npoor imitation of May, without her style, or it may be with a sense\r\nthat her tongue might become inconvenient if not repressed.  When he\r\nbegan to collect sporting guests of his own calibre in the shooting\r\nseason, the Canoness quietly advised her sister-in-law to regard them\r\nas gentlemen\'s parties, and send Ursula down to spend the evening with\r\nher cousins; and to this no objection was made.  Mr. Egremont wanted\r\nhis beautiful wife at the head of his table, and his guests never\r\ncomported themselves unsuitably before her; but nobody wanted the\r\nunformed girl, and she and Blanche were always happy together.\r\n\r\nThe chief restraint was when Mark was at home, and that was not always.\r\nHe made sundry visits and expeditions, and was altogether in an\r\nuncomfortable condition of reaction and perplexity as to his future.\r\nHe was a good and conscientious fellow, and had never been actually\r\nidle, but had taken education and life with the easiness of the\r\nprospective heir to a large property; and though he had acquitted\r\nhimself creditably, it was with no view of making his powers\r\nmarketable.  Though he had been entered at the Temple, it was chiefly\r\nin order to occupy himself respectably, and to have a nominal\r\nprofession, so as not to be wholly dependent on his uncle; and all that\r\nhe had acquired was the conviction that it would be half a lifetime, if\r\nnot a whole one, before the law would afford him a maintenance.\r\n\r\nHis father wished him to take Holy Orders with a view to the reversion\r\nof the Rectory, but Mark\'s estimate of clerical duty and vocation was\r\njust such as to make him shrink from them.  He was three-and-twenty, an\r\nawkward age for all those examinations that stand as lions in the face\r\nof youth intended for almost any sort of service, and seldom or never\r\nto be gagged by interest.  For one indeed, he went up and failed, and\r\nin such a manner as to convince him that cramming had more to do than\r\ngeneral culture with success.\r\n\r\nHe had a certain consciousness that most people thought another way\r\nopen to him, most decidedly his gentle aunt, and perhaps even his\r\nparents.  The matter came prominently before him one day at luncheon,\r\nwhen, some parochial affairs being on hand and Mr. Egremont out for the\r\nday, Alice, whose free forenoons enabled her to take a share in church\r\nand parish affairs, was there, as well as the curate and his wife.\r\n\r\nThese good people were in great commotion about a wedding about to take\r\nplace between a young farmer and his delicate first cousin, the only\r\nsurvivor of a consumptive family.\r\n\r\n\'"Proputty, proputty,"\' quoted the Canon.  \'James Johnson is what they\r\ncall a warm man.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is a sin and a shame,\' said Mrs. Edwards.  \'What can they expect?\r\nGeorge Johnson looks strong enough now, but they tell me his brother\r\nundoubtedly died of decline, though they called it inflammation; but\r\nthere was tubercular disease.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am afraid it is strong in the family,\' said the Canoness, \'they all\r\nhave those clear complexions; but I do believe George is heartily in\r\nlove with poor little Emily.\'\r\n\r\n\'First cousins ought to be in the table of degrees,\' said Mr. Edwards.\r\n\r\n\'It is always a question whether the multiplying of prohibitions\r\nwithout absolute necessity is expedient,\' said the Canon.\r\n\r\nHe spoke quite dispassionately, but the excellent couple were not\r\nremarkable for tact.  Mrs. Edwards gave her husband such a glance of\r\nwarning and consternation as violently inclined May to laugh, and he\r\nobediently and hesitatingly began, \'Oh yes, sir, I beg your pardon. Of\r\ncourse there may be instances,\' thereby bringing an intense glow of\r\ncarnation into Alice\'s cheeks, while the Canon, ready for the occasion,\r\nreplied, \'And George Johnson considers himself one of them. He will\r\nrepair the old moat house, I suppose.\'\r\n\r\nAnd his wife, though she would rather have beaten Mrs. Edwards,\r\ndemanded how many blankets would be wanted that winter.\r\n\r\nThe effect of this little episode was that Mark announced to his father\r\nthat evening his strong desire to emigrate, an intention which the\r\nCanon combated with all his might.  He was apparently a hale and hearty\r\nman, but he had had one or two attacks of illness that made him doubt\r\nwhether he would be long-lived; and not only could he not bear to have\r\nhis eldest son out of reach, but he dreaded leaving his family to such\r\na head as his brother.  Mark scarcely thought the reasons valid,\r\nconsidering the rapidity of communication with Canada, but it was not\r\npossible to withstand the entreaties of a father with tears in his\r\neyes; and though he could not bring himself to consent to preparing to\r\nbe his father\'s curate, he promised to do nothing that would remove him\r\nto another quarter of the world, and in two or three days more, started\r\nfor Monks Horton to see what advice his uncle and aunt there could give\r\nhim; indeed, Lord Kirkaldy\'s influence was reckoned on by his family\r\nalmost as a sure card in the diplomatic line.\r\n\r\nThe Kirkaldys were very fond of Mark, and had an odd feeling of being\r\naccountable for the discovery which had changed his prospects.  They\r\nwould have done anything for him that they could, but all Lord\r\nKirkaldy\'s interest was at the foreign office, or with his\r\nfellow-diplomates, and here he soon found an insuperable bar.  Mark\'s\r\neducation had stood still from the time of Miss Headworth\'s flight till\r\nhis father\'s second marriage, his energies having been solely devoted\r\nto struggles with the grim varieties of governess purveyed by his\r\ngrandmother, and he had thus missed all chance of foundation of foreign\r\nlanguages, and when once at school, he had shared in the average\r\nEnglish boy\'s contempt and aversion for the French masters who\r\noutscreamed a whole class.\r\n\r\nIn consequence, Lord Kirkaldy, an accurate and elegant scholar in\r\nEuropean tongues, besides speaking them with the cosmopolitan ease of\r\nan ambassador\'s son, was horrified, not only at Mark\'s pronunciation,\r\nbut at his attempts at letter-writing and translation, made with all\r\nthe good will in the world, but fit for nothing but to furnish the good\r\nstories which the kind uncle refrained from telling any one but his\r\nwife.  Unluckily, too, a Piedmontese family, some of them not strong in\r\ntheir English, were on a visit at Monks Horton, and the dialect in\r\nwhich the old marquis and Mark tried at times to interchange ideas\r\nabout pheasants was something fearful.  And as in the course of a week\r\nMark showed no signs of improvement in vernacular French or Italian,\r\nLord Kirkaldy\'s conscience would let him give no other advice than that\r\nhis nephew should stick to English law living still on the allowance\r\nhis father gave him, and hoping for one of the chance appointments open\r\nto an English barrister of good family and fair ability.\r\n\r\nOf course Mark had gone at once to carry tidings of \'Aunt Alice,\' as he\r\nscrupulously called her, to old Miss Headworth, whom his aunt had\r\ncontinued to visit at intervals.  That good lady had given up her\r\nboarders, having realised enough to provide for her own old age, and\r\nshe had joined forces with the Nugents, Mary being very thankful to\r\nhave her companionship for Mrs. Nugent, who was growing too blind and\r\nfeeble to be satisfactorily left alone all day.\r\n\r\nMark delighted the old ladies by his visits and accounts of their\r\ndarling\'s success and popularity, which he could paint so brightly that\r\nthey could not help exulting, even though there might be secret\r\nmisgivings as to the endurance of these palmy days.  He was a great\r\nhero in their eyes, and they had too good taste to oppress him with\r\ntheir admiration, so that he really was more at ease in their little\r\ndrawing-room than anywhere at Monks Horton, whither the Italians could\r\npenetrate.  The marchesino spoke English very well, but that was all\r\nthe worse for Mark, since it gave such a sense of inferiority.  He was\r\nan intelligent man too, bent on being acquainted with English\r\nindustries of all kinds; and thus it was that a party was organised to\r\nsee the umbrella factory.  It was conducted by Mr. Dutton, with whom\r\nLord Kirkaldy, between charities and public business, had become\r\nacquainted.\r\n\r\nTo Mark\'s secret shame, this manufacturer spoke French perfectly, and\r\neven got into such a lively conversation with the old marquis about\r\nCavour, that Lord Kirkaldy begged him to come to dinner and continue\r\nit.  They were all surprised, not only by the details of the\r\nmanufacture and the multitude of artizans, male and female, whom it\r\nemployed, but by the number of warehouse-clerks whom they found at\r\nwork, and who, it appeared, were in correspondence with agencies and\r\ndepots in London and all the principal towns in the kingdom.  Gerard\r\nGodfrey was there,--casting looks askance at the young Egremont, whom\r\nhe regarded as a kind of robber.\r\n\r\nThe marchesino asked from what class these young men were taken, and\r\nMr. Dutton made reply that most of them were sons of professional men.\r\nIf they could obtain a small capital and take shares in the business\r\nthey were encouraged to do so, and rose to the headship of the\r\nagencies, obtaining a fair income.\r\n\r\n\'And you don\'t exact an examination,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\n\'Except in handwriting and book-keeping,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Poor Mark, you look for your bugbear everywhere!\' sighed his aunt.\r\n\r\nThey went over the Institute, coffee-rooms, eating-rooms, and\r\nlodging-houses, by which the umbrella firm strove to keep their hands\r\nrespectable and contented, and were highly pleased with all, most\r\nespecially with Mr. Dutton, who, though his name did not come\r\nprominently forward, had been the prime mover and contriver of all\r\nthese things, and might have been a wealthier man if he had not\r\nundertaken expenses which he could not charge upon the company.\r\n\r\nGerard Godfrey came in to Mrs. Nugent\'s that evening in the lowest\r\nspirits.  He had a sister married to a curate in the same county with\r\nBridgefield, and she had sent him a local paper which \'understood that\r\na marriage was arranged between Mark de Lyonnais Egremont, Esquire, and\r\nUrsula, daughter of Alwyn Piercefield Egremont, Esquire, of Bridgefield\r\nEgremont,\' and he could not help coming to display it to Miss Headworth\r\nin all its impertinence and prematurity.\r\n\r\n\'Indeed he said nothing to me about it,\' said Miss Headworth, \'and I\r\nthink he would if it had been true.\'\r\n\r\n\'No doubt he intends it, and is trying to recommend himself through\r\nyou,\' said Gerard.\r\n\r\n\'I should not think he needed that,\' returned Aunt Ursel, \'though I\r\nshould be very glad, I am sure.  He is an excellent young man, and it\r\nis quite the obvious thing.\'\r\n\r\n\'People don\'t always do the obvious thing,\' put in Mary Nugent.\r\n\r\n\'Certainly it didn\'t look like it,\' said Miss Headworth,\' when he told\r\nus about the great annual Hunt Ball at Redcastle that Nuttie and his\r\nsister Blanche are to come out at; he said he did not intend to go home\r\nfor it if he could help it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Struggling against fate,\' said Miss Nugent.\r\n\r\n\'The puppy!\' burst out Gerard.\r\n\r\nHaving ascertained the particulars of this same Hunt Ball, Gerard\r\nbecame possessed with a vehement desire to visit his sister, and so\r\nearnestly solicited a few days\' leave of absence that it was granted to\r\nhim.  \'Poor boy, he may settle down when he has ascertained what an ass\r\nhe is,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Ah!\' said Mary.  \'I thought he was very bad when I saw he had not\r\nchanged the green markers for St. Luke\'s Day.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\n\r\nDETRIMENTALS.\r\n\r\n  \'That tongue of yours at times wags more than charity allows;\r\n   And if you\'re strong, be merciful, great woman of three cows.\'\r\n                                                     --J. O. MANGAN.\r\n\r\n\r\nNine miles was a severe distance through country lanes in November to\r\ngo to a ball; but the Redcastle Hunt Ball was the ball of the year,\r\nuniting all the county magnates; and young ladies were hardly reckoned\r\nas \'come out\' till they had appeared there.  Mrs. Egremont\'s position\r\nwould hardly be established till she had been presented to the\r\nnotabilities who lived beyond calling intercourse; and her husband\r\nprepared himself to be victimised with an amount of grumbling that was\r\nintended to impress her with the magnitude of the sacrifice, but which\r\nonly made her offer to forego the gaiety, and be told that she would\r\nnever have any common sense.\r\n\r\nSo their carriage led the way, and was followed by the Rectory\r\nwaggonette containing the ladies and Mark, who had been decisively\r\nsummoned home, since his stepmother disliked public balls without a\r\ngentleman in attendance, and his father was not to be detached from his\r\nfireside.\r\n\r\nAnd in a group near the door, got up as elaborately as his powers could\r\naccomplish, stood Gerard Godfrey.  He knew nobody there except a family\r\nin his sister\'s parish, who had good-naturedly given him a seat in\r\ntheir fly, and having fulfilled his duty by asking the daughter to\r\ndance, he had nothing to disturb him in watching for the cynosure whose\r\nattraction had led him into these unknown regions, and, as he\r\nremembered with a qualm, on the eve of St. Britius. However, with such\r\na purpose, one might surely grant oneself a dispensation from the vigil\r\nof a black letter saint.\r\n\r\nThere at length he beheld the entrance.  There was the ogre himself,\r\nhigh bred, almost handsome, as long as he was not too closely\r\nscrutinised, and on his arm the well-known figure, metamorphosed by\r\ndelicately-tinted satin sheen and pearls, and still more by the gentle\r\nblushing gladness on the fair cheeks and the soft eyes that used to\r\ndroop.  Then followed a stately form in mulberry moire and point lace,\r\nleaning on Gerard\'s more especial abhorrence,--\'that puppy,\' who had\r\nbeen the author of all the mischief; and behind them three girls, one\r\nin black, the other two in white, and, what was provoking, he really\r\ncould not decide which was Ursula.  The carefully-dressed hair and\r\nstylish evening dress and equipments had altogether transformed the\r\nlittle homely schoolgirl, so that, though he was sure that she was not\r\nthe fair-haired damsel with pale blue flowers, he did not know how to\r\ndecide between the white and daisies and the black and grasses.\r\nIndeed, he thought the two whites most likely to be sisters, and all\r\nthe more when the black lace halted to exchange greetings with some\r\none, and her face put on an expression so familiar to him, that he\r\nstarted forward and tried to catch her eye; but in vain, and he\r\nsuffered agonies of doubt whether she had been perverted by greatness.\r\n\r\nIt was some comfort that, when presently a rush of waiters floated by,\r\nshe was not with her cousin; but to provoke him still more, as the\r\ndaisies neared him, he beheld for a moment in the whirl the queer\r\nsmile, half-frightened, half-exultant, which he had seen on Nuttie\'s\r\nface when swinging sky-high!\r\n\r\nWhen the pause came and people walked about, the black lady stood\r\ntalking so near him that he ventured at last on a step forward and an\r\neager \'Miss Egremont,\' but, as she turned, he found himself obliged to\r\nsay, \'I beg your pardon.\'\r\n\r\n\'Did you mean my cousin.  We often get mistaken for each other,\' said\r\nMay civilly.\r\n\r\nHe brightened.  \'I beg your pardon,\' he said, \'I knew her at\r\nMicklethwayte.  I am here--quite by accident.  Mrs. Elmore was so good\r\nas to bring me.\'\r\n\r\nMay was rather entertained.  \'There\'s my cousin,\' she said, \'Lord\r\nPhilip Molyneux is asking her to dance,\' and she left him most\r\nunnecessarily infuriated with Lord Philip Molyneux.\r\n\r\nA steward introduced him to a dull-looking girl, but fortune favoured\r\nhim, for this time he did catch the real Nuttie\'s eye, and all herself,\r\nas soon as the dance was over, she came up with outstretched hands, \'Oh\r\nGerard! to think of your being here!  Come to mother!\'\r\n\r\nAnd, beautiful and radiant, Mrs. Egremont was greeting him, and there\r\nwere ten minutes of delicious exchange of news.  But \'pleasures are as\r\npoppies fled,\' Nuttie had no dance to spare, her card was full, and she\r\nhad not learnt fashionable effrontery enough to play tricks with\r\nengagements, and just then Mr. Egremont descended on them--\'I wish to\r\nintroduce you to the Duchess,\' he said to his wife; and on the way he\r\ndemanded--\'Who is that young cub?\'\r\n\r\n\'Gerard Godfrey--an old neighbour.\'\r\n\r\n\'I thought I had seen him racketing about there with Ursula.  I\'ll not\r\nhave those umbrella fellows coming about!\'\r\n\r\n\'Does he really make umbrellas, Nuttie?\' asked Blanche, catching her\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\'No such thing!\' said Nuttie hotly, \'he is in the office.  His father\r\nwas a surgeon; his sisters married clergymen!\'\r\n\r\n\'And he came here to meet you,\' said Annaple Ruthven.  \'Poor fellow,\r\nwhat a shame it is!  Can\'t you give him one turn!\r\n\r\n\'Oh dear!  I\'m engaged all through!  To Mark this time.\'\r\n\r\n\'Give him one of the extras!  Throw Mark over to me!  No,\' as she\r\nlooked at the faces of the two girls, \'I suppose that wouldn\'t do, but\r\nI\'m free this time--I\'m not the fashion.  Introduce me; I\'ll do my best\r\nas consolation.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie had just performed the feat, with great shyness, when Mark\r\nappeared, having been sent in quest of his cousin, when her father\r\nperceived that she had hung back.\r\n\r\nPoor Gerard led off Miss Ruthven the more gloomily, and could not help\r\nsighing out, \'I suppose that is an engagement!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! you believe that impertinent gossip in the paper,\' returned\r\nAnnaple.  \'I wonder they don\'t contradict it; but perhaps they treat it\r\nwith magnificent scorn.\'\r\n\r\n\'No doubt they know that it is only premature.\'\r\n\r\n\'If _they_ means the elders, I daresay they wish it, but we aren\'t in\r\nFrance or Italy.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then you don\'t think, Miss Ruthven, that it will come off?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t see the slightest present prospect,\' said Annaple, unable to\r\nresist the kindly impulse of giving immediate pleasure, though she knew\r\nthe prospect might be even slighter for her partner.\r\n\r\nHowever, he \'footed it\' all the more lightly and joyously for the\r\nassurance, and the good-natured maiden afterwards made him conduct her\r\nto the tea-room, whither Mark and Nuttie were also tending, and there\r\nall four contrived to get mixed up together; and Nuttie had time to\r\nhear of Monsieur\'s new accomplishment of going home for Mr. Dutton\'s\r\nluncheon and bringing it in a basket to the office, before fate again\r\ndescended; Mr. Egremont, who had been at the far end of the room among\r\nsome congeners, who preferred stronger refreshment, suddenly heard her\r\nlaugh, stepped up, and, with a look of thunder towards her, observed in\r\na low voice, \'Mark, you will oblige me by taking your cousin back to\r\nher mother.\'\r\n\r\n\'The gray tyrant father,\' murmured Annaple in sympathy.  \'That being\r\nthe case, I may as well go back in that direction also.\'\r\n\r\nThis resulted in finding Lady Delmar and the two Mrs. Egremonts\r\ntogether, comparing notes about the two different roads to Redcastle\r\nfrom their several homes.\r\n\r\nLady Delmar was declaring that her coachman was the most obstinate man\r\nin existence, and that her husband believed in him to any extent.\r\n\r\n\'Which way did you come?\' she asked.\r\n\r\n\'By Bankside Lane,\' said the Canoness.\r\n\r\n\'Over Bluepost Bridge!  There, Janet,\' said Annaple.\r\n\r\n\'So much the worse.  I know we shall come to grief over Bluepost\r\nBridge, and now there will be treble weight to break it down.  I dreamt\r\nit, I tell you, and there\'s second sight in the family.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, but you should tell what you did dream, Janet,\' said her sister.\r\n\'She thought Robinson, the coachman, was waltzing with her over it, and\r\nthey went into a hole and stuck fast, while the red-flag traction\r\nengineman prodded her with an umbrella till she was all over blood.\r\nNow, if it had been anything rational, I should have thought something\r\nof her second sight!  I tell her \'twas suggested by--\r\n\r\n         "London Bridge is broken down,\r\n          Dance o\'er my lady Lee!"\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Well, I am quite certain those traction-engines will break it some\r\ntime or other,\' said Lady Delmar.  \'I am always trying to get John to\r\nbring it before the magistrates, but he only laughs at me, and nothing\r\nwill induce Robinson to go the other way, because they have just been\r\nmending the road on Lescombe Hill!  Annaple, my dear, I can\'t allow you\r\nanother waltz; Mark must excuse you--I am going.  It is half-past two,\r\nand the carriage was ordered at two!  Robinson will be in a worse\r\ntemper than ever if we keep him waiting.\'\r\n\r\nShe bore her sister off to the cloak-room, and there, nearly an hour\r\nlater, the Egremonts found them still waiting the pleasure of the\r\nimplacable Robinson; but what was that in consideration of having kept\r\nher sister from such a detrimental as poor Mark had become?  So\r\nmuttered Mr. Egremont, in the satisfaction of having himself, with\r\ngentlemanly severity, intimated the insuperable gulf between Miss\r\nEgremont of Bridgefield and the Man of Umbrellas.\r\n\r\nMoreover, his sister-in-law took care that he should hear that the\r\nDuchess of Redcastle had pronounced his wife sweetly pretty and\r\nlady-like, and talked of inviting them for a visit of a few nights.\r\n\r\n\'A bore,\' observed he ungratefully, \'\'tis as dull as ditchwater.\' But,\r\nin truth, though the Canon\'s family, when in residence, were intimate\r\nwith the ducal family, Alwyn Egremont had never been at the castle\r\nsince the days of his earliest youth, and he was not quite prepared to\r\nowe his toleration there to his wife\'s charms, or the Canoness\'s\r\npatronage of her.\r\n\r\nAnd innocent Alice only knew that everybody had been very kind to her,\r\nand it was only a pity that her husband did not like her to notice poor\r\nGerard Godfrey.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\n\r\nGOING AGEE.\r\n\r\n  \'Gin ye were a brig as auld as me.\'--BURNS.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'What\'s the matter?\' exclaimed Mrs. Egremont, waking from a\r\ndoze,--\'that bridge?\'\r\n\r\n\'Bridge!  Don\'t be such a fool!  We aren\'t near it yet.\'\r\n\r\nThe servant, his face looking blurred through the window, came to\r\nexplain that the delay was caused by an agricultural engine, which had\r\nchosen this unlucky night, or morning, to travel from one farm to\r\nanother.  There was a long delay, while the monster could be heard\r\ncoughing frightfully before it could be backed with its spiky companion\r\ninto a field so as to let the carriages pass by; and meantime Mr.\r\nEgremont was betrayed into uttering ejaculations which made poor Nuttie\r\nround her eyes in the dark as she sat by his feet on the back seat, and\r\nAlice try to bury her ears in her hood in the corner.\r\n\r\nOn they went at last, for about a mile, and then came another sudden\r\nstop--another fierce growl from Mr. Egremont, another apparition of the\r\nservant at the window, saying, in his alert deferential manner, \'Sir,\r\nthe bridge have broke under a carriage in front.  Lady Delmar\'s, sir.\r\nThe horse is plunging terrible.\'\r\n\r\nThe door was torn open, and all three, regardless of ball costumes,\r\nprecipitated themselves out.\r\n\r\nThe moon was up, and they saw the Rectory carriage safe on the road\r\nbefore them, but on the bridge beyond was a struggling mass, dimly\r\nilluminated by a single carriage lamp. Mr. Egremont and the groom\r\nhurried forward where Mark and the Rectory coachman were already\r\nrendering what help they could.  May standing at the horses\' heads, and\r\nher mother trying to wrap everybody up, since stay in their carriages\r\nthey could not.  Transferring the horses to Nuttie, the two sisters\r\nhurried on towards the scene of action, but Blanche\'s white satin boots\r\ndid not carry her far, and she turned on meeting her uncle.  He spoke\r\nwith a briskness and alacrity that made him like another man in this\r\nemergency, as he assured the anxious ladies that their friends were\r\nsafe, but that they could not be extricated till the carriage was\r\nlifted from the hole into which it had sunk amid bricks, stones, and\r\nbroken timbers.  He sent his own coachman to assist, as being the\r\nstronger man, and, mounting the box, turned and drove off in quest of\r\nfurther help, at a wayside cottage, or from the attendants on the\r\nengine, whose weight had probably done the mischief, and prepared the\r\ntrap for the next comer.\r\n\r\nAs May came near, her brother made her available by putting the lamp\r\ninto her hand, bidding her hold it so as to light those who were\r\nendeavouring to release the horse, which had cleared the portion of the\r\nbridge before the break-down under the brougham, and now lay on the\r\nroad, its struggles quelled by a servant at its head.  Nearly the whole\r\nof the hind wheels and most of the door had disappeared on one side,\r\nand, though more was visible on the other, it was impossible to open\r\nthe door, as a mass of rubbish lay on it.  Annaple was on this side,\r\nand her voice was heard calling to May in fits of the laughter which is\r\nperhaps near akin to screams--\r\n\r\n         \'"London bridge is broken down,\r\n           Dance o\'er my lady Lee!"\r\n\r\n\r\nJanet will go in for second-sight ever after.  Yes, she\'s all right,\r\nexcept a scratch from the glass, and that I\'m sitting on her more or\r\nless.  How are they getting on?\'  \'The horse is all but out.  Not hurt,\r\nthey think.  Here\'s another man come to help--a gentleman--my dear, it\r\nis your partner, Nuttie\'s umbrella man.\'  \'Oh, making it\r\ncomplete--hopes, Janet--I\'m sorry, but I can\'t help squashing you! I\r\ncan\'t help subsiding on you!  What is it now?\' as the lamp-light\r\nvanished.\r\n\r\n\'They are looking for something to make levers of,\' returned May;\r\n\'these wooden rails are too rotten.\'\r\n\r\n\'Can\'t they get us through the window?\' sighed a muffled voice.\r\n\r\n\'Not unless we could be elongated, like the Hope of the Katzekopfs.\'\r\n\r\n\'We shall manage now,\' cried Mark; \'we have found some iron bars to the\r\nhatch down there.  But you must prepare for a shock or two before you\r\ncan be set free.\'\r\n\r\nThe two gentlemen and three servants strove and struggled, hoisted and\r\npushed, to the tune of suppressed sounds, half of sobs, half of\r\nlaughter, till at last the carriage was heaved up sufficiently to be\r\ndragged backwards beyond the hole; but even then it would not stand,\r\nfor the wheels on the undermost side were crushed, neither could either\r\ndoor be readily opened, one being smashed in, and the other jammed\r\nfast.  Annaple, however, still tried to keep up her own spirits and her\r\nsister\'s, observing that she now knew how to sympathise with Johnnie\'s\r\ntin soldiers in their box turned upside down.\r\n\r\nTwo sturdy labourers here made their appearance, having been roused in\r\nthe cottage and brought back by Mr. Egremont, and at last one door was\r\nforced open by main force, and the ladies emerged, Annaple, helping her\r\nsister, beginning some droll thanks, but pausing as she perceived that\r\nLady Delmar\'s dress was covered with blood.\r\n\r\n\'My dear Janet.  This is worse than I guessed.  Why did you not speak?\'\r\n\r\n\'It is not much,\' said the poor lady, rather faintly.  \'My neck--\'\r\n\r\nThe elder ladies came about her, and seated her on cushions, where, by\r\nthe light of May\'s lamp, Alice, who had been to an ambulance class at\r\nMicklethwayte, detected the extent of the cut, extracted a fragment of\r\nglass, and staunched the bleeding with handkerchiefs and strips of the\r\ngirls\' tulle skirts, but she advised her patient to be driven at once\r\nto a surgeon to secure that no morsel of glass remained.  Mr. Egremont,\r\ngratified to see his wife come to the front, undertook to drive her\r\nback to Redcastle.  Indeed, they must return thither to cross by the\r\nhigher bridge.  \'You will go with me,\' entreated Lady Delmar, holding\r\nAlice\'s hand; and the one hastily consigning Nuttie to her aunt\'s care,\r\nthe other giving injunctions not to alarm her mother to Annaple, who\r\nhad declared her intention of walking home, the two ladies went off\r\nunder Mr. Egremont\'s escort.\r\n\r\nJust then it was discovered that the Delmar coachman, Robinson, had all\r\nthis time been lying insensible, not dead, for he moaned, but\r\napparently with a broken leg, if nothing worse.  Indeed, the men had\r\nknown it all along, but, until the ladies had been rescued, nothing had\r\nbeen possible but to put his cushion under his head and his rug over\r\nhim.  The ladies were much shocked, and Mrs. William Egremont decided\r\nthat he must be laid at the bottom of the waggonette, and that she\r\nwould take him straight to the hospital.\r\n\r\nThey were only a mile and a half from Lescombe, and it was pronounced\r\nsafe to cross on foot by the remains of the bridge, so that Annaple,\r\nwho had a pair of fur boots, had already decided on going home on foot.\r\nThe other girls wanted to accompany her, and, as May and Nuttie both\r\nhad overshoes, they were permitted to do so, and desired to go to bed,\r\nand wait to be picked up by the waggonette, which must return to\r\nBridgefield by the Lescombe road.  Blanche, having a delicate throat,\r\nwas sentenced to go with her stepmother.  Mark undertook to ride the\r\nhorse through the river, and escort the three girls, and Gerard Godfrey\r\nalso joined them.  The place where he was staying lay a couple of miles\r\nbeyond Lescombe, and when Mrs. Elmore\'s fly had been met and turned\r\nback by Mr. Egremont, he had jumped off to render assistance, and had\r\ndone so effectively enough to win Mark\'s gratitude.\r\n\r\nIt was by this time about half-past five, as was ascertained by the\r\nlight of the waning moon, the carriage-lamp having burnt out.  It was a\r\nfine frosty morning, and the moon was still powerful enough to reveal\r\nthe droll figures of the girls.  May had a fur cloak, with the hood\r\ntied over her head by Mrs. Egremont\'s lace shawl; Nuttie had a huge\r\nwhite cloud over her head, and a light blue opera cloak; Annaple had\r\n\'rowed herself in a plaidie\' like the Scotch girl she was, and her eyes\r\nflashed out merrily from its dark folds.  They all disdained the\r\ngentlemen\'s self-denying offers of their ulsters, and only Nuttie\r\nconsented to have the carriage-rug added to her trappings, and\r\ningeniously tied on cloak-fashion with her sash by Gerard.  He and Mark\r\npiloted the three ladies over the narrow border of the hole, which\r\nlooked a very black open gulf.  Annaple had thanked the men, and bidden\r\nthem come to Lescombe the next day to be paid for their assistance.\r\nThen they all stood to watch Mark ride through the river, at the\r\nshallowest place, indicated both by her and the labourers.  It was\r\nperfectly fordable, so Annaple\'s were mock heroics when she quoted--\r\n\r\n         \'Never heavier man and horse\r\n          Stemmed a midnight torrent\'s force.\'\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd Nuttie responded in a few seconds--\r\n\r\n         \'Yet through good heart and our Ladye\'s grace\r\n          Full soon he gained the landing place.\'\r\n\r\n\r\nThey were both in high spirits, admiring each other\'s droll appearance,\r\nand speculating on the ghosts they might appear to any one who chanced\r\nto look out of window.  Annaple walked at the horse\'s head, calling him\r\npoor old Robin Hood, and caressing him, while Gerard and Nuttie kept\r\ntogether.\r\n\r\nMay began to repent of her determination to walk; Lescombe seemed very\r\nfar off, and she had an instinct that she was an awkward fifth wheel.\r\nEither because Robin Hood walked too fast for her weary limbs, or\r\nbecause she felt it a greater duty to chaperon Nuttie than Annaple, she\r\nfell back on the couple in the rear, and was rather surprised at the\r\ntenor of their conversation.\r\n\r\nThis \'umbrella man\' was telling of his vicar\'s delight in the beautiful\r\nchalice veil that had been sent by Mrs. Egremont, and Nuttie was\r\ncommunicating, as a secret she ought not to tell, that mother was\r\nworking a set of stoles, and hoped to have the white ones ready by the\r\ndedication anniversary; also that there was a box being filled for the\r\nSt. Ambrose Christmas tree.  They were trying to get something nice for\r\neach of the choir boys and of the old women; and therewith, to May\'s\r\nsurprise, this youth, whom she regarded as a sort of shopman, fell into\r\nfull narration of all the events of a highly-worked parish,--all about\r\nthe choral festival, and the guilds, and the choir, and the temperance\r\nwork.  A great deal of it was a strange language to May, but she\r\nhalf-disapproved of it, as entirely unlike the \'soberness\' of\r\nBridgefield ways, and like the Redcastle vicar, whom her father\r\ncommonly called \'that madman.\'  Still, she had a practical soul for\r\nparish work, and could appreciate the earnestness that manifested\r\nitself, and the exertions made for people of the classes whom she had\r\nalways supposed too bad or else too well off to come under clerical\r\nsupervision.  And her aunt and cousin and this young man all evidently\r\nhad their hearts in it!  For Nuttie--though her new world had put the\r\nold one apparently aside--had plunged into all the old interests, and\r\nasked questions eagerly, and listened to their answers, as if\r\nMicklethwayte news was water to the thirsty. The two were too happy to\r\nmeet, and, it must be confessed, had not quite manners enough, to feel\r\nit needful to include in their conversation the weary figure that\r\nplodded along at a little distance from them, hardly attending to the\r\ndetails of their chatter, yet deriving new notions from it of the\r\nformer life of Ursula and her mother, matters which she had hitherto\r\nthought beneath her attention, except so far as to be thankful that\r\nthey had emerged from it so presentable.  That it was a more actively\r\nreligious, and perhaps a more intellectual one than her own, she had\r\nthought impossible, where everything must be second-rate.  And yet,\r\nwhen her attention had wandered from an account of Mr. Dutton\'s\r\ndealings with a refractory choir boy bent on going to the races, she\r\nfound a discussion going on about some past lectures upon astronomy,\r\nand Nuttie vehemently regretting the not attending two courses promised\r\nfor the coming winter upon electricity and on Italian art, and\r\nmournfully observing, \'We never go to anything sensible here.\'\r\n\r\nMay at first thought, \'Impertinent little thing,\' and felt affronted,\r\nbut then owned to herself that it was all too true.  Otherwise there\r\nwas hardly anything said about the contrast with Nuttie\'s present life;\r\nGerard knew already that the church atmosphere was very different, and\r\nwith the rector\'s daughter within earshot, he could not utter his\r\ncommiseration, nor Nuttie her regrets.\r\n\r\nOnce there was a general start, and the whole five came together at the\r\nsight of a spectrally black apparition, with a huge tufted head on\r\nhigh, bearing down over a low hedge upon them.  Nobody screamed except\r\nNuttie, but everybody started, though the next moment it was plain that\r\nthey were only chimney-sweepers on their way.\r\n\r\n\'Retribution for our desire to act ghosts!\' said Annaple, when the\r\nsable forms had been warned of the broken bridge.  \'Poor May, you are\r\nawfully tired!  Shouldn\'t you like a lift in their cart?\'\r\n\r\n\'Or I could put you up on Robin Hood,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, I don\'t think I could stick on.  Is it much farther?\'\r\n\r\n\'Only up the hill and across the park,\' said Annaple, still cheerily.\r\n\r\n\'Take my arm, old woman,\' said Mark, and then there was a pause, before\r\nAnnaple said in an odd voice, \'You may tell her, Mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Annaple!  Mark! is it so?\' cried May joyously, but under her\r\nbreath; and with a glance to see how near the other couple were.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Annaple between crying and laughing.  \'Poor Janet, she\'ll\r\nthink we have taken a frightfully mean advantage of her, but I am sure\r\nI never dreamt of such a thing; and the queer thing is, that Mark says\r\nshe put it into his head!\'\r\n\r\n\'No, no,\' said Mark; \'you know better than that--\'\r\n\r\n\'Why, you told me you only found it out when she began to trample on\r\nthe fallen--\'\r\n\r\n\'I told you I had only understood my own heart.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I said very much the same--she made me so angry you see.\'\r\n\r\n\'I can\'t but admire your motives!\' said May, exceedingly rejoiced all\r\nthe time, and ready to have embraced them both, if it had not been for\r\nthe spectators behind.  \'In fact, it was opposition you both wanted.  I\r\nwonder how long you would have gone on not finding it out, if all had\r\nbeen smooth?\'\r\n\r\n\'The worst of it is,\' said Annaple, \'that I\'m afraid it is a very bad\r\nthing for Mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not a bit of it,\' retorted he.  \'It is the only thing that could have\r\nput life into my work, or made me care to find any!  And find it I will\r\nnow!  Must we let the whole world in to know before I have found it,\r\nAnnaple?\'\r\n\r\n\'I could not but tell my mother,\' said Annaple.  \'It would come out in\r\nspite of me, even if I wished to keep it back.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes!  Lady Ronnisglen is a different thing,\' said Mark.  \'Just as\r\nMay here is--\'\r\n\r\n\'And she will say nothing, I know, till we are ready--my dear old\r\nminnie,\' said Annaple.  \'Only, Mark, do pray have something definite to\r\nhinder Janet with if there are any symptoms of hawking her commodity\r\nabout.\'\r\n\r\n\'I _will_,\' said Mark.  \'If we could only emigrate!\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah, if we could!\' said Annaple.  \'Ronald is doing so well in New\r\nZealand, but I don\'t think my mother could spare me.  She could not\r\ncome out, and she must be with me, wherever I am.  You know--don\'t\r\nyou--that I am seven years younger than Alick.  I was a regular\r\nsurprise, and the old nurse at Ronnisglen said \'Depend upon it, my\r\nLeddy, she is given to be the comfort of your old age.\'  And I have\r\nalways made up my mind never to leave her.  I don\'t think she would get\r\non with Janet or any of them without me, so you\'ll have to take her\r\ntoo, Mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'With all my heart,\' he answered.  \'And, indeed, I have promised my\r\nfather not to emigrate.  I must, and will, find work at hand, and wake\r\na home for you both!\'\r\n\r\n\'But you will tell papa at once?\' said May.  \'It will hurt him if you\r\ndo not.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are right, May; I knew it when Annaple spoke of her mother, but\r\nthere is no need that it should go further.\'\r\n\r\nThe intelligence had lightened the way a good deal, and they were at\r\nthe lodge gates by this time.  Gerard began rather ruefully to take\r\nleave; but Annaple, in large-hearted happiness and gratitude, begged\r\nhim to come and rest at the house, and wait for daylight, and this he\r\nwas only too glad to do, especially as May\'s secession had made the\r\nconversation a little more personal.\r\n\r\nNuttie was in a certain way realising for the first time what her\r\nmother\'s loyalty had checked her in expressing, even if the tumult of\r\nnovelties had given her full time to dwell on it.\r\n\r\n\'Everybody outside is kind,\' she said to Gerard; \'they are nice in a\r\nway, and good, but oh! they are centuries behind in church matters and\r\nfeeling, just like the old rector.\'\r\n\r\n\'I gathered that; I am very sorry for you.  Is there no one fit to be a\r\nguide?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t know,\' said Nuttie.  \'I didn\'t think--I must, somehow, before\r\nLent.\'\r\n\r\n\'There is Advent close at hand,\' he said gravely.  \'If you could only\r\nbe at our mission services, we hope to get Father Smith!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, if only I could!  But mother never likes to talk about those kind\r\nof things.  She says our duty is to my father.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not the foremost.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, she would not say that.
