But oh, Gerard! if he should be making\r\nher worldly!\'\r\n\r\n\'It must be your work to hinder it,\' he said, looking at her\r\naffectionately.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Gerard! but I\'m afraid I\'m getting so myself.  I have thought a\r\ngreat deal about lawn-tennis, and dress, and this ball,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\'Somehow it has never quite felt real, but as if I were out on a visit.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are in it, but not of it,\' said Gerard admiringly.\r\n\r\n\'No, I\'m not so good as that!  I like it all--almost all.  I thought I\r\nliked it better till you came and brought a real true breath of\r\nMicklethwayte.  Oh! if I could only see Monsieur\'s dear curly head and\r\nbright eyes!\'\r\n\r\nThis had been the tenor of the talk, and these were the actual last\r\nwords before the whole five--just in the first streaks of\r\ndawn--coalesced before the front door, to be admitted by a sleepy\r\nservant; Mark tied up the horse for a moment, while Annaple sent the\r\nman to waken Sir John Delmar, and say there had been a slight accident,\r\nbut no one was much hurt; and, as they all entered the warm,\r\ndimly-lighted hall, they were keenly sensible that they had been\r\ndancing or walking all night.\r\n\r\nRest in the chairs which stood round the big hearth and smouldering\r\nwood-fire was so extremely comfortable, as they all dropped down, that\r\nnobody moved or spoke, or knew how long it was before there was a voice\r\non the stairs--\'Eh? what\'s this, Annaple?  An accident? Where\'s Janet?\'\r\nand a tall burly figure, candle in hand, in a dressing-gown and\r\nslippers, was added to the group.\r\n\r\n\'Janet will be at home presently, I hope,\' said Annaple, \'but she got a\r\ncut with some broken glass, and we sent her round by Dr. Raymond\'s to\r\nget it set to rights.  Oh, John! we came to grief on Bluepost Bridge\r\nafter all, and I\'m afraid Robinson has got his leg broken!\'\r\n\r\nSir John was a good-natured heavy man, whose clever wife thought for\r\nhim in all that did not regard horses, dogs, and game.  He looked\r\nperfectly astounded, and required to have all told him over again\r\nbefore he could fully take it in.  Then he uttered a suppressed\r\nmalediction on engines, insisted that all his impromptu guests should\r\nimmediately eat, drink, and sleep, and declared his intention of going\r\noff at once to Redcastle to see about his wife.\r\n\r\nThe two gentlemen were committed to the charge of the butler, and\r\nAnnaple took Nuttie and May to her sister\'s dressing-room, where she\r\nknew she should find fire and tea, and though they protested that it\r\nwas not worth while, she made them undress and lie down in a room\r\nprepared for them in the meantime.  It was a state chamber, with a big\r\nbed, far away from the entrance, shuttered and curtained up, and with\r\ndouble doors, excluding all noise.  The two cousins lay down, Nuttie\r\ndead asleep almost before her head touched the pillow, while May was\r\naching all over, declaring herself far too much tired and excited to\r\nsleep; and, besides that it was not worth while, for she should be\r\ncalled for in a very short time.  And she remained conscious of a great\r\ndread of being roused, so that when she heard her cousin moving about\r\nthe room, she insisted that they had scarcely lain down, whereupon\r\nNuttie laughed, declared that she had heard a great clock strike twelve\r\nbefore she moved, and showed daylight coming in through the shutters.\r\n\r\n\'We can\'t lie here any longer, I suppose,\' said May, sitting up\r\nwearily; \'and yet what can we put on?  It makes one shiver to think of\r\ngoing down to luncheon in a ball dress!\'\r\n\r\n\'Besides, mine is all torn to pieces to make bandages,\' said Nuttie. \'I\r\nmust put on the underskirt and my cloak again.\'\r\n\r\n\'Or Annaple might lend us something.  I must get out somehow to know\r\nhow poor Lady Delmar is, and what has become of everybody.  Ring,\r\nUrsula, please, and lie down till somebody comes.\'\r\n\r\nThe bell was answered by a maid, who told them that my lady had been\r\nbrought home by Mr. and Mrs. Egremont about an hour after their\r\narrival.  She was as well as could be expected, and there was no cause\r\nfor anxiety.  Mr. and Mrs. Egremont had then gone on to Bridgefield,\r\nleaving word that Mrs. William Egremont and Miss Blanche were sleeping\r\nat Redcastle, having sent home for their own dresses and the young\r\nladies\', and would call for the rest of their party on the way.\r\nIndeed, a box for the Miss Egremonts had been deposited by the Canon\r\nfrom the pony-carriage an hour ago, and was already in the\r\ndressing-room; but Miss Ruthven would not have them disturbed.  Miss\r\nRuthven,--oh yes, she was up, she had not been in bed at all.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\n\r\nA CASTLE OF UMBRELLAS.\r\n\r\n\r\nNo, Annaple Ruthven could not have slept, even if she had had time. Her\r\nfirst care had been to receive her sister, who had been met at the\r\nentrance of Redcastle by her husband.  There had been profuse offers of\r\nhospitality to Mr. and Mrs. Egremont, the latter of whom looked tired\r\nout, and offers of sending messengers to Bridgefield; but Mr. Egremont\r\nwould not hear of them, and every one suspected that he would not incur\r\nthe chance of rising without Gregorio and all his appliances.\r\n\r\nBy the time they were disposed of, and Lady Delmar safe in bed, it was\r\ntime to repair to her mother\'s room, so as to prevent her from being\r\nalarmed.  Lady Ronnisglen was English born.  She was not by any means\r\nthe typical dowager.  Her invalid condition was chiefly owing to an\r\naccident, which had rendered her almost incapable of walking, and she\r\nwas also extremely susceptible of cold, and therefore hardly ever went\r\nout; but there was so much youth and life about her at sixty-three that\r\nshe and Annaple often seemed like companion sisters, and her shrewd,\r\nkeen, managing eldest-born like their mother.\r\n\r\nAnnaple lay down beside her on her bed in the morning twilight, and\r\ngave her the history of the accident in playful terms indeed. Annaple\r\ncould never help that, but there was something in her voice that made\r\nLady Ronnisglen say, when satisfied about Janet\'s hurt, \'You\'ve more to\r\nsay, Nannie dear.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, minnie mine, I walked home with Mark Egremont.\'\r\n\r\n\'And--?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, minnie.  He is going to work and make a home--a real, true,\r\nhomely home for you and me.\'\r\n\r\n\'My child, my child, you have not hung the old woman about the poor\r\nboy\'s neck!\'\r\n\r\n\'As if I would have had him if he did not love her, and make a mother\r\nof her!\'\r\n\r\n\'But what is he going to do, Nan?  This is a very different thing\r\nfrom--\'\r\n\r\n\'Very different from Janet\'s notions!\' and they both laughed, the\r\nmother adding to the mirth by saying--\r\n\r\n\'Poor Janet, congratulating herself that no harm had been done, and\r\nthat you had never taken to one another!\'\r\n\r\n\'Did she really now?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, only yesterday, and I bade her not crow too soon, for I\r\nthought I saw symptoms--\'\r\n\r\n\'You dear darling minnie!  Think of that!  Before we either of us knew\r\nit, and when he is worth ever so much than he was before!  Not but that\r\nI am enraged when people say he has acted nobly, just as if there had\r\nbeen anything else for him to do!\'\r\n\r\n\'I own that I am glad he has proved himself.  I was afraid he would be\r\ndragged in the way of his uncle.  Don\'t be furious, Nannie.  Not at all\r\ninto evil, but into loitering; and I should like to know what are his\r\nprospects now.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, mother, I don\'t think he has any.  But he means to have.  And\r\nnot a word is to be said to anybody except you and his father and May\r\ntill he has looked over the top of the wall, and seen his way.  We need\r\nnot bring Janet down on us till then.\'\r\n\r\n\'I must see him, my dear.  Let me see him before he goes away.  He\r\nalways has been a very dear lad, a thoroughly excellent right-minded\r\nfellow.  Only I must know what he means to do, and whether there is any\r\nreasonable chance of employment or fixed purpose.\'\r\n\r\nLady Ronnisglen\'s maid here arrived with her matutinal cup of tea; and\r\nAnnaple, beginning to perceive that she was very stiff, went off in\r\nhopes that her morning toilette would deceive her hardworked little\r\nframe into believing it had had a proper night\'s rest.\r\n\r\nShe was quite ready to appear at the breakfast table, though her eldest\r\nniece, a long-haired, long-limbed girl, considerably the bigger of the\r\ntwo, was only too happy to preside over the cups.  All the four young\r\npeople were in the greatest state of excitement, welcoming, as the\r\nheroes of the night, Mark and Mr. Godfrey, and clamouring to be allowed\r\nto walk down after breakfast with their father and the gentlemen to see\r\nthe scene of the catastrophe and the remains of the carriage and the\r\nbridge.\r\n\r\nSir John made a courteous reference to the governess, but there was a\r\ngeneral sense that the cat was away, and presently there was a rush\r\nupstairs to prepare for the walk.  Annaple had time in the course of\r\nall the bustle, while the colour came back to her cheeks for a moment,\r\nto tell Mark that her mother had been all that was good, and wanted to\r\nsee him.\r\n\r\nHe must manage to stay till after eleven o\'clock; she could not be\r\nready before.  Then he might come to her sitting-room, which, as well\r\nas her bedroom, was on the ground floor.\r\n\r\nMark had to work off his anxiety by an inspection of the scene of the\r\ndisaster and a circumstantial explanation of the details to the young\r\nDelmars, who crowded round him and Mr. Godfrey, half awed, half\r\ndelighted, and indeed the youngest--a considerable tomboy--had nearly\r\ngiven the latter the opportunity of becoming a double hero by tumbling\r\nthrough the broken rail, but he caught her in time, and she only\r\nincurred from Sir John such a scolding as a great fright will produce\r\nfrom the easiest of fathers.\r\n\r\nAfterwards Mark put Gerard on the way to his brother-in-law\'s living,\r\nasking him on the road so many questions about the umbrella business\r\nthat the youth was not quite sure how to take it, and doubted whether\r\nthe young swell supposed that he could talk of nothing else; but his\r\npetulance was mitigated when he was asked, \'Supposing a person wished\r\nto enter the business, to whom should he apply?\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you know any one who wishes for anything of the kind?\' he asked.\r\n\'Are you making inquiries for any one?\' and on a hesitating\r\naffirmation, \'Because I know there is an opening for a man with capital\r\njust at present.  Dutton won\'t advertise--\'tis so risky; and he wants\r\nsome knowledge of a person\'s antecedents, and whether he is likely to\r\ngo into it in a liberal, gentlemanly spirit, with good principles, you\r\nsee, such as would not upset all we are doing for the hands.\'\r\n\r\n\'What amount of capital do you mean?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, from five hundred to a thousand!  Or more would not come amiss. If\r\nI only had it!  What it would be to conduct an affair like that on true\r\nprinciples!  But luck is against me every way.\'\r\n\r\nMark was at the sitting-room door as the four quarters began to strike\r\nin preparation for eleven, but Lady Ronnisglen had been in her chair\r\nfor nearly half-an-hour, having been rapid and nervous enough to hurry\r\neven the imperturbable maid, whom Annaple thought incapable of being\r\nhastened.  She was a little slight woman, with delicate features and\r\npale complexion, such as time deals with gently, and her once yellow\r\nhair now softened with silver was turned back in bands beneath the\r\nsimple net cap that suited her so well.  There was a soft yet sparkling\r\nlook about her as she held out her hands and exclaimed, \'Ah, Master\r\nMark, what mischief have you been doing?\'\r\n\r\nMark came and knelt on one knee beside her and said: \'Will you let me\r\nwork for you both, Lady Ronnisglen?  I will do my best to find some.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! that is the point, my dear boy.  I should have asked and wished\r\nfor definite work, if you had come to me before that discovery of\r\nyours; and now it is a mere matter of necessity.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Mark; then, with some hesitation, he added: \'Lady\r\nRonnisglen, do you care whether I take to what people call a\r\ngentleman\'s profession?  I could, of course, go on till I am called to\r\nthe bar, and then wait for something to turn up; but that would be\r\nwaiting indeed!  Then in other directions I\'ve taken things easy, you\r\nsee, till I\'m too old for examinations.  I failed in the only one that\r\nwas still open to me.  Lord Kirkaldy tried me for foreign office work,\r\nand was appalled at my blunders.  I\'m not fit for a parson.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should have thought you were.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not I,\' said Mark.  \'I\'m not up to the mark there.  I couldn\'t say\r\nhonestly that I was called to it.  I wish I could, for it would be the\r\neasiest way out of it; but I looked at the service, and I can\'t.\r\nThere--that\'s a nice confession to come to you with!  I can\'t think how\r\nI can have been so impudent.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mark, you are a dear good lad.  I respect and honour you ever so much\r\nmore than before all this showed what stuff was in you!  But the\r\nquestion is, What\'s to be done?  My child is verily the "penniless lass\r\nwith a high pedigree," for she has not a poor thousand to call her own.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I have no right to anything in my father\'s lifetime, though I have\r\nno doubt he would give me up my share of my mother\'s portion--about\r\n\xa33000.  Now this is what has occurred to me: In the place where I found\r\nmy uncle\'s wife--Micklethwayte, close to Monks Horton--there\'s a great\r\numbrella factory, with agencies everywhere.  There are superior people\r\nbelonging to it.  I\'ve seen some of them, and I\'ve been talking to the\r\nyoung fellow who helped us last night, who is in the office.  I find\r\nthat to go into the thing with such capital as I might hope for, would\r\nbring in a much larger and speedier return than I could hope for any\r\nother way, if only my belongings would set aside their feelings.  And\r\nyou see there are the Kirkaldys close by to secure her good society.\'\r\n\r\nLady Ronnisglen put out her transparent-looking, black-mittened hand,\r\nand gave a little dainty pat to his arm.  \'I like to see a man in\r\nearnest,\' said she.  Her little Skye terrier was seized with jealousy\r\nat her gesture, and came nuzzling in between with his black nose. \'Mull\r\nobjects!\' she said, smiling; but then, with a graver look, \'And so will\r\nyour father.\'\r\n\r\n\'At first,\' said Mark; \'but I think he will give way when he has had\r\ntime to look at the matter, and sees how good you are.  That will make\r\nall the difference.\'\r\n\r\nSo Annaple, who had been banished for a little while, was allowed to\r\nreturn, and mother, daughter, and lover built themselves a little\r\ncastle of umbrellas, and bestowed a little arch commiseration on poor\r\nLady Delmar; who, it was agreed, need know nothing until something\r\ndefinite was arranged, since Annaple was clearly accountable to no one\r\nexcept her mother.  She would certainly think the latter part of her\r\ndream only too well realised, and consider that an unfair advantage had\r\nbeen taken of her seclusion in her own room.  In spite of all loyal\r\nefforts to the contrary, Mark, if he had been in a frame of mind to\r\ndraw conclusions, would have perceived that the prospect of escaping\r\nfrom the beneficent rule of Lescombe was by no means unpleasant to Lady\r\nRonnisglen.  The books that lay within her reach would hardly have\r\nfound a welcome anywhere else in the house.  Sir John was not\r\nbrilliant, and his wife had turned her native wits to the practical\r\nrather than the intellectual line, and had quite enough to think of in\r\nkeeping up the dignities of Lescombe with a large family amid\r\nagricultural difficulties.\r\n\r\nAnnaple remembered at last that she ought to go and look after her\r\nguests, assisted therein by the pleasure of giving May a hearty kiss\r\nand light squeeze, with a murmur that \'all was right.\'\r\n\r\nShe brought them downstairs just as the gong was sounding, and the rush\r\nof girls descending from the schoolroom, and Lady Ronnisglen being\r\nwheeled across the hall in her chair.  Nuttie, who had expected to see\r\na gray, passive, silent old lady like Mrs. Nugent, was quite amazed at\r\nthe bright, lively face and voice that greeted the son-in-law and\r\ngrandchildren, May and herself, congratulating these two on having been\r\nso well employed all the morning, and observing that she was afraid her\r\nNannie could not give so good an account of herself.\r\n\r\n\'Well,\' said Sir John, \'I am sure she looks as if she found plodding\r\nalong the lanes as wholesome as sleeping in her bed!  Nan Apple-cheeks,\r\neh?\'\r\n\r\nWhereupon Annaple\'s cheeks glowed all the more into resemblance of the\r\nbaby-name which she had long ceased to deserve; but May could see the\r\ndarkness under her eyes, betraying that it was only excitement that\r\ndrove away fatigue.\r\n\r\nSir John had not gone far in his circumstantial description of the\r\ninjuries to his unfortunate carriage when the Canon arrived, with his\r\nwife and Blanche.  Mark would have given worlds in his impatience to\r\nhave matters settled between the two parents then and there; but Lady\r\nRonnisglen had already warned him that this would not be possible, and\r\nassured him that it would be much wiser to prepare his father\r\nbeforehand.\r\n\r\nThen he fixed his hopes on a solitary drive with his father back in the\r\npony carriage, but he found himself told off to take that home, and had\r\nto content himself with May as a companion.  Nor was his sister\'s mode\r\nof receiving the umbrella plan reassuring.  She had smiled too often\r\nwith her stepmother over Nuttie\'s having been brought up among\r\numbrellas to be ready to accept the same lot for her brother and her\r\nfriend, and she was quite sure that her father would never consent.\r\n\'An Egremont an umbrella-maker! how horrible!  Just fancy seeing\r\nDutton, Egremont and Co. on the handle of one\'s umbrella!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, you need not patronise us,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\n\'But is it possible that Lady Ronnisglen did not object?\' said May.\r\n\r\n\'She seemed to think it preferable to driving pigs in the Texas, like\r\nher son Malcolm.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, but then that _was_ the Texas.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh May, May, I did not think you were such a goose!\'\r\n\r\n\'I should have thought the folly was in not being patient.  Stick to\r\nyour profession, and something must come in time.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ay, and how many men do you think are sticking to it in that hope? No,\r\nMay, \'tis not real patience to wear out the best years of my life and\r\nhers in idleness, waiting for something not beneath an Egremont to do!\'\r\n\r\n\'But is there nothing to do better than that?\'\r\n\r\n\'Find it for me, May.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\n\r\nINFRA DIG.\r\n\r\n  \'Till every penny which she told,\r\n  Creative Fancy turned to gold.\'--B. LLOYD.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Blueposts Bridge had produced a good deal of effect.  Ursula\r\nEgremont in special seemed to herself to have been awakened from a\r\nstrange dream, and to have resumed her real nature and affections. She\r\nfelt as if she would give all her partners at the ball for one shake of\r\nMonsieur\'s fringed paws; her heart yearned after Aunt Ursel and Miss\r\nMary; she longed after the chants of the choir; and when she thought of\r\nthe effort poor Gerard Godfrey had made to see her, she felt him a\r\nhero, and herself a recreant heroine, who had well-nigh been betrayed\r\ninto frivolity and desertion of him, and she registered secret\r\nresolutions of constancy.\r\n\r\nShe burned to pour out to her mother all the Micklethwayte tidings, and\r\nall her longings to be there; but when the Rectory party set her down\r\nat the door, the footman, with a look of grave importance, announced\r\nthat Mr. Egremont was very unwell.  \'Mr. Gregory thinks he has taken a\r\nchill from the effect of exposure, sir, and Dr. Hamilton has been sent\r\nfor.\'\r\n\r\nThe Canon and his wife both got out on this intelligence, and Mrs.\r\nEgremont was summoned to see them.  She came, looking more frightened\r\nthan they thought the occasion demanded, for she was appalled by the\r\nsevere pain in the head and eyes; but they comforted her by assuring\r\nher that her husband had suffered in the same manner in the spring, and\r\nshe saw how well he had recovered; and then telling Nuttie to bring\r\nword what the doctor\'s report was, and then spend the evening at the\r\nRectory, they departed, while poor Nuttie only had one kiss, one\r\ninquiry whether she were rested, before her mother fled back to the\r\npatient.\r\n\r\nNor did she see her again till after the doctor\'s visit, and then it\r\nwas only to desire her to tell her uncle that the attack was pronounced\r\nto be a return of the illness of last spring, and that it would be\r\nexpedient to go abroad for the winter.\r\n\r\nGo abroad!  It had always been a vision of delight to Nuttie, and she\r\ncould not be greatly concerned at the occasion of it; but she did not\r\nfind the Rectory in a condition to converse and sympathise.  Blanche\r\nwas lying down with a bad headache.  The Edwardses and a whole party of\r\nsemi-genteel parish visitors had come in to inquire about the accident,\r\nand had to be entertained with afternoon tea; and May, though helping\r\nher stepmother to do her devoir towards them, seemed more preoccupied\r\nthan ever.\r\n\r\nAs indeed she was, for she knew that Mark was putting his fate to the\r\ntouch with his father in the study.\r\n\r\nThe Canon heard the proposal with utter consternation and dismay at the\r\nperverseness of the two young people, who might have been engaged any\r\ntime these two years with the full approbation of their families, and\r\nnow chose the very moment when every one was rejoicing at their freedom.\r\n\r\n\'When a young man has got into a pickle,\' he said, \'the first thing is\r\nto want to be married!\'\r\n\r\n\'Exactly so, sir, to give him a motive for getting out of the pickle.\'\r\n\r\n\'Umbrellas!  I should like to hear what your grandfather would have\r\nsaid!\'\r\n\r\n\'These are not my grandfather\'s days, sir.\'\r\n\r\n\'No indeed!  There was nothing to do but to give a hint to old Lord de\r\nLyonnais, and he could get you put into any berth you chose. Interest\r\nwas interest in those days!  I don\'t see why Kirkaldy can\'t do the\r\nsame.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not unless I had foreign languages at my tongue\'s end.\'\r\n\r\nWhereat the Canon groaned, and Mark had to work again through all the\r\ndifficulties in the way of the more liberal professions; and the upshot\r\nwas that his father agreed to drive over to Lescombe the next day and\r\nsee Lady Ronnisglen.  He certainly had always implicitly trusted his\r\nson\'s veracity, but he evidently thought that there must have been much\r\nwarping of the imagination to make the young man believe the old\r\nScottish peeress to have consented to her daughter\'s marrying into an\r\numbrella factory.\r\n\r\nNuttie was surprised and gratified that both Mark and May put her\r\nthrough an examination on the habits of Micklethwayte and the position\r\nof Mr. Godfrey, which she thought was entirely due to the favourable\r\nimpression Gerard had produced, and she felt proportionably proud of\r\nhim when Mark pronounced him a very nice gentlemanly young fellow.  She\r\ncould not think why her uncle, with more testiness than she had ever\r\nseen in that good-natured dignitary, ordered May not to stand\r\nchattering there, but to give them some music.\r\n\r\nThe Canon drove to Lescombe the next day under pretext of inquiring\r\nafter Lady Delmar, and then almost forgot to do so, after he had\r\nascertained that she was a prisoner to her dressing-room, and that Sir\r\nJohn was out shooting.  The result of his interview filled him with\r\nastonishment.  Lady Ronnisglen having had a large proportion of sons to\r\nput out in life on very small means had learnt not to be fastidious,\r\nand held that the gentleman might ennoble the vocation instead of the\r\nvocation debasing the gentleman.  Moreover, in her secret soul she felt\r\nthat her daughter Janet\'s manoeuvres were far more truly degrading than\r\nany form of honest labour; and it was very sore to her to have no power\r\nof preventing them, ridicule, protest, or discouragement being all\r\nalike treated as the dear mother\'s old-world unpractical romance.  It\r\ngalled her likewise that she could perceive the determination that\r\nAnnaple Ruthven should be disposed of before Muriel Delmar came on the\r\nscene; and the retiring to ever so small a home of their own had been\r\ndiscussed between mother and daughter, and only put aside because of\r\nthe pain it would give their honest-hearted host and their hostess, who\r\nreally loved them.\r\n\r\nThus she did her best to persuade her old friend that there were few\r\nopenings for a man of his son\'s age, and that if the Micklethwayte\r\nbusiness were all that Mark imagined, it was not beneath the attention\r\neven of a well-born gentleman in these modern days, and would involve\r\nless delay than any other plan, except emigration, which was equally\r\ndreaded by each parent.  Delay there must be, not only in order to\r\nascertain the facts respecting the firm, but to prove whether Mark had\r\nany aptitude for the business before involving any capital in it.\r\nHowever, every other alternative would involve much longer and more\r\ndoubtful waiting.  And altogether the Canon felt that if a person of\r\nLady Ronnisglen\'s rank did not object, he had scarcely a right to do\r\nso.  However, both alike reserved consent until full inquiry should\r\nhave been made.\r\n\r\nThe Canon wrote to Lord Kirkaldy, and in the meantime wanted to gather\r\nwhat information he could from his sister-in-law; but he found her\r\nabsolutely engrossed as her husband\'s nurse, and scarcely permitted to\r\nsnatch a meal outside the darkened room.  He groaned and grumbled at\r\nhis brother\'s selfishness, and declared that her health would be\r\ndamaged, while his shrewder lady declared that nothing would be so good\r\nfor her as to let Alwyn find her indispensable to his comfort, even\r\nbeyond Gregorio.\r\n\r\nThis absorption of her mother fell hard on Ursula, especially when the\r\nfirst two days\' alarm was over, and her mother was still kept an entire\r\nprisoner, as companion rather than nurse.  As before, the rheumatic\r\nattack fastened upon the head and eyes, causing lengthened suffering,\r\nand teaching Mr. Egremont that he had never had so gentle, so skilful,\r\nso loving, or altogether so pleasant a slave as his wife, the only\r\nperson except Gregorio whom, in his irritable state, he would tolerate\r\nabout him.\r\n\r\nHis brother could not be entirely kept out, but was never made welcome,\r\nmore especially when he took upon himself to remonstrate on Alice\'s\r\nbeing deprived of air, exercise, and rest.  He got no thanks; Mr.\r\nEgremont snarled, and Alice protested that she was never tired, and\r\nneeded nothing.  The Rectory party were, excepting the schoolroom\r\ngirls, engaged to make visits from home before going into residence at\r\nRedcastle, and were to begin with Monks Horton.  They offered to escort\r\nUrsula to see her great aunt at Micklethwayte--Oh joy of joys!--but\r\nwhen the Canon made the proposition in his brother\'s room, Mr. Egremont\r\ncut it short with \'I\'m not going to have her running after those\r\numbrella-mongers.\'\r\n\r\nThe Canon\'s heart sank within him at the tone, and he was really very\r\nsorry for his niece, who was likely to have a fortnight or three weeks\r\nof comparative solitude before her father was ready to set out on the\r\njourney.\r\n\r\n\'Can\'t she help you, in reading to her father--or anything?\' he asked\r\nAlice, who had come out with him into the anteroom to express her warm\r\nthanks for the kind proposal.\r\n\r\nShe shook her head.  \'He would not like it, nor I, for her.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should think not!\' exclaimed the Canon, as his eye fell on the title\r\nof a yellow French book on the table.  \'I have heard of this! Does he\r\nmake you read such as this to him, Alice?\'\r\n\r\n\'Nothing else seems to amuse him,\' she said.  \'Do you, think I ought\r\nnot?  I don\'t understand much of that kind of modern French, but Nuttie\r\nknows it better.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not _that kind_, I hope,\' said the Canon hastily.  No, no, my dear,\'\r\nas he saw her colour mantling, \'small blame to you.  You have only to\r\ndo the best you can with him, poor fellow!  Then we\'ll take anything\r\nfor you.  We\'ve said nothing to Nuttie, Jane said I had better ask you\r\nfirst.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, that was kind!  I am glad she is spared the disappointment.\'\r\n\r\nNot that she was.  For when she learnt her cousins\' destination, she\r\nentreated to go with them, and had to be told that the proposal had\r\nbeen made and refused.\r\n\r\nThere is no denying that she behaved very ill.  It was the first real\r\nsharp collision of wills.  She had differed from, and disapproved of,\r\nher father all along, but what had been required of her had generally\r\nbeen pleasant to one side at least of her nature; but here she was\r\ncondemned to the dulness of the lonely outsider to a sick room, when\r\nher whole soul was leaping back to the delights of her dear old home at\r\nMicklethwayte.\r\n\r\nShe made her mother\'s brief meal with her such a misery of protests and\r\ninsistences on pleadings with her father that poor Alice was fain to\r\nrejoice when the servants\' presence silenced her, and fairly fled from\r\nher when the last dish was carried out.\r\n\r\nWhen they met again Nuttie demanded, \'Have you spoken to my father?\'\r\n\r\n\'I told you, my dear, it would be of no use?\'\r\n\r\n\'You promised.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, Nuttie, I did not.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sure I understood you to say you would if you could.\'\r\n\r\n\'It was your hopes, my dear child.  He is quite determined.\'\r\n\r\n\'And you leave him so.  Mother, I can\'t understand your submitting to\r\nshow such cruel ingratitude!\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was very angry, though she was shocked at the burning colour and\r\nhot tears that she beheld as, half choked, her mother said: \'Oh, my\r\ndear, my dear, do not speak so!  You know--you know it is not in my\r\nheart, but my first duty, and yours too, is to your father.\'\r\n\r\n\'Whatever he tells us?\' demanded Nuttie, still hot and angry.\r\n\r\n\'I did not say that,\' returned her mother gently, \'but you know,\r\nNuttie, Aunt Ursel herself would say that it is our duty to abide by\r\nhis decision here.\'\r\n\r\n\'But you could speak to him,\' still argued Nuttie, \'what\'s the use of\r\nhis being so fond of you if he won\'t do anything you want?\'\r\n\r\n\'Hush! hush, Nuttie! you know that is not a right way of speaking.  I\r\ncannot worry him now he is ill.  You don\'t know what that dreadful pain\r\nis!\'\r\n\r\nHappily Nuttie did refrain from saying, \'No doubt it makes him very\r\ncross;\' but she muttered, \'And so we are to be cut off for ever from\r\nAunt Ursel, and Miss Mary, and--and--every thing good--and nice--and\r\ncatholic?\'\r\n\r\n\'I hope not, indeed, I hope not.  Only he wants us to get the good\r\nsociety manners and tone--like your cousins, you know.  You are young\r\nenough for it, and a real Egremont, you know Nuttie, and when you have\r\nlearnt it, he will trust you there,\' said the mother, making a very\r\nmild version of his speech about the umbrella-mongers.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, he wants to make me worldly, so that I should not care, but that\r\nhe never shall do, whatever you may let him do to you.\'\r\n\r\nHis bell rang sharply, and away hurried Alice, leaving her daughter\r\nwith a miserably sore and impatient heart, and the consciousness of\r\nhaving harshly wounded the mother whom she had meant to protect.  And\r\nthere was no hugging and kissing to make up for it possible.  They\r\nwould not meet till dinner-time, and Nuttie\'s mood of stormy repentance\r\nhad cooled before that time into longing to be more tender than usual\r\ntowards her mother, but how was that possible during the awful\r\nhousehold ceremony of many courses, with three solemn men-servants\r\nministering to them?\r\n\r\nAnd poor Alice jumped up at the end, and ran away as if afraid of fresh\r\nobjurgations, so that all Nuttie could do was to rush headlong after\r\nher, catch her on the landing, kiss her face all over, and exclaim,\r\n\'Oh, mother, mother, I was dreadfully cross!\'\r\n\r\n\'There, there!  I knew you would be sorry, dear, dear child, I know it\r\nis very hard, but let me go.  He wants me!\'\r\n\r\nAnd a very forlorn and deplorable person was left behind, feeling as if\r\nher father, after carrying her away from everything else that she\r\nloved, had ended by robbing her of her mother.\r\n\r\nShe stood on the handsome staircase, and contrasted it with the little\r\ncosy entrance at her aunt\'s.  She felt how she hated all these fine\r\nsurroundings, and how very good and unworldly she was for so doing.\r\nOnly, was it good to have been so violent towards her mother?\r\n\r\nThe Rectory folks were dining out, so she could only have recourse to\r\nMudie\'s box to try to drive dull care away.\r\n\r\nA few days more and they were gone.  Though Mr. Egremont was gradually\r\nmending, he still required his wife to be in constant attendance.  In\r\npoint of fact Alice could not, and in her loyalty would not, tell her\r\ndignified brother-in-law, far less her daughter, of the hint that the\r\ndoctor had given her, namely, that her husband was lapsing into the\r\nconstant use of opiates, founded at first on the needs of his malady,\r\nbut growing into a perilous habit, which accounted for his shutting\r\nhimself up all the forenoon.\r\n\r\nWhile under medical treatment it was possible to allowance him, and\r\nkeep him under orders, but Dr. Hamilton warned her not to allow the\r\nquantity to be exceeded or the drugs to be resorted to after his\r\nrecovery, speaking seriously of the consequences of indulgence.  He\r\nspoke as a duty, but as he looked at the gentle, timid woman, he saw\r\nlittle hope of her doing any good!\r\n\r\nPoor Alice was appalled.  All she could do was to betake herself to\r\n\'the little weapon called All-Prayer,\' and therewith to use all\r\nvigilance and all her arts of coaxing and cheering away weariness and\r\nlanguor, beguiling sleeplessness, soothing pain by any other means. She\r\nhad just enough success to prevent her from utterly despairing, and to\r\nkeep her always on the strain, and at her own cost, for Mr. Egremont\r\nwas far more irritable when he was without the narcotic, and the\r\nserenity it produced was an absolute relief.  She soon found too that\r\nGregorio was a contrary power.  Once, when he had suggested the dose,\r\nand she had replied by citing the physician\'s commands, Mr. Egremont\r\nhad muttered an imprecation on doctors, and she had caught a horrible\r\ngrin of hatred on the man\'s face, which seemed to her almost\r\ndiabolical.  She had prevailed then, but the next time her absence was\r\nat all prolonged, she found that the opiate had been taken, and her\r\ndread of quitting her post increased, though she did not by any means\r\nalways succeed.  Sometimes she was good-humouredly set aside, sometimes\r\nroughly told to mind her own business; but she could not relinquish the\r\nstruggle, and whenever she did succeed in preventing the indulgence she\r\nfelt a hopefulness that--in spite of himself and Gregorio, she might\r\nyet save him.\r\n\r\nAnother hint she had from both the Canon and his wife.  When they asked\r\nwhat place was chosen, Mr. Egremont said he had made Alice write to\r\ninquire of the houses to be had at various resorts--Mentone, Nice,\r\nCannes, and the like.  She was struck by the ardour with which they\r\nboth began to praise Nice, Genoa, Sorrento, any place in preference to\r\nMentone, which her husband seemed to know and like the best.\r\n\r\nAnd when she went downstairs with them the Canon held her hand a\r\nmoment, and said, \'Anywhere but Mentone, my dear.\'\r\n\r\nShe looked bewildered for a moment, and the Canoness added, \'Look in\r\nthe guide-books.\'\r\n\r\nThen she remembered Monte Carlo, and for a moment it was to her as\r\nshocking a warning as if she had been bidden to keep her husband out of\r\nthe temptation of thieving.\r\n\r\nShe resolved, however, to do her best, feeling immediately that again\r\nit was a pull of her influence against Gregorio\'s.  Fortune favoured\r\nher so far that the villa favoured by Mr. Egremont was not to be had,\r\nonly the side of the bay he disliked, and that a pleasant villa offered\r\nat Nice.\r\n\r\nShould she close with it?  Well--was there great haste?  Gregorio knew\r\na good many people at Mentone, and could ascertain in his own way if\r\nthey could get the right side of the bay by going to the hotel and\r\nwaiting.  Alice, however, pressed the matter--represented the danger of\r\nfalling between two stools, pleaded personal preference, and whereas\r\nMr. Egremont was too lazy for resistance to any persuasion, she\r\nobtained permission to engage the Nice villa.  The next day Gregorio\r\nannounced that he had heard that the proprietor of Villa Francaleone at\r\nMentone was giving up hopes of his usual tenants, and an offer might\r\nsecure it.\r\n\r\n\'Villa Eugenie at Nice is taken,\' said Alice, and she received one of\r\nthose deadly black looks, which were always like a stab.\r\n\r\nOf all this Nuttie knew nothing.  She was a good deal thrown with the\r\nschoolroom party and with the curate\'s wife for companionship.  Now\r\nMrs. Edwards did not approve of even the canonical Egremonts, having an\r\nideal far beyond the ritual of Bridgefield; and she was delighted to\r\nfind how entirely Miss Egremont sympathised with her.\r\n\r\nNuttie described St. Ambrose\'s as a paradise of church observances and\r\nparish management, everything becoming embellished and all shortcomings\r\nforgotten in the loving mists of distance.  The harmonium was never out\r\nof tune; the choir-boys were only just naughty enough to show how\r\nwisely Mr. Spyers dealt with them; the surplices, one would think,\r\nnever needed washing; Mr. Dutton and Gerard Godfrey were paragons of\r\nlay helpers, and district visitors never were troublesome.  Mrs.\r\nEdwards listened with open ears, and together they bewailed the\r\nimpracticability of moving the Canon to raising Bridgefield to anything\r\napproaching to such a standard; while Nuttie absolutely cultivated her\r\nhome sickness.\r\n\r\nAccording to promise Blanche wrote to her from Monks Horton, and told\r\nher thus much--\'We have been all over your umbrella place.  It was very\r\ncurious.  Then we called upon Miss Headworth, who was quite well, and\r\nwas pleased to hear of you.\'\r\n\r\nBlanche was famous for never putting into a letter what her\r\ncorrespondent wanted to hear, but her stepmother wrote a much longer\r\nand more interesting letter to Mrs. Egremont.\r\n\r\n\'You will be glad to hear that we found your aunt quite well.  I\r\nsuppose it is not in the nature of things that you should not be\r\nmissed; but I should think your place as well supplied as could be\r\nhoped by that very handsome and superior Miss Nugent, with whom she\r\nlives.  I had a good deal of conversation with both; for you will be\r\nsurprised to hear that the Canon has consented to Mark\'s making the\r\nexperiment of working for a year in Greenleaf and Dutton\'s office, with\r\na view to entering the firm in future.  I was very anxious to\r\nunderstand from such true ladies what the position would be socially. I\r\nlonged to talk it over with you beforehand; but Alwyn could never spare\r\nyou, and it was not a subject to be broached without ample time for\r\ndiscussion.  We felt that though the Kirkaldys could tell us much, it\r\nwas only from the outside, whereas Miss Headworth could speak from\r\nwithin.  The decision is of course a blow to his father, and will be\r\nstill more so to the De Lyonnais family, but they have never done\r\nanything to entitle them to have a voice in the matter, and the\r\nKirkaldys agree with us that, though not a path of distinction, it is\r\none of honourable prosperity; and with this, if Mark is content, we\r\nhave no right to object, since his mind is set on present happiness\r\nrather than ambition.\'\r\n\r\nIt was a letter gratifying to Alice in its confidential tone, as well\r\nas in the evident approval of those surroundings which she loved so\r\nwell.  She read it to her husband, as she was desired to give him a\r\nmessage that the Canon had not written out of consideration for his\r\neyes.  He laughed the laugh that always jarred on her.  \'So Master Mark\r\nhas got his nose to the grindstone, has he?\' was his first exclamation,\r\nand, after some cogitation, \'The fellow wants to be married, depend on\r\nit!\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you think so?\' returned Alice wistfully.\r\n\r\n\'Think!  Why, you may see it in Jane\'s letter!  I wonder who it is! The\r\nlittle yellow Ruthven girl, most likely!  The boy is fool enough for\r\nanything!  I thought he would have mended his fortunes with Ursula, but\r\nhe\'s too proud to stomach that, I suppose!\'\r\n\r\n\'I did wish that!\' said Alice.  \'It would have set everything straight,\r\nand it would have been so nice for her.\'\r\n\r\n\'You should have cut out your daughter after your own pattern,\' he\r\nanswered; \'not let her be such a raw insignificant little spitfire.\r\n\'Tis a pity.  I don\'t want the estate to go out of the name, though I\r\nwon\'t leave it to an interfering prig like Mark unless he chooses to\r\ntake my daughter with it!\'\r\n\r\nThe latter part of this amiable speech was muttered and scarcely heard\r\nor attended to by Alice in her struggle to conceal the grief she felt\r\nat the uncompromising opinion of her child.  Nuttie might outgrow being\r\nraw, but there seemed less rather than more prospect of a better\r\nunderstanding with her father.  About a week later Mark made his\r\nappearance, timing it happily when his uncle was making his toilette,\r\nso that his aunt was taking a turn on the sunny terrace with Nuttie\r\nwhen the young man came hurrying up the garden.\r\n\r\n\'Mark!  What?  Are you come home?\'\r\n\r\n\'Not the others.  They are at Mr. Condamine\'s, I came last night--by\r\nway of Lescombe.  Edda, dear, it is all right!  Oh, I forgot you did\r\nnot know!  There was no seeing you before we went away.  Ah! by the by,\r\nhow is my uncle?\'\r\n\r\n\'Much better, except that using his eyes brings on the pain.  \'What is\r\nit, Mark?  Ah! I can guess,\' she said, aided no doubt by that\r\nconjecture of her husband\'s.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, yes, yes!\' he answered, with a rapidity quite unlike himself.\r\n\'Why, Nuttie, how mystified you look!\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sure I don\'t wonder at any one being glad to live at dear old\r\nMicklethwayte,\' said Nuttie slowly.  \'But, somehow, I didn\'t think it\r\nof you, Mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, that\'s not all!\' said her mother.\r\n\r\n\'Oh!\' cried Nuttie, with a prolonged intonation.  \'Is it?--Oh, Mark!\r\ndid you _do it_ that night when you led the horse home?\'\r\n\r\n\'Even so, Nuttie!  And, Aunt Alice, Lady Ronnisglen is the best and\r\nbravest of old ladies, and the wisest.  Nobody objects but Lady Delmar,\r\nand she declares she shall not consider it an engagement till\r\nRonnisglen has been written to in Nepaul, as if he had anything to do\r\nwith it; but that matters the less, since they all insist on our\r\nwaiting till I\'ve had a year\'s trial at the office!  I suppose they\r\ncould not be expected to do otherwise, but it is a pity, for I\'m afraid\r\nLady Delmar will lead Annaple and her mother a life of it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Dear Mark, I am delighted that it is all going so well.\'\r\n\r\n\'I knew you would be!  I told them I must tell _you_, though it is not\r\nto go any farther.\'\r\n\r\nSo that hope of Mark\'s restoration to the inheritance faded from Alice,\r\nand yet she could not be concerned for him.  She had never seen him in\r\nsuch good spirits, for the sense of failure and disappointment had\r\nalways been upon him; and the definite prospect of occupation, gilded\r\nby his hopes of Annaple, seemed to make a new man of him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\n\r\nAN OLD FRIEND.\r\n\r\n  \'My heart untravelled still returns to thee.\'--GOLDSMITH.\r\n\r\n\r\nTo go abroad!  Such had been the fairy castle of Nuttie\'s life.  She\r\nhad dreamed of Swiss mountains, Italian pictures, Rheinland castles, a\r\nperpetual panorama of delight, and here she was in one of the great\r\nhotels of Paris, as little likely to see the lions of that city as she\r\nhad been to see those of London.\r\n\r\nThe party were halting for two days there because the dentist, on whom\r\nMr. Egremont\'s fine show of teeth depended, practised there; but Nuttie\r\nspent great part of the day alone in the sitting-room, and her hand-bag\r\nand her mother\'s, with all their books and little comforts, had been\r\nlost in the agony of landing.  Her mother\'s attendance was required all\r\nthe morning, or what was worse, she expected that it would be, and\r\nthough Nuttie\'s persistence dragged out the staid, silent English maid,\r\nwho had never been abroad before, to walk in the Tuilleries gardens,\r\nwhich they could see from their windows, both felt half-scared the\r\nwhole time.  Nuttie was quite unused to finding her own way\r\nunprotected, and Martin was frightened, cross, and miserable about the\r\nbags, which, she averred, had been left by Gregorio\'s fault.  She so\r\nhated Gregorio that only a sort of adoration which she entertained for\r\nMrs. Egremont  would have induced her to come tete-a-tete with him, and\r\nperhaps he was visiting his disappointment about Mentone on her.  In\r\nthe afternoon nothing was achieved but a drive in the Bois de Boulogne,\r\nwhen it was at once made evident that Mr. Egremont would tolerate no\r\nquestions nor exclamations.\r\n\r\nHis mouth was in no condition for eating in public, and he therefore\r\ndecreed that his wife and daughter should dine at the table d\'hote,\r\nwhile he was served alone by Gregorio.  This was a great boon to\r\nNuttie, and to her mother it recalled bridal days long past at Dieppe;\r\nbut what was their astonishment when on entering the room they beheld\r\nthe familiar face of Mr. Dutton!  It was possible for him to place\r\nhimself between them, and there is no describing the sense of rest and\r\nprotection his presence imparted to them, more especially to Nuttie.\r\n\r\nHe had come over, as he did from time to time, on business connected\r\nwith the materials he used, and he was beguiled into telling them of\r\nhis views of Mark, whom he had put in the way of learning the\r\npreliminaries needful to an accountant.  He had a deep distrust of the\r\nbusiness capacities and perseverance of young gentlemen of family,\r\nespecially with a countess-aunt in the neighbourhood, and quoted Lord\r\nEldon\'s saying that to make a good lawyer of one, it was needful for\r\nhim to have spent both his own and his wife\'s fortune to begin with,\r\nbut he allowed that young Mr. Egremont was a very favourable specimen,\r\nand was resolutely applying himself to his work, and that he himself\r\nfelt it due to him to give all the assistance possible.\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth, he could not deny, had aged, but far less than Mrs.\r\nNugent in the past year, and it really was a great comfort to Miss Mary\r\nto have the old ladies together.  He told too how the mission, now\r\nlately over, had stirred the Micklethwayte folk into strong excitement,\r\nand how good works had been undertaken, evil habits renounced,\r\nreconciliations effected, religious services frequented. Would it last?\r\nNobody, he said, had taken it up so zealously as Gerard Godfrey, who\r\nseemed as if he would fain throw everything up, and spend his whole\r\nlife in some direct service as a home missionary or something of the\r\nkind.  \'He is a good fellow,\' said Mr. Dutton, \'and it is quite\r\ngenuine, but I made him wait at least a year, that he may be sure that\r\nthis is not only a passing impulse.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie thought that she knew what was the impulse that had actuated\r\nhim, and felt a pleasant elation and self-consciousness even while she\r\nrepressed a sigh of pity for herself and for him.  Altogether the dip\r\ninto the Micklethwayte world was delightful, but when Mr. Dutton began\r\nto ask Nuttie what she had seen, she burst out with, \'Nothing--nothing\r\nbut just a walk and a drive in the Bois de Boulogne;\' and her mother\r\nexplained that \'in Mr. Egremont\'s state of health,\' etc.\r\n\r\n\'I wonder,\' asked Mr. Dutton, \'if I might be allowed--\'\r\n\r\nNuttie\'s eyes sparkled with ecstasy.\r\n\r\nIt ended in her mother, who had been wondering how Mr. Egremont could\r\nbe amused all the long evening, arranging that Mr. Dutton should come\r\nin an hour\'s time to call on him, on the chance of being admitted, and\r\nthat then the offer might be made when she had prepared him for it,\r\nadvising Nuttie to wait in her own room.  She was beginning to learn\r\nhow to steer between her husband and her daughter, and she did not\r\nguess that her old friend was sacrificing one of the best French plays\r\nfor the chance.\r\n\r\nIt turned out well; Mr. Egremont was conscious of a want of variety. He\r\ndemanded whether it was the young fellow, and being satisfied on that\r\npart, observed in almost a good-humoured tone, \'So, we are in for\r\numbrellas, we may as well go in for the whole firm!\' caused the lights\r\nto be lowered under pretext of his eyes--to conceal the lack of\r\nteeth--did not absolutely refuse to let Nuttie take advantage of the\r\nescort, and when Mr. Dutton did come to the anteroom of the apartment,\r\nhe was received with full courtesy, though Gregorio looked unutterable\r\ncontempt.  Mr. Dutton was a man who could talk, and had seen a good\r\ndeal of the world at different times.  Mr. Egremont could appreciate\r\nintelligent conversation, so that they got on wonderfully well\r\ntogether, over subjects that would have been a mere weariness to Nuttie\r\nbut for the exceeding satisfaction of hearing a Micklethwayte voice.\r\nAt last Mr. Dutton said something about offering his escort to the\r\nladies, or to Miss Egremont, who used, he said in a paternal way, to be\r\na little playfellow of his; Mr. Egremont really smiled, and said, \'Ay,\r\nay, the child is young enough to run after sights. Well, thank you, if\r\nyou are so good as to take the trouble, they will be very grateful to\r\nyou, or if her mother cannot go with her, there\'s the maid.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie thought she had never known him so amiable, and hardly durst\r\nbelieve her good fortune would not turn the wheel before morning. And\r\nit so far did that her mother found, or thought she found, that it\r\nwould not do to be out of call, and sent the silent Martin in her\r\nstead.  But Mr. Dutton had set telegraphs to work and recovered the\r\nbags, which Gregorio had professed to give up in despair.\r\n\r\nA wonderful amount of lionising was contrived by Mr. Dutton, who had\r\nlived a few years at Paris in early youth, and had made himself\r\nacquainted alike with what was most worth seeing, and the best ways and\r\nmeans of seeing it, so that as little time as possible was wasted on\r\nthe unimportant.  It was one of the white days of Nuttie\'s life,\r\nwanting nothing but her mother\'s participation in the sight of the St.\r\nMichael of the Louvre, of the Sainte Chapelle, of the vistas in Notre\r\nDame, and of poor Marie Antoinette\'s cell,--all that they had longed to\r\nsee together.\r\n\r\nShe had meant to tell Mr. Dutton that it was all her father\'s\r\nselfishness, but somehow she could not say so, there was something\r\nabout him that hindered all unbefitting outbreaks of vexation.\r\n\r\nAnd thus, when she mentioned her disappointment at not being allowed to\r\ngo to Micklethwayte with her uncle, he answered, \'You could not of\r\ncourse be spared with your father so unwell.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, he never let me come near him!  I wasn\'t of the slightest use to\r\nhim!\'\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Egremont would have missed you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Really he never gave her time.  He perfectly devours her, body and\r\nsoul.  Oh dear, no!  \'Twas for no good I was kept there, but just pride\r\nand ingratitude, though mother tried to call it being afraid for my\r\nmanners and my style.\'\r\n\r\n\'In which, if you lapse into such talk, you fully justify the\r\nprecaution.  I was just thinking what a young lady you had grown into,\'\r\nhe answered in a tone of banter, under which, however, she felt a\r\nrebuke; and while directing her attention to the Pantheon, he took care\r\nto get within hearing again of Martin.\r\n\r\nAnd in looking at these things, he carried her so far below the\r\nsurface.  St. Michael was not so much Raffaelle\'s triumph of art as the\r\neternal victory over sin; the Sainte Chapelle, spite of all its modern\r\nunsanctified gaudiness, was redolent of St. Louis; and the cell of the\r\nslaughtered queen was as a martyr\'s shrine, trod with reverence.  There\r\nwere associations with every turn, and Nuttie might have spent years at\r\nParis with another companion without imbibing so many impressions as on\r\nthis December day, when she came home so full of happy chatter that the\r\nguests at the table d\'hote glanced with amusement at the eager girl as\r\nmuch as with admiration at the beautiful mother.  Mr. Dutton had been\r\ninvited to come and take coffee and spend the evening with them again,\r\nbut Mr. Egremont\'s affairs with the dentist had been  completed, and he\r\nhad picked up, or, more strictly speaking, Gregorio had hunted up for\r\nhim, a couple of French acquaintances, who appeared before long and\r\nengrossed him entirely.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton sat between the two ladies on a stiff dark-green sofa on the\r\nopposite side of the room, and under cover of the eager,\r\nhalf-shrieking, gesticulating talk of the Frenchmen they had a quiet\r\nlow-toned conversation, like old times, Alice said.  \'More than old\r\ntimes,\' Nuttie added, and perhaps the others both agreed with her.\r\n\r\nWhen the two Englishwomen started at some of the loud French tones,\r\nalmost imagining they were full of rage and fury, their friend smiled\r\nand said that such had been his first notion on coming abroad.\r\n\r\n\'You have been a great deal abroad?\' Mrs. Egremont asked; \'you seem\r\nquite at home in Paris.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, mamma, he showed me where the school was that he went to, and the\r\nhouse where he lived!  Up such an immense way!\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton was drawn on to tell more of his former life than ever had\r\nbeen known to them.  His father, a wine merchant, had died a bankrupt\r\nwhen he was ten years old, and a relation, engaged in the same business\r\nat Paris, had offered to give him a few years of foreign schooling, and\r\nthen make him useful in the business.\r\n\r\nHis excellent mother had come with him, and they had lived together on\r\nvery small means, high up in a many-storied lodging-house, while he\r\ndaily attended the Lycie.  His reminiscences were very happy of those\r\ndays of cheerful contrivance, of her eager desire to make the tiny\r\nappartement a home to her boy, of their pleasant Sundays and holidays,\r\nand the life that in this manner was peculiarly guarded by her\r\ninfluence, and the sense of being all she had upon earth.  He had\r\nscarcely ever spoken of her before, and he dwelt on her now with a\r\ntenderness that showed how she had been the guiding spirit of his life.\r\n\r\nAt fifteen he was taken into the office at Marseilles, and she went\r\nthither with him, but the climate did not agree with her; she drooped,\r\nand, moreover, he discovered that the business was not conducted in the\r\nhonourable manner he had supposed.  After a few months of weighing his\r\nobligations to his kinsman against these instincts, the question was\r\nsolved by his cousin\'s retiring.  He resolved to take his mother back\r\nto England at any loss, and falling in with one of the partners of the\r\numbrella firm in quest of French silk, he was engaged as foreign\r\ncorrespondent, and brought his mother to Micklethwayte, but not in time\r\nto restore her health, and he had been left alone in the world just as\r\nhe came of age, when a small legacy came to him from his cousin, too\r\nlate for her to profit by it. It had been invested in the business, and\r\nhe had thus gradually risen to his present position.  Mrs. Egremont was\r\namazed to hear that his mother had only been dead so short a time\r\nbefore she had herself come to Micklethwayte; and fairly apologised for\r\nthe surprise she could not help betraying at finding how youthful he\r\nhad then been, and Nuttie exclaimed, in her original unguarded fashion:\r\n\r\n\'Why, Mr. Dutton, I always thought you were an old bachelor!\'\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie, my dear!\' said her mother in a note of warning, but Mr. Dutton\r\nlaughed and said:\r\n\r\n\'Not so far wrong!  They tell me I never was a young man.\'\r\n\r\n\'You had always to be everything to your mother,\' said Mrs. Egremont\r\nsoftly.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' he said, \'and a very blessed thing it was for me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! you don\'t regret now all that you must have always been giving up\r\nfor her,\' returned Alice.\r\n\r\n\'No, indeed.  Only that I did not give up more.\'\r\n\r\n\'That is always the way.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is indeed.  One little knows the whips that a little self-will\r\nprepares.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie thought he said it for her admonition, and observed, \'But she\r\nwas good,\' only, however, in a mumble, that the other two thought it\r\ninexpedient to notice, though it made both hearts ache for her, even\r\nAlice\'s--with an additional pang of self-reproach that she herself was\r\nnot good enough to help her daughter better.\r\n\r\nNeither of them guessed at the effect that a glimpse of the lovely\r\nyoung seeming widow had had on the already grave self-restrained young\r\nman in the home lately made lonely, how she had been his secret object\r\nfor years, and how, when her history was revealed to him, he had still\r\nhoped on for a certainty which had come at last as so fatal a shock and\r\noverthrow to all his dreams.\r\n\r\nA life of self-restraint and self-conquest had rendered it safe for him\r\nto thoroughly enjoy the brief intercourse, which had come about by the\r\naccident of his having come to dine at the Hotel de Louvre, to meet a\r\nfriend who had failed him.\r\n\r\nThese were two completely happy hours to all the three, and when they\r\nsaid \'good-night\' there was a sense of soothing and invigoration on\r\nAlice\'s mind; and on Nuttie\'s that patience and dutifulness were the\r\nbest modes of doing justice to her Micklethwayte training, although he\r\nhad scarcely said a word of direct rebuke or counsel.\r\n\r\nWhile Mr. Dutton sped home to tell Miss Headworth that Mrs. Egremont\r\nlooked lovelier than ever, and was--yes she was--more of an angel, that\r\nher husband had been very pleasant, much better than he expected, and,\r\nindeed, might come to anything good under such influence; and as to\r\nlittle Nuttie--she was developing fast, and had a brave constant heart,\r\naltogether at Micklethwayte.  But that servant who was acting as\r\ncourier was an insolent scoundrel, who was evidently cheating them to\r\nthe last degree.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\n\r\nA FRIEND IN NEED.\r\n\r\n  \'True courage often is in frightened eyes.\'--\r\n                                 Thoughts and Verses.\r\n\r\n\r\nAll the preliminaries of the sojourn at Nice had been settled in\r\ncorrespondence, and the Egremont family had nothing to do, after\r\narriving at the station, but to drive up to Villa Eugenie, whose\r\nflower-wreathed balconies were like a vision of beauty.  Servants had\r\nbeen hired through agencies known to Mr. Egremont, and Gregorio looked\r\nvery black at his mistress keeping the reins in her hand, and tried to\r\nmake her feel herself inefficient.\r\n\r\nIt was not an eventful or very interesting part of Ursula\'s life. She\r\nwas almost wild with the novelty and beauty of the South at first, but\r\nexcept for what she could thus see, there was little variety.  The\r\nmould of the day was as much as possible after the Bridgefield fashion,\r\nexcept that there were no cousins at the Rectory, no parish interests,\r\nvery little society, as far as the ladies were concerned.  Mr. Egremont\r\nhad old acquaintance and associates with whom he spent afternoons and\r\nevenings, after his own fashion, but they were not people to whom he\r\nwished to introduce his wife and daughter.\r\n\r\nAnd the superior English habitues of Nice, the families who formed the\r\nregular society, knew Mr. Egremont\'s reputation sufficiently to feel by\r\nno means disposed to be cordial to the fair wife and grown-up daughter\r\nwhom he so unexpectedly produced on the scene.  It had been different\r\nat home, where he had county standing, and the Canon and Canoness\r\nanswered for the newcomers; but here, where all sorts of strange people\r\ncame to the surface, the respectable felt it needful to be very\r\ncautious, and though of course one or two ladies had been asked to call\r\nthrough the intervention of Lady Kirkaldy or of Mrs. William Egremont,\r\nand had been assured on their authority that it was \'all right,\' their\r\nattentions were clogged by doubt, and by reluctance to involve their\r\nmankind in intimacy with the head of the family.  Thus very little of\r\nthe proverbial gaiety of Nice offered itself to Nuttie and her mother,\r\nand, except by a clerical family who knew Mr. Spyers, they were kept at\r\na distance, which Mr. Egremont perceived and resented by permitting no\r\nadvances.  The climate suited him so well that, to his wife\'s great\r\nrelief, he seemed to have dropped his inclination for sedatives; but\r\nhis eyes would not bear much, and she felt bound to be always on the\r\nalert, able to amuse him and hinder his feeling it dull.  Gregorio\r\nhighly disapproved of the house and servants, and was always giving\r\nhints that Mentone would agree far better with his master; but every\r\nday that Mr. Egremont seemed sufficiently amused at Nice was so much\r\ngain, and she had this in her favour, that he was always indolent and\r\nhard to move. Moreover, between his master\'s levee and late dinner\r\nGregorio was hardly ever to be found.  No doubt he knew the way to\r\nMonte Carlo well enough, and perhaps preferred that the family should\r\nbe farther off, for he soon ceased to show himself discontented with\r\ntheir present abode.  Once when his absence was inconvenient, Mr.\r\nEgremont abused him roundly as a good-for-nothing gambler, but when\r\nAlice hoped that he might be called to a reckoning, the wrath had\r\nsubsided with the immediate vexation, and as usual she was told \'All\r\nthose fellows were alike.\'\r\n\r\nThe foreign servants were not to be induced to give the early-rising\r\nladies more than a roll and cup of coffee, and Nuttie felt ravenous\r\ntill she learned to lay in a stock of biscuits, and, with Martin\'s\r\nconnivance, made tea on her own account, and sustained her mother for\r\nthe morning\'s walk before the summons to Mr. Egremont.\r\n\r\nHe always wanted his wife much earlier in the day, during his hours of\r\ndeshabille, and letting her write his letters and read the papers to\r\nhim.  She was pleased with this advance, but it gave Nuttie a great\r\ndeal more solitude, which was sometimes judiciously spent, but it was\r\nvery hard not to be desultory in spite of learning lessons in French,\r\nItalian, and drawing.\r\n\r\nLater in the day came the drive or the visit to the public gardens when\r\nthe band was playing, but this became less frequent as Mr. Egremont\r\nobserved the cold civility shown to his wife, and as he likewise grew\r\nstronger and made more engagements of his own.  Then Nuttie had happy\r\nafternoons of driving, donkey-riding, or walking with her mother,\r\nsketching, botanising, admiring, and laying up stores for the long\r\ndescriptive letters that delighted the party in St. Ambrose\'s Road,\r\ndrinking in all the charm of the scenery, and entering into it\r\nintelligently.  They spent a good many evenings alone together\r\nlikewise, and it could not but give Alice a pang to see the gladness\r\nher daughter did not repress when this was the case, even though to\r\nherself it meant relaxation of the perpetual vigilance she had to exert\r\nwhen the father and daughter were together to avert collisions.  They\r\nwere certainly not coming nearer to one another, though Nuttie was\r\nbehaving very well and submissively on the whole, and seldom showing\r\nsymptoms of rebellion.  This went on through the early part of their\r\nstay, but latterly there was a growing sense upon the girl that she and\r\nher mother were avoided by some young ladies to whom they had been\r\nintroduced, and whom they saw regularly at the daily services at St.\r\nMichael\'s Church.  They were pleasant-looking girls, with whom Nuttie\r\nlonged to fraternise, and she was mortified at never being allowed to\r\nget beyond a few frigidly civil words in the street, more especially\r\nwhen she came upon sketching parties and picnics in which she was never\r\nincluded.\r\n\r\nIt was all very well for her mother to answer her murmurs and\r\nwonderings with \'You know people are very exclusive, my dear.\' Nuttie\r\nbegan to guess that her father and her name were the real reason, and\r\nher eyes were further opened later in the spring when Mr. Egremont, who\r\nhad recovered unusual health and vigour, took his ladies to Mentone to\r\nspend a day or two in the newer beauties there. Alice had her\r\nmisgivings, but the visit was avowedly to show the place to her, and\r\nshe could not reasonably object.  He was in unusual good humour, and\r\neven tolerated their ecstasies at the scenery and the flowers, dined at\r\nthe table d\'hote and found acquaintance, enjoyed himself, and in the\r\nforenoon, while Nuttie was out wondering and admiring, and going as far\r\nas she could drag Martin, he expressed to his wife that she would be\r\nastonished at the gardens and the music of Monte Carlo.\r\n\r\nThere, however, Alice made a stand.  \'Thank you, it is very kind, but\r\nif you please, I should not like to take Ursula to Monte Carlo, or to\r\ngo there myself,\' she said in an apologetic tone.\r\n\r\nHe laughed.  \'What! you are afraid of making the little one a confirmed\r\ngambler?\'\r\n\r\n\'You know I am not, but--\'\r\n\r\n\'You think the little prig will be contaminated, eh?\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I think it will be happier for her if she never sees\r\nanything--of the kind.\'\r\n\r\n\'You little foolish Edda, as if her eyes or ears need see anything but\r\nflowers and music and good company.\'\r\n\r\n\'I know that, but I had so much rather not.  It was a sweet face and\r\ncaressing voice that implored, and he still was good humoured.\r\n\r\n\'Well, well, I don\'t want to drag you, old lady, against your will,\r\nthough I fancy you would be rather surprised at the real aspect of the\r\nabode of iniquity your fancy depicts.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, thank you, thank you so much!\'\r\n\r\n\'What an absurd little woman it is!  I wonder if you would thank me as\r\nheartily supposing I cleared a round thousand and gave you--say a\r\ndiamond necklace?\'\r\n\r\n\'I am sure I should not!\'\r\n\r\n\'No, I don\'t believe you would.  That restless little conscience of\r\nyours would be up on end.  After all, I don\'t know that you are the\r\nworse for it, when it looks so prettily out of your brown eyes.  I\r\nwonder what you expect to see?  The ruined gamester shooting himself on\r\nevery path, eh?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, no; I don\'t suppose I should see anything horrid or even\r\ndisagreeable.  I know it is all very beautiful; but then every person\r\nwho goes for the innocent pleasures\' sake only helps to keep up the\r\nwhole thing--evil and all.\'\r\n\r\n\'And what would the old women of all sorts here and at Nice do without\r\nsuch a choice temple of scandal to whet their teeth upon? Well, I\r\nsuppose you and your precious daughter can take care of yourselves.\r\nThere are the gardens, or you can tell Gregorio to order you a\r\ncarriage.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then you are going?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I promised Grafton.  Don\'t be afraid, Mistress Edda, I\'m not\r\ngoing to stake Bridgefield and reduce you to beggary.  I\'m an old hand,\r\nand was a cool one in my worst days, and whatever I get I\'ll hand over\r\nto appease you.\'\r\n\r\nThat was all she could obtain, and she secretly hoped there would be no\r\nwinnings to perplex her.  Thankful that she had not made him angry by\r\nthe resistance for which she had prepared herself with secret prayer\r\never since the Mentone scheme had been proposed, she placed herself at\r\nNuttie\'s disposition for the rest of the day.\r\n\r\nThey had a charming donkey-ride, and, still unsatiated with beauty,\r\nUrsula made her mother come out again to wonder at the trees in the\r\npublic gardens.  Rather tired, they were sitting on a shaded bench,\r\nwhen a voice close to them exclaimed, \'It is; yes, it must be; \'tis the\r\nvoice--yes, and the face prettier than ever.  Little Alice--ah! you\r\ndon\'t know me.  Time has been kinder to you than to me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!  I know you now!  I beg your pardon,\' cried Alice, recognising in\r\nthe thin nutcracker parchment visage and shabbily-dressed figure the\r\nremnant of the brilliant aquiline countenance and gay attire of\r\neighteen years ago.  \'Mrs. Houghton!  I am so glad to have met you, you\r\nwere so kind to me.  And here she is.\'\r\n\r\n\'What! is this the child?  Bless me, what a proof how time goes! Young\r\nlady, you\'ll excuse my not knowing you.  You were a very inconvenient\r\npersonage not quite born when I last met your mother. What a likeness!\r\nI could have known her for Alwyn Egremont\'s daughter anywhere!\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, they all say she is a thorough Egremont.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then it is all right.  I saw Alwyn Egremont, Esquire, and family among\r\nthe arrivals at Nice, but I hardly durst expect that it was you.  It\r\nseemed too good to be true, though I took care the knot should be tied\r\nfaster than my gentleman suspected.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, please!\' cried Alice deprecatingly, at first not apprehending the\r\nforce of the words, having never known the gulf from which Mrs.\r\nHoughton had saved her, and that lady, seeing that the girl was\r\nlistening with all her ears, thought of little pitchers and restrained\r\nher reminiscences, asking with real warm interest, \'And how was it?\r\nHow did you meet him again?\'\r\n\r\n\'He came and found me out,\' said Alice, with satisfaction in her voice.\r\n\r\n\'Indeed!  Not at Dieppe; for he was en garcon when I nearly came across\r\nhim ten years ago at Florence.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh no!  He inquired at Dieppe, but they had lost the address my aunt\r\nleft.\'\r\n\r\n\'Indeed!  I should not have thought it of old Madame Leroux, she seemed\r\nso thoroughly interested in la pauvre petite.  What did you do?  Your\r\naunt wrote to me when your troubles were safely over, and she thought\r\nhim lost in the poor Ninon, that she meant to settle in a place with an\r\nawfully long Yorkshire name.\'\r\n\r\n\'Micklethwayte; yes, we lived there, and got on very well.  We had\r\nboarders, and I had some dear little pupils; but last year Mark\r\nEgremont--you remember dear little Mark--was in the neighbourhood, and\r\nhearing my name, he told his uncle, who had been seeking us ever since.\r\nAnd he came, Mr. Egremont, and took us home, and oh, the family have\r\nbeen so kind!\'\r\n\r\n\'What?  The parson, and that awful old she-lion of a grandmother, whose\r\nvery name scared you out of your wits?\'\r\n\r\n\'She is dead, and so is dear good Lady Adelaide.  Canon Egremont is\r\nkindness itself.  It was all the old lady\'s doing, and he knew nothing\r\nabout it.  He was gone to Madeira with Lady Adelaide and got none of\r\nour letters, and he never knew that his brother was married to me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Trust Alwyn for that,\' Mrs. Houghton muttered.  \'Well, all\'s well that\r\nends well, and I hope he feels due gratitude to me for doing him a good\r\nturn against his will.  I tried to get at him at Florence to find out\r\nwhat he had done with you, but unluckily I was ill, and had to send\r\nthrough poor Houghton, and he mismanaged it of course, though I\r\nactually wrote down that barbarous address, Mickle something, on a\r\ncard.  I believe he only got as far as the man instead of the master.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!  I wanted to ask for Captain Houghton,\' said Alice, glad to lead\r\nthe conversation away from revelations of which she had an instinctive\r\ndread.\r\n\r\n\'Gone, my dear! two years ago.  Poor fellow! it was low fever, but\r\nquite as much want of luck, I shall always believe,\' she said.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I am sorry!  He was so kind to me!\' said Alice, squeezing her\r\nhand, and looking up with sweet tender commiseration.\r\n\r\n\'There, there, don\'t, you pretty creature!\' said Mrs. Houghton, putting\r\nher hand across her eyes.  \'I declare, you\'ve almost made me cry--which\r\nI\'ve not done--well, hardly, since I parted with you at Dieppe,\r\nthinking you a sweet little flower plucked and thrown away to die,\r\nthough I had done my best to bind it to him.  What care I took not to\r\nlet Houghton disabuse him about Jersey marriages!\'\r\n\r\nThere is a difference between hearing and hearkening, and Alice\r\nEgremont\'s loving and unsuspecting heart was so entirely closed against\r\nevil thoughts of her husband, and so fully occupied with her old\r\nfriend\'s condition, that she never took in the signification of all\r\nthis, while Nuttie, being essentially of a far more shrewd and less\r\nconfiding nature, and already imbued with extreme distrust of her\r\nfather, was taking in all these revelations with an open-eyed, silent\r\nhorror of conviction that her old impressions of the likeness to\r\nMarmion or Theseus had been perfectly correct.  It was all under her\r\nhat, however, and the elder ladies never thought of her, Alice bringing\r\nback the conversation to Mrs. Houghton herself.  \'Oh, my dear, I drag\r\non as I can.  I\'ve got a fragment of our old income, and when that\'s\r\nrun too low, I go up to Monte Carlo--I always had the lucky hand, you\r\nknow, and \'tis only restitution after all!  I\'m sick of it all though,\r\nand sometimes think I\'ll take my good sister Anne\'s offers and go home.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh do, do!\' cried Alice.\r\n\r\n\'But,\' went on the poor woman, \'humble pie goes against me, and think\r\nwhat an amount would be before me--heigh ho!--after nearly\r\nfive-and-twenty years; yes, five-and-twenty years it is--since\r\nHoughton, poor fellow, told me I was too bright and winsome for a\r\nlittle country lawyer\'s house in a poky street.  What would they think\r\nof me now?\' and she laughed with a sound that was painful to hear.\r\n\'Well, Sycorax had done one good deed, and when I look at you, queening\r\nit there, I feel that so have I.\'\r\n\r\n\'You were very good to me, I know; but oh, if you would go home to your\r\nsister!\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, you little know what you ask!  Anne!  Why, she is the prime\r\ndistrict lady, or whatever you call it, of Dockforth.  Think what it\r\nwould be to her to have this battered old vaurien thrown on her hands,\r\nto be the stock subject for all the righteous tongues. Besides,\' as she\r\ncoughed, \'the English climate would make an end of me outright.  I\'m in\r\na bad way enough here, where I can sit among the lemon trees half the\r\ndays in the winter, but the English fireside in a stuffy parlour--\' and\r\nshe shuddered.\r\n\r\nThat shiver reminded all that it was getting late, too late for Mrs.\r\nHoughton to be out of doors, and near the time when Mr. Egremont was to\r\nmeet his ladies at the hotel.  Alice begged for Mrs. Houghton\'s\r\naddress, and it was given with a short ironical laugh at her promise to\r\ncall again if possible.  \'Ay, if possible,\' the poor woman repeated.\r\n\'I understand!  No, no,\' as Alice was about to kiss her. \'I won\'t have\r\nit done.\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s no one in sight.\'\r\n\r\n\'As if that made a difference!  Alice, child, you are as innocent as\r\nthe little dove that flew aboard the Ninon.  How have you done it? Get\r\nalong with you!  No kisses to such as me!  I don\'t know whether it\r\nbreaks my heart, or binds it up to look at the face of you. Anyway, I\r\ncan\'t bear it.\'\r\n\r\nShe hurried away, and made some steps from them.  A terrible paroxysm\r\nof coughing came on, and Mrs. Egremont hurried towards her, but she\r\nwaved back all help, shook her head, and insisted on going home. Alice\r\nkept her in sight, till she dived into a small side street.\r\n\r\n\'Mother,\' said Nuttie.  Then there was a pause.  \'Mother, did you know\r\nall this?\'\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t talk of it, Nuttie.  It is not a thing to be talked about to any\r\none or by any one.  I wish you had not been there.\'\r\n\r\n\'But, mother, this once!  Did you know?\'\r\n\r\n\'I knew that I knew not what I did when I went on board that yacht, but\r\nthat God\'s kind providence was over me in a way that I little deserved.\r\nThat is all I care to know, and, Ursula, I will have not another word\r\nabout it.  No, I will not hear it.\'\r\n\r\n\'I was only going to ask whether you would tell my father.\'\r\n\r\n\'Certainly; but not before you.\'\r\n\r\nThe tone of decision was unwonted, and Nuttie knew she must abide by\r\nit, but the last shreds of filial respect towards Mr. Egremont were\r\ntorn away by what Mrs. Houghton had implied, and the girl dashed up and\r\ndown her bedroom muttering to herself, \'Oh, why have I such a father?\r\nAnd she, she will not see it, she is wilfully blind!  Why not break\r\nwith him and go home to dear Aunt Ursel and Gerard and Mr. Dutton at\r\nonce, instead of this horrid, horrid grandeur?  Oh, if I could fling\r\nall these fine things in his face, and have done with him for ever.\r\nSome day I will, when I am of age, and Gerard has won his way.\'\r\n\r\nMeantime Alice, in some trepidation, but with resolution at the bottom,\r\nhad told her husband of the meeting with Mrs. Houghton, of her\r\nwidowhood, sickness, and poverty.\r\n\r\nHe did not like the intelligence of their meeting, and hoped no one had\r\nseen it; then, when reassured on this score, he hummed a little and\r\nexclaimed, \'Poor old Flossy Houghton!  I don\'t wonder!  They went the\r\npace!  Well, what do you want?  Twenty pounds for her!  Why, \'twill all\r\nbe at Monte Carlo in three days\' time.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is very good of you, but I want more than that.  She is so ill and\r\nwretched, you know.\'\r\n\r\n\'I can\'t have you visiting her, if that\'s what you mean.
