Why, after\r\nall the pains I\'ve been at to get you on your proper level at home,\r\nhere\'s my Lady Louisa and all her crew, in their confounded insolence,\r\nfighting shy of you, and you can\'t give them a better colour for it\r\nthan by running after a woman like that--divorced to begin with, and\r\nknown at every gambling table in Europe.\'\r\n\r\n\'I know that, Alwyn, dear Alwyn\' (it was very seldom that she called\r\nhim so, and she put her clasped hands on his shoulder); \'but I am sure\r\nshe is dying, and she was so good to me, I can\'t bear doing nothing for\r\nher.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, there\'s twenty--fifty, if you like.\'\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, thank you, but you know I never meant to visit\r\nher--like--like society; only to go sometimes privately and--\'\r\n\r\n\'And how about your daughter?\'\r\n\r\n\'I would not take her on any account.  What I want to do is this. Mrs.\r\nHoughton spoke of her sister, a kind good woman in England, who would\r\ntake her home, and love her, if only she could bring herself to go.\r\nNow, I think I could persuade her to write, or let me write to the\r\nsister--and if only the two were together again!  It is very dreadful\r\nto think of her dying alone, in the way she is going on!\'\r\n\r\n\'What, little saint, you want to try your hand on her?  I should say\r\nshe was too tough an old sinner for you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Alwyn! her heart was very near, though she tried to keep it back.\r\nI do not want to--to do what you mean--only to get her to let her\r\nsister come.  I\'m sure that would do the rest.\'\r\n\r\n\'If any sister does more than you, you little witch,\' he said.\r\n\r\nAlice pressed him no more then, but a day or two later, when she knew\r\nhe had an engagement, she arranged to dispose of Nuttie with the\r\nclergyman\'s wife, and then begged permission to go by train to Mentone,\r\nand come back in the evening.  He did not like it--no more did she--for\r\nshe was perfectly unaccustomed to travelling alone, but there was a\r\ndeep sense of sacred duty upon her, only strengthened by her\r\nunwillingness to realise how much she owed to Mrs. Houghton.\r\n\r\nShe telegraphed that she was coming, and found her friend more touched\r\nthan she chose to allow at the fact of her visit, declaring that she\r\nmust have wonderful power over Alwyn Egremont, if she knew how to use\r\nit; indeed, the whole tone was of what Alice felt flattery, intended to\r\nturn away anything more serious.  Poor woman, she was as careful of\r\ndoing no injury to her young friend\'s reputation as Mr. Egremont could\r\nhave desired.  Alice had come resolved that she should have one good\r\nmeal, but she would not hear of eating anywhere in public where either\r\ncould be recognised, and the food was brought to a private room in the\r\nhotel.  To her lodgings she still would not take Alice, nor would she\r\ngive her sister\'s address.  Except for a genuine shower of tears when\r\nAlice insisted on kissing her there seemed no ground gained.\r\n\r\nBut Alice went again on her husband\'s next visit to Mentone.  He was,\r\nto a certain degree, interested in her endeavours, and really wished\r\nthe poor woman to be under the charge of her relations, instead of\r\ndying a miserable lonely death among strangers.\r\n\r\nThis time Alice had to seek her friend in the dreary quatrieme of the\r\ntall house with the dirty stone stairs. It was a doleful empty room,\r\nwhere, with a mannish-looking dressing-gown and a torn lace scarf tied\r\nhood-fashion over her scanty hair, Mrs. Houghton sat over a pan of\r\ncharcoal oppressive to Alice\'s English lungs.\r\n\r\n\'Come again!\' she cried.  \'Well, I really shall begin to think that\r\nangels and ministers of grace exist off the stage!  You pretty thing!\r\nLet me look at you.  Where did you get that delicious little bonnet?\'\r\n\r\n\'Why, it is perfectly plain!\'\r\n\r\n\'So it is!  \'Tis only the face that is in it.  Now if some folks put\r\nthis on--sister Anne, for example, what dowdies they would be.  Poor\r\nold Anne, you must know she had a turn for finery, only she never knew\r\nhow to gratify it.  To see the contortions of her crinolines was the\r\ndelight of all the grammar school.  It was a regular comedy for them to\r\nsee her get into our pew edgeways, and once unconsciously she carried\r\noff a gentleman\'s hat on her train.\'\r\n\r\nSo she went on talking, coughing at intervals, and generally using a\r\nhalf-mocking tone, as if defying the tenderness that awoke in spite of\r\nher, but always of her original home, and especially of her sister.\r\nAlice ventured to ask whether they often heard from one another.\r\n\r\n\'Good soul, she always writes at Christmas and on my birthday.  I know\r\nas well as possible that I shall find a letter poste restante wherever\r\nshe heard of me last, and that she hasn\'t done--I\'m ashamed to say for\r\nhow long--really, I think not since I let her know that I couldn\'t\r\nstand Ivy Lodge, Dockforth, at any price, when she wrote to Monaco on\r\nseeing poor Houghton\'s death in the paper.\'\r\n\r\nThere was a good deal of rambling talk of this kind, to which Alice\r\nlistened tenderly and compassionately, making no attempt at persuasion,\r\nonly doing what was possible for the poor lady\'s comfort. She had\r\nprocured on her way some fruit and jelly, and some good English tea, at\r\nwhich Mrs. Houghton laughed, saying, \'Time was, I called it cat-lap!\r\nSomehow it will seem the elixir of life now, redolent, even milkless,\r\nof the days when we were young.\'\r\n\r\nThen she revealed something of her long, suffering, almost ghastly\r\nnights, and Alice gently told how her old friend, Mrs. Nugent, suffered\r\nfrom sleeplessness, and kept a store of soothing psalms and hymns in\r\nher memory.  There was a little laugh.  \'That\'s for you good folk.  I\r\nhaven\'t such a thing about me!  Come, Par exemple!\' and Alice repeated\r\nthe first thing she could remember, the verse beginning \'God, who\r\nmadest earth and heaven.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s one of your charms, is it?  Well, it would not be too much for\r\nme if my poor old memory would hold it.  Say it again.\'\r\n\r\nAlice generally had about her a tiny prayer-book with \'Hymns, Ancient\r\nand Modern,\' attached.  It had been a gift from Mary Nugent, and she\r\nwas fond of it, but the opportunity was not to be lost, and she took it\r\nout, saying she would bring a larger one and reclaim it.  And, as she\r\nwas finally taking leave, she said with a throbbing heart, \'Do you know\r\nthat you have betrayed your sister\'s address?  I shall write to her\r\nnow.\'\r\n\r\n\'If you do--!\' cried Mrs. Houghton, in a tone like threatening\r\ndeprecation, but with a little of her strange banter in it besides.\r\nAlice\'s mind had been made up to do the thing, and she had not felt it\r\nhonest not to give due warning of her intentions.  Even now she was not\r\ncertain of the lady\'s surname, but she trusted to her husband\'s\r\nknowledge of Mrs. Houghton\'s previous history; and not in vain.  Mr.\r\nEgremont amused himself with a little ridicule at his wife\'s quixotry,\r\nand demanded whether Flossy Houghton was a promising convert; but\r\nconfessed himself very glad that the poor thing should be off their\r\nhands, declaring that it was quite time her own people looked after\r\nher, and happily he recollected her maiden name.  So the letter was\r\nwritten, after numerous attempts at expressing it suitably, explaining\r\nMrs. Houghton\'s illness and the yearnings she was too proud and ashamed\r\nto express to her sister, and was answered at once by a few short words\r\nof earnest gratitude, and an assurance that Miss Reade was preparing to\r\nstart at once.  Could Mrs. Egremont meet her and prepare her sister?\r\n\r\nTo Alice\'s disappointment this could not be.  Mr. Egremont had invited\r\nsome friends to the villa, and would not spare her.  She could only\r\nsend a note, assuring Miss Reade that she believed that preparation\r\nwould do more harm than good, and she waited and watched anxiously.  A\r\ncard came by the post in Mrs. Houghton\'s scrawled writing.  \'Naughty\r\nlittle wretch!\' was all it said, but thence she gathered hope.\r\n\r\nThe spring was advancing, and Mr. Egremont was in haste to be gone, but\r\nAlice obtained one more run to Mentone, and once more climbed up the\r\ndark and dirty stairs to the room, where the well-known voice answered\r\nher tap, \'Come in!  Ah, there she is, the wicked little angel!\'\r\n\r\nA substantial little roly-poly business-like little woman hurried\r\nforward with tearful eyes and outstretched hands.  \'Oh, Mrs. Egremont!\r\ncan I ever thank you enough?\'\r\n\r\n\'You can\'t, Anne, so don\'t try.  It will be a relief to all parties,\'\r\ninterposed Mrs. Houghton.  \'Sentiment is not permitted here.\'\r\n\r\nNevertheless she hugged Alice almost convulsively.  She was sitting in\r\na comfortable arm-chair, one about which Mrs. Egremont knew something,\r\nand the whole aspect of the room had changed indescribably for the\r\nbetter, as much indeed as Mrs. Houghton\'s own personal array, which had\r\nno longer the desolate neglected look of old.\r\n\r\nA little stool was close to her chair, as if the two sisters could not\r\nbear to be far apart, and the look of love and content in their eyes as\r\nthey turned to one another was perfect joy to Alice.  She had no longer\r\nany doubt that Anne Reade, who had found the wanderer yet a great way\r\noff, would yet bring her back to the home, spiritually if not outwardly.\r\n\r\nMrs. Houghton spoke, of better rooms when the winter visitors had fled,\r\nAnne spoke of her being able to return to Dockforth.  Whether that\r\nwould ever be seemed entirely doubtful to Alice\'s eyes, especially as\r\nthe patient\'s inclination was evidently otherwise. There was nothing to\r\nbe done but to leave the sisters together, obtaining Miss Reade\'s ready\r\npromise to write, and putting into her hands a sum of money which could\r\nbe sincerely called \'only a debt of gratitude from my husband and me,\'\r\nand which would smooth the way either to remaining or returning to\r\nEngland.\r\n\r\nNor was there any return.  Ere many weeks had passed Mrs. Egremont\r\nheard from Miss Reade how a fresh cold had made it impossible to move,\r\nand summer heat had brought on low fever, which had destroyed the\r\nfeeble strength, but not till \'childhood\'s star\' had again arisen, and\r\na deeply and truly repentant woman had passed away, saved, as it\r\nseemed, through that one effort on behalf of the young girl whose\r\ninnocence she had protected.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX.\r\n\r\nTHE VORTEX.\r\n\r\n  \'With one black shadow at her feet.\'--TENNYSON.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe rebuffs that society had bestowed on his wife and daughter at Nice\r\nhad rendered Mr. Egremont the more determined on producing them in\r\nLondon and establishing their position.  He secured a furnished house\r\nin Westburnia before leaving Nice, and, travelling leisurely home\r\nwithout visiting Bridgefield, he took possession the second week in May.\r\n\r\nThere had not been much correspondence with the Rectory, and on the\r\nfirst forenoon, as Mrs. Egremont and Nuttie were trying to enliven the\r\ndrawing-room with the flowers sent up to meet them, they were surprised\r\nby the entrance of Blanche, full of kisses and welcomes.\r\n\r\n\'Oh! didn\'t you know?  I\'m with the Kirkaldys just round the corner.\r\nAunt Margaret has undertaken to do the part of a noble aunt by me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then you are here for the season?  And May?\'\r\n\r\n\'May wouldn\'t come, except just for a week to see the pictures, and lay\r\nin a stock of talk.  She\'s grown more parochial than ever, and we\r\nbelieve it is all Hugh Condamine.  Oh! I forgot you were gone before we\r\ncame home last autumn.  He is mamma\'s nephew, you know, and was\r\nordained last year to the curacy of the next parish to his father\'s\r\nplace.  If the Edwardses only would take themselves off, we would have\r\nhim at home, and then we should have flowers on the altar, and all\r\nsorts of jolly things.  Papa would stand ever so much more from him\r\nthan from the old Edwardses.\'\r\n\r\n\'But is he engaged to May, then?\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, no, not exactly.  I believe he does not think it right till he\r\nhas done preparing for priest\'s orders.  He\'s ever so strict, you know,\r\nand he hasn\'t got much either; but he means it.  Lucy, his sister, you\r\nknow, told me all about it, and that altogether the elders had settled\r\nit was better for both that he should attend to his preparation, and\r\nMay should not bind herself, though they really understand one another,\r\nand so she won\'t come to London.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, that\'s very good of her!\' cried Nuttie; \'but why won\'t they let\r\nthem settle their minds and be engaged?\'\r\n\r\n\'People are always tiresome,\' said Blanche; \'and I do believe the\r\nliving is at the bottom of it, at least Lucy thought so.  I mean\r\neverybody wants to wait--all the old ones, I mean--not Hugh or May, of\r\ncourse--to know whether Mark will stick to the umbrellas, or turn back\r\nand be a clergyman, because, then, of course, he would have the living;\r\nand if he doesn\'t, they want to be certain whether Uncle Alwyn, or you,\r\nNuttie, would promise it to Hugh if he married May!\'\r\n\r\n\'Me!\' exclaimed Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'My dear, I don\'t like to hear you talk of such things,\' said Mrs.\r\nEgremont gently.\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, I know--it\'s all very dreadful.  I was only telling you what\r\nis in the old people\'s heads, and what would settle it, and make it all\r\nright with them.\'\r\n\r\n\'And how is Mark?  Is Miss Ruthven in London?\' asked Mrs. Egremont,\r\nglad to turn away the conversation from the contingencies of which\r\nBlanche spoke with the hardness of youth, as yet not realising sorrow.\r\n\r\n\'I daresay you know nearly as much of Mark as we do, now the Kirkaldys\r\nare up here.  All his letters go to Lescombe.  Oh no, Annaple is not in\r\nLondon.  The Delmars can\'t afford it, you know, though I believe my\r\nlady would have made a stretch if Annaple hadn\'t been bespoke--but now\r\nshe reserves herself for Muriel.\'\r\n\r\nAlice looked with some discomfort at the soft fair-haired creature who\r\nwas uttering all this worldly jargon in a tone that would have been\r\nflippant if it had not been so childish.  She asked if Lord. Ronnisglen\r\nhad written.\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, long ago.  Lady Delmar had tried to make him nasty about it,\r\nbut he wouldn\'t be, so that\'s all right; and Mark seems to get on very\r\nwell, though it must be horridly dull for him now the Kirkaldys are\r\naway, and he can\'t spend all his Sundays at Monks Horton.\'\r\n\r\n\'He will get more into the spirit of the place,\' said Nuttie, whereat\r\nBlanche shrugged her shoulders a little, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\'You\'ve got out of it at any rate, Nuttie!\'\r\n\r\n\'I hope not!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, then, the look of it!  I never saw any one so improved!  Isn\'t\r\nshe, Aunt Alice?  She\'s grown, I declare!  Yes\'--measuring herself\r\nagainst her cousin--\'I was a leetle bit taller when you came, and now\r\nyou\'ve got above me! and what a duck of a way of doing your hair! You\r\nmust show me!  I must tell May there\'s no fear of your being taken for\r\none another now; Aunt Margaret will be quite surprised.\'\r\n\r\nIt was true that Ursula had developed a good deal during the last year,\r\nand, under the experienced hands of Martin, had lost her schoolgirl\r\nair, and turned into a young lady capable of becoming the Paris outfit\r\nwhich her father had enjoined.  Without positive beauty, she was a\r\npleasing, intelligent, animated girl, with the reputation of being an\r\nheiress, with a romance in the background, and there was nothing to\r\nprevent her from being a success.  The family connections, with Lady\r\nKirkaldy to set the example, had determined on giving full support to\r\nMrs. Egremont, and, as of course every one liked to look at so lovely a\r\nface, the way of both was smoothed in a manner that delighted her\r\nhusband when they encountered any of those who had looked coldly on her\r\nat Nice.\r\n\r\nHe would have had her presented, but her own reluctance and the united\r\ncounsels of Lady Kirkaldy and the Canoness prevailed on him to drop the\r\nidea; and then there was a fight with Ursula, who declared that she\r\nwould not go to court if her mother did not; but she was overruled at\r\nlast by that mother\'s tears at her defiance; and let herself be\r\npresented, together with Blanche, by Lady Kirkaldy.\r\n\r\nTo Ursula it was altogether a strange time, full of the same kind of\r\nreckless swing and sense of intoxication that had possessed her at\r\nBridgefield.  Not that there was an excessive amount of actual gaiety.\r\nHot rooms and late hours were soon found not to agree with Mrs.\r\nEgremont.  She looked faded and languid after evening parties; and, as\r\nher husband really cared more to have her ready to wait upon him and\r\namuse him than for anything else, he did not insist on her going out\r\nmore than might be needful to establish her position, or when it suited\r\nhim to show her off.  The other purposes were quite as well served by\r\nletting Ursula go out with Lady Kirkaldy, who was warmly interested in\r\nmother and daughter, glad of a companion for Blanche, and still more\r\nglad of a companion for herself.  For she was not slow to discover that\r\nexhibitions, which were merely fashionable gapeseed to her niece, were\r\nto Nuttie real delights, viewed intelligently, and eliciting comments\r\nand questions that Lady Kirkaldy and even her husband enjoyed in their\r\nfresh interest, but which were unendurable weariness to Blanche, unless\r\nshe had some one to chatter with.  Lectures and lessons, which the aunt\r\nhoped to render palatable by their being shared by the two cousins,\r\nonly served to show the difference between a trained and eager, and an\r\nuntrained and idle, nature.  With the foreign society to be met at Lord\r\nKirkaldy\'s, Blanche was less at a loss than her brother, and could get\r\non by the help of nods and becks and wreathed smiles; but Nuttie, fresh\r\nfrom her winter abroad, could really talk, and was often in request as\r\na useful person to help in entertaining.  She thus saw some of the\r\nchoicest society in London, and, in addition, had as much of the\r\nyouthful gaiety as Lady Kirkaldy thought wholesome for the two girls.\r\nAlso there were those ecclesiastical delights and privileges which had\r\nbeen heard of at Micklethwayte, and were within reach, greatly enjoyed\r\nby Mrs. Egremont whenever she could share them, though her daughter\r\nchafed at her treating all except the chief service on Sunday as more\r\nindulgence than duty.\r\n\r\nNuttie was strong, with that spring of energy which unbroken health and\r\na quiet life lays up, and, in her own phrase, she went in for\r\neverything, from early services to late balls, thinking all right\r\nbecause it was seldom that her day did not begin with matins or\r\nCelebration, and because she was not taken to more than two balls a\r\nweek, and conversed at times with superior people, or looked at those\r\nwith world-famed names.  Possibly the whirl was greater than if it had\r\nbeen mere gaiety, for then the brain would not have participated in it.\r\nChurch functions, with the scurry to go at all, or to obtain a seat,\r\nfine music, grand sermons, religious meetings, entertainments for the\r\npoor, lectures, lessons, exhibitions, rides, drives, kettle-drums,\r\ngarden-parties, concerts, theatres, operas, balls, chattering,\r\nlaughing, discussing, reading up current subjects, enjoying attention,\r\nexcitement as to what should be done and how,--one thing drove out\r\nanother in perpetual succession, and the one thing she never did or\r\ncould do was to sit still and think!  Rest was simply dreamless sleep,\r\ngenerally under the spell of a strong will to wake at the appointed\r\nhour for church.  The short intervals of being alone with her mother\r\nwere spent in pouring out histories of her doings, which were received\r\nwith a sympathy that doubled their pleasure, excepting when Nuttie\r\nthought proper to grumble and scold at her mother\'s not coming to some\r\nChurch festival at an hour when she thought Mr. Egremont might want her.\r\n\r\nOf him Nuttie saw very little.  He did not want her, and cared little\r\nwhat she did, as long as she was under the wing of Lady Kirkaldy, whose\r\npatronage was a triumphant refutation of all doubts.  He went his own\r\nway, and had his own club, his own associates, and, with his wife\r\nalways at his beck and call, troubled himself very little about\r\nanything else.\r\n\r\nAlice spent a good deal of time alone, chiefly in waiting his pleasure;\r\nbut she had her own quiet occupations, her books, her needlework, her\r\nhousekeeping, and letter-writing, and was peacefully happy as long as\r\nshe did not displease Nuttie.  There were no collisions between father\r\nand daughter, and the household arrangements satisfied that fastidious\r\ntaste.  She was proud of Ursula\'s successes, but very thankful not to\r\nbe dragged out to share them, though she was much less shy, and more\r\nable on occasion to take her place.\r\n\r\nOne pain she had.  Good old Mrs. Nugent was rapidly decaying, and she\r\nshared with all her loving heart in the grief this was to Mary and to\r\nMiss Headworth, and longed to help them in their nursing.  She would\r\nnot grieve Nuttie by dwelling constantly on the bad accounts, and the\r\ngirl hardly attended to them in the tumult of occupations; and so at\r\nlast, when the final tidings came in the second week in July, they were\r\nan absolute shock to Nuttie, and affected her as the first grief\r\nsometimes does.  Mrs. Nugent was really the first person of her own\r\nintimate knowledge who had died, and in the excited state in which she\r\nwas, the idea of the contrast between her own occupations and Mary\'s\r\nwas so dreadful to her that she wept most bitterly, with the sobs of\r\nchildhood, such as she really did not know how to restrain.\r\n\r\nIt was an unfortunate day, for it was one of the few on which Mr.\r\nEgremont wanted to take out his ladies.  There was to be a great\r\ngarden-party at Richmond, given by one of his former set, who had\r\nlately whitewashed himself by marrying a very fast and fashionable\r\nlady.  Nuttie had heard strong opinions on the subject at Lord\r\nKirkaldy\'s; but her father was quite elated at being in a position to\r\ncountenance his old friends.  Alice, in the midst of her sorrow,\r\nrecollected this with consternation.\r\n\r\n\'My dear, my dear, hush!  You must stop yourself!  Remember we have to\r\ngo out.\'\r\n\r\n\'Go--out,\' cried Nuttie, her sobs arrested by very horror.  \'You\r\nwouldn\'t go--!\'\r\n\r\n\'I am afraid your father would be very much vexed--\'\r\n\r\n\'Let him!  It is a horrid wicked place to go to at all; and now--when\r\ndear, dear old Mrs. Nugent is lying there--and--\'\r\n\r\nThe crying grew violent again, and in the midst in walked Mr. Egremont\r\nwith an astonished \'What is all this?\'\r\n\r\n\'We have lost one of our dear kind old friends at Micklethwayte,\' said\r\nAlice, going towards him; \'dear old Mrs. Nugent,\' and she lifted up her\r\ntear-stained face, which he caressed a little and said, \'Poor old\r\nbody;\' but then, at a sob, \'Can\'t you stop Ursula from making such a\r\nrow and disfiguring herself?  You must pick up your looks, Edda, for I\r\nmean you to make a sensation at Jerningham\'s.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Alwyn, if you could let us stay at home!  Mrs. Nugent was so good\r\nto us, and it does seem unkind--\' The tears were in her eyes again.\r\n\r\n\'Nonsense!\' he said impatiently.  \'I promised Jerningham, and it is\r\nabsurd to have you shutting yourself up for every old woman at\r\nMicklethwayte.\'\r\n\r\nThereupon Ursula wiped away her tears, and stood up wrathful before\r\nhim.  \'I am not going,\' she said.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, indeed!\' he returned in a tone that made her still more angry.\r\n\'Hein\'! a French ejaculation which he had the habit of uttering in a\r\nmost exasperating manner.\r\n\r\n\'No,\' she said.  \'It is scarcely a place to which we even ought to be\r\nasked to go, and certainly not when--\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you hear that, Mrs. Egremont?\' he asked.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Nuttie, Nuttie, dear!\' she implored; \'don\'t.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, mother,\' said Nuttie, with flashing eyes; \'if you care so little\r\nfor your best friends as to let yourself be dragged out among all sorts\r\nof gay, wicked people when your dear friend is lying dead, I\'m sure I\r\nshan\'t go with you.\'\r\n\r\nHer father laughed a little.  \'A pretty figure you are, to make a\r\nfavour of accompanying us!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, go away, go away, Nuttie,\' entreated her mother.  \'You don\'t know\r\nwhat you are saying.\'\r\n\r\n\'I do know,\' said Nuttie, exasperated perhaps by the contrast in the\r\nmirror opposite between her own swelled, disfigured face, and the soft\r\ntender one of her mother with the liquid eyes.  \'I know how much you\r\ncare for the dear friends who took care of us when we were forsaken!\'\r\n\r\nAnd with this shaft she marched out of the room, while her father again\r\nlaughed, and said, \'Have they been training her for the tragic stage?\r\nNever mind, Edda, the little vixen will come to her senses upstairs,\r\nand be begging to go.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t think she will,\' said Alice sadly; \'she is not that sort of\r\nstuff, and she was very fond of Mrs. Nugent.  Oh, Alwyn! if you could\r\nlet us off.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not after that explosion, certainly,\' he said.  \'Besides, I promised\r\nJerningham, and such an excuse would never hold water.  She is not even\r\na relation.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, but she was very good to me.\'\r\n\r\n\'The more reason why you should not stay at home and be hipped. Never\r\nmind that silly girl.  She will be all right by and by.\'\r\n\r\nOn the contrary, she did not come down to luncheon, and when, about an\r\nhour later, Alice, after writing a few tender loving words to the\r\nmourners, went up to her daughter\'s room, it was to find a limp and\r\ndeplorable figure lying across the bed, and to be greeted with a fresh\r\noutburst of sobs and inarticulate exclamations.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Nuttie, dear, this will not do!  It is not right.  Dear good Mrs.\r\nNugent herself would tell you that this is not the way any one so good\r\nand so suffering should be grieved for.  Think--\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I know all that!\' cried Nuttie, impatiently; \'but she--she was the\r\ndearest--and nobody cares for her but me.  Not even you--\'\r\n\r\nAgain Alice tried to debate the point, and urge on her the duties of\r\nmoderation, self-control, and obedience, but the poor gentle mother was\r\nat a great disadvantage.\r\n\r\nIn the first place, she respected and almost envied her daughter\'s\r\nresistance, and really did not know whether it was timidity or\r\nprinciple that made it her instinct to act otherwise; in the next,\r\nUrsula could always talk her down; and, in the third, she was, and\r\ngreatly she reproached herself for that same, in great dread of setting\r\nherself off into tears that might become hysterical if she once gave\r\nway to them.  And what would be her husband\'s feelings if she too\r\ncollapsed and became unpresentable.\r\n\r\nSo, having once convinced herself that even if Nuttie had been a\r\nconsenting party, no amount of cold water and eau-de-cologne would\r\nbring those bloodshot eyes, swollen lids, and mottled cheeks to be fit\r\nto be seen, she fled as fast as possible from the gasps of barbed\r\nreproaches which put her own composure in peril, and dressed with the\r\nheaviest of hearts, coupled with the utmost solicitude to look her\r\nbest.  If she had not thought it absolutely wrong, she would even have\r\nfollowed Martin\'s suggestion, and put on a soupcon of rouge; but by the\r\ntime she was summoned to the carriage the feverishness of her effort at\r\nself-control had done the work, and her husband had paid her the\r\ncompliment of observing that she looked pretty enough for two.\r\n\r\nNuttie heard them drive off, with a burst of fresh misery of\r\nindignation against her mother--now as a slave and a victim--now as\r\nforgetting her old home.  It was chiefly in mutterings; she had pretty\r\nwell used up her tears, for, unconsciously perhaps, she had worked them\r\nup as a defensive weapon against being carried to the party; and now\r\nthat the danger was over, her head throbbed, her eyes burnt, and her\r\nthroat ached too much for her to wish to cry any more. She had not felt\r\nphysically like this, since the day, seven years ago, when she and\r\nMildred Sharpe had been found suspiciously toying with the key of the\r\narithmetic, and had been debarred from trying for the prize.  Then she\r\nfelt debased and guilty; now she felt, or ought to feel, like a heroine\r\nmaintaining the right.\r\n\r\nShe got up and set herself to rights as well as she could.  Martin, who\r\nhad been allowed to know that she had lost an old friend, petted and\r\npitied her, and brought her a substantial meal with her tea, after\r\nwhich she set out to evensong at the church at the end of the square,\r\nwell veiled under a shady hat, and with a conviction that something\r\nought to happen.\r\n\r\nNothing did, however, happen; she met no one whom she knew, the psalms\r\nwere not particularly appropriate, and her attention wandered away to\r\nthe scene at home.  She did not come back, as she was sure she ought to\r\nhave done, soothed, exhilarated, and refreshed, but rather in a rasped\r\nstate of mind, and a conscience making a vehement struggle to believe\r\nitself in the right--a matter in which she thoroughly succeeded.\r\n\r\nShe wrote a long letter to Mary Nugent, and shed some softer tears over\r\nit, then she built a few castles on her future escape from the power of\r\nher father; and then she picked up Reata, and became absorbed in it,\r\nregretting only the weakness of her eyes, and the darkening of the\r\nsummer evening.\r\n\r\nShe was still reading when the others came home.  Her mother kissed\r\nher, but looked so languid and tired-out that Nuttie was shocked, and\r\nMartin declared that she ought not to go down to dinner.\r\n\r\nA tete-a-tete dinner between father and daughter was too dreadful to\r\nAlice\'s imagination to be permitted, so she dressed and went down,\r\nlooking like a ghost.  Mr. Egremont scowled at Nuttie, Nuttie scowled\r\nat him, each considering it the fault of the other, and when at last it\r\nwas over, Alice gave up the struggle, and went off to bed, leaving a\r\ncontrite message that her headache would be better to-morrow.\r\n\r\n\'All your accursed folly and obstinacy,\' observed Mr. Egremont, when\r\nNuttie, with a tone of monition gave him the message.\r\n\r\n\'I should call it the consequence of being dragged out with a sore\r\nheart,\' returned Nuttie--a little speech she had prepared ever since\r\nshe had seen how knocked up her mother was.\r\n\r\n\'Then I should recommend keeping your ideas to yourself,\' he answered,\r\nlooking at her in his annihilating manner.\r\n\r\nShe was put down. She thought afterwards of a hundred things that she\r\ncould have said to him, but she was crushed for the present, and when\r\nhe went out she could only betake herself to Reata, and forget all\r\nabout it as much as she could.\r\n\r\nWhen she went upstairs, at the end of the third volume, Martin was on\r\nthe watch, and would not let her go into the room.\r\n\r\n\'I have been at hand, ma\'am, without her guessing it, and I am happy to\r\nsay her tears has had a free course when she was in bed.  Yes, ma\'am,\r\nsuppressed grief is always dangerous.\'\r\n\r\nMrs. Egremont was still prostrate with fatigue and headache the next\r\nday, and Nuttie had all the quiet luxuriating in reminiscences she\r\ndesired.  Her father was vexed and angry, and kept out of the way, but\r\nit must be confessed that Nuttie\'s spirits had so much risen by the\r\nafternoon that it was a sore concession to consistency when she found\r\nherself not expected at Blanche\'s last little afternoon dance at Lady\r\nKirkaldy\'s!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX.\r\n\r\nWOLF.\r\n\r\n  \'If I cannot once or twice in a quarter bear out a knave against\r\n  an honest man, I have but very little credit with your Worship.\'\r\n                                               --II. King Henry IV.\r\n\r\n\r\nAnother cause besides Ursula\'s recalcitrance and her mother\'s ailment\r\ncontributed to disturb Mr. Egremont, and bring him home.  His agent, by\r\nname Bulfinch, a solicitor at Redcastle, came to him with irrefragable\r\nproofs of gross peculation on the part of the bailiff who managed the\r\nhome farm which supplied the house and stables, and showed him that it\r\nwas necessary to make a thorough investigation and change of system.\r\n\r\nIn point of fact, Mr. Egremont greatly preferred being moderately\r\ncheated to exerting himself to investigate, but this was going beyond\r\nmoderation, and the explosion had been too public to be passed over. So\r\nhe came home and sat by, while his wife and Mr. Bulfinch did the work\r\nfor him, and made it evident to him that the frauds had been of long\r\nstanding, and carried on with the connivance of the coachman, of\r\nGregorio,--who had before Mrs. Egremont\'s arrival acted as house\r\nsteward,--and of the former cook.  Indeed, it was the housekeeper whom\r\nMrs. Egremont had left in charge, whose refusal to connive had brought\r\nabout the discovery.\r\n\r\nGregorio\'s share in all was sufficiently evident, and Alice\'s heart\r\nleapt with hope.  Her husband would be wholly her own if his evil\r\ngenius were once departed, but Mr. Egremont would not see it.  He had\r\nno objection to sacrifice the coachman and all his underlings, with the\r\nbailiff and his entire family, and felt none of the pity that Alice had\r\nfor the pretty, silly, half-educated daughters; but as to the\r\nvalet--Pooh! pooh! the poor fellow had been out of the way all this\r\ntime--whatever he had done had been in the dark, ages long ago, before\r\nBridgefield knew its mistress; he was a foreigner, and that was enough\r\nto prevent him from forgathering with the English.  It was all their\r\nEnglish prejudice.\r\n\r\n\'I can show you facts and figures, sir,\' said Mr. Bulfinch.\r\n\r\n\'I daresay, a year or more old.  Why, I was an unprotected carcase\r\nthen--a mere prey--the fellow only did after his kind.\'\r\n\r\nAlice held her tongue then, but made an effort in private.  \'Indeed, I\r\ndon\'t think you know; I am afraid Gregorio is not altered.  I found him\r\nout in his charges about the wine, and the servants\' wages at Nice,\r\nonly you wouldn\'t listen.\'\r\n\r\n\'His little perquisites, my dear child!  Come, nonsense, these foreign\r\nfellows don\'t pretend to have the morals you ascribe to the native\r\nflunkey--generally without foundation either--they are much of a\r\nmuchness as to that; but your Frenchman or Italian does it more neatly,\r\nand is a dozen times better servant than the other is.\'\r\n\r\n\'But--\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, ay!  I know you don\'t like him.  But he knows his manners to you,\r\nI hope?\' said Mr. Egremont, with a suddenness that made her wish she\r\ncould truthfully say he did not.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, he always is--is respectful, but somehow I see it is under\r\nprotest.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont laughed.  \'Rivals--yes, I see; why, you don\'t consider the\r\nsore trial of having a full-grown mistress turned in upon him! Look\r\nhere, you keep the keys already, but the new fellow at the farm and all\r\nthe rest of them shall account to you for everything--Gregorio and all.\r\nWon\'t that satisfy you?\'\r\n\r\n\'\'Tis not only the money, but I think Gregorio is a bad--not a\r\ngood--man.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ho, ho! she wants to advertise for a pious footman and coachman! eh?\r\nNo, I thank you, my dear Edda, I agree with--who was it who said,\r\n"Volez moi, mais sans m\'ennuyer."\'\r\n\r\nThe Rectory likewise had hoped for Gregorio\'s dismissal, and there were\r\ngrave looks when Alice had to confess that nothing would move her\r\nhusband against him.  The Canon even lashed himself up to say, \'I tell\r\nyou how it is, Alwyn, you\'ll never do any good with your household,\r\nwhile you keep that fellow.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am not aware what description of good you expect me to do with it,\r\nWill,\' coolly answered the elder brother in a disconcerting tone.\r\n\r\nPoor Alice, on her side, thought of the Little Master, and then\r\nwondered if it was uncharitable to do so.  For she knew it had become\r\nwar to the knife with Gregorio!  Whether his master told him, or\r\nwhether it were his own evil conscience, or the wonderful intuition of\r\nservants, he certainly knew of the pressure for his dismissal, and he\r\nvisited it on her as much as he durst.\r\n\r\nOutwardly deferential, he could thwart and annoy her in a hundred ways,\r\nfrom making love to the housemaids to making evil suggestions to his\r\nmaster, yet never giving her any overt cause of complaint.  He could\r\nworry and sting her under the politest exterior, and he knew very well\r\nthat the most effectual form of annoyance was the persuading his master\r\nthat any discomfort or lassitude was to be removed by some form of\r\nnarcotic.  This would have the further advantage of stupefying Mr.\r\nEgremont, and making him more ready to lapse under the old influence;\r\nwhile the duration and strength of the new one was already a surprise\r\nto Gregorio.\r\n\r\nBut there was no doubt that Mrs. Egremont had profited by her year of\r\ntraining.  She looked tired, and less youthful and pretty, but she had\r\ngained in grace and importance as well as in style, and was much more\r\nreally the mistress of Bridgefield.  Her shyness had passed away, and\r\nshe knew now to take her place in society, though still she was\r\nsomewhat silent.  And her husband depended upon her entirely for all\r\nhis correspondence, for much of his occupation and amusement, and even\r\nfor the regulation of his affairs.  In the household, Gregorio was\r\nlittle more than his personal attendant, and she had the general\r\nmanagement, even of the other men-servants.  The Canoness might well\r\nsay it had turned out better than she expected.\r\n\r\nAnd Nuttie had become more womanly, and had acquired the indefinable\r\npolish given by a London season.  She had learnt the art of\r\nconversation, and could make herself agreeable to her uncle, or to any\r\none else who came in her way.  Even May allowed that she had something\r\nin her, and cultivated her more than before; but, on the other hand,\r\neven the Rectory could perceive that there was now an absolute\r\nalienation between her and her father, and what might before have been\r\nfear had become dislike.  If she had to refer to him, especially if her\r\nplans for herself or her mother were crossed, there was always a tone\r\nof bitterness or of sarcasm about her; and her greater boldness and\r\nfreedom of speech would occasionally manifest itself towards him.  This\r\nwas not indeed often, since not only did his cool contempt make her\r\ncome off the worst in the encounters, but the extreme distress they\r\ngave to her mother made her refrain whenever her temper, or what she\r\nthought her conscience, would let her; but still there was always a\r\ndanger which kept poor Alice on thorns whenever there was a possible\r\ndifference of schemes or opinions.\r\n\r\nMrs. William Egremont was seriously considering of representing to\r\nUrsula that her conduct was bad taste, bad policy, and, moreover, was\r\ndoing her mother\'s spirits and health serious harm; but it was a\r\ndelicate matter in which to meddle, and the good lady could not make up\r\nher mind how far to surrender her brother-in-law\'s character and allow\r\na partial justification to Ursula.  She was a cautious woman, and\r\nwaited and watched her opportunities.\r\n\r\nIn the beginning of October Mr. and Mrs. Egremont were invited to a\r\ngreat shooting party at Sir James Jerningham\'s.  The invitation did not\r\ninclude Ursula.  Perhaps she had never dawned on their hostess\'s\r\nimagination; perhaps it was that Lady Jerningham was well known to\r\ndislike girls, or any one who might absorb young men\'s attention. At\r\nany rate the omission was a cause of thankfulness to the party\r\nconcerned, and she did not neglect to worry her mother by a protest\r\nagainst keeping such company as would be met at Waldicotes.\r\n\r\nAlice smiled a little faintly and said, \'I don\'t think it hurts me, my\r\ndear; I don\'t understand half of what they talk about, and they are\r\nalways kind to me.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t think you ought to go among them or countenance them.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear child,\'--and the colour rose--\'I don\'t feel as if I had a\r\nright to set myself above any one.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mother!\'\r\n\r\n\'People might have said just the same of me.\'\r\n\r\n\'And whose fault was that?\' muttered Nuttie under her breath, but Mrs.\r\nEgremont would not hear.  She only pleaded, as perhaps mother ought not\r\nto have done with child.\r\n\r\n\'You know, Nuttie, it is not for my own pleasure, but your father\'s\r\neyesight makes him dislike to go anywhere without me now; and I really\r\nshould be uneasy about him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; he is all you care for,\' said Nuttie.  \'You sacrifice everything\r\nyou used to think essential, just to his will and pleasure.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Nuttie, I hope not; I don\'t think I do!\'\r\n\r\n\'If I thought it was doing him any good I should not so much mind,\'\r\nwent on the girl; \'but he is just the same, and I am always thinking of\r\n"As the husband is, the wife is--"\'\r\n\r\n\'Hush! hush!  You have no right to think in that way of your father. I\r\nwill not hear it.  I have let you say too much already, Nuttie.\' Then\r\nafter a pause she added, gently and wistfully, \'You have been better\r\ntaught, and are clearer headed than ever I was, my Nuttie, and it is\r\nquite right that you should hate what seems evil to you.  I can only go\r\non trying to do what seems my duty from day to day.  I know,\' she added\r\nwith rising tears, \'that the sin and folly of my younger days worked a\r\ndifficult position for us both; but we can only act according to our\r\nlights, and pray God to direct us; and please--please bear with me, my\r\ndear one, if the same course does not always seem right to us both.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie had never heard her say anything so fully showing that she\r\nrealised these difficulties, and, greatly touched, she asked pardon,\r\nkissed and caressed her mother.  There was a calm over them for the\r\nnext few days, and Nuttie actually refrained from bitter comments when\r\nher mother was not allowed to go to evensong on Sunday, on the plea of\r\nher being tired, but, as the girl believed, in order that she might\r\nread the newspapers aloud.\r\n\r\nShe knew that her silence was appreciated by the way her mother kissed\r\nher and called her a dear, good, considerate girl.\r\n\r\nOn Monday Mr. and Mrs. Egremont went away at what was a strangely early\r\nhour for the former, Nuttie spending her days at the Rectory.\r\n\r\nOn the Tuesday Blanche went with her little sister and the governess on\r\na shopping expedition to Redcastle, and in relating her adventures on\r\nher return, she said, \'Oh, by the bye, I met Annaple in Park\'s shop!\'\r\n\r\n\'Full of Micklethwayte news, I suppose,\' said May.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, of course.  Did you know, Nuttie, that your aunt was ill?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, indeed, I did not.  What was the matter?\'\r\n\r\n\'Bronchitis, I believe--brown titus, as Betty Butter calls it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Bronchitis!  Oh dear! oh dear!  Are you quite sure, Blanche?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes!  I am quite certain Annaple said Mark told her that Miss\r\nHeadworth was laid up with bronchitis.\'\r\n\r\n\'And nobody has written to us all this week!\' sighed Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'I should think that a sign there could not be much in it,\' observed\r\nMay; \'it may be only a bad cold.\'\r\n\r\n\'But Aunt Ursel had bronchitis four years ago, and was very ill\r\nindeed,\' persisted Nuttie.  \'I\'m sure it is bronchitis, and that she\r\nwon\'t let Miss Mary write to us.\'\r\n\r\nShe was in much distress about it, though May privately told her that\r\nshe ought to know Blanche\'s way better than to trust implicitly to any\r\nof her reports; and her aunt said much the same thing in more general\r\nterms, even proposing that if she did not hear the next morning she\r\nshould go over to Lescombe to ascertain what Mark had really said.\r\n\r\nThis pacified her a little, but on her way home the alarm grew upon\r\nher, and, moreover, she recollected the opposition that she believed\r\nthat her father was certain to make to either her mother or herself\r\ngoing to nurse her aunt.  It flashed upon her that if she were to\r\nhasten to Micklethwayte on this alarm before there could be a\r\nprohibition, it would be no disobedience, and perfectly justifiable,\r\nnot to say noble.  Her parents were to return on Thursday evening, and\r\nshe made up her mind that, unless she were fully reassured as to Miss\r\nHeadworth\'s state, she would go off at once to Micklethwayte before any\r\none could gainsay her.  She had plenty of money, and she consulted the\r\ntime-table in the hall before going upstairs.  It only concerned the\r\nnearest line, but she calculated that if she caught the express, she\r\nshould reach her destination in time to write to her mother at\r\nWaldicotes, and prevent needless shocks.  Her eagerness for the plan\r\ngrew upon her, so that it seemed like liberation; she could hardly\r\nsleep for thinking of it, and certainly was not as much disappointed as\r\nshe believed herself when the post came in--a blank.\r\n\r\nMartin was away with her mistress, so Nuttie explained matters to the\r\nupper housemaid, who was very sympathetic, carried down her orders for\r\nthe carriage, procured for her both breakfast and provision for the\r\njourney, and packed her clothes.  Ursula would fain have been off\r\nbefore the Rectory was aware, but the two little girls came up with a\r\nmessage about the plans for the day, just as she was beginning an\r\nexplanatory note, and she entrusted to them the information that she\r\nwas so uneasy about Miss Headworth that she had decided on going to see\r\nfor herself.\r\n\r\nSo in dashed Adela and Rosalind to their mother\'s room full of\r\nexcitement with the news that Cousin Nuttie was gone off by the train,\r\nbecause her aunt was very ill indeed.\r\n\r\n\'Gone, Adela? are you sure?  Really gone?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, mamma!  The dogcart was coming round, and she said she wanted\r\nto catch the 10.05 train, and was very sorry she had not time to write\r\na note to you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Was there a letter?  What had she heard?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, only that her aunt was so very ill!  She did not tell us--did she,\r\nRosie?\'\r\n\r\n\'There was something about being in time to write to Aunt Alice,\'\r\nsuggested Adela.\r\n\r\n\'I am very sorry about this.  I am afraid it will be a great shock to\r\nAlice,\' observed the mother, as she imparted the news at her husband\'s\r\ndressing-room door.\r\n\r\n\'Young girls are so precipitate!\' said the Canon.\r\n\r\n\'Your brother won\'t like it at all,\' the lady continued.\r\n\r\n\'Not he.  But after all, it is just as well that he was not asked. They\r\ndo owe that poor old lady a good deal, and Alwyn\'s not the man to see\r\nit.  I\'m not sorry the girl took the matter into her own hands, though\r\nI couldn\'t have advised it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Except that it will all fall on Alice.\'\r\n\r\n\'He is very fond of Alice.  She has done more with him than I ever\r\nthought possible.  Kept him respectable this whole year, and really it\r\ngrows on him.  He makes ever so much more of her now than when he first\r\nbrought her home--and no wonder.  No, no; he won\'t fall foul of her.\'\r\n\r\n\'Perhaps not; but it is just as bad, or worse, for her if he falls foul\r\nof her daughter.  Besides, she is very much attached to her aunt.  I\r\nwish I knew what the account was, or whether she knows anything about\r\nit.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI.\r\n\r\nURSULA\'S RECEPTION.\r\n\r\n  \'Thy wish was father, Harry, to that thought.\'--SHAKESPEARE.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was at half-past seven o\'clock that Ursula Egremont\'s cab stopped at\r\nSt. Ambrose\'s Road.  She had missed the express train, and had to come\r\non by a stopping one.  But here at last she was, with eyes even by\r\ngaslight full of loving recognition, a hand full of her cab-fare, a\r\nheart full of throbbing hope and fear, a voice full of anxiety, as she\r\ninquired of the astonished servant, \'Louisa, Louisa, how is Aunt\r\nUrsel!\' and, without awaiting the reply, she opened the adjoining door.\r\nThere sat, with their evening meal on the table, not only Mary Nugent,\r\nbut Miss Headworth herself.\r\n\r\nNuttie rushed at her, and there was an incoherency of exclamations, the\r\nfirst thing that made itself clear to the senses of the traveller\r\nbeing, \'Ill, my dear?  No such thing!  Only I had a bad cold, and Mary\r\nhere is only too careful of me.\'\r\n\r\n\'But Mark said you had bronchitis.\'\r\n\r\n\'What could have put that into his head?  He did not write it, surely?\'\r\n\r\n\'He wrote it to Annaple Ruthven, and she told Blanche.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!\' and Mary Nugent\'s tone was rather nettling.\r\n\r\n\'And then it was such a terrible time since we had heard anything,\'\r\nadded Nuttie, on the defensive.\r\n\r\n\'Did not your mother get my letter?\' said Miss Headworth.  \'I wrote to\r\nher at--what\'s the name of that place?  I hope I addressed it right.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, but I was not there.  I didn\'t go with them.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah, yes, I remember.  Then did not she send you?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, I came off this morning.  I heard this yesterday evening, and I\r\ndetermined that nothing should stop me if there was no news by the\r\npost.\'\r\n\r\n\'Dear child!  But will your father not be displeased?\' said Miss\r\nHeadworth.\r\n\r\n\'He hasn\'t any right to object,\' cried Nuttie, with flashing eyes and a\r\nlook that made Miss Nugent anxious; but at the moment there could be\r\nlittle thought save of welcome to the warm-hearted girl.  Louisa was\r\nalready brewing fresh tea, and extemporising additions to the meal, and\r\nNuttie was explaining how she hoped to have arrived a couple of hours\r\nsooner.\r\n\r\n\'By the bye, I meant to have written to mother for her to have it\r\nto-morrow before leaving Waldicotes.  Is there time?\'\r\n\r\nNo, the pillar at hand was cleared at seven, and the regular\r\npost-office could not be reached in time; so they satisfied themselves\r\nwith the knowledge that Mrs. Egremont must have had Aunt Ursel\'s\r\ncheerful letter, and Mary recommended telegraphing to the Canon the\r\nfirst thing in the morning.  Then they gave themselves up to enjoyment.\r\n\r\n\'At any rate, I\'m here,\' said Nuttie, \'and I\'ll make the most of it.\'\r\n\r\nAnd her handsome furs were laid aside, and her boots taken off, and she\r\nresigned herself to absolute ease and luxury, while Mary poured out the\r\ntea, and her aunt heaped her plate with eggs and rashers \'such as one\r\ndoesn\'t get anywhere else,\' said Nuttie, declaring herself quite\r\nvoracious, while her aunt fondly admired her growth and improvement,\r\nand she inquired into the cold, not quite gone yet; and there were\r\nspeculations over what Mark could have got into his head. Mary\r\nremembered having met him coming to call, and having told him that she\r\nhad persuaded Miss Headworth to keep her bed because her colds were apt\r\nto be severe, and it was agreed to lay the exaggeration at the door of\r\nthe lovers and Blanche.  Miss Headworth laughed, and said she ought to\r\nbe flattered that an old woman\'s sore throat should be thought worthy\r\nof mention by a fine young gentleman like Mr. Mark.  \'A very good young\r\nman he is,\' she added.  \'You would never have thought how kind he is in\r\ncoming in here to tell me everything he hears about your dear mother,\r\nNuttie.\'\r\n\r\n\'He makes himself very useful while Mr. Dutton is away,\' added Mary,\r\n\'taking his young men\'s class and all.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! is Mr. Dutton away?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; he has had to be in London a great deal of late.  I am afraid he\r\nmay have to live there altogether.\'\r\n\r\n\'What a grievous pity!\'\r\n\r\n\'He won\'t be anywhere without doing good,\' said Miss Headworth, \'but I\r\nsometimes wish we had his cool good sense here.\'\r\n\r\n\'And how is Mr. Spyers,\' asked Nuttie.  She felt shy of asking for\r\nGerard Godfrey, or perhaps she thought she ought to be shy of his name,\r\nand kept hoping that it would come in naturally.\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Spyers is very well.  Very busy of course, and very much delighted\r\nwith your mother\'s gifts to the church.  All her own work, isn\'t it,\r\nNuttie?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; every bit.  She does lots of embroidery and work of all kinds\r\nwhen she is waiting for _him_ or sitting with him, and luckily it has\r\nnever occurred to him to ask what it is for.\'\r\n\r\nThe two ladies knew well what was meant by him, but they would not\r\npursue the subject, and proceeded to put Nuttie au courant with St.\r\nAmbrose affairs--how last year\'s mission had produced apparently an\r\nimmense effect in the town, and how the improvement had been ebbing\r\never since, but had left various individual gains, and stirred up more\r\nthan one good person who had hitherto thought it enough to save one\'s\r\nown soul and let other people alone; how Mr. Spyers was endeavouring to\r\nbind people together in a guild; how a violent gust of temperance\r\norators had come down upon the place, and altogether fascinated and\r\ncarried away Gerard Godfrey.\r\n\r\nThere was his name at last, and Nuttie was rather gratified to feel\r\nherself blushing as she asked, \'Ah! poor Gerard--how is he?\'\r\n\r\n\'As good and sincere as ever,\' said Miss Nugent, \'but not much wiser.\r\nHe is so excitable and vehement.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Miss Headworth.  \'I don\'t understand the kind of thing. In\r\nmy time a steady young clerk used to be contented after hours with\r\nplaying at cricket in the summer, or learning the flute in the\r\nwinter--and a great nuisance it was sometimes, but now Gerard must get\r\nhimself made a sort of half clergyman.\'\r\n\r\n\'A reader,\' suggested Mary.\r\n\r\n\'Minor orders.  Oh, how delightful!\' cried Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'People, don\'t half understand it,\' added Miss Headworth.  \'Mrs.\r\nJeffreys will have it that he is no better than a Jesuit, and really I\r\ndid not know what to say, for he talked, to me by the hour about his\r\nbeing an external brother to something.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not to the Jesuits, certainly,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I told her that; but she thinks all monks are Jesuits, you know,\r\nand that all brothers are monks; and he does wear his cassock--his\r\nchoir cassock, I mean--when he has his service in the iron room at the\r\nsandpits.  And now he has taken up temperance, and flies about giving\r\nthe pledge, and wanting one to wear bits of blue ribbon.  I told him I\r\nnever did take, and never had taken, more than a little hot wine and\r\nwater when I had a cold, and I couldn\'t see what good it would do to\r\nGeorge Jenkins and the poor fellows at the Spread Eagle if I took ever\r\nso many vows.\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s a regular blue-ribbon fever set in,\' said Miss Nugent. \'Gerard\r\ntold me I was supporting the cause of intemperance yesterday because I\r\nwas so wicked as to carry the rest of your bottle of port, Miss\r\nHeadworth, to poor Anne Crake.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well! he is a dear boy, and youth wouldn\'t be youth if it were not\r\nsometimes rather foolish,\' said Miss Headworth, \'and it is better it\r\nshould be for good than evil.\'\r\n\r\n\'Eager in a cause and not for selfishness,\' said Mary.  \'Poor Gerard, I\r\nwonder where he will be safely landed!\'\r\n\r\nSo did Nuttie, who had a secret flattering faith in being the cause of\r\nall the poor young fellow\'s aberrations, and was conscious of having\r\nbegun the second volume of her life\'s novel.  She went to bed in the\r\nelated frame of mind proper to a heroine.  There was a shade over all\r\nin the absence of dear old Mrs. Nugent, and in Mary\'s deep mourning,\r\nbut there is more tenderness than poignancy in sorrow for shocks of\r\ncorn gathered in full season, and all was cheerful about her.\r\n\r\nShe had quite a triumph the next day, as old friends dropped in for the\r\nchance of seeing her.  The least agreeable encounter was that with\r\nMark, who came in on his way to the office, having just received by the\r\nsecond post a letter from his father inquiring into Miss Headworth\'s\r\nstate.  He met Nuttie in the vestibule, with her hat on, and in a great\r\nhurry, as she wanted to walk with Mary to the School of Art, Gerard\r\nGodfrey accompanying them as far as the office; and she did not at all\r\nlike the being called to account, and asked what could have possessed\r\nher to take alarm.\r\n\r\n\'Why, you wrote yourself!\'\r\n\r\n\'I!\'\r\n\r\n\'To Annaple Ruthven.\'\r\n\r\n\'What am I supposed to have written?\'\r\n\r\n\'That Aunt Ursel was very ill with bronchitis.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'ll be bound that Miss Ruthven said no such thing.  You don\'t pretend\r\nthat you heard it from herself?\'\r\n\r\n\'No; but Blanche did.\'\r\n\r\n\'Blanche!  Oh, that accounts for it!  Though I should have thought you\r\nknew Blanche by this time.\'\r\n\r\n\'But what did you say?\'\r\n\r\n\'I believe I said I couldn\'t get a knitting pattern Miss Headworth was\r\nto send Lady Ronnisglen because she was in bed with a cold.  What you\r\nand Blanche could contrive to make of a simple thing like that--\'\r\n\r\n\'And Annaple!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well,\' but checking himself with a smile, \'we will not fight about\r\nthat.  I only hope it has not brought you into an awkward scrape,\r\nNuttie.\'\r\n\r\n\'I can\'t help that,\' she answered with her head rather high.\r\n\r\n\'You have written and explained?\' he said anxiously.\r\n\r\n\'To my mother, of course.\'\r\n\r\n\'If I were you,\' he said, lowering his voice, \'I should write or send a\r\nspecial message to your father.\'\r\n\r\n\'I can\'t see why.  It was a mistake.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yours was a strong measure, and he won\'t like it.  Be advised, Nuttie.\r\nRecollect your mother.  The best way would be to go home at once.  I\r\ncould get a day to take you--if you would start this afternoon.\'\r\n\r\n\'Thank you; I\'m not going back till I hear,\' she said proudly.\r\n\r\nTime being up, Mark took his leave hastily, and as he shut the door,\r\nNuttie uttered half aloud the words she had scarcely repressed, \'No, I\r\nthank you, Mr. Mark, I am not going back like a dog in a string.\'\r\n\r\n\'What, was that what he expected of you?\' said Gerard Godfrey, whom she\r\nhad not intended to hear her, but who had come out of the sitting-room\r\non the sounds of departure.\r\n\r\n\'He said he would take me home if I could go at once.\'\r\n\r\n\'Wouldn\'t he have liked it!\' exclaimed Gerard.\r\n\r\n\'It might be the best way,\' said Miss Nugent, who had followed young\r\nGodfrey.\r\n\r\n\'Now, Miss Mary,\' cried Nuttie, \'as if I could shorten my holiday now\r\nthat I have it.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I don\'t see what business he had to call you to account,\' said\r\nGerard.  \'A stuck-up fellow.\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course all the Egremonts are set against my being here,\' said\r\nNuttie.\r\n\r\n\'I thought the Canon offered to bring you last year,\' said Mary gently.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, that was only to Monks Horton!  It would have been simply\r\ntantalising.\'\r\n\r\n\'Lady Kirkaldy is an excellent person,\' said Miss Nugent.\r\n\r\n\'Is she at home now?\' asked Ursula.\r\n\r\n\'Coming next week, they tell me,\' said Gerard.\r\n\r\n\'He--your cousin--will always be loafing up there now, giving up all\r\nthat he had undertaken, I suppose.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not very likely,\' said Mary quietly.\r\n\r\nIt is a mere Scottish anti-church influence,\' said Gerard, turning\r\nround at the swing-door of his office.  \'Why else will Egremont not\r\ntake the pledge?\'\r\n\r\nWherewith he disappeared, blue ribbon and all, while Mary smiled,\r\nthough she was vexed; and Nuttie observed, \'Poor Gerard; but I can\'t\r\nsee why he should be jealous of Mark _now_.\'\r\n\r\nMary did not choose to understand what Nuttie implied in her\r\nsimplicity, and made answer, \'He is rather blue ribbon mad.  Besides, I\r\nam afraid the fact of being a "swell" does not conduce to your cousin\'s\r\npopularity among the clerks.\'\r\n\r\n\'Surely he does not give himself airs,\' said Ursula, her family\r\nfeelings awaking.\r\n\r\n\'No; but I fancy he is rather reserved.\'\r\n\r\n\'What\'s this about giving up what he has undertaken?  What is it?\'\r\n\r\n\'When Mr. Dutton went to London, he asked Mark to take his Sunday\r\nafternoons with the big lads.  He thought they wanted some one with\r\nmore resources and variety than there is in poor Gerard, who didn\'t at\r\nall like being passed over.\'\r\n\r\n\'I never should have thought it of Mark.  He never dreamt of teaching\r\nanybody at home.\'\r\n\r\n\'Very likely not, but there is an atmosphere at St. Ambrose\'s.\'\r\n\r\n\'And oh, how glad I am to be in it!  I wonder how long they will let me\r\nstay!  The dear little mother will try to get me a Sunday here, if she\r\ndares.  Indeed, I can\'t hear before Saturday, and then there would\r\nhardly be time to get home!  Oh, that\'s jolly!  I\'ll go to the nursery\r\ngardens, and get _such_ flowers for the vases!\'\r\n\r\nSaturday brought Nuttie a letter, but not from her mother--\r\n\r\n\r\n\'My Dear Ursula--I write because we are anxious to keep your mother as\r\nquiet as possible.  It was a serious shock to her to find that you had\r\nleft home, and she naturally supposed that Miss Headworth was in great\r\ndanger.  Your father was greatly displeased, and she has been much\r\novercome, and very unwell, but we hope by keeping her perfectly quiet\r\nthat worse consequences may be prevented.  Your father desires you to\r\nremain where you are for the present, as he will not have her disturbed\r\nagain.  Your mother sends her love both to you and to your aunt, and\r\ndesires me to say that she will write in a day or two, and that she\r\nthinks you had better not come back till she is better and your\r\nfather\'s vexation has diminished.\r\n\r\n\'I wish you had informed us of your intentions, as then we could have\r\nascertained the grounds of the report that terrified you so\r\nstrangely.--I remain your affectionate aunt,\r\n\r\n                                         JANE M. EGREMONT.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Poor mother! he has been sneering at us all in his dreadful cynical\r\nway, and knocked her up into one of her awful headaches,\' said Nuttie,\r\nwho felt extremely angered by the grave tone of rebuke in the letter,\r\nand tossed it over to her aunt without absolutely reading it all.  Miss\r\nHeadworth was a good deal distressed, and anxious to know what Mrs.\r\nWilliam Egremont meant; but Nuttie positively declared, \'Oh, it is her\r\nheadaches!  You know she always had them more or less, and they have\r\ngrown a great deal worse since she has taken to sitting in that horrid,\r\nstuffy, perfumy, cigar-ry room, and doesn\'t take half exercise enough.\'\r\n\r\nAnd when Miss Headworth showed herself much concerned about the state\r\nof things, Nuttie coaxed her, and declared that she should fancy\r\nherself unwelcome, and have to go and beg a lodging somewhere instead\r\nof enjoying her reprieve.  And Aunt Ursel was far less impervious to\r\ncoaxing than she used to be when she was the responsible head of a\r\nboarding house.  She did most thoroughly enjoy the affection of her\r\ngreat niece, and could not persuade herself to be angry with her,\r\nespecially when the girl looked up smiling and said, \'If the worst came\r\nto the worst and he did disinherit me, the thing would only right\r\nitself.  I always meant to give it back to Mark.\'\r\n\r\nNo great aunt in the world could fail to admire the generous spirit of\r\nthe girl who came back from the great world of luxury, so loving and\r\nhappy in her humble surroundings.  The only sighs were for poor Alice,\r\nin the hands of a man of whom Miss Headworth knew so much evil.  If she\r\nwere not wretched and a victim--and Nuttie did not think her such--she\r\nmust surely be getting spoilt and worldly.  Her daughter implied fears\r\nof this kind, yet who could read her letters and think so?\r\n\r\nNuttie was fortunately too much in awe of the Canoness to write all the\r\npertnesses that tingled at her fingers\' ends, and she sent a proper and\r\nfairly meek letter, intimating, however, that she was only too happy to\r\nremain at Micklethwayte.\r\n\r\nIt was two or three days more before she heard again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'My Own Dear Child--They have let me write at last, and I can say how\r\nmuch I like to think of your nestling up to dear Aunt Ursel, and how\r\nglad I am to find that she was well enough to enjoy you. It is almost\r\nlike being there to hear of you, and the only thing that grieves me is\r\nthat your father was very much vexed at your setting off in that sudden\r\nway, and at my being so foolish about it.  His eyes have been very bad,\r\nand he missed me sadly while I was laid up.  We are neither of us very\r\nstrong, and we think--if Aunt Ursel and Mary can keep you for a little\r\nlonger--it will be better for you to stay on with them, as it might be\r\nas dreary for you as it was last winter, especially as the Rectory folk\r\nwill soon be going into residence.  I will write to them about it and\r\npersuade them to take something for your board, so as to make it easy\r\nfor them.  And then you can have a fire in your room; you must not\r\nleave it off now you are used to it.  My dear, I wish you would write a\r\nlittle apology to your father.  I ought not to conceal that he is\r\nreally very angry, and I think it would be well if you expressed some\r\nregret, or if you cannot truthfully do that, asked his pardon for your\r\nimpetuosity; for you know he cannot be expected to realise all that\r\ndear Aunt Ursel is to us.  You cannot think how kind your Aunt Jane has\r\nbeen to me; I did not think she could have been so tender.  This is the\r\nfirst letter I ever had to write to you, my own dear child.  I miss you\r\nevery moment, but after all it is better you should be away till your\r\nfather has overlooked this hurried expedition of yours.  I am sure he\r\nwould if you wrote him a real nice letter, telling how you were really\r\nfrightened, and that it was not a mere excuse.  Pray do, and then you\r\ncan come back to your loving little mother.\r\n\r\n                                \'A. E.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\'As if I would or could,\' quoth Nuttie to herself.  \'Apologise to him\r\nindeed, for loving the aunt who toiled for us when he deserted us. Poor\r\nlittle mother, she can\'t really expect it of me.  Indeed, I don\'t think\r\nshe quite knows what she wants, or whether she likes me to be here or\r\nat Bridgefield!  My belief is that he bullies her less when I am out of\r\nthe way, because she just gives way to him, and does not assert any\r\nprinciple.  I\'ve tried to back her up, and it is of no use, and I am\r\nsure I don\'t want such a winter as the last.  So I am much better here;\r\nand as to begging pardon, when I have done nothing wrong, I am sure I\r\nwon\'t, to please anybody.  I shall tell her that she ought to know me\r\nbetter than to expect it!\'\r\n\r\nBut Nuttie did not show the letter either to Aunt Ursel or Mary Nugent;\r\nnor did she see that in which Alice had satisfied them that it might be\r\nbetter that her daughter should pay them a long visit, while Mr.\r\nEgremont\'s health required constant attendance, and the Canon\'s family\r\nwere at Redcastle.  And as her husband was always open-handed, she\r\ncould make Ursula\'s stay with them advantageous to their slender means,\r\nwithout hurting their feelings.\r\n\r\nShe told them as much as she could, but there was more that no living\r\ncreature might know, namely, the advantage that Gregorio had gained\r\nover that battlefield, his master, during her days of illness.  The\r\nfirst cold weather had brought on pain, and anger and anxiety, nervous\r\nexcitement and sleeplessness, which the valet had taken upon him to\r\ncalm with a narcotic under a new name that at first deceived her till\r\nshe traced its effects, and inquired of Dr. Hammond about it.\r\nUnwillingly, on her account, he enlightened her, and showed her that,\r\nthough the last year\'s care had done much to loosen the bonds of the\r\nsubtle and alluring habit, yet that any resumption of it tended to\r\nplunge its victim into the fatal condition of the confirmed\r\nopium-eater, giving her every hope at the same time that this\r\npropensity might be entirely shaken off, and that the improvement in\r\nMr. Egremont\'s health and habits which had set in might be confirmed,\r\nand raise him above the inclination.\r\n\r\nCould she have been rid of Gregorio, she would have felt almost sure of\r\nvictory; but as it was, she believed the man absolutely meant to baffle\r\nher, partly out of a spiteful rivalry, partly because his master\'s\r\ntorpid indolence could be used to his own advantage.  She was\r\nabsolutely certain that his sneering tone of remark made her husband\r\ndoubly disinclined to let any religious book be near, or to permit her\r\nto draw him to any Sunday observance.\r\n\r\nThe battle must be fought out alone.  The gentle woman could have no\r\nearthly helper in the struggle.  The Canon and Mark, the only persons\r\nwho could have given her the slightest aid, were both at a distance,\r\neven if her loyal heart could have brooked confession to them, and she\r\nonly hoped that Nuttie would never know of it.  Only aid from above\r\ncould be with her in the daily, hourly effort of cheerfulness,\r\npatience, and all the resources of feminine affection, to avert the\r\ntemptation; and she well knew that the presence of the ardent,\r\nunsubdued, opinionative girl would, alas! only double the difficulties.\r\nSo she acquiesced, at least for the present, in Nuttie\'s grand\r\nachievement of having broken away from all the wealth and luxury of\r\nBridgefield to return to her simple home and good old aunt.  Mark was a\r\ngood deal vexed, but Nuttie did not care about that, attributing this\r\ndispleasure to Egremont clanship; Mary Nugent was doubtful and anxious,\r\nand thought it her duty to reconcile herself to her father; but Miss\r\nHeadworth, who, be it remembered, had reason to have the worst\r\nimpressions of Mr. Egremont, rejoiced in her young niece having escaped\r\nfrom him for the time, and only sighed over the impossibility of\r\nAlice\'s doing the same.  And when Nuttie described, as she constantly\r\ndid, the various pleasures she had enjoyed during the past year, the\r\ngood old lady secretly viewed her as a noble Christian heroine for\r\nresigning all this in favour of the quiet little home at Micklethwayte,\r\nthough reticent before her, and discussed her excellence whenever she\r\nwas alone with Mary.\r\n\r\nNor would Miss Nugent vex her with contradictions or hints that what\r\nNuttie was giving up at present might be a dull house, with her mother\r\nengrossed by an irritable semi-invalid, and the few gaieties to be\r\nenjoyed by the help of the Canon\'s family at Redcastle.  She did ask\r\nthe girl whether Mrs. Egremont, being avowedly not quite well, might\r\nnot need her assistance; but Nuttie vehemently disavowed being of any\r\npossible use to her father; he never let her read to him! oh no! he\r\ncalled her music schoolgirly, a mere infliction; he never spoke to her\r\nif he could help it, and then it was always with a sort of sneer; she\r\nbelieved he could not bear the sight of her, and was ashamed of it, as\r\nwell he might be!  For Mrs. Houghton\'s disclosures had rankled ever\r\nsince within her, and had been confirmed by her aunt.\r\n\r\n\'But that is very sad,\' said Mary.  \'I am so sorry for you.  Ought you\r\nnot to try hard to conquer his distaste?\'\r\n\r\n\'I--why, he cares for nothing good!\'\r\n\r\n\'Nay,\' smiling.  \'Not for your mother?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!  She\'s pretty, you know; besides, she makes herself a regular\r\nslave to him, and truckles to him in everything, as I could never do.\'\r\n\r\n\'Perhaps she is overcoming evil with good.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am afraid it is more like being overcome of evil.  No, no, dear Miss\r\nMary, don\'t be shocked.  The dear little mother never would be anything\r\nbut good in her own sweet self, but it is her nature not to stand up\r\nfor anything, you know.  She seems to me just like a Christian woman\r\nthat has been obliged to marry some Paynim knight. And it perfectly\r\nprovokes me to see her quite gratified at his notice, and ready to\r\nsacrifice anything to him, now I know how he treated her.  If I had\r\nbeen in her place, I wouldn\'t have gone back to him; no, not if he had\r\nbeen ready to crown me after I was dead, like Ines de Castro.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t know that you would have had much choice in that case.\'\r\n\r\n\'My very ghost would have rebelled,\' said Nuttie, laughing a little.\r\n\r\nAnd Mary could believe that Mrs. Egremont, with all her love for her\r\ndaughter, might find it a relief not to have to keep the peace between\r\nthe father and child.  \'Yet,\' she said to herself, \'if Mr. Dutton were\r\nhere, he would have taken her back the first day.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII.\r\n\r\nDISENCHANTMENT.\r\n\r\n  \'He promised to buy me a bunch of blue ribbons.\'\r\n\r\n\r\nSt. Ambrose\'s road was perfectly delightful as long as there was any\r\nexpectation of a speedy recall.  Every day was precious; every meeting\r\nwith an old face was joyful; each interchange of words with Mr. Spyers\r\nor Gerard Godfrey was hailed as a boon; nothing was regretted but the\r\nabsence of Monsieur and his master, and that the favourite choir boy\'s\r\nvoice was cracked.\r\n\r\nBut when there was reason to think that success had been complete, when\r\nMiss Headworth had been persuaded by Mary that it was wiser on all\r\naccounts not to mortify Alice by refusing the two guineas a week\r\noffered for Miss Egremont\'s expenses; when a couple of boxes of clothes\r\nand books had arrived, and Ursula found herself settled at\r\nMicklethwayte till after Christmas, she began first to admit to herself\r\nthat somehow the place was not all that it had once been to her.\r\n\r\nHer mother was absent, that was one thing.  Mrs. Nugent was gone, that\r\nwas another.  There was no Monsieur or Mr. Dutton to keep her in awe of\r\nhis precision, even while she laughed at it.  There were no boarders to\r\npatronise and play with, and her education at the High School was over.\r\nIf she saw a half-clothed child, it was not half so interesting to buy\r\nan ulster in the next shop, as it was to turn over the family rag-bag,\r\nknit, sew, and contrive!  Somehow things had a weariness in them, and\r\nthe little excitements did not seem to be the exquisite delights they\r\nused to be.  After having seen Patience at the Princess\'s it was not\r\neasy to avoid criticising a provincial Lady Jane, and it was the like\r\nwith other things of more importance.  Even the ritual of St. Ambrose\'s\r\nChurch no longer struck her as the ne plus ultra of beauty, and only\r\nincited her to describe London churches.\r\n\r\nShe resumed her Sunday-school classes, and though she talked at first\r\nof their raciness and freedom, she soon longed after the cleanliness,\r\nrespectfulness, and docility of the despised little Bridgefordites, and\r\nuttered bitter things of Micklethwayte turbulence, declaring--perhaps\r\nnot without truth--that the children had grown much worse in her\r\nabsence.\r\n\r\nAnd as Mr. Godfrey had been superintendent during the latter half of\r\nthe time, this was a cruel stroke.  He wanted to make her reverse her\r\nopinions.  And they never met without \'Now, Ursula, don\'t you remember\r\nJem Burton putting on Miss Pope\'s spectacles, and grinning at all the\r\nclass.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; and how Mr. Dutton brought him up to beg her pardon.  Now, was\r\nany notice taken when that horrid boy--I don\'t know his name--turned\r\nthe hymn they were saying to her into "Tommy, make room for your\r\nuncle"?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Albert Cox!  It is no use doing anything to him, he would go off\r\nat once to the Primitives.\'\r\n\r\n\'Let him!\'\r\n\r\n\'I cannot make him a schismatic.\'\r\n\r\n\'I wonder what he is now!\'\r\n\r\n\'Besides, Miss Pope perfectly provokes impertinence.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then I wouldn\'t give her work she can\'t do.\'\r\n\r\nSuch an argument as this might be very well at the moment of\r\nprovocation, but it became tedious when recurred to at every meeting.\r\nNuttie began to wonder when Monks Horton would be inhabited again, and\r\nhow much notice Lady Kirkaldy would take of her, and she was a good\r\ndeal disappointed when Mark told her that Lord Kirkaldy had been begged\r\nto undertake a diplomatic mission which would keep them abroad all the\r\nwinter.\r\n\r\nThere was a certain weariness and want of interest.  It was not exactly\r\nthat there was nothing intellectual going on.  There were the lectures,\r\nbut they were on chemistry, for which Nuttie cared little. There were\r\ngood solid books, and lively ones too, but they seemed passe to one who\r\nhad heard them discussed in town.  Mary and Miss Headworth read and\r\ntalked them over, and perhaps their opinions were quite as wise, and\r\nMiss Nugent\'s conversation was equal to that of any of Nuttie\'s London\r\nfriends, but it was only woman\'s talk after all--the brilliancy and\r\npiquancy, the touch and go, she had enjoyed in Lady Kirkaldy\'s\r\ndrawing-room was lacking.\r\n\r\nMr. Spyers was too much immersed in parish matters to read anything\r\nsecular, and neither he nor Gerard Godfrey seemed ever to talk of\r\nanything but parish matters.  There was not the slightest interest in\r\nanything beyond.  Foreign politics, European celebrities,--things in\r\nwhich Nuttie had learnt to take warm interest when with the Kirkaldys,\r\nwere nothing to them.  Even Mary wondered at her endeavours to see the\r\nday\'s paper, and she never obtained either information or sympathy\r\nunless she came across Mark.  It seemed to her that Gerard cared less\r\nfor the peace or war of an empire than for a tipsy cobbler taking the\r\npledge.  The monotony and narrowness of the world where she had once\r\nbeen so happy fretted and wearied her, though she was ashamed of\r\nherself all the time, and far too proud to allow that she was tired of\r\nit all.  Aunt Ursel at her best had always been a little dry and grave,\r\nan authority over the two nieces; and though softened, she was not\r\nexpansive, did not invite confidences, and home was not home without\r\nthe playfellow-mother.\r\n\r\nAnd most especially was she daily tired of Gerard Godfrey!  Had he\r\nalways talked of nothing but \'the colours,\' chants, E. C. U., classes,\r\nand teetotalism?
