Whatever she began it always came back to one or\r\nother of these subjects, and when she impatiently declared that she was\r\nperfectly sick of hearing of the Use of Sarum, he looked at her as\r\nguilty of a profanity.\r\n\r\nPerhaps it was true that he was narrower than he had been.  He was a\r\ngood, honest, religiously-minded lad, but with no great depth or grasp\r\nof intellect; Ursula Egremont had been his companion first and then his\r\nromance, and the atmosphere of the community in which he lived had been\r\nstudious and intelligent.  His expedition to Redcastle had convinced\r\nhim that the young lady lived in a different world, entirely beyond his\r\nreach, and in the reaction of his hopelessness, he had thrown himself\r\ninto the excitement of the mission, and it had worked on him a zealous\r\npurpose to dedicate himself totally to a religious life, giving up all\r\nworldly aims, and employing the small capital he could call his own in\r\npreparing for the ministry.  Mr. Dutton had insisted that he should\r\ntest his own steadfastness and resolution by another year\'s work in his\r\npresent situation before he took any steps.\r\n\r\nHe had submitted, but still viewed himself as dedicated, and so far as\r\nbusiness hours permitted, gave his services like a clerical pupil to\r\nSt. Ambrose\'s with the greatest energy, and perhaps somewhat less\r\njudgment than if Mr. Dutton had been at hand.  Being without natural\r\ntaste for intellectual pursuits, unless drawn into them by his\r\nsurroundings, he had dropped them entirely, and read nothing but the\r\nephemeral controversial literature of his party, and not much of that,\r\nfor he was teaching, preaching, exhorting, throughout his spare time;\r\nwhile the vicar was in too great need of help to insist on deepening\r\nthe source from which his zealous assistant drew.  As Miss Nugent\r\nobserved, teetotalism was to him what dissipation was to other young\r\nmen.\r\n\r\nOn this vehemence of purpose descended suddenly Ursula Egremont once\r\nmore; and the human heart could not but be quickened with the idea, not\r\nentirely unfounded, that it was to him that she had flown back, and\r\nthat her exile proved that she cared for him more than for all the\r\ndelights she had enjoyed as heiress of Bridgefield.  The good youth was\r\nconscientious to the back-bone, and extremely perplexed between his\r\nself-dedication and the rights that their implied understanding might\r\ngive to her.  Was she to be the crowning blessing of his life, to be\r\nsaved partly through his affection from worldly trials and temptations,\r\nand bestowing on him a brilliant lot in which boundless good could be\r\neffected?  Or was she a syren luring him to abandon his higher and\r\nbetter purposes?\r\n\r\nThe first few days of her stay, the former belief made him feel like\r\ntreading on air, or like the hero of many a magazine story; but as time\r\nwent on this flattering supposition began to fail him, when Nuttie\r\nshowed her weariness of the subjects which, in his exclusiveness, he\r\ndeemed the only ones worthy of a Christian, or rather of a Catholic.\r\nBoth of them had outgrown the lively, aimless chatter and little jests\r\nthat had succeeded the games of childhood, and the growth had been in\r\ndifferent directions, so that Ursula felt herself untrue to her old\r\nromance when she became weary of his favourite topics, disappointed by\r\nhis want of sympathy and comprehension, fretted by his petty\r\ndisapprovals, and annoyed by his evident distaste for Mark, to whom she\r\nturned as to one of her proper world.\r\n\r\nAt last, after many tossings, Gerard fixed upon a test.  If she endured\r\nit she would be the veritable maiden of his imagination, and they would\r\nstand by one another, come what would; if not, he would believe that\r\nthe past had been fancy, not love, or love that had not withstood the\r\nattractions of fashionable life.  A great temperance meeting was coming\r\non, and Gerard, eager at once to fill the room, and to present a goodly\r\nroll of recruits, watched anxiously for his moment, and came on Nuttie\r\nwith his hands full of bills in huge letters, and his pockets of badges.\r\n\r\n\'Excellent speakers,\' he cried.  \'We shall have the hall crowded.\r\nYou\'ll come, Ursula?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t know what Miss Mary will do.  I don\'t think she means it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, if you insist, if we both insist, she will.  Look at the paper--we\r\nare to have some splendid experiences.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie made a face.  \'I\'ve heard all about those,\' she said.  \'That\r\nman,\' pointing to one of the names, \'regularly rants about it; he is\r\nlike a madman.\'\r\n\r\n\'He does go rather far, but it is quite necessary, as you will hear.\r\nOh, Nuttie, if you would only be one of us!  I\'ve brought a card!  If\r\nyou would!\'\r\n\r\n\'Why, what\'s the use, Gerard!  I don\'t like wine, I never do drink it,\r\nexcept a little claret-cup sometimes when I can\'t get water.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then it would cost you nothing.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, it would.  It would make me ridiculous.\'\r\n\r\n\'You used not to heed the sneers of the world.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not for anything worth doing--but this is not.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is the greatest cause of the day!\' he cried, in an eager exalted\r\nmanner, which somewhat inclined her to laugh.  \'Do away with alcohol\r\nand you would do away with crime!\'\r\n\r\n\'Thank you for the compliment, Gerard; I never found that the\r\ninfinitesimal drop of alcohol that I suppose there is in a tumbler of\r\nclaret-cup disposed me to commit crimes.\'\r\n\r\n\'Why won\'t you understand me, Ursula!  Can\'t you give up that for the\r\nsake of saving others!\'\r\n\r\n\'I wonder whom it would save.\'\r\n\r\n\'Example saves!  If you put on this\'--taking out the badge--\'how many\r\nshould you not lead at your home?\'\r\n\r\n\'Just nobody!  Mother and I should have a bad time of it, that\'s all.\'\r\n\r\n\'And if you endured, what would not your testimony effect in the\r\nhousehold and village?\'\r\n\r\n\'Nothing!  I have nothing to do with the men-servants, and as to the\r\nvillage, it is very sober.  There\'s only one public house, and that is\r\nkept by Uncle William\'s old butler, and is as orderly as can be.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! that\'s the way you all deceive yourselves.  Moderate drinkers are\r\nten times more mischievous than regular drunkards.\'\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, Gerard!  And outrageous abstainers are more mischievous\r\nthan either of them, because they make the whole thing so utterly\r\nfoolish and absurd.\'  She was really angry now, and so was Gerard.\r\n\r\n\'Is that your ultimatum?\' he asked, in a voice that he strove to render\r\ncalm.\r\n\r\n\'Certainly; I\'m not going to take the pledge.\'\r\n\r\nHaving quarrelled in childhood, made quarrelling now easier, and Gerard\r\nanswered bitterly:\r\n\r\n\'Very well, I hope you will have no cause to repent it.\'\r\n\r\n\'\'Tis not the way to make me repent it, to see how it seems to affect\r\nsome people\'s common sense.  It is just as if all your brains had run\r\nto water!\' said Nuttie, laughing a little; but Gerard was desperately\r\nserious, and coloured vehemently.\r\n\r\n\'Very well, Miss Egremont, I understand.  I have had my answer,\' he\r\nsaid, gathering up his papers and marching out of the room.\r\n\r\nShe stood still, offended, and not in the least inclined to run after\r\nhim and take back her words.  He, poor fellow, stumbled down the steps,\r\nand held by the garden rail to collect his senses and compose himself.\r\n\r\n\'What\'s the matter, Gerard, are you ill or giddy?\' asked Miss Nugent,\r\ncoming up in the winter twilight.\r\n\r\n\'No, oh no!  Only the dream of my life is over,\' he answered, scarce\r\nknowing what he said.\r\n\r\n\'You haven\'t--\' cried Mary aghast.\r\n\r\n\'Oh no,\' he said, understanding the blank, \'only she won\'t take the\r\npledge!\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t see how she could or ought,\' responded Mary.  \'Is that all?\'\r\n\r\n\'I had made it the test,\' muttered poor Gerard.  \'It is right!  It is\r\nall over now.  I shall know how to go on my way.  It is best so--I know\r\nit is--only I did not know whether anything was due to her.\'  It was\r\nalmost a sob.\r\n\r\n\'Dear old Gerard,\' said Mary, \'I see you meant to do right.  It is well\r\nyour mind should be settled.  I think you\'ll find comfort in your good\r\nwork.\'\r\n\r\nHe wrung her hand, and she went in, half amused, for she was fully\r\naware of the one-sidedness of the mania for temperance under which he\r\nacted, yet honouring his high, pure motives, and rejoicing that he had\r\nfound this indirect mode of gauging Nuttie\'s feelings towards him--that\r\nis, if he was right about them, and there was no revulsion.\r\n\r\nFar from it.  Nuttie was still angry.  \'Gerard had been so ridiculous,\'\r\nshe said, \'teasing her to take the pledge, and quite incapable of\r\nunderstanding her reasons.  I can\'t think why Gerard has grown so\r\nstupid.\'\r\n\r\n\'Enthusiasms carry people away,\' returned Mary.\r\n\r\n\'If Mr. Dutton had only stayed, he would have kept Gerard like\r\nhimself,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\nBut there was no relenting.  The two young people avoided each other;\r\nand perhaps Nuttie was secretly relieved that the romance she had\r\noutgrown no longer entangled her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\r\n\r\nA FAILURE.\r\n\r\n  \'Would I had loved her more!\'--Mrs. Hemans.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'On the 14th of January, at Bridgefield Egremont, the wife of Alwyn\r\nPiercefield Egremont, Esquire, a son and heir.\'\r\n\r\nUrsula had been prepared for this event for about a fortnight by a long\r\ntender letter from her mother, mourning over the not meeting at\r\nChristmas, and the long separation, but saying that she had wished to\r\nspare the long anxiety, and that it had been a trying time which she\r\nfelt herself able to cope with better alone, than even with her dear\r\nNuttie, knowing her to be happy and safe with Aunt Ursel.  Now, if all\r\nwent well, they would have a happy meeting, and begin on a new score.\r\n\'If the will of God should be otherwise,\' added Alice, \'I am sure I\r\nneed not entreat my Nuttie to do and be all that she can to her father.\r\nMy child, you do not know how sorely he needs such love and tendance\r\nand prayer as you can give him.  I know you have thought I have set you\r\naside--if not better things, for his sake.  Indeed I could not help\r\nit.\'  Then there was something tear-stained and blotted out, and it\r\nended with, \'He is beginning to miss your step and voice about the\r\nhouse.  I believe he will really be glad to see you, when the bright\r\nspring days come, and I can kiss my own Nuttie again.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was very much delighted, but a little hurt that her aunt and\r\nMary should have been in the secret, and pledged to say nothing to her\r\ntill her mother should write.  She found, moreover, that Miss Headworth\r\nwas extremely anxious and not altogether reassured by Mrs. William\r\nEgremont\'s letter of announcement, which filled Nuttie with delight.\r\nHow happy the little mother must be to have a baby in her arms again,\r\nand though she herself did not profess to have a strong turn for infant\r\nhumanity, it was the greatest possible relief to be no longer an\r\nheiress, excepting that the renunciation in favour of Mark was no\r\nlonger practicable.\r\n\r\nThe residence at Redcastle was not over, but the Canoness had come to\r\nnurse her sister-in-law, and kept up the correspondence.  The son and\r\nheir was reported to be a perfect specimen, and his father was greatly\r\nelated and delighted, but the letters showed anxiety about the mother,\r\nwho did not get on as she ought, and seemed to have no power of rally\r\nabout her.  At length came a letter that seemed to burn itself into\r\nNuttie\'s brain--\r\n\r\n\r\n\'My Dear Ursula--Your mother is longing to see you.  You had better\r\ncome home directly.  Your aunt saved her before.  Tell her if she will\r\ncome, she shall have my deepest gratitude.  I shall send to meet the\r\n5.11 train.--Your affectionate father,\r\n\r\n                                       A. P. EGREMONT.\'\r\n\r\n\r\nMrs. William Egremont wrote at more length.  Symptoms had set in which\r\nfilled the doctors and nurses with double anxiety.  Advice had been\r\nsent for from London, and Mr. Egremont was in an uncontrollable state\r\nof distress.  She had undertaken to summon Ursula home, and to beg Miss\r\nHeadworth to undertake the journey.  She evidently did not know that\r\nher brother-in-law had written himself, and before they could start a\r\ntelegram terrified them, but proved to contain no fresh tidings, only a\r\nrenewed summons.\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth forgot all her resolutions about Mr. Egremont\'s\r\nhospitality--her Alice was her only thought, and all the remedies that\r\nhad been found efficacious at Dieppe.  The good lady had a certain\r\nconfidence in her own nursing and experience of Alice, which buoyed her\r\nup with hope, while Ursula seemed absolutely stunned.  She had never\r\nthought of such a frightful loss or grief, and her mental senses were\r\nalmost paralysed, so that she went through the journey in a kind of\r\nsurface trance, observing all around her much as usual, looking out for\r\nthe luggage and for the servant who had come to meet them with the\r\nreport \'No change.\'  She did the honours of the carriage, and covered\r\nMiss Headworth with the fur rug.  They wanted it, for they were\r\nshivering with anxiety.\r\n\r\nCanon Egremont came out to the front hall to meet them, and put his\r\narms round Nuttie tenderly, saying, \'My poor dear child!\' then as he\r\nsaw he had frightened them, \'No, no!  She is alive--conscious they say,\r\nonly so very weak.\'  Then with something of his usual urbane grace, he\r\nheld out his hand, \'Miss Headworth, it is very good in you to come.\r\nYou have a great deal to forgive.\'\r\n\r\nHe took them into the tent-room, where tea was standing, interrupting\r\nhimself in the account he was giving to bid Nuttie let her aunt have\r\nsome.  It was plain from his manner that he had given up hope, and in\r\nanother minute in hurried his brother, looking terribly haggard and\r\nwith bloodshot eyes, giving his hand to each, with, \'That\'s right, Miss\r\nHeadworth, thank you.  Come, let me know what you think of her!\'\r\n\r\n\'Does she know they are come?\' said the Canon.  \'No?  Then, Alwyn, let\r\nthem have some tea, and take off their things.  I can tell you, the\r\nnurses will never let them in just off a journey.\'\r\n\r\nMiss Headworth seconded this, and Mr. Egremont submitted, allowing that\r\nshe had not asked for Nuttie since the morning, and then had smiled and\r\nsqueezed his hand when he said she was coming with her aunt; but he\r\nwalked up and down in direful restlessness, his whole mind apparently\r\nbent on extracting from Miss Headworth that she had been as ill or\r\nworse at Dieppe.\r\n\r\nAlas! when Mrs. William Egremont came down to fetch Nuttie; there was\r\nno question that matters were much worse.  The sweet face was perfectly\r\nwhite and wasted, and the heavy lids of the dark eyes scarcely lifted\r\nthemselves, but the lips moved into a smile, and the hand closed on\r\nthat of the girl, who stood by her as one frozen into numbness.  There\r\nwas the same recognition when her aunt was brought to her side, the\r\npoor old lady commanding herself with difficulty, as the loving glance\r\nquivered over the face.\r\n\r\nTime passed on, and she still held Nuttie\'s hand.  Once, when a little\r\nrevived by some stimulant at her lips, she made an effort and said,\r\n\'Stay with him!  Take care of him!  _Love_ him!  And your little\r\nbrother, my Nuttie!  Promise!\'\r\n\r\n\'I promise,\' the girl answered, scarce knowing what she said.\r\n\r\nAnd the eyes closed with an air of peace and rest.\r\n\r\nAgain when Miss Headworth was doing something to ease her position she\r\nsaid, \'Thank you,\' and then more vigorously, \'Thank you, dear aunt, for\r\nall you have been to us.\'\r\n\r\nThere was little more.  She asked Nuttie for \'her hymn,\' the evening\r\nhymn with which mother and daughter used nightly to go to sleep, and\r\nwhich, in her strange dreamy way, the girl managed to say.\r\n\r\nThen a little murmur and sign passed between the elder ladies, and Mrs.\r\nWilliam Egremont fetched her husband.  As he opened his book to find\r\nthe commendatory prayer, thinking her past all outward consciousness,\r\nand grieved by the look of suffering, her eyes again unclosed and her\r\nlips said, \'Failed.\'\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t think of that!  God can make failures success.\'\r\n\r\nThere was a half smile, a look of peace.  \'_He_ makes up,\' she said;\r\nand those were the last audible words before it was over, and the\r\ntender spirit was released from its strife, some time later, they only\r\nknew when by the failure of the clasp on her husband\'s hand.\r\n\r\nOld Miss Headworth did not understand the meaning of that sad word till\r\nthe next forenoon.  Then,--as she sat in the darkened tent-room, crying\r\nover her letters,--while the stunned and bewildered Nuttie was, under\r\nher Aunt Jane\'s direction, attending to the needful arrangements, Canon\r\nEgremont wandered in upon her in the overflow of confidence of a man\r\nwith a full heart, wanting to talk it all out, communicating the more,\r\nbecause she was a discreet woman, and asked no questions.  He had tried\r\nto see his brother, but Gregorio had not admitted him.  He was aware\r\nnow of the whole state of things.  Dr. Hammond had told him, when first\r\nbeginning to be alarmed for his patient, that the principal cause for\r\nanxiety was the exhaustion caused by the long strain on her spirits and\r\nstrength consequent on her efforts to wean her husband from his fatal\r\npropensity.  There had been other \'complications,\' as the doctor called\r\nthem, and more immediate causes of danger, but both he and his\r\ncolleague, summoned from London, believed that she would have\r\nsurmounted them if she had had more strength to rally.  But her nurses\r\ndated the decided turn for the worse from the day when she had gazed up\r\ninto Mr. Egremont\'s face, and detected the look in his eyes that she\r\nhad learnt too well to understand.\r\n\r\nShe would fain have lived, and, according to her obedient nature, had\r\nsubmitted to all the silence and stillness enforced on her; but she had\r\ntold Dr. Hammond that she must see her brother-in-law before she was\r\ntoo far gone.  And the doctor, knowing all, took care it should be\r\nbrought about.\r\n\r\nAnd then she had spoken of her failure in the effort of these years.\r\n\'If I had begun better,\' she said, \'it might not have been so with him.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, indeed you have nothing to blame yourself for.  You were\r\ngrievously sinned against by us all.  Alwyn was no saint when he drew\r\nyou into it--and you, you have been his good angel, doing all and more\r\ntoo,\' said the Canon, almost breaking down.\r\n\r\n\'I tried--but if I had been a better woman--And to leave him to that\r\nman!\'\r\n\r\n\'Child, child, victories sometimes come this way!\' he cried, scarce\r\nknowing how it was put into his mouth, but glad to see the light in her\r\neye.\r\n\r\n\'Thanks,\' she replied.  \'No, I ought not to have said that.  I leave\r\nhim to God, and my poor Nuttie.  I want you to tell her, if I can\'t,\r\nwhat she must try to do.  If I had but brought them together more! But\r\nI tried for the best.\'\r\n\r\nThen she begged for her last communion, saying, \'I do pray for that\r\npoor Gregorio.  Isn\'t that forgiving him?\'  And the attempt to exchange\r\nforgiveness with the Canon for their mutual behaviour at the time of\r\nher marriage overcame them both so much that they had to leave it not\r\nhalf uttered.  Indeed, in speaking of the scene, William Egremont was\r\nutterly overwhelmed.\r\n\r\n\'And that\'s the woman that I treated as a mere outcast!\' he cried,\r\nwalking about the little room.  \'Oh God, forgive me!  I shall never\r\nforgive myself.\'\r\n\r\nPoor Miss Headworth!  In past days she had longed for any amount of\r\nretribution on Alice\'s hard-hearted employers, but it was a very\r\ndifferent thing to witness such grief and self reproach.  He had in\r\ntruth much more developed ideas of duty, both as man and priest, than\r\nwhen he had passively left a disagreeable subject to his mother-in-law,\r\nas lying within a woman\'s province; and his good heart was suffering\r\nacutely for the injustice and injury in which he had shared towards one\r\nnow invested with an almost saintly halo.\r\n\r\nIn the gush of feeling he had certainly revealed more to Miss\r\nHeadworth, than his wife, or even he himself, in his cooler moments,\r\nwould have thought prudent, and he ended by binding her to secrecy; and\r\nsaying that he should only tell his niece what was necessary for her to\r\nknow.\r\n\r\nNuttie was going about, dry-eyed and numbed, glad of any passing\r\noccupation that would prevent the aching sense of desolation at her\r\nheart from gaining force to overwhelm her; courting employment, and\r\nshunning pity and condolence, but she could not escape when her uncle\r\ntook her hand, made her sit down by him, with \'I want to speak to you,\r\nmy dear;\' and told her briefly and tenderly what her mother\'s effort\r\nhad been, and of the message and task she had bequeathed. The poor\r\ngirl\'s heart fainted within her.\r\n\r\n\'Oh! but, Uncle William, how can I?  How can I ever?  Mother could do\r\nthings I never could!  He _did_ care for her!  He does not care for me!\'\r\n\r\n\'You must teach him to do so, Nuttie.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!\' she said, with a hopeless sound.\r\n\r\nThe Canon did think it very hopeless in his heart, but he persevered,\r\nas in duty bound.  \'I told your dear mother that perhaps you would\r\nsucceed where she thought she had failed, though indeed she had done\r\nmuch.  It made her happy.  So, my dear child, you are bound to do your\r\nbest.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes;\' then, after a pause--\'But mother could coax him and manage him.\r\nMother was with him day and night; she could always get at him. What\r\ncan I do?\'\r\n\r\n\'I think you will find that he depends upon you more,\' said the Canon,\r\n\'and it may be made easier to you, if you only set your will to it.\'\r\n\r\n\'If I ought, I\'ll try,\' said poor Nuttie, more humbly perhaps than she\r\nhad ever spoken before, but in utter dejection, and her uncle answered\r\nher like a child.\r\n\r\n\'There, that\'s a good girl.  Nobody can do more.\'\r\n\r\nFor the Canon had one hope.  He had not thought it becoming to speak to\r\nher of the counter influence, but he could not help thinking it\r\npossible that if he and his son, backed by doctor and lawyer, made a\r\nlong pull, a strong pull, and a pull altogether, they might induce his\r\nbrother to part with Gregorio, and this would render Ursula\'s task far\r\nless impossible.\r\n\r\nHe was confirmed in this hope by finding that Mark\'s arrival was not\r\nunwelcome to Mr. Egremont, who seemed to have forgotten the\r\nunpleasantness with which he had regarded the engagement, and only\r\nremembered that his nephew had been Alice\'s champion, resuming old\r\ncustoms of dependence, making him act as amanuensis, and arraigning the\r\ndestiny that had restored so lovely and charming a creature only to\r\nsnatch her away, leaving nothing but a headstrong girl and a helpless\r\nbaby.\r\n\r\nThat poor little fellow was all that could be desired at his age, but\r\nNuttie felt her beautiful mother almost insulted when the elder ladies\r\ntalked of the wonderful resemblance that the Canoness declared to have\r\nbeen quite startling in the earlier hours of his life.  For the\r\nconvenience of one of the sponsors, he was to be christened in the\r\nafternoon following the funeral, the others being--by his mother\'s\r\nspecial entreaty--his sister and Mark.  Egremont customs were against\r\nthe ladies going to the funeral, so that Nuttie was kept at home, much\r\nagainst her will; but after the luncheon she escaped, leaving word with\r\nher aunts that she was going to walk down to church alone, and they\r\nwere sorry enough for her to let her have her own way, especially as\r\nher father, having been to the funeral, had shut himself up and left\r\nall the rest to them.\r\n\r\nThe Egremont family had a sort of enclosure or pen with iron rails\r\nround it close to the church wall, where they rested under flat slabs.\r\nThe gate in this was open now, and the new-made grave was one mass of\r\nwhite flowers,--wreaths and crosses, snowdrops, hyacinths, camellias,\r\nand the like,--and at the feet was a flowerpot with growing plants of\r\nthe white hyacinth called in France \'lys de la Vierge.\'  These, before\r\nthey became frequent in England, had been grown in Mr. Dutton\'s\r\ngreenhouse, and having been favourites with Mrs. Egremont, it had come\r\nto be his custom every spring to bring her the earliest plants that\r\nbloomed.  Nuttie knew them well, the careful tying up, the neat\r\narrangement of moss over the earth, the peculiar trimness of the whole;\r\nand as she looked, the remembrance of the happy times of old, the sick\r\nlonging for all that was gone, did what nothing had hitherto\r\neffected--brought an overwhelming gush of tears.\r\n\r\nThere was no checking them now that they had come.  She fled into\r\nchurch on the first sounds of arrival and hid herself in the friendly\r\nshelter of the great family pew; but she had to come out and take her\r\nplace, though she could hardly utter a word, and it was all that she\r\ncould do to keep from sobbing aloud; she could not hand the babe, and\r\nthe Canon had to take on trust the name \'Alwyn Headworth,\' for he could\r\nnot hear the words that were on her trembling lips.\r\n\r\nIt was soon over; and while the baby and his attendants, with Miss\r\nHeadworth, were being packed into the carriage, and her uncle and aunt\r\nbowing off the grand god-father, she clutched her cousin\'s arm, and\r\nsaid, \'Mark; where\'s Mr. Dutton?\'\r\n\r\n\'I--I didn\'t know he was coming, but now you ask, I believe I saw him\r\nthis morning.\'\r\n\r\n\'I know he is here.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you want to see him?\' said Mark kindly.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, if I might!\'\r\n\r\nThen, with a sudden impulse, she looked back into the church, and\r\nrecognised a black figure and slightly bald head bowed down in one of\r\nthe seats.  She pointed him out.  \'No doubt he is waiting for us all to\r\nbe gone,\' said Mark in a low voice.  \'You go into the Rectory, Nuttie;\r\nthere\'s a fire in the study, and I\'ll bring him to you there. I\'ll get\r\nhim to stay the night if I can.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, thank you!\' and it was a really fervent answer.\r\n\r\nMark waited, and when Mr. Dutton rose, was quite shocked at his\r\npaleness and the worn look on his face, as of one who had struggled\r\nhard for resignation and calm.  He started, almost as if a blow had\r\nbeen struck him, as Mark uttered his name in the porch, no doubt having\r\nnever meant to be perceived nor to have to speak to any one; but in one\r\nmoment his features had recovered their usual expression of courteous\r\nreadiness.  He bowed his head when Mark told him that Ursula wanted to\r\nshake hands with him, and came towards the Rectory, but he entirely\r\ndeclined the invitation to sleep there, declaring that he must return\r\nto London that night.\r\n\r\nMark opened the study door, and then went away to secure that the man\r\nwhom he had learnt to esteem very highly should at least have some\r\nrefreshment before he left the house.\r\n\r\nThose few steps had given Mr. Dutton time to turn from a mourner to a\r\nconsoler, and when Nuttie came towards him with her hand outstretched,\r\nand \'Oh, Mr. Dutton, Mr. Dutton!\' he took it in both his, and with a\r\ncalm broken voice said, \'God has been very good to us in letting us\r\nknow one like her.\'\r\n\r\n\'But oh! what can we do without her?\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah, Nuttie! that always comes before us.  But I saw your work and your\r\ncomfort just now.\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor little boy!  I shall get to care about him, I know, but as yet I\r\ncan only feel how much rather I would have _her_.\'\r\n\r\n\'No doubt, but it is _her_ work that is left you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Her work?  Yes!  But oh, Mr. Dutton, you don\'t know how dreadful it\r\nis!\'\r\n\r\nHe did not know what she meant.  Whether it was simply the burthen on\r\nany suddenly motherless girl, or any special evil on her father\'s part,\r\nbut he was soon enlightened, for there was something in this old friend\r\nthat drew out her confidence beyond all others, even when he repressed\r\nher, and she could not help telling him in a few murmured furtive words\r\nsuch as she knew she ought not to utter, and he felt it almost treason\r\nto hear.  \'Opiates! she was always trying to keep my father from them!\r\nIt was too much for her!  My uncle says I must try to do it, and I\r\ncan\'t.\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor child!\' said Mr. Dutton kindly, though cut to the heart at the\r\nrevelation of sweet Alice\'s trial; \'at least you can strive, and there\r\nis always a blessing on resolution.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, if you knew! and he doesn\'t like me.  I don\'t think I\'ve ever been\r\nnice to him, and that vexed her!  I haven\'t got her ways.\'\r\n\r\n\'No,\' said Mr. Dutton, \'but you will learn others.  Look here, Nuttie.\r\nYou used to be always craving for grand and noble tasks, the more\r\ndifficult the better.  I think you have got one now, more severe than\r\never could have been thought of--and very noble.  What are those lines\r\nabout the task "bequeathed from bleeding sire to son"?  Isn\'t it like\r\nthat?  You are bound to go on with her work, and the more helpless you\r\nfeel, and the more you throw yourself on God, the more God will help\r\nyou.  He takes the will for the deed, if only you have will enough;\r\nand, Nuttie, you can pray that you may be able to love and honour him.\'\r\n\r\nTeacups were brought in, followed by Mark, and interrupted them; and,\r\nafter a short interval, they parted at the park gate, and Ursula walked\r\nhome with Mark, waked from her dull numb trance, with a crushed feeling\r\nas if she had been bruised all over, and yet with a purpose within her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV.\r\n\r\nFARMS OR UMBRELLAS.\r\n\r\n  \'He tokin into his handis\r\n   His londis and his lode.\'--CHAUCER.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Mark!  Mark!\'  A little figure stood on the gravel road leading\r\nthrough Lescombe Park, and lifted up an eager face, as Mark jumped down\r\nfrom his horse.  \'I made sure you would come over.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, but I could not get away earlier.  And I have so much to say to\r\nyou and your mother, Annaple; there\'s a great proposition to be\r\nconsidered.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh dear! and here is John bearing down upon us.  Never mind.  We\'ll\r\nget into the mither\'s room and be cosy!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, Mark,\' said Sir John\'s hearty voice, \'I thought you would be\r\nhere.  Come to luncheon?  That\'s right!  And how is poor Egremont? I\r\nthought he looked awful at the funeral.\'\r\n\r\n\'He is fairly well, thank you; but it was a terrible shock.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should think so.  To find such a pretty sweet creature just to lose\r\nher again.  Child likely to live, eh?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, he is a fine fellow, and has never had anything amiss with\r\nhim.\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor little chap!  Doesn\'t know what he has lost!  Well, Nannie,\' as\r\nthey neared the house, \'do you want a tete-a-tete or to take him in to\r\nyour mother?  Here, I\'ll take the horse.\'\r\n\r\n\'Come to her at once,\' said Annaple; \'she wants to hear all, and\r\nbesides she is expecting me.\'\r\n\r\nMark was welcomed by Lady Ronnisglen with inquiries for all concerned,\r\nand especially for that \'poor girl.  I do pity a young thing who has to\r\ntake a woman\'s place too soon,\' she said.  \'It takes too much out of\r\nher!\'\r\n\r\n\'I should think Ursula had plenty of spirit,\' said Annaple.\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t know whether spirit is what is wanted,\' said Mark.  \'Her\r\nmother prevailed more without it than I am afraid she is likely to do\r\nwith it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Complements answer better than parallels sometimes, but not always,\'\r\nsaid Lady Ronnisglen.\r\n\r\n\'Which are we?\' asked Annaple demurely.\r\n\r\n\'Not parallels certainly, for then we should never meet,\' responded\r\nMark.  \'But here is the proposal.  My father and all the rest of us\r\nhave been doing our best to get my uncle to smooth Ursula\'s way by\r\ngetting rid of that valet of his.\'\r\n\r\n\'The man with the Mephistopheles face?\'\r\n\r\n\'Exactly.  He is a consummate scoundrel, as we all know, and so does my\r\nuncle himself, but he has been about him these twelve or fourteen\r\nyears, and has got a sort of hold on him--that--that-- It is no use to\r\ntalk of it, but it did not make that dear aunt of mine have an easier\r\nlife.  In fact I should not be a bit surprised if he had been a\r\nhindrance in the hunting her up.  Well, the fellow thought proper to\r\nupset some arrangements my mother had made, and then was more insolent\r\nthan I should have thought even he could have been towards her.  I\r\nsuppose he had got into the habit with poor Aunt Alice.  That made a\r\nfulcrum, and my father went at my uncle with a will.  I never saw my\r\nfather so roused in my life.  I don\'t mean by the behaviour to his\r\nwife, but at what he knew of the fellow, and all the harm he had done\r\nand is doing.  And actually my uncle gave in at last, and consented to\r\ntell Gregorio to look out for another situation, if he has not\r\nfeathered his nest too well to need one, as I believe he has.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, that will make it much easier for Ursula!\' cried Annaple.\r\n\r\n\'If he goes,\' put in her mother.\r\n\r\n\'I think he will.  I really had no notion how much these two years have\r\nimproved my uncle!  To be sure, it would be hard to live with such a\r\nwoman as that without being the better for it!  But he really seems to\r\nhave acquired a certain notion of duty!\'\r\n\r\nThey did not smile at the simple way in which Mark spoke of this vast\r\nadvance, and Lady Ronnisglen said, \'I hope so, for the sake of his\r\ndaughter and that poor little boy.\'\r\n\r\n\'I think that has something to do with it,\' said Mark.  \'He feels a\r\nresponsibility, and still more, I think he was struck by having a\r\ncreature with him to whom evil was like physical pain.\'\r\n\r\n\'It will work,\' said Lady Ronnisglen.\r\n\r\n\'Then,\' went on Mark, \'he took us all by surprise by making me this\r\nproposal--to take the management of the estate, and become a kind of\r\nprivate secretary to him.  You know he gets rheumatism on the optic\r\nnerve, and is almost blind at times.  He would give me \xa3300 a year, and\r\ndo up the house at the home farm, rent free.  What do you say to that,\r\nAnnaple?\'\r\n\r\nThere was a silence, then Annaple said: \'Give up the umbrellas!  Oh!\r\nWhat do you think, Mark?\'\r\n\r\n\'My father wishes it,\' said Mark.  \'He would, as he had promised to do,\r\nmake over to me my share of my own mother\'s fortune, and that would, I\r\nhave been reckoning, bring us to just what we had thought of starting\r\nupon this spring at Micklethwayte.\'\r\n\r\n\'The same now,\' said Lady Ronnisglen, after some reckoning, \'but what\r\ndoes it lead to?\'\r\n\r\n\'Well--nothing, I am afraid,\' said Mark; \'as you know, this is all I\r\nhave to reckon upon.  The younger children will have hardly anything\r\nfrom their mother, so that my father\'s means must chiefly go to them.\'\r\n\r\n\'And this agency is entirely dependent on your satisfying Mr. Egremont?\'\r\n\r\n\'True, but that\'s a thing only too easily done.  However, as you say,\r\nthis agency has no future, and if that came to an end, I should only\r\nhave to look out for another or take to farming.\'\r\n\r\n\'And ask poor John if that is a good speculation nowadays!\' said\r\nAnnaple.\r\n\r\n\'Fortunes are and have been made on the umbrellas,\' said Mark.\r\n\'Greenleaf has a place almost equal to Monks Horton, and Dutton, though\r\nhe makes no show, has realised a considerable amount.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, let us stick to the umbrellas!\' cried Annaple; \'you\'ve made\r\nthe plunge, so it does not signify now, and we should be so much more\r\nindependent out of the way of everybody.\'\r\n\r\n\'You would lose in society,\' said Mark, \'excepting, of course, as to\r\nthe Monks Horton people; but they are often away.\'\r\n\r\n\'Begging your pardon, Mark, is there much to lose in this same\r\nneighbourhood?\' laughed Annaple, \'now May will go.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is not so much a question of liking,\' added her mother, \'as of what\r\nis for the best, and where you may wish to be--say ten years hence.\'\r\n\r\nLooked at in this way, there could be no question but that the umbrella\r\ncompany promised to make Mark a richer man in ten years\' time than did\r\nthe agency at Bridgefield Egremont.  He had a salary from the office\r\nalready, and if he purchased shares in the partnership with the portion\r\nhis father would resign to him, his income would already equal what he\r\nwould have at Bridgefield, and there was every prospect of its\r\nincrease, both as he became more valuable, and as the business\r\ncontinued to prosper.  If the descent in life had been a grievance to\r\nthe ladies, the agency would have been an infinite boon, but having\r\nswallowed so much, as Annaple said, they might as well do it in\r\nearnest, and to some purpose.  Perhaps, too, it might be detected that\r\nunder the circumstances Annaple would prefer the living in a small way\r\nout of reach of her sister\'s visible compassion.\r\n\r\nSo the matter was settled, but there was an undercurrent in Mark\'s mind\r\non which he had not entered, namely, that his presence at home might\r\nmake all the difference in that reformation in his uncle\'s habits which\r\nAlice had inaugurated, and left in the hands of others. With him at\r\nhand, there was much more chance of Gregorio\'s being dispensed with,\r\nUrsula\'s authority maintained, little Alwyn well brought up, and the\r\nestate, tenants, and household properly cared for, and then he smiled\r\nat his notion of supposing himself of so much importance.  Had he only\r\nhad himself to consider, Mark would have thought his duty plain; but\r\nwhen he found Miss Ruthven and her mother so entirely averse, he did\r\nnot deem it right to sacrifice them to the doubtful good of his uncle,\r\nnor indeed to put the question before them as so much a matter of\r\nconscience that they should feel bound to consider it in that light.\r\nHe did indeed say, \'Well, that settles it,\' in a tone that led Annaple\r\nto exclaim: \'I do believe you want to drop the umbrellas!\'\r\n\r\n\'No,\' he answered, \'it is not that, but my father wished it, and\r\nthought it would be good for my uncle.\'\r\n\r\n\'No doubt,\' said Annaple, \'but he has got a daughter, also a son, and a\r\nbrother, and agents are plentiful, so I can\'t see why all the family\r\nshould dance attendance on him.\'\r\n\r\nLady Ronnisglen, much misdoubting Mr. Egremont\'s style of society, and\r\ndreading that Mark might be dragged into it, added her word, feeling on\r\nher side that it was desirable and just to hinder the family from\r\nsacrificing Mark\'s occupation and worldly interest to a capricious old\r\nroue, who might very possibly throw him over when it would be almost\r\nimpossible to find anything else to do.  Moreover, both she and Annaple\r\nbelieved that the real wish was to rescue the name of Egremont from\r\nassociation with umbrellas, and they held themselves bound to combat\r\nwhat they despised and thought a piece of worldly folly.\r\n\r\nSo Mark rode home, more glad that the decision was actually made than\r\nat the course it had taken.  His father was disappointed, but could not\r\nbut allow that it was the more prudent arrangement; and Mr. Egremont\r\nshowed all the annoyance of a man whose good offer has been rejected.\r\n\r\n\'\'Tis that little giggling Scotch girl!\' he said.  \'Well, we are quit\r\nof her, anyway.  \'Tis a pity that Mark entangled himself with her, and\r\na mother-in-law into the bargain!  I was a fool to expect to get any\r\ngood out of him!\'\r\n\r\nThis was said to his daughter, with whom he was left alone; for Miss\r\nHeadworth could not bear to accept his hospitality a moment longer than\r\nneedful, and besides had been so much shaken in nerves as to suspect\r\nthat an illness was coming on, and hurried home to be nursed by Mary\r\nNugent.  Canon Egremont was obliged to go back to Redcastle to finish\r\nhis residence, and his wife, who had been absent nearly a month from\r\nher family, thought it really wisest to let the father and daughter be\r\nthrown upon one another at once, so that Ursula might have the benefit\r\nof her father\'s softened mood.\r\n\r\nThere could be no doubt that he was softened, and that he had derived\r\nsome improvement from the year and a half that his wife had been with\r\nhim.  It might not have lifted him up a step, but it had arrested him\r\nin his downward course.  Selfish and indolent he was as ever, but there\r\nhad been a restraint on his amusements, and a withdrawal from his worst\r\nassociates, such as the state of his health might continue, above all\r\nif Gregorio could be dispensed with.  The man himself had become aware\r\nof the combination against him, and, though reckoning on his master\'s\r\ninertness and dependence upon him, knew that a fresh offence might\r\ncomplete his overthrow, and therefore took care to be on his good\r\nbehaviour.\r\n\r\nThus Nuttie\'s task might be somewhat smoothed; but the poor girl felt\r\nunspeakably desolate as she ate her breakfast all alone with a dull\r\npost-bag, and still more so when, having seen the housekeeper, who,\r\nhappily for her, was a good and capable woman, and very sorry for her,\r\nshe had to bethink herself what to do in that dreary sitting-room\r\nduring the hour when she had always been most sure of her\r\nsister-mother\'s dear company.  How often she had grumbled at being\r\ncalled on to practise duets for her father\'s evening lullaby!  She\r\nsupposed she ought to get something up, and she proceeded to turn over\r\nand arrange the music with a sort of sick loathing for whatever was\r\nconnected with those days of impatient murmurs, which she would so\r\ngladly have recalled.  Everything had fallen into disorder, as Blanche\r\nand May had left it the last time they had played there; and the\r\noverlooking it, and putting aside the pieces which she could never use\r\nalone, occupied her till Gregorio, very meek and polite, came with a\r\nmessage that Mr. Egremont would be glad if she would come to his room.\r\nIn some dread, some distaste, and yet some pity and some honest\r\nresolution, she made her way thither.\r\n\r\nThere he sat, in dressing-gown, smoking-cap, and blue spectacles, with\r\nthe glittering February sunshine carefully excluded.  He looked worse\r\nand more haggard than when she had seen him at dinner in the evening,\r\nmade up for company, and her compassion increased, especially as he not\r\nonly held out his hand, but seemed to expect her to kiss him, a thing\r\nshe had never done since their first recognition.  It was not pleasant\r\nin itself, but it betokened full forgiveness, and indeed he had never\r\nspoken to her in his sneering, exasperating voice since her mournful\r\nreturn home.\r\n\r\n\'Have you seen the boy?\' he asked.\r\n\r\n\'Yes; they are walking him up and down under the south wall,\' said\r\nNuttie, thankful that she had peeped under the many wraps as he was\r\ncarried across the hall.\r\n\r\n\'Here!  I want you to read this letter to me.  A man ought to be\r\nindicted for writing such a hand!\'\r\n\r\nIt was really distinct penmanship, though minute; but, as Nuttie found,\r\nher father did not like to avow how little available were his eyes.  He\r\ncould write better than he could read, but he kept her over his\r\ncorrespondence for the rest of the morning, answering some of the\r\nletters of condolence for him in her own name, writing those of\r\nbusiness, and folding and addressing what he himself contrived to\r\nwrite.  Her native quickness stood her in good stead, and, being rather\r\nnervous, she took great pains, and seldom stumbled; indeed, she only\r\nonce incurred an exclamation of impatience at her stupidity or slowness.\r\n\r\nShe guessed rightly that this forbearance was owing to tender\r\npersuasions of her mother, and did not guess that a certain fear of\r\nherself was mingled with other motives.  Her father had grown used to\r\nwoman\'s ministrations; he needed them for his precious little heir, and\r\nhe knew his daughter moreover for a severe judge, and did not want to\r\nalienate her and lose her services; so they got on fairly well\r\ntogether, and she shared his luncheon, during which a message came up\r\nabout the carriage; and as there had been an application for some\r\nnursery needment, and moreover black-edged envelopes had run short,\r\nthere was just purpose enough for a drive to the little town.\r\n\r\nThen Nuttie read her father to sleep with the newspaper; rushed round\r\nthe garden in the twilight to stretch her young limbs; tried to read a\r\nlittle, dressed, dined with her father; finished what he had missed in\r\nthe paper, then offered him music, and was told \'if she pleased,\' and\r\nas she played she mused whether this was to be her life.  It looked\r\nvery dull and desolate, and what was the good of it all?  But there\r\nwere her mother\'s words, \'Love him!\'  How fulfil them?  She could pity\r\nhim now, but oh! how could she love one from whom her whole nature\r\nrecoiled, when she thought of her mother\'s ruined life? Mr. Dutton too\r\nhad held her new duties up to her as capable of being ennobled.  Noble!\r\nTo read aloud a sporting paper she did not want to understand, to be\r\nready to play at cards or billiards, to take that dawdling drive day by\r\nday, to devote herself to the selfish exactions of burnt-out\r\ndissipation.  Was this noble?  Her mother had done all this, and never\r\neven felt it a cross, because of her great love.  It must be Nuttie\'s\r\ncross if it was her duty; but could the love and honour possibly come\r\nthough she tried to pray in faith?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV.\r\n\r\nTHE GIGGLING SCOTCH GIRL.\r\n\r\n  \'For every Lamp that trembled here,\r\n  And faded in the night,\r\n  Behold a Star serene and clear\r\n  Smiles on me from the height.\'--B. M.\r\n\r\n\r\nNuttie was not mistaken in supposing that this first day would be a\r\nfair sample of her life, though, of course, after the first weeks of\r\nmourning there were variations; and the return of the Rectory party\r\nmade a good deal of brightening, and relieved her from the necessity of\r\nfinding companionship and conversation for her father on more than half\r\nher afternoons and evenings.\r\n\r\nHe required her, however, almost every forenoon, and depended on her\r\nincreasingly, so that all her arrangements had to be made with\r\nreference to him.  It was bondage, but not as galling in the fact as\r\nshe would have expected if it had been predicted to her a few months\r\npreviously.  In the first place, Mr. Egremont never demanded of her\r\nwhat was actually against her conscience, except occasionally giving up\r\na Sunday evensong to read the paper to him, and that only when he was\r\nmore unwell than usual.  He was, after all, an English gentleman, and\r\ndid not ask his young daughter to read to him the books which her\r\nmother had loathed.  Moreover, Gregorio was on his good behaviour,\r\nperfectly aware that there was a family combination against him, and\r\nhaving even received a sort of warning from his master, but by no means\r\nintending to take it, and therefore abstaining from any kind of offence\r\nthat could furnish a fresh handle against him; and thus for the\r\npresent, Dr. Hammond\'s regimen was well observed, and Mr. Egremont was\r\nhis better self in consequence, for, under his wife\'s guardianship, the\r\nperilous habit had sufficiently lost strength to prevent temper and\r\nspirits from manifestly suffering from abstinence.\r\n\r\nThe first time Nuttie found herself obliged to make any very real\r\nsacrifice to her father\'s will was on the occasion of Mark\'s marriage\r\nat Easter.  Things had arranged themselves very conveniently for him at\r\nMicklethwayte, though it seemed to Nuttie that she only heard of\r\naffairs there in a sort of distant dream, while such events were taking\r\nplace as once would have been to her the greatest possible revolutions.\r\n\r\nAunt Ursel reached home safely, but her expectations of illness were\r\nrealised.  She took to her bed on arriving, and though she rose from\r\nit, there was reason to think she had had a slight stroke, for her\r\nactivity of mind and body were greatly decayed, and she was wholly\r\ndependent on Mary Nugent for care and comfort.  Mary, remembering the\r\nconsequences of the former alarm, made the best of the old lady\'s\r\ncondition; and Nuttie, ashamed of having once cried \'wolf,\' did not\r\nrealise the true state of the case, nor indeed could she or would she\r\nhave been spared to go to Micklethwayte.\r\n\r\nThe next news told that Gerard Godfrey, at the end of the year required\r\nby Mr. Dutton, had resigned his situation, and at the close of his\r\nquarter\'s notice was going to prepare for Holy Orders under the\r\ntraining of a clergyman who would employ him in his parish, and assist\r\nhim in reading up to the requirements for admission to a theological\r\ncollege.  Poor dear old Gerard!  It gave Nuttie a sort of pang of\r\nself-reproach to own how good and devoted he was, and yet so narrow and\r\nstupid that she could never have been happy with him.  Was he too good,\r\nor was he too dull for her?  Had she forsaken him for the world\'s sake,\r\nor was it a sound instinct that had extinguished her fancy for him?  No\r\none could tell, least of all the parties concerned.  He might be far\r\nabove her in spiritual matters, but he was below her in intellectual\r\nones, and though they would always feel for one another that peculiar\r\ntenderness left by the possibilities of a first love, no doubt the\r\nquarrel over the blue ribbon had been no real misfortune to either.\r\n\r\nThe next tidings were still more surprising.  Mr. Dutton was leaving\r\nthe firm.  Though his father had died insolvent, and he had had to\r\nstruggle for himself in early life, he was connected with wealthy\r\npeople, and change and death among these had brought him a fair share\r\nof riches.  An uncle who had emigrated to Australia at the time of the\r\ngreat break up had died without other heirs, leaving him what was the\r\nmore welcome to him that Micklethwayte could never be to him what it\r\nhad been in its golden age.  He had realised enough to enable him to be\r\nbountiful, and his parting gift to St Ambrose\'s would complete the\r\nchurch; but he himself was winding up the partnership, and withdrawing\r\nhis means from Greenleaf and Co. in order to go out to Australia to\r\ndecide what to do with his new possessions.\r\n\r\nMark Egremont purchased a number of the shares, though, to gratify the\r\nfamily, the shelter of the Greenleaf veiled his name under the \'Co.,\'\r\nand another, already in the firm, possessed of a business-like\r\nappellation, gave designation to the firm as Greenleaf, Goodenough, and\r\nCo.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton\'s well-kept house, with the little conservatory and the\r\nmagnolia, was judged sufficient for present needs, and the lease was\r\ntaken off his hands, so that all was in order for the marriage of Mark\r\nand Annaple immediately after Easter.\r\n\r\nLady Delmar had resigned herself to the inevitable, and the wedding was\r\nto take place at Lescombe.  Nuttie, whose chief relaxation was in\r\nhearing all the pros and cons from May and Blanche, was asked to be one\r\nof the bridesmaids by Annaple, who had come over to the Rectory in a\r\ndroll inscrutable state of mischief, declaring that she had exasperated\r\nJanet to the verge of insanity by declaring that she should have little\r\numbrellas like those in the Persian inscriptions on her cards, and that\r\nMark was to present all the bridesmaids with neat parasols.  If\r\ncrinolines had not been gone out they could have all been dressed\r\nappropriately.  Now they must wear them closely furled.  All this\r\nbanter was hardly liked by May and Blanche, whose little sisters were\r\nlaughed at again for needing the assurance that they were really to\r\nwear white and rowan leaves and berries--the Ronnisglen badge.  Nuttie,\r\nwho had drawn much nearer to May, refrained from relating this part of\r\nthe story at home, but was much disappointed when, on telling her\r\nfather of the request, she was answered at once:\r\n\r\n\'Hein!  The 24th?  You\'ll be in London, and a very good thing too.\'\r\n\r\n\'Are we to go so soon?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes.  Didn\'t I tell you to take that house in Berkshire Road from the\r\n20th?\'\r\n\r\n\'I did not think we were to start so soon.  Is there any particular\r\nreason?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes.  That Scotch girl ought to have known better than to ask you in\r\nyour deep mourning.  I thought women made a great point of such things.\'\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Jane did not seem to think it wrong,\' said Nuttie, for she really\r\nwished much for consent.  Not only had she grown fond both of Mark and\r\nAnnaple, but she had never been a bridesmaid, and she knew that not\r\nonly the Kirkaldys but Mr. Dutton had been invited; she had even\r\nventured on offering to lodge some of the overflowing guests of the\r\nRectory.\r\n\r\n\'Their heads are all turned by that poverty-stricken Scotch peerage,\'\r\nreturned Mr. Egremont; \'or the Canoness should have more sense of\r\nrespect.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie\'s wishes were so strong that she made one more attempt, \'I need\r\nnot be a bridesmaid.  They would not mind if I wore my black.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should, then!\' said her father curtly.  \'If they don\'t understand\r\nthe proprieties of life, I do.  I won\'t have you have anything to do\r\nwith it.  If you are so set upon gaiety, you\'ll have enough of weddings\r\nat fitter times!\'\r\n\r\nIt was the old sneering tone.  Nuttie felt partly confounded, partly\r\nindignant, and terribly disappointed.  She did care for the sight of\r\nthe wedding--her youthful spirits had rallied enough for that, but far\r\nmore now she grieved at missing the sight of Mr. Dutton, when he was\r\ngoing away, she knew not where, and might perhaps come on purpose to\r\nsee her; and it also made her sore and grieved at being accused of\r\ndisregard to her mother.  She was silenced, however, and presently her\r\nfather observed, in the same unpleasant tone, \'Well, if you\'ve digested\r\nyour disappointment, perhaps you\'ll condescend to write to the agent,\r\nthat I expect the house to be ready on the 21st.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie got through her morning\'s work she hardly knew how, though her\r\nfather was dry and fault-finding all the time.  Her eyes were so full\r\nof tears when she was released that she hardly saw where she was going,\r\nand nearly ran against her aunt, who had just walked into the hall.\r\nMrs. Egremont was too prudent a woman to let her burst out there with\r\nher grievance, but made her come into the tent-room before she\r\nexclaimed, \'He is going to take me away to London; he won\'t let me go\r\nto the wedding.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am sorry for your disappointment,\' said her aunt quietly, \'but I am\r\nold-fashioned enough to be glad that such strong respect and feeling\r\nshould be shown for your dear mother.  I wish Annaple had spoken to me\r\nbefore asking you, and I would have felt the way.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sure it is not want of feeling,\' said Nuttie, as her tears broke\r\nforth.\r\n\r\n\'I did not say it was,\' returned her aunt, \'but different generations\r\nhave different notions of the mode of showing it; and the present\r\ncertainly errs on the side of neglect of such tokens of mourning.  If I\r\ndid not think that Annaple and her mother are really uncomfortable at\r\nLescombe, I should have told Mark that it was better taste to wait till\r\nthe summer.\'\r\n\r\n\'If I might only have stayed at home--even if I did not go to the\r\nwedding,\' sighed Nuttie, who had only half listened to the Canoness\'s\r\nwisdom.\r\n\r\n\'Since you do not go, it is much better that you should be out of the\r\nway,\' said Mrs. Egremont.  \'Is your father ready to see me?\'\r\n\r\nSo Nuttie had to submit, though she pouted to herself, feeling\r\ngrievously misjudged, first as if she had been wanting in regard to the\r\nmemory of her mother, who had been so fond of Mark, and so rejoiced in\r\nhis happiness; and then that her vexation was treated as mere love of\r\ngaiety, whereas it really was disappointment at not seeing Mr. Dutton,\r\nthat good, grave, precise old friend, who could not be named in the\r\nsame breath with vanity.  Moreover, she could not help suspecting that\r\nrespect to her mother was after all only a cloak to resentment against\r\nMark and his marriage.\r\n\r\nHowever, she bethought herself that her mother had often been\r\ndisappointed and had borne it cheerfully, and after having done what\r\nAunt Ursel would have called \'grizzling\' in her room for an hour, she\r\nwrote her note to Miss Ruthven and endeavoured to be as usual, feeling\r\nkeenly that there was no mother now to perceive and gratefully commend\r\none of her only too rare efforts for good humour. On other grounds she\r\nwas very sorry to leave Bridgefield.  May had, in her trouble, thawed\r\nto her, and they were becoming really affectionate and intimate\r\ncompanions, by force of propinquity and relationship, as well as of the\r\nviews that May had imbibed from Hugh Condamine.  Moreover Nuttie felt\r\nher aunt\'s watch over the baby a great assistance to her own ignorance.\r\n\r\nHowever the Canoness had resigned to the poor little heir the perfect\r\nand trustworthy nurse, whom Basil had outgrown, and who consented to\r\nthe transfer on condition of having her nursery establishment entirely\r\napart from the rest of the household.  Her reasons were known though\r\nunspoken, namely, that the rejection of one or two valets highly\r\nrecommended had made it plain that there had been no dislodgment of\r\nGregorio.  The strong silent objection to him of all good female\r\nservants was one of the points that told much against him.  Martin and\r\nthe housekeeper just endured him, and stayed on for the present chiefly\r\nbecause their dear lady had actually begged them not to desert her\r\ndaughter if they could help it, at least not at first.\r\n\r\nNuttie bound over her cousins to give her a full account of the\r\nwedding, and both of them wrote to her.  Blanche\'s letter recorded\r\nsundry scattered particulars,--as to how well the rowan-trimmed tulle\r\ndresses looked--how every one was packed into the carriages for the\r\nlong drive--how there had been a triumphal arch erected over the\r\nBluepost Bridge itself, and Annaple nearly choked with laughing at the\r\nappropriateness--how, to her delight, a shower began, and the\r\nprocession out of the church actually cried out for umbrellas--how\r\npapa, when performing the ceremony, could not recollect that the\r\nbride\'s proper name was Annabella, and would dictate it as Anna-Maria,\r\nSir John correcting him each time sotto voce--how Basil and little\r\nHilda Delmar walked together and \'looked like a couple of ducks,\'\r\nwhich, it was to be hoped, was to be taken metaphorically--how\r\ndreadfully hard the ice on the wedding-cake was, so that when Annaple\r\ntried to cut it the knife slipped and a little white dove flew away and\r\nhit May, which everyone said was a grand omen that she would be the\r\nnext bride, while of course Annaple was perfectly helpless with mirth.\r\nEvery one said it was the merriest wedding ever seen, for the bride\'s\r\nonly tears were those of laughter.  What Nuttie really cared for most\r\ncame just at the end, and not much of that. \'Your Mr. Dutton is just\r\ngone.  He got on famously with Hugh Condamine, and I forgot to tell you\r\nthat he has given Mark such a jolly present, a lovely silver\r\ncoffee-pot, just the one thing they wanted, and Lady Delmar said he\r\ndidn\'t look near so like a tradesman as she expected.  I see May is\r\nwriting too, but I don\'t know what you will get out of her, as Hugh\r\nCondamine came for the day.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie, however, had more hopes from May.  Her letter certainly was\r\nfuller of interest, if shorter.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'My Dear Nuttie--Blanche has no doubt told you all the externals.  I\r\nsuppose there never was a brighter wedding, for as Annaple keeps her\r\nmother with her, there was no real rending asunder of ties.  Indeed I\r\nalmost wish her excitement did not always show itself in laughing, for\r\nit prevents people from understanding how much there is in her.\r\n\r\n\r\n(Plainly Hugh Condamine had been rather scandalised by the \'giggling\r\nScotch girl.\')\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Dear old Lady Ronnisglen was delightful.  If there were any tears,\r\nthey were hers, and Lady Delmar was very cordial and affectionate. Of\r\ncourse Hugh and Mr. Dutton missed much that one would have liked in a\r\nwedding.  I drove back with them afterwards, and it was very\r\ninteresting to listen to their conversation about church matters. Hugh\r\nis very much struck with your friend; he had heard a good deal about\r\nMicklethwayte before, and says that such a lay worker is perfectly\r\ninvaluable.  It is a great pity that he is not going on in the firm, it\r\nwould make it so much nicer for Mark, but he says he has duties towards\r\nhis new property.  I think he was sorry not to find you at home, but he\r\nplainly never thought it possible you should be at the wedding.  I\r\ndon\'t know whether I ought to tell you this, but I think you ought to\r\nknow it.  There is a lovely new wreath of Eucharis lilies and\r\nmaiden-hair at dear Aunt Alice\'s grave, close against the rails at the\r\nfeet, and Hugh told me that he looked out of his window very early\r\nyesterday morning and saw Mr. Dutton standing there, leaning on the\r\nrail, with his bare head bowed between his hands.  You can\'t think how\r\nit impressed Hugh.  He said he felt reverent towards him all through\r\nthat day, and he was quite angry with Rosalind and Adela for jesting\r\nbecause, when the shower began as we were coming out of church, Mr.\r\nDutton rushed up with an umbrella, being the only person there who had\r\none, I believe.  Hugh says you may be proud of such a friend.  I wish\r\nyou could have seen Hugh.--Your affectionate cousin,\r\n\r\n                                             \'MARGARET EGREMONT.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI.\r\n\r\nTHREE YEARS LATER.\r\n\r\n  \'There\'s something rotten in the State.\'--Hamlet.\r\n\r\n\r\nOn an east-windy afternoon in March Mary Nugent emerged from the School\r\nof Art, her well-worn portfolio under her arm, thinking how many\r\nsuccessive generations of boys and girls she had drilled through\r\n\'free-hand,\' \'perspective,\' and even \'life\' with an unvarying average\r\nof failure and very moderate success, and how little talent or\r\noriginality had come to the front, though all might be the better for\r\nknowing how to use eyes and fingers.\r\n\r\nOn the whole her interest as well as her diligence did not flag; but a\r\nsense of weariness and monotony would sometimes come after a recurrence\r\nof well-known blunders of her pupils, and she missed the sense of going\r\nhome to refreshment and enjoyment which had once invigorated her.  St.\r\nAmbrose\'s Road had had its golden age, but the brightness had been\r\ndimmed ever since that festival at Monks Horton. One after another of\r\nthe happy old society had dropped away.  The vicar had received\r\npromotion, and she only remained of the former intimates, excepting old\r\nMiss Headworth, who was no longer a companion, but whom affection\r\nforbade her to desert in feeble old age.  Had her thoughts of the old\r\ntimes conjured up a figure belonging to them?  There was the\r\nwell-brushed hat, the natty silk umbrella, the perfect fit of garments,\r\nthe precise turn-out, nay, the curly lion-shaven poodle, with all his\r\nfringes, leaping on her in recognition, and there was that slightly\r\nFrench flourish of the hat, before--with a bounding heart--she met the\r\nhand in an English grasp.\r\n\r\n\'Miss Nugent!\'\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton!\'\r\n\r\n\'I thought I should meet you here!\'\r\n\r\n\'When did you come?\'\r\n\r\n\'Half an hour ago.  I came down with George Greenleaf, left my things\r\nat the Royal Hotel, and came on to look for you.\'\r\n\r\n\'You will come and spend the evening with us?\'\r\n\r\n\'If you are so good as to ask me.  How is Miss Headworth?\'\r\n\r\n\'Very feeble, very deaf; but she will be delighted to see you.  There\r\nis no fear of her not remembering you, though she was quite lost when\r\nMrs. Egremont came in yesterday.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Egremont!\' he repeated with a little start.\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Mark.  Ah! we have got used to the name--the Honourable Mrs.\r\nEgremont, as the community insist on calling her.  What a sunny\r\ncreature she is!\'\r\n\r\n\'And Miss Egremont, what do you hear of her?\'\r\n\r\n\'She writes long letters, poor child.  I hope she is fairly happy. Are\r\nyou come home for good, or is this only a visit?\'\r\n\r\n\'I have no intention of returning.  I have been winding up my good\r\ncousin\'s affairs at Melbourne.\'\r\n\r\nMary\'s heart bounded again with a sense of joy, comfort, and\r\nprotection; but she did not long keep Mr. Dutton to herself, for every\r\nthird person they met gladly greeted him, and they were long in getting\r\nto St. Ambrose\'s Road, now dominated by a tall and beautiful spire,\r\naccording to the original design.  They turned and looked in at the\r\npillared aisles, stained glass, and handsome reredos.\r\n\r\n\'Very different from our struggling days,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Mary, with half a sigh.  \'There\'s the new vicar,\' as he\r\npassed with a civil nod.  \'He has three curates, and a house of\r\nSisters, and works the parish excellently.\'\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t speak as if you were intimate.\'\r\n\r\n\'No.  His womankind are rather grand--quite out of our beat; and in\r\nparish work I am only an estimable excrescence.  It is very well that I\r\nam not wanted, for Miss Headworth requires a good deal of attention,\r\nand it is only the old Adam that regrets the days of importance.  Ah,\r\ndo you see?\'\r\n\r\nThey were passing Mr. Dutton\'s old home.  On the tiny strip of lawn in\r\nfront was a slender black figure, with yellow hair, under a tiny black\r\nhat, dragging about a wooden horse whereon was mounted a sturdy boy of\r\ntwo, also yellow-locked and in deep mourning under his Holland blouse.\r\n\r\n\'Billy-boy is riding to meet his daddy!\' was merrily called out both by\r\nmother and son before they perceived the stranger.\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton,\' said Mary.\r\n\r\nAnnaple bowed, but did not put out her hand, and such a flush was on\r\nher face that Miss Nugent said, \'I am sure that is too much for you!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh no--\' she began; but \'Allow me,\' said Mr. Dutton, and before she\r\ncould refuse he was galloping round and round the little lawn, the boy\r\nscreaming with delight as Monsieur raced with them.\r\n\r\n\'So he is come!\' she said in a low doubtful voice to Mary.\r\n\r\n\'Yes.  He has met Mr. Greenleaf in London.  I always think he has the\r\ncontrary to the evil eye.  Whatever he takes in hand rights itself.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'ll hope so.  Oh, thank you!  Billy-boy, say thank you!  What a ride\r\nyou have had!\'\r\n\r\n\'Why are they in such deep mourning?\' asked Mr. Dutton, after they had\r\nparted.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, did you not know? for good old Lady Ronnisglen.  She had a bad\r\nfall about two years ago, and never left her bed again; and this last\r\nautumn she sank away.\'\r\n\r\n\'They have had a great deal of trouble, then.  I saw the death of Canon\r\nEgremont in the Times soon after I went out to Australia.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; he had heart disease, and died quite suddenly.  The living is\r\ngiven to Mr. Condamine, who married the eldest daughter, and the widow\r\nis gone to live under the shadow of Redcastle Cathedral.\'\r\n\r\nTherewith Miss Nugent opened her own door, and Miss Headworth was soon\r\nmade aware of the visitor.  She was greatly changed, and had the\r\nindescribable stony look that tells of paralysis; and though she knew\r\nMr. Dutton, and was delighted to see him, his presence made her expect\r\nto see Alice and Nuttie come in, though she soon recollected herself\r\nand shed a few helpless tears.  Then--in another mood--she began to\r\ndisplay with pride and pleasure the photographs of \'Alice\'s dear little\r\nboy.\'  She had a whole series of them, from the long-clothed babe on\r\nhis sister\'s knee to the bright little fellow holding a drum--a very\r\nbeautiful child, with a striking resemblance to his mother, quite\r\nstartling to Mr. Dutton, especially in the last, which was coloured,\r\nand showed the likeness of eyes and expression.\r\n\r\n\'Nuttie always sends me one whenever he is taken,\' said the old lady.\r\n\'Dear Nuttie!  It is very good for her.  She is quite a little mother\r\nto him.\'\r\n\r\n\'I was sure it would be so,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Mary, \'he is the great interest and delight of her life.\r\nHer letters are full of his little sayings and doings.\'\r\n\r\n\'Is she at home now?\'\r\n\r\n\'No; at Brighton.  Her father seems to have taken a dislike to\r\nBridgefield since his brother\'s death, and only goes there for a short\r\ntime in the shooting season.  He has taken a lease of a house in\r\nLondon, and spends most of the year there.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!\' as she showed him the address, \'that is near the old house where\r\nI used to stay with my grand-aunt.  We thought it altogether in the\r\ncountry then, but it is quite absorbed now, and I have dazzling offers\r\nfrom building companies for the few acres of ground around it. Have you\r\nseen her?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh no; I believe she is quite necessary to her father.  I only hear of\r\nher through Lady Kirkaldy, who has been very kind to her, but, I am\r\nsorry to say, is now gone with her Lord to the East.  She says she\r\nthinks that responsibility has been very good for Nuttie; she is\r\ngentler and less impetuous, and a good deal softened by her affection\r\nfor the child.\'\r\n\r\n\'She was certain to develop.  I only dreaded what society her father\r\nmight surround her with.\'\r\n\r\n\'Lady Kirkaldy says that all has turned out better than could have been\r\nexpected.  You see, as she says, Mr. Egremont has been used to good\r\nwomen in his own family, and would not like to see her in a slangy fast\r\nset.  All her own gaieties have been under Lady Kirkaldy\'s wing, or\r\nthat of Mrs. William Egremont\'s relations, and only in a quiet moderate\r\nway.  Her father gets his own old set about him, and they have not been\r\nvery choice, but they are mostly elderly men, and gentlemen, and know\r\nhow to behave themselves to her. Indeed, her cousin Blanche, who was\r\nhere in the winter, gave us to understand that Ursula knows how to take\r\ncare of herself, and gets laughed at as rather an old maidish model of\r\npropriety, if you can believe it of your little Nuttie.\'\r\n\r\n\'I could quite believe in her on the defensive, unprotected as she is.\'\r\n\r\n\'What did that young lady--Miss Blanche--tell us about that gentleman,\r\nMary?\' asked Miss Headworth, hearing and uttering what Miss Nugent\r\nhoped had passed unnoticed.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I think that was all gossip!\' returned Mary, \'and so I am sure did\r\nthe Mark Egremonts.  She said there was one of Mr. Egremont\'s friends,\r\nMr. Clarence Fane, I think she called him, rather younger than the\r\nothers, who, she was pleased to say, seemed smitten with Nuttie, but I\r\nhave heard nothing more about it, and Mrs. Mark scouted the idea,\' she\r\nadded in haste, as she saw his expression vary in spite of himself.\r\n\r\n\'Do you see much of your neighbours?\'\r\n\r\n\'We are both too busy to see much of one another, but we have our\r\nlittle talks over the wall.  What a buoyant creature she is.  It seems\r\nas if playfulness was really a sustaining power in her, helping her to\r\nget diversion out of much that others might stumble at.  You know\r\nperhaps that when she arrived the work-people had got up a beautiful\r\nparasol for her, white, with a deep fringe and spray of rowan.  Little\r\nSusie Gunner presented her with it, and she was very gracious and nice\r\nabout it.  But then what must Mr. Goodenough do but dub it the\r\nAnnabella sunshade, and blazon it, considerably vulgarised, in all the\r\nrailway stations, and magazines.\'\r\n\r\n\'I know!  I had the misfortune to see it in the station at Melbourne;\r\nand my mind misgave me from that hour.\'\r\n\r\n\'Her husband was prepared to be very angry, but she fairly laughed him\r\nout of it, made all sorts of fun out of the affair, declared it her\r\nonly opening to fame, and turned it into a regular joke; so that indeed\r\nthe Greenleafs, who were vexed at the matter, and tried to apologise,\r\nwere quite perplexed in their turn, and not at all sure that the whole\r\nconcern was not being turned into ridicule.\'\r\n\r\n\'I wonder it did not make him cut the connection,\' said Mr. Dutton,\r\nmuttering \'I only wish it had.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Greenleaf is very funny about her, \'added Mary, \'proud of the\r\nHonourable Mrs. Egremont, as they insist on calling her, yet not quite\r\npleased that she should be the junior partner\'s wife; and decidedly\r\nresenting her hardly going into society at all, though I really don\'t\r\nsee how she could; for first there was the Canon\'s death, and then just\r\nafter the boy was born came Lady Ronnisglen\'s accident, and for the\r\nnext year and a half there was constant attendance on her.  They fitted\r\nup a room on the ground floor for her, the one opening into your\r\ndrawing-room, and there they used to sit with her.  I used to hear them\r\nreading to her and singing to her, and they were always as merry as\r\npossible, till last autumn, when something brought on erysipelas, and\r\nshe was gone almost before they took alarm.  The good little daughter\r\nwas beaten down then, really ill for a week; but if you can understand\r\nme, the shock seemed to tell on her chiefly bodily, and though she was\r\nhalf broken-hearted when her husband in a great fright brought me up to\r\nsee her, and say whether her sister should be sent for, she still made\r\nfun of him, and described the impossible advice they would bring on\r\nthemselves.  I had to take care of her while he went away to the\r\nfuneral in Scotland, and then I learnt indeed to like her and see how\r\nmuch there is in her besides laughter.\'\r\n\r\n\'Did the old lady leave them anything?\'\r\n\r\n\'I believe she had nothing to leave.  Her jointure was not much, but I\r\nam sure they miss that, for Mrs. Egremont has parted with her nurse,\r\nand has only a little girl in her stead, driving out the perambulator\r\noften herself, to the great scandal of the Greenleafs, though she would\r\nhave one believe it is all for want of occupation.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you think they have taken any alarm?\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s no judging from her joyous surface, but I have thought him\r\nlooking more careworn and anxious than I liked.  Mr. Dutton, don\'t\r\nanswer if I ought not to ask, but is it true that things are going\r\nwrong?  I know you have been seeing Mr. Greenleaf, so perhaps you are\r\nin his confidence and cannot speak.\'\r\n\r\n\'Tell me, what is known or suspected?\'\r\n\r\n\'Just this, that Mr. Goodenough has been the ruin of the concern.  He\r\nhas been quite different ever since his voyage to America.  You were\r\ngone, old Mr. Greenleaf has been past attending to business ever since\r\nhe had that attack, and George Greenleaf has been playing the country\r\nsquire at Horton Bishop, and not looking after the office work, and Mr.\r\nEgremont was inexperienced.  One could see, of course, that the whole\r\ncharacter of the business was changed--much more advertising, much more\r\ncheap and flashy work--to be even with the times, it was said, but the\r\nold superior hands were in despair at the materials supplied to them,\r\nand the scamped work expected.  You should have heard old Thorpe\r\nmourning for you, and moralising over the wickedness of this world.\r\nHis wife told me she really thought he would go melancholy mad if he\r\ndid not leave the factory, and he has done so.  They have saved enough\r\nto set up a nice little shop at Monks Horton.\'\r\n\r\n\'I must go and see them!  Good old Thorpe!  I ought never to have put\r\nthose poor young things into the firm when I ceased to have any control\r\nover it.  I shall never forgive myself--\'\r\n\r\n\'Nothing could seem safer then!  No one could have guessed that young\r\nMr. Greenleaf would be so careless without his father to keep him up to\r\nthe mark, nor that Mr. Goodenough should alter so much.  Is it very\r\nbad?  Is there worse behind?  Speculation, I suppose--\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course.  I do not see to the bottom of it yet; poor George seemed\r\nto reckon on me for an advance, but I am afraid this is more than a\r\nmere temporary depression, such as may be tided over, and that all that\r\ncan be looked to is trying to save honourable names by an utter break\r\nup, which may rid them of that--that--no, I won\'t call him a scoundrel.\r\nI thought highly of him once, and no doubt he never realised what he\r\nwas doing.\'\r\n\r\nBefore the evening was far advanced Mark Egremont knocked at the door,\r\nand courteously asked whether Mr. Dutton could be spared to him for a\r\nlittle while.  Mary Nugent replied that she was just going to help Miss\r\nHeadworth to bed, and that the parlour was at their service for a\r\nprivate interview, but Mark answered, \'My wife is anxious to hear.  She\r\nknows all that I do, and is quite prepared to hear whatever Mr. Dutton\r\nmay not object to saying before her.\'\r\n\r\nSo they bade good-night to Mary, and went on together to the next\r\nhouse, Mr. Dutton saying \'You have much to forgive me, Mr. Egremont; I\r\nfeel as if I had deserted the ship just as I had induced you to embark\r\nin it.\'\r\n\r\n\'You did not guess how ill it would be steered without you,\' returned\r\nMark, with a sigh.  \'Do not fear to speak out before my wife, even if\r\nwe are sinking.  She will hear it bravely, and smile to the last.\'\r\n\r\nThe room which Mr. Dutton entered was not like the cabin of a sinking\r\nship, nor, as in his own time, like the well-ordered apartment of a\r\nbachelor of taste.  Indeed, the house was a great puzzle to Monsieur,\r\nwho entered by invitation, knowing his way perfectly, thinking himself\r\nat home after all his travels, and then missing his own particular mat,\r\nand sniffing round at the furniture.
