It was of the modified aesthetic\r\ndate, but arranged more with a view to comfort than anything else, and\r\nby the light of the shaded lamp and bright fire was pre-eminently\r\nhome-like, with the three chairs placed round the hearth, and\r\nbright-haired Annaple rising up from the lowest with her knitting to\r\ngreet Mr. Dutton, and find a comfortable lair for Monsieur.\r\n\r\n\'Miss Nugent says that you set everything right that you do but look\r\nat, Mr. Dutton,\' she said; \'so we are prepared to receive you as a good\r\ngenius to help us out of our tangle.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton was afraid that the tangle was far past unwinding, and of\r\ncourse the details, so far as yet known, were discussed.  There was, in\r\ntruth, nothing for which Mark could be blamed.  He had diligently\r\nattended to his office-work, which was mere routine, and, conscious of\r\nhis own inexperience, and trusting to the senior partners, he had only\r\nbecome anxious at the end of the year, when he perceived Goodenough\'s\r\navoidance of a settlement of accounts, and detected shuffling.  He had\r\nnot understood enough of the previous business to be aware of the\r\ndeterioration of the manner of dealing with it, though he did think it\r\nscarcely what he expected.  If he had erred, it was in acting too much\r\nas a wheel in the machinery, keeping his thoughts and heart in his own\r\nhappy little home, and not throwing himself into the spirit of the\r\nbusiness, or the ways of those concerned in it, so that he had been in\r\nno degree a controlling power.  He had allowed his quality of gentleman\r\nto keep him an outsider, instead of using it to raise the general level\r\nof the transactions, so that the whole had gone down in the hands of\r\nthe unscrupulous Goodenough.\r\n\r\nAnnaple listened and knitted quietly while the affairs were explained\r\non either hand.  Mark had had one serious talk with George Greenleaf,\r\nand both had had a stormy scene with Goodenough.  Then Mr. Dutton had\r\ntelegraphed his arrival, and Greenleaf had met him in London, with\r\nhopes, bred of long and implicit trust, that his sagacity and perhaps\r\nhis wealth would carry the old house through the crisis.\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Dutton, though reserving his judgment till the books should\r\nhave been thoroughly examined and the liabilities completely\r\nunderstood, was evidently inclined to believe that things had gone too\r\nfar, and that the names of Greenleaf and Egremont could only be\r\npreserved from actual dishonour by going into liquidation, dissolving\r\npartnership, and thus getting quit of Goodenough.\r\n\r\nMark listened resignedly, Annaple with an intelligence that made Mr.\r\nDutton think her the more clearheaded of the two, though still she\r\ncould not refrain from her little jokes.  \'I\'m sure I should not mind\r\nhow liquid we became if we could only run off clear of Goodenough,\' she\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\'You know what it means?\' said her husband.\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, I know what it means.  It is the fine word for being sold up.\r\nWell, Mark, never mind, we are young and strong, and it will not be a\r\nbit the worse for the Billy-boy in the end to begin at the bottom of\r\neverything.\'\r\n\r\n\'I hope--may I ask--is everything embarked in the poor old firm?\' said\r\nMr. Dutton with some hesitation.\r\n\r\n\'All that is mine,\' said Mark, with his elbow on the table and his chin\r\non his hand.\r\n\r\n\'But I\'ve got a hundred a year, charged on poor old Ronnisglen\'s\r\nestate,\' said Annaple.  \'All the others gave theirs up when they\r\nmarried, and I wanted to do so, but my dear mother would not let me;\r\nshe said I had better try how I got on first.  Think of that, Mark, a\r\nhundred a year!  Why, old Gunner or Thorpe would think themselves\r\nrolling in riches if they only heard that they had a hundred a year!\'\r\n\r\n\'You won\'t find it go far!\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I shall, for I shall make you live on porridge, with now and then\r\na sheep\'s head for a treat!  Besides, there will be something to do.\r\nIt will be working up again, you know.  But seriously, Mr. Dutton, I\r\nhave some things here of my dear mother\'s that really belong to\r\nRonnisglen, and I was only keeping till he comes home. Should not they\r\nbe got out of the way?\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, we are not come to that yet!  I hope it may be averted!\'\r\ncried Mark.\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Dutton agreed with the young wife that it would be much better\r\nto send these things away before their going could excite suspicion.\r\nThere was only a tiny silver saucepan, valued as a gift of \'Queen\'\r\nClementina to an ancestress, also a silver teapot and some old point,\r\nand some not very valuable jewellery, all well able to go into a small\r\nbox, which Mr. Dutton undertook to deposit with Lord Ronnisglen\'s\r\nbankers.  He was struck with the scrupulous veracity with which Annaple\r\ndecided between what had become her own property and the heirlooms,\r\nthough what she claimed might probably be sacrificed to the creditors.\r\n\r\nMark could hardly endure to see what made the crisis so terribly real.\r\n\'That I should have brought you to this!\' he said to his wife, when\r\ntheir visitor had at length bidden them good-night.\r\n\r\n\'If we begin at that work,\' said Annaple, \'it was I who brought you! I\r\nhave often thought since it was rather selfish not to have consented to\r\nyour helping poor Ursula with her heavy handful of a father!  It was\r\nall money grubbing and grabbing, you see, and if we had thought more of\r\nour neighbour than ourselves we might have been luxuriating at the Home\r\nFarm, or even if your uncle had quarrelled with you, he would not have\r\ndevoured your substance.  I have thought so often, ever since I began\r\nto see this coming.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear child, you don\'t mean that you have seen this coming!\'\r\n\r\n\'My prophetic soul!  Why, Mark, you have as good as inferred it over\r\nand over again.  I\'ve felt like scratching that Badenough whenever I\r\nmet him in the street.  I must indulge myself by calling him so for\r\nonce in strict privacy.\'\r\n\r\n\'You have guessed it all the time, while I only thought how unconscious\r\nyou were.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not to say stupid, considering all you told me.  Besides, what would\r\nhave been the use of howling and moaning and being dismal before the\r\ntime?  For my part, I could clap my hands even now at getting rid of\r\nGoodenough, and his jaunty, gracious air!  Come, Mark, it won\'t be so\r\nbad after all, you\'ll see.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nothing can be "so bad," while you are what you are, my Nan.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s right.  While we have each other and the Billy-boy, nothing\r\nmatters much.  There\'s plenty of work in us both, and that good man\r\nwill find it for us; or if he doesn\'t, we\'ll get a yellow van, and knit\r\nstockings, and sell them round the country.  How jolly that would be!\r\nImagine Janet\'s face.  There, that\'s right,\' as her mimicry evoked a\r\nsmile, \'I should be ashamed to be unhappy about this, when our good\r\nname is saved, and when there is a blessing on the poor,\' she added in\r\na lower voice, tenderly kissing her husband\'s weary brow.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII.\r\n\r\nTHE BOY OF EGREMONT.\r\n\r\n  \'And the boy that walked beside me,\r\n  He could not understand\r\n  Why, closer in mine, ah, closer,\r\n  I press\'d his warm soft hand.\'--LONGFELLOW.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe agony of a firm like Greenleaf, Goodenough, and Co. could not be a\r\nrapid thing, and Mr. Dutton lived between London and Micklethwayte for\r\nseveral weeks, having much to endure on all sides.  The senior partners\r\nthought it an almost malicious and decidedly ungrateful thing in him\r\nnot to throw in his means, or at any rate, offer his guarantee to tide\r\nthem over their difficulties.  Goodenough\'s tergiversations and\r\nconcealments needed a practised hand and acute head to unravel them,\r\nand often deceived Mr. Greenleaf himself; and when, for a time, he was\r\nconvinced that the whole state was so rotten that a crash was\r\ninevitable, his wife\'s lamentations and complaints of Mr. Dutton would\r\nundo the whole, and it was as if he were doing them an injury that the\r\npair accepted the comfortable prospect he was able to offer them in\r\nAustralia.\r\n\r\nHe would have made the like proposal to the Egremonts, but found that\r\nMark held himself bound by his promise to his father not to emigrate,\r\nand thought of some kind of office-work.  Before trying to procure this\r\nfor him, however, Mr. Dutton intended to see his uncle, and try whether\r\nthe agency, once rejected, could still be obtained for him. Learning\r\nfrom Miss Nugent that the Egremonts were in town, he went up thither\r\nwith the purpose of asking for an interview.\r\n\r\nThere was a new church in the immediate neighbourhood of his house in a\r\nstate of growth and development congenial to the St. Ambrose trained\r\nmind, and here Mr. Dutton, after old Micklethwayte custom, was\r\nattending the early matins, when, in the alternate verses of the psalm,\r\nhe heard a fresh young voice that seemed to renew those days gone by,\r\nand looking across the central aisle his eyes met a pair of dark ones\r\nwhich gave a sudden glitter of gladness at the encounter. That was all\r\nhe saw or cared to see.  He did not take in the finished completeness\r\nof the very plain dark dress and hat, nor the womanly air of the little\r\nfigure, until they clasped hands in the porch, and in the old tones\r\nNuttie exclaimed:  \'I\'ve been hoping you would come to London.  How is\r\nMonsieur?\'\r\n\r\n\'In high health, thank you, the darling of the steamer both going and\r\ncoming.  I hope your charges are well?\'\r\n\r\n\'My father is tolerable, just as usual, and my little Alwyn is getting\r\nmore delicious every day.  He will be so delighted to see Monsieur.  I\r\nhave told him so many stories about him!\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you think I may call on Mr. Egremont?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh do!  He is ready to be called on between two and three, and we\r\nalways have Wynnie downstairs then, so that you will see him too. And\r\nyou have been at Micklethwayte.  I am afraid you found a great change\r\nin Aunt Ursel.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; but she is very peaceful and happy.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I have to leave her altogether to dear excellent Miss Nugent. It\r\nseems very, very wrong, but I cannot help it!  And how about Mark and\r\nAnnaple?\'\r\n\r\n\'I think she is the bravest woman I ever met.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then things are really going badly with the dear old firm?\'\r\n\r\n\'I am hoping to talk to Mr. Egremont about it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!\'\r\n\r\nNuttie paused.  Towards Mr. Dutton she always had a stronger impulse of\r\nconfidence than towards any one else she had ever met; but she felt\r\nthat he might think it unbecoming to say that she had perceived a\r\ncertain dislike on her father\'s part towards Mark ever since the\r\nrejection of the agency and the marriage, which perhaps was regarded as\r\na rejection of herself.  He had a habit of dependence on Mark, which\r\nresulted in personal liking, when in actual contact, but in absence the\r\ndistaste and offence always revived, fostered, no doubt, by Gregorio;\r\nand Canon Egremont\'s death had broken the link which had brought them\r\ntogether.  However, for his brother\'s sake, and for the sake of the\r\nname, the head of the family might be willing to do something.  It was\r\none of Nuttie\'s difficulties that she never could calculate on the way\r\nher father would take any matter.  Whether for better or for worse, he\r\nalways seemed to decide in diametrical opposition to her expectation.\r\nAnd, as she was certainly less impetuous and more dutiful, she parted\r\nwith Mr. Dutton at her own door without any such hint.\r\n\r\nThese three years had been discipline such as the tenderest, wisest\r\nhand could not have given her, though it had been insensible.  She had\r\nbeen obliged to attend to her father and watch over her little brother,\r\nand though neither task had seemed congenial to her disposition, the\r\nhonest endeavour to do them rightly had produced the affection born of\r\nsolicitude towards her father, and the strong warm tenderness of the\r\ntrue mother-sister towards little Alwyn.\r\n\r\nUrsula Egremont was one of those natures to which responsibility is the\r\nbest training.  If she had had any one to guard or restrain her, she\r\nmight have gone to the utmost limits before she yielded to the curb.\r\nAs it was, she had to take care of herself, to bear and forbear with\r\nher father, to walk warily with her household, and to be very guarded\r\nwith the society into which she was thrown from time to time.  It was\r\nno sudden change, but one brought about by experience. An outbreak of\r\nimpatience or temper towards her father was sure to be followed by his\r\ngalling sneer, or by some mortification to her desires; any act of\r\nmismanagement towards the servants brought its own punishment; and if\r\nshe was tempted by girlish spirits to relax the quiet, stiff courtesy\r\nwhich she observed towards her father\'s guests, there followed jests,\r\nor semi-patronage, or a tone of conversation that offended her, and\r\nmade her repent it.  Happily, Mr. Egremont did not wish her to be\r\notherwise.  One day, when she had been betrayed into rattling and\r\ngiggling, he spoke to her afterwards with a cutting irony which\r\nbitterly angered her at the moment, and which she never forgot.  Each\r\nirksome duty, each privation, each disappointment, each recurrence of\r\nthe sweeping sense of desolation and loneliness had had one effect--it\r\nhad sent her to her knees.  She had no one else to go to.  She turned\r\nto her Father in heaven. Sometimes, indeed, it was in murmuring and\r\ncomplaint at her lot, but still it was to Him and Him alone, and\r\nrepentance sooner or later came to aid her, while refreshments sprang\r\nup around her--little successes, small achievements, pleasant hours,\r\ntokens that her father was pleased or satisfied, and above all, the\r\ngrowing charms of little Alwyn.\r\n\r\nThe special grievance, Gregorio\'s influence, had scarcely dwelt on her\r\nat first as it had done on her mother.  The man had been very cautious\r\nfor some time, knowing that his continuance in his situation was in the\r\nutmost jeopardy, and Mr. Egremont had, in the freshness of his grief\r\nfor his wife, abstained from relapsing into the habits from which she\r\nhad weaned him.  When, however, the Canon was dead, and his son at a\r\ndistance, Gregorio began to feel more secure, and in the restless\r\nsorrow of his master over the blow that had taken away an only brother,\r\nhe administered soothing drugs under another name, so that Ursula, in\r\nher inexperience, did not detect what was going on, and still fancied\r\nthat the habit had been renounced.  All she did know was that it was\r\nentirely useless for her to attempt to exert any authority over the\r\nvalet, and that the only way to escape insolently polite disobedience\r\nwas to let him alone.  Moreover, plans to which her father had agreed,\r\nwhen broached by her, had often been overthrown after his valet had\r\nbeen with him.  It was a life full of care and disappointment, yet\r\nthere was a certain spring of trust that kept Ursula\'s youth from being\r\ndimmed, and enabled her to get a fair share of happiness out of it,\r\nthough she was very sorry not to be more at Bridgefield, where she\r\ncould have worked with all her heart with May Condamine.  Moreover,\r\nLady Kirkaldy\'s absence from London was a great loss to her, for there\r\nwas no one who was so kind or so available in taking her into society;\r\nand Nuttie, though mistress of her father\'s house, was not yet\r\ntwenty-two, and strongly felt that she must keep within careful bounds,\r\nand not attempt to be her own chaperon.\r\n\r\nBut the very sight of her old friend, and the knowledge that he was in\r\nthe neighbourhood, filled her heart with gladness, and seemed like a\r\ncompensation for everything.  Mr. Egremont was in a gracious mood, and\r\nreadily consented to see Mr. Dutton--the friend who had been so\r\npleasant and helpful at Paris--and Nuttie gave her private instructions\r\nto the footman to insure his admittance.\r\n\r\nHis card was brought in just as the father and daughter were finishing\r\nluncheon, and he was received in Mr. Egremont\'s sitting-room, where the\r\nfirst civilities had hardly passed before the door was opened, and in\r\ntrotted the golden-haired boy, so beautiful a child that it would have\r\nbeen impossible not to look at him with delight, even for those to whom\r\nhis dark eyes and sweet smile did not recall those that had once been\r\nso dear.\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont\'s voice took a fresh tone: \'Ah! here he comes, the old\r\nfellow\'--and he held out his hands; but the boy was intent on his own\r\npurpose.\r\n\r\n\'Where\'s black doggie?\' he asked in a silver-bell of a little voice,\r\nbut lisping a good deal; \'Wyn got penny for him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Wynnie must be a good boy.  Kiss papa first, and Mr. Dutton,\'\r\nremonstrated the sister; and Alwyn obeyed so far as to submit to his\r\nfather\'s embrace, and then raising those velvety eyes to the visitor\'s\r\nface, he repeated: \'Where black doggie?  Wyn want to see him buy bun.\'\r\n\r\n\'There! your fame has preceded you,\' said Mr. Egremont, \'or rather your\r\ndog\'s.\'\r\n\r\n\'You shall see him,\' said Mr. Dutton, taking the pretty boy almost\r\nreverently on his knee, \'but he is at home now.  I could not leave him\r\nout on the street, and I did not know if I might bring him in.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Mr. Dutton! as if Monsieur would not be welcome,\' cried the Nuttie\r\nof old times.  \'I only wish I had stipulated for him, dear old fellow.\'\r\n\r\n\'Wyn want to see him,\' reiterated the child.\r\n\r\n\'May I take him to see the performance?\' said Mr. Dutton.  \'I live only\r\nat the corner of Berkshire Road, and there\'s a dairy just opposite\r\nwhere Monsieur has been allowed to keep up his accomplishment.\'\r\n\r\nAlwyn\'s legs, arms, and voice, were all excitement and entreaty; and\r\nMr. Egremont himself proposed that they should all come and witness the\r\nfeat; so Nuttie, in great glee, climbed the stairs with her little\r\nbrother to get ready; and when she came down again, found the gentlemen\r\ndeep, not in Mark Egremont\'s umbrellas, but in the gas and smoke\r\ngrievances which had arisen since the lease of the house had been\r\ntaken, and in which sympathy might be expected from a fellow-inhabitant\r\nof the district.  Little Alwyn was, however, plainly the lord of the\r\nascendant, and unused to see anything else attended to in his presence.\r\nHe took possession of Mr. Dutton\'s hand, and his tongue went fast, nor\r\ndid his father or sister seem to desire any better music.  They reached\r\nan old-walled garden, with lilac and laburnum and horse-chestnut\r\nblossoming above, and showing a mass of greenery through the iron\r\nrailing that surmounted, the low wall on the street side, where Mr.\r\nDutton halted and took out his key.\r\n\r\n\'Is this yours?\' exclaimed Nuttie, \'I have so often wondered whose it\r\ncould be.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, it was a country-house when I was of the age of this little man,\r\nthough you might not think it.\'\r\n\r\n\'The increase of London had not been on that side,\' said Mr. Egremont.\r\n\'This must be a very valuable property!\'\r\n\r\nAnd Nuttie perceived that such an inheritance made Mr. Dutton much more\r\nin his eyes than an ex-umbrella-monger; but no sooner was the tall iron\r\ngate opened than Monsieur, beautifully shaved, with all his curly tufts\r\nin perfection, came bounding to meet his master, and Alwyn had his arms\r\nround the neck in a moment.  Monsieur had in his time been introduced\r\nto too many children not to understand the situation, and respond\r\npolitely; and he also recognised Ursula, and gave unmistakable proofs\r\nof being glad to see her.\r\n\r\nThen the halfpenny was presented to him.  He wagged his queer tail,\r\nsmiled with his intelligent brown eyes, took it between his teeth, and\r\ntrotted across the street in the most business-like way, the others\r\nfollowing, but detaining the boy from keeping too close. They found the\r\ncreature sitting upright, tapping the floor with his tail, the centre\r\nof rapturous admiration to all the customers already in the dairy shop.\r\nHe received his bun, and demurely dropping on his front legs, walked\r\nback with it to his master, and crossed the road with it uneaten,\r\nrather to Alwyn\'s disappointment, but Mr. Dutton said he would probably\r\ndispose of it in some hiding-place in the garden until his evening\r\nappetite came on.  It was well he was a dog of moderation, for there\r\nwas great temptation to repeat the entertainment more than was\r\nwholesome for him.\r\n\r\n\'There, Wynnie,\' said Nuttie in a voice of monition, \'Monsieur doesn\'t\r\neat all his goodies at once, he keeps them for bedtime.\'\r\n\r\nIt might be perceived that the over-supply of sweets was a matter of\r\nanxiety to the elder sister.  To the nurse, who waited in readiness,\r\nAlwyn was consigned for his walk, while his father and sister accepted\r\nMr. Dutton\'s invitation to look round his domain.  It would have been\r\nsmall in the country, but it was extensive for the locality, and there\r\nwas a perfect order and trimness about the shaven lawn, the little\r\nfountain in the midst, the flower-beds gay with pansies,\r\nforget-me-nots, and other early beauties, and the freshly-rolled gravel\r\npaths, that made Nuttie exclaim: \'Ah! I should have known this for\r\nyours anywhere.\'\r\n\r\n\'I have not had much to do to it,\' he said.  \'My old aunts had it well\r\nkept up, even when they could only see it from their windows. Their old\r\ngardener still lives in the cottage behind the tool-house, though he is\r\ntoo infirm for anything but being wheeled about in the sun in their\r\nBath-chair.\'\r\n\r\n\'You keep a large amount lying idle by retaining it as it is,\' said Mr.\r\nEgremont.\r\n\r\n\'True, but it is well to preserve an oasis here and there.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie knew well that it was not for himself alone, and as they entered\r\nthe little conservatory, and her eye fell on the row of white\r\nhyacinths, the very scent carried her back to the old times, and her\r\neyes grew moist while Mr. Dutton was cutting a bouquet for her in\r\naccordance with well-known tastes.\r\n\r\n\'I shall put them in my room.  It will feel like home,\' she said, and\r\nthen she saw that she had said what her father did not like; for he was\r\nalways sensitive as to any reference to her early life.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton, however, took this opportunity of saying that he had been\r\nbackwards and forwards to Micklethwayte several times this spring.\r\n\r\n\'I hope you are well out of the concern there,\' said Mr. Egremont.\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, sir; I have no share in it at present.\'\r\n\r\n\'So much the better!\'\r\n\r\n\'But I am very anxious about my friends.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!\'  But Mr. Egremont\'s attention was drawn off at the entrance of\r\nthe house by a new-fashioned stove of which Mr. Dutton did the honours,\r\nconducting father and daughter into the drawing-room, where obvious\r\ntraces of the old ladies remained, and thence into his own\r\nsitting-room, smelling pleasantly of Russia leather, and recalling that\r\ninto which Nuttie had been wont, before her schooldays, to climb by the\r\nwindow, and become entranced by the illustrations of a wonderful old\r\nedition of Telemaque, picked up at Paris.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton made them sit and rest, for this had been a good deal of\r\nexercise for Mr. Egremont; coffee was brought in, having been ordered\r\non their arrival, and therewith Mr. Dutton entered on an exposition of\r\nthe affairs of Greenleaf and Goodenough, which was listened to with a\r\ngood deal of interest, though Nuttie could not quite detect whether it\r\nwere altogether friendly interests in Mark\'s misfortunes, or if there\r\nwere not a certain triumph in the young man having run into trouble by\r\nrejecting his offer.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton explained that his present object was to induce the friends\r\nof the family to prevent annoyance by preserving the furniture and\r\npersonals at a valuation; and Mr. Egremont readily agreed to contribute\r\nto doing this, though he said the sisters and stepmother were well able\r\nalso to do their share.\r\n\r\n\'And then to give the young people a fresh start,\' added Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'There are some men who are always wanting fresh starts,\' said Mr.\r\nEgremont, \'just as there are some vessels that are always unlucky. And\r\nif you observe, it is just those men who are in the greatest haste to\r\nhang an expensive wife and family round their necks.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t think poor Annaple can be accused of being expensive, papa,\'\r\nsaid Nuttie.  \'Only think, when Wynnie has two nurses always after him,\r\nher Willie has only the fraction of a little maid, who does all sorts\r\nof work besides.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I never saw more resolute and cheerful exertion than Mrs. Mark\r\nEgremont\'s,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'She owes him something,\' said Mr. Egremont, \'for she has been the ruin\r\nof him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Of his worldly prospects in one sense,\' said Mr. Dutton quietly; while\r\nNuttie felt how much better and wiser an answer it was than the\r\nindignant denial that trembled on her tongue.  \'There can be no doubt\r\nthat they made a grievous mistake in their choice, and I unfortunately\r\nwas concerned in leading them into it; but no one can see how they meet\r\ntheir troubles without great respect and admiration, and I am\r\nespecially bound to seek for some new opening for them.  I have little\r\ndoubt that some office work might be found for him in London, but they\r\nare essentially country people, and it would be much better for them if\r\nhe could have some agency.  I suppose the situation you offered him\r\nbefore, sir, is filled up?\'\r\n\r\n\'Not really,\' cried Nuttie.  \'We have only a very common sort of\r\nuneducated bailiff, who would be much better with some one over him.\r\nYou said so, papa.\'\r\n\r\n\'Did he request you to apply to me?\' said Mr. Egremont sharply, looking\r\nat Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Neither he nor she has the least idea of my intention; I only thought,\r\nsir, you might be willing to consider how best to assist a nephew, who\r\nhas certainly not been wanting either in industry or economy, and who\r\nbears your name.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I will think it over,\' said Mr. Egremont, rising to take leave.\r\n\r\nThe carriage had been bidden to await them at the door for their daily\r\ndrive, and as Mr. Egremont leant back with the furs disposed over him\r\nhe observed: \'That\'s a man who knows how to take care of himself.  I\r\nwonder where he gets his coffee, I\'ve not drunk any like it since I was\r\nat Nice.\'  And Nuttie, though well knowing that Mr. Dutton\'s love of\r\nperfection was not self-indulgence, was content to accept this as high\r\napprobation, and a good augury for Mark and Annaple.  Indeed, with Mr.\r\nDutton settled near, and with the prospect of a daily walk from church\r\nwith him, she felt such a complete content and trust as she had not\r\nknown since she had been uprooted from Micklethwayte.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII.\r\n\r\nA BRAVE HEART.\r\n\r\n  \'One furnace many times the good and bad may hold,\r\n  Yet what consumes the chaff will only cleanse the gold.\'\r\n                                     Archbishop TRENCH.\r\n\r\n\r\nNever was there a truer verse than that which tells us that in seeking\r\nduty we find pleasure by the way, and in seeking pleasure we meet pain.\r\nIt might be varied to apply to our anticipations of enjoyment or the\r\nreverse.  Ursula had embraced her lot as a necessity, and found it\r\nenlivened by a good many sunshiny hours; and when she looked upon Mr.\r\nDutton\'s neighbourhood as a continual source of delight and\r\nsatisfaction, she found that it gave rise to a continual course of\r\nsmall disappointments.\r\n\r\nIn the first place, he did not walk home from church with her every\r\nmorning.  She looked for him in vain, even when she knew he was in\r\ntown.  He only appeared there on Sundays, and at intervals when he had\r\nsome especial reason for speaking to her.  At first she thought he must\r\nhave grown lazy or out of health to have thus dropped his old\r\nMicklethwayte habits, but after a time she discovered by accident that\r\nhe frequented another church, open at a still earlier hour and a little\r\nfarther off, and she was forced to come to the conclusion that he acted\r\nout of his characteristic precise scrupulosity, which would not\r\nconsider it as correct for her to walk home every day with him. She\r\nchafed, and derided \'the dear old man\' a little in her own mind, then\r\nended with a sigh.  Was there any one who cared so much about what was\r\nproper for her?  And, after all, was he really older than Mr. Clarence\r\nFane, whom everybody in her father\'s set called Clarence, or even\r\nClare, and treated as the boy of the party, so that she had taken it as\r\nquite natural that he should be paired off with her.  It was quite a\r\ndiscovery!\r\n\r\nThere was another and more serious disappointment.  Mr. Egremont had\r\nnot seemed disinclined to consider the giving the agency to Mark, and\r\nNuttie had begun to think with great satisfaction of May Condamine\'s\r\ndelight in welcoming him, and of the good influence that would be\r\nbrought to bear on the dependents, when suddenly there came a coolness.\r\nShe could trace the moment, and was sure that it was, when Gregorio\r\nbecame aware of what was intended.  He had reason to dread Mark as an\r\nenemy, and was likely to wish to keep him at a distance; and it had\r\nbeen Ursula\'s great hope that an absolute promise might have been given\r\nbefore he heard of the plan; but Mr. Egremont was always slow to make\r\nup his mind, except when driven by a sudden impulse, and had never\r\nactually said that the post should be offered to his nephew.  Nuttie\r\nonly detected the turn of the tide by the want of cordiality, the hums\r\nand haws, and by and by the resumption of the unkind ironical tone when\r\nMark and Annaple were mentioned; and at last, when she had been reading\r\nto him a letter from Mrs. William Egremont full of anxiety for the\r\nyoung people, and yet of trust in his kindness to them, he exclaimed,\r\n\'You\'ve not been writing to her about this absurd proposal?\'\r\n\r\n\'I have not mentioned any proposal at all.  What do you mean?\'\r\n\r\n\'Why, this ridiculous idea about the agency.  As if I was going to put\r\nmy affairs into the hands of a man who has made such a mull of his own.\'\r\n\r\n\'But that was not Mark\'s fault, papa.  He was junior, you know, and had\r\nno power over that Goodenough.\'\r\n\r\n\'He ought, then!  Never sail with an unlucky captain.  No, no, Mark\'s\r\nhonourable lady would not let him take the agency when he might have\r\nhad it, and I am not going to let them live upon me now that they have\r\nnothing of their own.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, papa, but you almost promised!\'\r\n\r\n\'Almost!\' he repeated with his ironical tone; \'that\'s a word capable of\r\na good deal of stretching.  This is what you and that umbrella fellow\r\nhave made out of my not giving him a direct refusal on the spot.  He\r\nmay meddle with Mark\'s affairs if he chooses, but not with mine.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie had learnt a certain amount of wisdom, and knew that to argue a\r\npoint only made her father more determined, so she merely answered,\r\n\'Very well;\' adding in a meek voice, \'Their furniture, poor things!\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh ay.  Their umbrella friend is making a collection for them.  Yes, I\r\nbelieve I said I would contribute.\'\r\n\r\nHot blood surged up within Nuttie at the contemptuous tone, and she bit\r\nher lip to keep down the answer, for she knew Mr. Dutton intended to\r\ncall the next afternoon for her father\'s ultimatum before going down to\r\nMicklethwayte, where the crisis was fast approaching, and she had so\r\nmuch faith in his powers that she dreaded to forestall him by an\r\nimprudent word.  Alas, Gregorio must have been on his guard, for,\r\nthough Nuttie was sure she heard her friend\'s ring at the usual time,\r\nno entrance followed.  She went up to put on her habit to ride with her\r\nfather, and when she came down Mr. Egremont held out a card with the\r\nname \'Philip Dutton,\' and the pencilled request below to be allowed to\r\nsee Mr. Egremont later in the day.\r\n\r\n\'He has been denied!\' exclaimed she in consternation.\r\n\r\n\'Hein!  Before we go out, sit down and write a note for me.\'  And he\r\ndictated--\r\n\r\n     \'Dear Sir--I will not trouble you to call again this\r\n      afternoon, as I have decided on reflection that there\r\n      is no employment on my estate suited to my nephew,\r\n      Mark Egremont.\r\n\r\n     \'As I understand that you are raising a family\r\n      subscription for rescuing his furniture from the\r\n      creditors, I enclose a cheque for \xa350 for the purpose.\r\n\r\n                                           --I remain--\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Yours--what--papa?--\' asked Ursula, with a trembling voice, full of\r\ntears.\r\n\r\n\'Yours, etc., of course.  Quite intimate enough for an\r\nex-umbrella-monger. Here, give it to me, and I\'ll sign it while you\r\nfill up the cheque for me.\'\r\n\r\nThat such should be the first letter that Nuttie ever addressed to Mr.\r\nDutton, since the round-hand one in \'which Miss Ursula wished Mr. Duton\r\nto have the onner of a tee with me on my birthday, and I am your\r\naffected little Nuttie\'!\r\n\r\nShe hoped to explain and lament the next morning, after church.  He\r\nwould surely come to talk it over with her; but he only returned a\r\ncivil note with his receipt, and she did not see him again before his\r\ndeparture.  She was greatly vexed; she had wanted so much to tell him\r\nhow it was, and then came an inward consciousness that she would\r\nprobably have told him a great deal too much.\r\n\r\nWas it that tiresome prudence of his again that would think for her and\r\nprevent impulsive and indignant disclosures?  It made her bring down\r\nher foot sharply on the pavement with vexation as she suspected that he\r\nthought her so foolish, and then again her heart warmed with the\r\nperception of self-denying care for her.  She trusted to that same\r\nprudence for no delusive hopes having been given to Mark and his wife.\r\n\r\nShe did so justly.  Mr. Dutton had thought the matter far too uncertain\r\nto be set before them.  The Canoness\'s vague hopes had been the fruit\r\nof a hint imprudently dropped by Nuttie herself in a letter to Blanche.\r\nShe had said more to Miss Nugent, but Mary was a nonconductor.  Mr.\r\nDutton\'s heart sank as he looked at the houses, and he had some\r\nthoughts of going to her first for intelligence, but Annaple had spied\r\nhim, and ran out to the gate to welcome him.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Mr. Dutton, I\'m so glad!  Mark will be delighted.\'\r\n\r\n\'Is he at home?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh no, at the office, wading through seas of papers with Mr.\r\nGreenleaf, but he will come home to eat in a quarter of an hour.  So\r\ncome in;\' then, as her boy\'s merry voice and a gruffer one were heard,\r\n\'That\'s the bailiff.  He is Willie\'s devoted slave.\'\r\n\r\n\'I hoped to have been in time to have saved you that.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I\'m convinced that among the much maligned races are bailiffs. I\r\nwonder what I could get by an article on prejudice against classes! I\r\nwas thinking how much beer I should have to lay in for this one, and\r\nbehold he is a teetotaller, and besides that amateur nurse-maid,\r\nparlour-maid, kitchen-maid, etc. etc.--\'\r\n\r\n\'What bailiff could withstand Mrs. Egremont?  Perhaps you have tamed\r\nhim?\'\r\n\r\n\'Not I.  The cook did that.  Indeed I believe there\'s a nice little\r\nidyll going on in the kitchen, and besides he wore the blue ribbon, and\r\nwas already a devoted follower of young Mr. Godfrey!\'\r\n\r\n\'However, if the valuation is ready, I hope you may be relieved from\r\nhim, if you won\'t be too much concerned at the parting!\'\r\n\r\n\'Mrs. Egremont told us that our people are very good to us,\' said\r\nAnnaple, \'and don\'t mean to send us out with nothing but a pack at our\r\nbacks.  It is very kind in them and in you, Mr. Dutton, to take the\r\ntrouble of it!  No, I\'ll not worry you with thanks.  The great point\r\nis, hope for something for Mark to do.  That will keep up his spirits\r\nbest!  Poor Mr. Greenleaf is so melancholy that it is all I can do to\r\nkeep him up to the mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'I have been making inquiries, and I have three possible openings, but\r\nI hardly like to lay them before you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, we are not particular about gentility!  It is work we want, and if\r\nit was anything where I could help that would be all the better! I\'m\r\nsure I only wonder there are so many as three.  I think it is\r\nsomebody\'s doing.  Ah! there\'s Mark,\' and she flew out to meet him.\r\n\'Mark!\' she said, on the little path, \'here\'s the good genius, with\r\nthree chances in his pocket.  Keep him to luncheon.  I\'ve got plenty.\r\nPoor old man, how hot you look!  Go and cool in the drawing-room, while\r\nI wash my son\'s face.\'\r\n\r\nAnd she disappeared into the back regions, while Mark, the smile she\r\nhad called up vanishing from his face, came into the drawing-room, and\r\nheld out a cordial, thankful hand to his friend, whose chief\r\nintelligence was soon communicated.  \'Yes,\' said Mark, when he heard\r\nthe amount entrusted by the family to Mr. Dutton, \'that will save all\r\nmy wife\'s poor little household gods.  Not that I should call them so,\r\nfor I am sure she does not worship them.  I don\'t know what would\r\nbecome of me if she were like poor Mrs. Greenleaf, who went into\r\nhysterics when the bailiff arrived, and has kept her room ever since. I\r\nsometimes feel as if nothing could hurt us while Annaple remains what\r\nshe is.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton did not wonder that he said so, when she came in leading her\r\nlittle son, with his sunny hair newly brushed and shining, and carrying\r\na little bouquet for the guest of one La Marque rosebud and three\r\nlilies of the valley.\r\n\r\n\'Take it to Mr. Dutton, Billy-boy; I think he knows how the flowers\r\ncame into the garden.  You shall have daddy\'s button-hole to take to\r\nhim next.  There, Mark, it is a pansy of most smiling countenance, such\r\nas should beam on you through your accounts.  I declare, there\'s that\r\nparagon of a Mr. Jones helping Bessy to bring in dinner!  Isn\'t it very\r\nkind to provide a man-servant for us?\'\r\n\r\nIt might be rattle, and it might be inconsequent, but it was much\r\npleasanter than hysterics.  Billy-boy was small enough to require a\r\ngood deal of attention at dinner, especially as he was more disposed to\r\nopen big blue eyes at the stranger, than to make use of his spoon, and\r\nAnnaple seemed chiefly engrossed with him, though a quick keen word at\r\nthe right moment showed that she was aware of all that was going on, as\r\nMark and Mr. Dutton discussed the present situation and future measures.\r\n\r\nIt was quite true that a man concerned in a failure was in great danger\r\nof being left out of the race for employment, and Mr. Dutton did not\r\nthink it needful to mention the force of the arguments he was using to\r\nback his recommendation of Mark Egremont.  The possibilities he had\r\nheard of were a clerkship at a shipping agent\'s, another at a warehouse\r\nin their own line, and a desk at an insurance office.  This sounded\r\nbest, but had the smallest salary to begin with, and locality had to be\r\ntaken into account.  Mr. Dutton\'s plan was, that as soon as Mark was no\r\nlonger necessary for what Annaple was pleased to call the fall of the\r\nsere and withered leaf, the pair should come to stay with him, so that\r\nMark could see his possible employers, and Annaple consider of the\r\nsituations.  They accepted this gratefully, Mark only proposing that\r\nshe should go either to his stepmother or her own relations to avoid\r\nthe final crisis.\r\n\r\n\'As if I would!\' she exclaimed.  \'What sort of a little recreant goose\r\ndo you take me for?\'\r\n\r\n\'I take you for a gallant little woman, ready to stand in the breach,\'\r\nsaid Mark.\r\n\r\n\'Ah, don\'t flatter yourself!  There is a thing I have not got courage\r\nto face--without necessity, and that\'s Janet\'s triumphant pity.  Mr.\r\nDutton lives rather too near your uncle, but he is a man, and he can\'t\r\nbe so bad.\'\r\n\r\nThis of course did not pass till Mr. Dutton had gone in to greet the\r\nladies next door, to promise to tell them of their child at length when\r\nthe business hours of the day should be over.\r\n\r\nShall it be told?  There was something in his tone--perfectly\r\nindefinable, with which he spoke of \'Miss Egremont,\' that was like the\r\nold wistfully reverential voice in which he used to mention \'Mrs.\r\nEgremont.\'  It smote Mary Nugent\'s quiet heart with a pang.  Was it\r\nthat the alteration from the old kindly fatherliness of regard to\r\n\'little Nuttie\' revealed that any dim undefined hope of Mary\'s own must\r\nbe extinguished for ever; or was it that she grieved that he should\r\nagain be wasting his heart upon the impracticable?\r\n\r\nA little of both, perhaps, but Mary was as ready as ever to sympathise,\r\nand to rejoice in hearing that the impetuous child had grown into the\r\nforbearing dutiful woman.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX.\r\n\r\nA FRESH START.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Did you say that Mark and his wife were come to Springfield House?\'\r\n\r\n\'They come the day after to-morrow,\' answered Ursula.  \'Mark could not\r\nfinish up the business sooner.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I suppose we must have them to dinner for once.  He has made a\r\nfool of himself, but I won\'t have the Canoness complaining that I take\r\nno notice of him; and it is easier done while he is there than when he\r\nhas got into some hole in the City--that is if he ever gets anything to\r\ndo.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton has several situations in view for him.\'\r\n\r\n\'In view.  That\'s a large order.  Or does it mean living on Dutton and\r\ndoing something nominal?  I should think Dutton too old and sharp a\r\nhand for that, though he is quartering them on himself.\'\r\n\r\n\'I believe there is nothing Mr. Dutton would like better, if he thought\r\nit right for them, but I am quite sure Mark and Annaple would not\r\nconsent.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ha, ha!\' and Mr. Egremont laughed.  \'Their nose is not brought to the\r\ngrindstone yet!  Say Saturday, then, Ursula.\'\r\n\r\n\'Am I to ask Mr. Dutton?\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course; I\'m not going to have a tete-a-tete with Master Mark.\'\r\n\r\nSo Ursula had the satisfaction of writing a more agreeable note to Mr.\r\nDutton than her last, and her invitation was accepted, but to her\r\nvexation Mr. Egremont further guarded himself from anything\r\nconfidential by verbally asking Mr. Clarence Fane on that very day, and\r\nas that gentleman was a baronet\'s son, she knew she should fall to his\r\nlot at dinner, and though she was glad when this was the case at their\r\nordinary parties, it was a misfortune on the present occasion.  She had\r\nnot seen Annaple since her marriage, except at the family gathering on\r\nthe Canon\'s death, when she was very much absorbed by the requirements\r\nof the stricken household; and Nuttie expected to see her in the same\r\nsubdued condition.  All Mr. Dutton had said or Mary Nugent had written\r\nabout her courage and cheerfulness had given the impression of\r\n\'patience smiling at grief,\' and in a very compassionate mood she\r\nstarted for a forenoon call at Springfield House; but, early as it was,\r\nnobody was at home, unless it might be the little boy, whose voice she\r\nthought she heard while waiting at the gate.\r\n\r\nShe was out driving with her father afterwards in the long summer\r\nevening, and only found Mark\'s card on returning just in time to dress.\r\nIt was a bright glaring day, and she was sitting by the window, rather\r\ninattentively listening to Mr. Fane\'s criticism of a new performance at\r\none of the theatres, when she heard the bell, and there entered the\r\nslight, bright creature who might still have been taken for a mere\r\ngirl.  The refined though pronounced features, the transparent\r\ncomplexion, crispy yellow hair and merry eyes, were as sunbeam-like as\r\nat the Rectory garden-party almost five years ago, and the black dress\r\nonly marked the contrast, and made the slenderness of the figure more\r\nevident.\r\n\r\nMark looked older, and wrung his cousin\'s hand with a pressure of\r\ngratitude and feeling, but Annaple\'s was a light little gay kiss, and\r\nthere was an entire unconsciousness about her of the role of poor\r\nrelation.  She made an easy little acknowledgment of the introduction\r\nof Mr. Fane, and, as Mr. Egremont appeared the next moment, exchanged\r\ngreetings with him in a lively ordinary fashion.\r\n\r\nThis was just what he liked.  He only wanted to forget what was\r\nunpleasant, and, giggling Scotch girl as she was, he was relieved to\r\nfind that she could not only show well-bred interest in the surface\r\nmatters of the time, but put in bright flashes of eagerness and\r\noriginality, well seconded by Mr. Dutton.  Mr. Fane was always a\r\nprofessor of small talk, and Nuttie had learnt to use the current\r\nchange of society, so that though Mark was somewhat silent, the dinner\r\nwas exceedingly pleasant and lively; and, as Mr. Fane remarked\r\nafterwards, he had been asked to enliven a doleful feast to ruined\r\nkindred, he could only say he wished prosperity always made people so\r\nagreeable.\r\n\r\n\'This is all high spirit and self-respect,\' thought Nuttie.  \'Annaple\r\nis talking as I am, from the teeth outwards.  I shall have it out with\r\nher when we go upstairs!  At any rate my father is pleased with her!\'\r\n\r\nNuttie made the signal to move as soon as she could, and as they went\r\nupstairs, put her arm round the slim waist and gave a sympathetic\r\npressure, but the voice that addressed her had still the cheery ring\r\nthat she fancied had been only assumed.\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sorry I missed you, but we set out early and made a day of it; and\r\noh! we\'ve been into such funny places as I never dreamt of!  You didn\'t\r\nsee my boy?\'\r\n\r\n\'No.  I thought I heard him.  I must see him to-morrow.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I must see yours.  May it not be a pleasure to-night?  I\'ve no\r\ndoubt you go and gloat over him at night.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I do generally run up after dinner; but after your day, I can\'t\r\nthink of dragging you up all these stairs.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, that\'s nothing!  Only you see it is jollier to have my Billy-boy\r\nin the next room.\'\r\n\r\nThey were mounting all the time, and were received in the day nursery\r\nby the old Rectory nurse, much increased in dignity, but inclined to be\r\npathetic as she inquired after \'Mr. Mark,\' while Annaple, like a little\r\ninsensible being, answered with provoking complacency as to his perfect\r\nhealth, and begged Mrs. Poole to bring Master Alwyn to play in the\r\ngarden at Springfield with her Willie.  In fact there was a general\r\ninvitation already to Alwyn to play there, but his attendants so much\r\npreferred the society of their congeners in the parks that they did not\r\navail themselves of it nearly as often as Ursula wished.\r\n\r\nLittle Alwyn asleep was, of course, a beautiful sight, with a precious\r\nold headless rabbit pressed tight to his cheek; Annaple\'s face grew\r\ntender as she looked at the motherless creature; and she admired him to\r\nany extent except saying that he excelled her own. Being more than a\r\nyear the elder, there could be no rivalry as to accomplishments; but as\r\nsoon as they were out of the nursery hush, Annaple laughed her way down\r\nagain with tales of Billy-boy\'s wonder at his first experiences of\r\ntravelling.  They sat down among the plants in the balcony, as far from\r\nthe lamps as possible, and talked themselves into intimacy over\r\nMicklethwayte.  There are two Eden homes in people\'s lives, one that of\r\nlater childhood, the other the first of wedded happiness, and St.\r\nAmbrose Road had the same halo to both of these; for both had been\r\nuprooted from it against their will; the chief difference being that\r\nUrsula could cast longing, lingering looks behind, while Annaple held\r\nherself resolutely steeled against sentiment, and would only turn it\r\noff by something absurd.  Nothing was absolutely settled yet; Mark had\r\nbeen presenting himself at offices, and she had been seeing rooms and\r\nlodgings.\r\n\r\n\'The insurance office sounds the best, and would be the least shock to\r\nour belongings,\' said Annaple; \'but it seems to lead to nothing. He\r\nwould not get on unless we had capital to invest, and even if we had\r\nany, you wouldn\'t catch us doing that again!\'\r\n\r\n\'Does Mr. Dutton advise that?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, he only thought we should like it better; but we are quite past\r\ncaring for people\'s feelings in the matter.  They couldn\'t pity us\r\nworse than they do.  I incline to Stubbs and Co.  One of them was once\r\nin the Greenleaf office, and has a regard for anything from thence;\r\nbesides Mark would have something to do besides desk work. He would\r\nhave to judge of samples, and see to the taking in and storing of\r\ngoods.  He does know something about that, and I\'m sure it would agree\r\nwith him better than an unmitigated high stool, with his nose to a\r\ndesk.\'\r\n\r\n\'I should like it better.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s right!  Now I have got some one to say so.  Besides, rising is\r\npossible, if one gets very useful.  I mean to be Mrs. Alderman, if not\r\nmy Lady Mayoress, before we have done.  Then they have a great big\r\nalmost deserted set of rooms over the warehouse, where we might live\r\nand look after the place.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! but should you like that?\'\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton wants us to live out in some of the suburban places, where\r\nit seems there is a perfect population of clerks\' families in\r\nsemi-detached houses.  He says we should save Mark\'s railway fare,\r\nrent, and all in doctors\' bills.  But people, children and all, do live\r\nand thrive in the City; and I think Mark\'s health will be better looked\r\nafter if I am there to give him his midday bite and sup, and brush him\r\nup, than if he is left to cater for himself; and as to exercise for the\r\nBilly-boy, \'tis not so far to the Thames Embankment. The only things\r\nthat stagger me are the blacks!  I don\'t know whether life is long\r\nenough to be after the blacks all day long, but perhaps I shall get\r\nused to them!\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, I think that would be worse.\'\r\n\r\n\'Perhaps it would; and at any rate, if the blacks do beat me, we could\r\nmove.  Think, no rent, nor rates, nor taxes--that is an inducement to\r\nswallow--no--to contend with, any number of blackamoors, isn\'t it? even\r\nif they settle on the tip of Billy-boy\'s nose.\'\r\n\r\n\'I could come to see you better there than out in a suburb,\' said\r\nNuttie.  \'But what do these rooms look out upon?\'\r\n\r\n\'On one side into their own court, on the other into Wulstan Street--a\r\nquiet place on the whole--all walls and warehouses; and there\'s an\r\nexcellent parish church, Mr. Underwood\'s; so I think we might do worse.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was very sorry that the gentlemen came up, and Mr. Fane wandered\r\nout and began asking whether they were going to the rose show.  Somehow\r\non that evening she became conscious that Annaple looked at her and Mr.\r\nFane rather curiously; and when they met again the next day, and having\r\ngrown intimate over the introduction of the two little boys, were\r\ndriving out together, there were questions about whether she saw much\r\nof him.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I don\'t know!  He is the nicest, on the whole, of papa\'s friends;\r\nhe can talk of something besides\'--Nuttie paused over her\r\n\'besides,\'--\'horseyness, and all that sort of thing--he is not so like\r\nan old satyr as some of them are; and so he is a resource.\'\r\n\r\n\'I see.  And you meet him elsewhere, don\'t you, in general society?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t go out much now that Lady Kirkaldy is not in town; but he\r\nalways seems to turn up everywhere that one goes.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ursula, I\'m very glad of that tone of yours.  I was afraid--\'\r\n\r\n\'Afraid of what?\' cried Nuttie in a defiant tone.\r\n\r\n\'That you liked him, and he is not really nice, Nuttie.  Mark knows all\r\nabout him; and so did I when I lived with the Delmars.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie laughed rather bitterly.  \'Thank you, Annaple.  As if I could\r\ncare for that man--or he for me, for that matter!  I know but too\r\nwell,\' she added gravely, \'that nobody nice is ever intimate at home.\'\r\n\r\n\'I beg your pardon.  I would not have worried you about it, only I\r\nthink you must take care, Nuttie, for Blanche mentioned it to us last\r\nwinter.\'\r\n\r\n\'Blanche is an arrant gossip!  If she saw a grandfather and great\r\ngrandmother gossiping she would say they were going to be married.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, as Mark says, one always swallows Blanche with a qualification.\'\r\n\r\n\'You may be quite sure, Annaple, that nothing like that will ever be\r\ntrue about me!  Why, what would ever become of my poor little Wyn if I\r\nwas so horrid as to want to go and marry?\'\r\n\r\nShe said it with an ineffable tone of contempt, just like the original\r\nNuttie, who seemed to be recalled by association with Annaple.\r\n\r\nThat sojourn of Mark and his wife at Springfield House was a bright\r\nspot in that summer.  If it had been only that Annaple\'s presence gave\r\nthe free entree to such an island of old Micklethwayte, it would have\r\nbeen a great pleasure to her; but there was besides the happiness of\r\nconfidence and unrestraint in their society, a restful enjoyment only\r\nto be appreciated by living the guarded life of constraint that was\r\nhers.  She was so seldom thrown among people whom she could admire and\r\nlook up to.  Annaple told her husband of Nuttie\'s vehement repudiation\r\nof any intention of marriage.  \'I am sure she meant it,\' she observed,\r\n\'it was only a little too strong. I wonder if that poor youth who came\r\nto her first ball, and helped to pick us out of the hole in Bluepost\r\nBridge, had anything to do with it.\'\r\n\r\nAnnaple had an opportunity of judging.  Mr. Dutton would not have\r\nbrought about a meeting which might be painful and unsettling to both;\r\nbut one afternoon, when Nuttie was \'off duty\' with her father, and had\r\ncome in to share Annaple\'s five o\'clock tea, Gerard Godfrey, looking\r\nthe curate from head to foot, made his appearance, having come up from\r\nthe far east, about some call on Mr. Dutton\'s purse.\r\n\r\nThe two shook hands with pleased surprise, and a little heightening of\r\ncolour, but that was all.  Nuttie had been out to luncheon, and was\r\ndressed \'like a mere fashionable young lady\' in his eyes; and when,\r\nafter the classes and clubs and schools of his district had been\r\ndiscussed, he asked, \'And I suppose you are taking part in everything\r\nhere?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, that I can\'t!\'\r\n\r\n\'Indeed!  I know Porlock, the second curate here very well, and he\r\ntells me that his vicar has a wonderful faculty of finding appropriate\r\nwork for every one.  Of course you know him?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, I don\'t;\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Miss Egremont has her appropriate work,\' said Mr. Dutton, and the\r\ndeacon felt himself pushed into his old position at Micklethwayte. He\r\nknew the clergy of the district very well, and how persistently either\r\nMr. Egremont, or perhaps Gregorio, prevented their gaining admittance\r\nat his house; and he guessed, but did not know, that Nuttie could not\r\nhave got into personal intercourse with them without flat disobedience.\r\n\r\nAnnaple threw herself into the breach, and talked of St. Wulstan\'s; and\r\nthe encounter ended, leaving the sense of having drifted entirely away\r\nfrom one another, and being perfectly heart whole, though on the one\r\nhand Ursula\'s feeling was of respect and honour; and Gerard\'s had a\r\nconsiderable element of pity and disapprobation.\r\n\r\n\'No!\' said Annaple when they were gone, \'he will not cry like the\r\nkloarek in the Breton ballad who wetted three great missals through\r\nwith his tears at his first mass.  He is very good, I am sure, but he\r\nis a bit of a prig!\'\r\n\r\n\'It is very hard to youth to be good without priggishness,\' said Mr.\r\nDutton.  \'Self-assertion is necessary, and it may easily be carried too\r\nfar.\'\r\n\r\n\'Buttresses are useful, but they are not beauties,\' rejoined Annaple.\r\n\r\nThe warehouse arrangement was finally adopted, and after the three\r\nweeks necessary for the cleaning and fitting of their floor, and the\r\nbringing in of their furniture, Mark and Annaple began what she termed\r\n\'Life among the Blacks.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie had great designs of constantly seeing Annaple, sending her\r\nsupplies from the gardens and preserves at Bridgefield, taking her out\r\nfor drives, and cultivating a friendship between Alwyn and Willie, who\r\nhad taken to each other very kindly on the whole.  They could not\r\nexactly understand each other\'s language, and had great fights from\r\ntime to time over toys, for though there was a year between them they\r\nwere nearly equal in strength; but they cared for each other\'s company\r\nmore than for anything else, were always asking to go to one another,\r\nand roared when the time of parting came; at least Alwyn did so\r\nunreservedly, for Nuttie had begun to perceive with compunction that\r\nBilly-boy was much the most under control, and could try to be good at\r\nhis mother\'s word, without other bribe than her kiss and smile.  Ah!\r\nbut he had a mother!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX.\r\n\r\nNUTTIE\'S PROSPECTS.\r\n\r\n  \'Three hundred pounds and possibilities.\'--Merry Wives of Windsor.\r\n\r\n\r\nAgain Nuttie\'s plans were doomed to be frustrated.  It did not prove to\r\nbe half so easy to befriend Mr. and Mrs. Mark Egremont as she expected,\r\nat the distance of half London apart, and with no special turn for\r\nbeing patronised on their side.\r\n\r\nHer father took a fancy for almost daily drives with her in the park,\r\nbecause then he could have Alwyn with him; and the little fellow\'s\r\nchatter had become his chief amusement.  Or if she had the carriage to\r\nherself, there was sure to be something needful to be done which made\r\nit impossible to go into the city to take up and set down Mrs. Mark\r\nEgremont; and to leave her to make her way home would be no kindness.\r\nSo Nuttie only accomplished a visit once before going out of town, and\r\nthat was by her own exertions--by underground railway and cab.  Then\r\nshe found all going prosperously; the blacks not half so obnoxious as\r\nhad been expected (of course not, thought Nuttie, in the middle of the\r\nsummer); the look-out over the yard very amusing to Billy-boy; and the\r\nlarge old-fashioned pannelled rooms, so cool and airy that Annaple was\r\nquite delighted with them, and contemned the idea of needing a holiday.\r\nShe had made them very pretty and pleasant with her Micklethwayte\r\nfurniture, whose only fault was being on too small a scale for these\r\nlarger spaces, but that had been remedied by piecing, and making what\r\nhad been used for two serve for one.\r\n\r\nThe kitchen was on the same floor, close at hand, which was well, for\r\nAnnaple did a good deal there, having only one young maid for the\r\nrougher work.  She had taken lessons in the School of Cookery, and\r\npractised a good deal even at Micklethwayte, and she was proud of her\r\nskill and economy.  Mark came in for his mid-day refreshment, and\r\nlooked greatly brightened, as if the worst had come and was by no means\r\nso bad as he expected.  All the time he had been at Mr. Dutton\'s he had\r\nbeen depressed and anxious, but now, with his boy on his knee, he was\r\nmerrier than Nuttie had ever known him.  As to exercise, there were\r\ndelightful evening walks, sometimes early marketings in the long summer\r\nmornings before business began--and altogether it seemed, as Nuttie\r\ntold her father afterwards, as if she had had a glimpse into a little\r\nCity Arcadia.\r\n\r\n\'Hein!\' said he, \'how long will it last?\'\r\n\r\nAnd Nuttie was carried away to Cowes, where he had been persuaded to\r\nrecur to his old favourite sport of yachting.  She would have rather\r\nliked this if Clarence Fane had not been there too, and continually\r\nhaunting them.  She had been distrustful of him ever since Annaple\'s\r\nwarning, and it became a continual worry to the motherless girl to\r\ndecide whether his civil attentions really meant anything, or whether\r\nshe were only foolish and ridiculous in not accepting them as freely\r\nand simply as before.\r\n\r\nOf one thing she became sure, namely, that Gregorio was doing whatever\r\nin him lay to bring them together.\r\n\r\nIn this seaside temporary abode, great part of the London establishment\r\nwas left behind, and Gregorio condescended to act the part of butler,\r\nwith only a single man-servant under him, and thus he had much more\r\nopportunity of regulating the admission of visitors than at home; and\r\nhe certainly often turned Mr. Fane in upon her, when she had intended\r\nthat gentleman to be excluded, and contrived to turn a deaf or\r\nuncomprehending ear when she desired that there should be no admission\r\nof visitors unless her father was absolutely ready for them; and also\r\nthere were times when he must have suggested an invitation to dinner,\r\nor a joining in a sail.  No doubt Gregorio would have been delighted to\r\nsee her married, and to be thus free from any counter influence over\r\nhis master; but as she said to herself, \'Catch me!  Even if I cared a\r\nrush for the man, I could not do it.  I don\'t do my poor father much\r\ngood, but as to leaving poor little Alwyn in his clutches--I must be\r\nperfectly demented with love even to think of it.\'\r\n\r\nThere was a desire on the valet\'s part to coax and court little Alwyn\r\nof which she felt somewhat jealous.  The boy was naturally the pet of\r\nevery one in the household, but he was much less out of Gregorio\'s\r\nreach in the present confined quarters, and she could not bear to see\r\nhim lifted up in the valet\'s arms, allowed to play with his watch, held\r\nto look at distant sails on board the yacht, or even fed with sweet\r\nbiscuits or chocolate creams.\r\n\r\nThe Rectory nursery had gone on a strict regimen and nurse was as angry\r\nas Nuttie herself; but there was no preventing it, for his father was\r\nnot above cupboard love, and never resisted the entreaties that were\r\nalways excited by the sight of dainties, only laughing when Nuttie\r\nremonstrated, or even saying, \'Never mind sister, Wynnie, she\'s got\r\nMrs. Teachem\'s cap on,\' and making the child laugh by pretending to\r\nsmuggle in papers of sweets by stealth, apart from the severe eyes of\r\nsister or nurse.\r\n\r\nThat cut Nuttie to the heart.  To speak of the evils for which\r\nself-indulgence was a preparation would only make her father sneer at\r\nher for a second Hannah More.  It was a language he did not understand;\r\nand as to the physical unwholesomeness, he simply did not choose to\r\nbelieve it.  She almost wished Alwyn would for once be sick enough to\r\nfrighten him, but that never happened, nor would he accept nurse\'s\r\nstatement of the boy being out of order.\r\n\r\nPoor little Alwyn, he was less and less of an unmixed joy to her as he\r\nwas growing out of the bounds of babyhood, and her notions of\r\ndiscipline were thwarted by her father\'s unbounded indulgence.  To her\r\nthe child was a living soul, to be trained for a responsible position\r\nhere and for the eternal world beyond; to her father he was a\r\ndelightful plaything, never to be vexed, whose very tempers were\r\namusing, especially when they teased the serious elder sister.\r\n\r\n\'Oh father! do you ever think what it will come to?\' Nuttie could not\r\nhelp saying one day when Mr. Egremont had prevented her from carrying\r\nhim off in disgrace to the nursery for tying the rolls up in dinner\r\nnapkins to enact Punch and Judy, in spite of his own endeavours to\r\nprevent the consequent desolation of the preparations.\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont shrugged his shoulders, and only observed, \'An excuse for\r\na little home tyranny, eh?  No, no, Wyn; we don\'t want tame little\r\nmuffs here.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie was obliged to run out of the room and--it must be\r\nconfessed--dance and stamp out her agony of indignation and misery that\r\nher father should be bent on ruining his child, for she could not\r\nunderstand that all this was simply the instinctive self-indulgence of\r\na drugged brain and dulled conscience.\r\n\r\nShe did, however, get a little support and help during a brief stay in\r\nthe shooting season at Bridgefield.  The Canoness was visiting the\r\nCondamines at the Rectory, and very soon understood all the state of\r\nthings, more perhaps from her former nurse than from Ursula.  She was\r\nwitness to one of those trying scenes, when Nuttie had been forbidding\r\nthe misuse of a beautiful elaborate book of nursery rhymes, where Alwyn\r\nthought proper to \'kill\' with repeated stabs the old woman of the shoe,\r\nwhen preparing to beat her progeny.\r\n\r\nJust as she was getting the dagger paper-knife out of his little hand,\r\nand was diverting the pout on his swelling lip, his father became aware\r\nof the contest, and immediately the half conquered boy appealed to him.\r\n\'Sister naughty.  Won\'t let Wynnie kill cross ugly old woman, beating\r\npoor little children.\'\r\n\r\n\'A fellow feeling! eh, sister?  Kill her away, boy, tear her out! Yes,\r\ngive her to sister, and tell her that\'s the way to serve sour females!\r\nI declare, Ursula, she has got something of your expression.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh Wynnie, Wynnie!\' said Nuttie, as he trotted up to her, \'is sister\r\ncross and ugly?\' and she opened her arms to him.\r\n\r\n\'Sister, Wyn\'s own sister,\' said the child affectionately, letting\r\nhimself be kissed as he saw her grieved.  \'She shan\'t be ugly old\r\nwoman--ugly old woman go in fire.\'\r\n\r\nSo perilously near the flame did he run to burn the old woman that Mr.\r\nEgremont shouted to her that in spite of all that humbug, she was\r\nperfectly careless of the child, although if she had withheld him she\r\nwould probably have been blamed for thwarting him.\r\n\r\n\'Are you quite fair towards Ursula?\' the aunt ventured to say when the\r\ngirl had gone to dress for walking down with her to the Rectory. \'It is\r\nhard on her, and not good for the boy to upset her authority.\'\r\n\r\n\'Eh?  Why, the girl is just a governess manquee, imbued with the spirit\r\nof all those old women who bred her up.  A nice life the poor child\r\nwould have of it, but for me.\'\r\n\r\n\'I am sure she is devotedly attached to him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Hein!  So she thinks; but trust human nature for loving to wreak\r\ndiscipline on the child who has cut her out.\'\r\n\r\n\'That is scarcely just, Alwyn.  She was greatly relieved to be cut out.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont laughed at this, and his sister-in-law indignantly added\r\nwith all the authority of a successful parent, \'Anyway, nothing is so\r\nbad for a child as collision between the authorities in a family.\r\nUrsula is doing her best to act as a mother to that child, and it will\r\nbe very injurious to him to interfere with her influences.\'\r\n\r\n\'She\'s a good girl enough--gives very little trouble,\' he allowed, \'but\r\nI\'m not going to have the boy sat upon.\'\r\n\r\nAs he spoke the words, Nuttie returned, and as soon as she was out of\r\nthe house and out of hearing she exclaimed, \'Oh, Aunt Jane, you see how\r\nit is!  How am I to prevent my boy from being utterly ruined?\'\r\n\r\n\'I have been speaking to your father,\' said Mrs. Egremont, \'but he does\r\nnot seem to understand.  Men don\'t.  A child\'s faults and fancies seem\r\nsuch trifles to them that they can\'t see the harm of indulging them,\r\nand, besides, they expect to be amused.\'\r\n\r\n\'And is that poor dear little fellow to grow up spoilt?\' said Nuttie,\r\nher eyes hot with unshed tears.\r\n\r\n\'I hope not, Ursula.  I have great confidence in your influence, for I\r\nsee you are a sensible girl.\'  This was astonishing praise from the\r\nCanoness.  \'But you will throw away your chances if you keep up a\r\ncontinual opposition to what your father allows.  It will be much less\r\nhurtful if Alwyn does get too much indulgence, and does a little\r\nunnecessary mischief, than for him to learn to think you the enemy of\r\nhis pleasures, always wanting to check and punish him.  Oh yes,\' as\r\nNuttie was going to answer, \'I know it is for his real good, but how is\r\nthat baby to understand that?  Indeed, my dear, I know how it is; I\r\nhave gone through the same sort of thing with Basil.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, it could never have been so bad!\'\r\n\r\n\'No, of course not; but I have had to allow what I did not like for the\r\nchild rather than let him see the shadow of difference of opinion\r\nbetween us, and I don\'t think it has done him any harm.  The great\r\npoint is that you should keep that poor little fellow\'s affection and\r\nrespect, and make him unwilling to vex you.\'\r\n\r\n\'That he is, dear little man.  He is sorry when he sees sister grieved.\r\nHe is always distressed if anything is hurt or pained.  He is really\r\ntender-hearted.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, but boys are boys.  That feeling will fail you if you work it too\r\nhard, and especially if you show vexation at his pleasures.  Keep that\r\nfor real evils, like falsehood or cruelty.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not for disobedience?\'\r\n\r\n\'The evil of disobedience depends much more upon the authority of an\r\norder than on the child itself.  If he disobeys you under his father\'s\r\nlicence, you cannot make much of it.  You have him a good deal to\r\nyourself?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then make use of that time to strengthen his principles and sense of\r\nright and wrong, as well as to secure his affections.  My dear, I never\r\nsaw a girl in a more difficult position than yours, but I see you are\r\ndoing your utmost; only I am afraid the love of sedatives is the same.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh aunt, I did think he had given it up!\'\r\n\r\n\'You are inexperienced, my dear.  I see it in his eyes.  Well, I\'m\r\nafraid there is no stopping that.\'\r\n\r\n\'Mother--\' and Nuttie\'s voice was choked.\r\n\r\n\'She did her best, but you have not the same opportunities.  It can\'t\r\nbe helped with a man of that age.  Mark might have done something, but\r\nhe is out of the question now, poor fellow!\'\r\n\r\n\'Indeed, Aunt Jane, I think Mark and Annaple are some of the happiest\r\npeople I ever saw.  I only wish my poor Alwyn were as forward as their\r\nBilly, but I\'m not even allowed to teach him his letters, because once\r\nhe cried over them.\'\r\n\r\n\'I wish they had anything to fall back upon,\' said Mrs. Egremont\r\nanxiously.  \'They are so unwilling to let any one know of their\r\ndifficulties that I feel as if I never knew in what straits they may\r\nbe.  You will be sure to let me know, Ursula, if there is anything that\r\nI can do for them.\'\r\n\r\nThat conversation was a great comfort and help to Nuttie, who was\r\npleased to find herself treated as a real friend by her aunt, and\r\nperceived the wisdom of her advice.  But the watching over the Mark\r\nEgremonts was a very difficult matter to accomplish, for when she went\r\nback to London she was warned that Billy had the whooping cough,\r\nrendering them unapproachable all the winter, so that she could only\r\nhear of them through Mr. Dutton, whom she continued to see occasionally\r\nwhenever there was anything to communicate.  Mr. Egremont rather liked\r\nhim, and on meeting him in the street, would ask him casually in to\r\ndinner, or to make up a rubber, or play piquet, for he excelled in\r\nthese arts, and still more in chess, and an evening with Mr. Dutton was\r\nquite a red-letter time with Nuttie. It gave her an indefinable sense\r\nof safety and protection; but it was not always to be had, for her\r\nfriend had many engagements, being one of the active lay church\r\nworkers, and devoting two regular evenings in each week to Gerard\r\nGodfrey\'s eastern district, where he kept all the accounts, had a model\r\ncourt and evening class, besides hospitably resting tired clergymen and\r\ntheir wives in his pleasant quiet house.\r\n\r\nIn the spring Mr. Egremont was laid up with the worst rheumatic attack\r\nhe had yet had, in consequence of yielding to the imperious will of his\r\nson, who had insisted on standing in a bleak corner to see the Life\r\nGuards pass by.  On this occasion Nuttie did not prove herself the\r\nheaven-born nurse that the true heroine ought to be, but was extremely\r\nfrightened, and altogether dependent on Gregorio, who knew all about\r\nthe symptoms, and when to send for the doctor and a garde-malade.\r\nGregorio always talked French to Nuttie when he felt himself in the\r\nascendant, as he certainly was at present; but he became much less\r\ngracious when he heard that Mrs. William Egremont might be expected,\r\ndeclaring that madame would only excite his master, and that her\r\npresence was quite unnecessary.  Her coming had been volunteered, but\r\nit was a great boon to Ursula, who was thus helped out in many\r\nperplexities, although Mrs. Egremont was a great deal at her\r\nstep-son\'s, and neither lady was of much avail in the sick-room, during\r\nthe stress of the illness.  It was never actually dangerous, but there\r\nwas great suffering and much excitement, and for four or five days the\r\ndistress and anxiety were considerable.  After this passed off Ursula\r\nwas surprised to find her company preferred to that of her aunt.  She\r\nwas a better souffre-douleur, was less of a restraint, and was besides\r\nhis regular reader and amanuensis, so that as the force of the attack\r\nabated, he kept her a good deal in his room during the latter part of\r\nthe day, imparting scraps of intelligence, skimming the papers for him,\r\nand reading his letters.\r\n\r\nThere was a lease to be signed, and, as soon as might be, Mr. Bulfinch,\r\nthe Redcastle solicitor, brought it up, and had to be entertained at\r\nluncheon.  While he was waiting in the drawing-room for Mr. Egremont to\r\nbe made ready for him, he looked with deep interest on the little heir,\r\nwhom Ursula presently led off to the other end of the room to the hoard\r\nof downstair toys; and an elaborate camp was under construction, when\r\nby the fireside, the Canoness inquired in a low confidential tone, \'May\r\nI ask whether you came about a will?\'\r\n\r\n\'No, Mrs. Egremont.  I wish I were.  It is only about the lease of\r\nSpinneycotes farm.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then there is none?\'\r\n\r\n\'None that I am aware of.  None has ever been drawn up by us. Indeed, I\r\nwas wishing that some influence could be brought to bear which might\r\nshow the expedience of making some arrangement.  Any melancholy event\r\nis, I trust, far distant, but contingencies should be provided for.\'\r\n\r\n\'Exactly so.  He is recovering now, but these attacks always leave\r\neffects on the heart, and at his age, with his habits, no one knows\r\nwhat may happen.  Of course it would not make much difference to the\r\nboy.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, the Court of Chancery would appoint the most suitable natural\r\nguardians.\'\r\n\r\n\'But,\' said Mrs. Egremont, \'I am afraid that the personal property when\r\ndivided would not be much of a provision for her.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are right.  The investments are unfortunately and\r\ndisproportionately small.\'\r\n\r\n\'She ought either to have them all, or there should be a charge on the\r\nestate,\' said the Canoness decisively.  \'If possible, he must be made\r\nto move.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, don\'t!\' cried Nuttie, jumping up from the floor.  \'He mustn\'t be\r\nupset on any account.\'\r\n\r\n\'My dear, I had no notion that you heard us!\' exclaimed her aunt.  \'I\r\nthought Alwyn was making too much noise with his soldiers.\'\r\n\r\n\'I beg your pardon,\' said Nuttie, \'perhaps I should have spoken sooner,\r\nbut indeed he must not be worried and disturbed,\' she added, somewhat\r\nfiercely.\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t be afraid, my dear,\' said her aunt.  \'Mr. Bulfinch knows that\r\nyour father is in no condition to have such matters brought before him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Certainly,\' said the old lawyer politely;\' and we will trust that Miss\r\nEgremont\'s prospects may soon come forward on a more auspicious\r\noccasion.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie could have beaten him, but she was obliged to content herself\r\nwith such a sweeping charge of her Zulus among Alwyn\'s Englishmen, that\r\ntheir general shrieked out in indignation against such a variation of\r\nthe accustomed programme of all their games.\r\n\r\nNuttie thought she had defended her patient sufficiently, but she found\r\nshe had been mistaken, for when her aunt had left them, some days\r\nlater, her father began, \'We are well quit of her.  Those troublesome\r\ndictatorial women always get worse when they are left widows--taking\r\nupon them to say what their dear husbands would have said, forsooth.\'\r\n\r\n\'Aunt Jane was very kind to me,\' said Ursula, not in the least knowing\r\nwhat he was thinking of.\r\n\r\n\'To you.  Ay, I should think so, taking upon her to lecture me about\r\nsecuring a provision for you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!  I hoped--\'\r\n\r\n\'What?\' he broke in.  \'You knew of it!  You set her on, I suppose.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! no, no, father.  She and Mr. Bulfinch began about it, not meaning\r\nme to hear--about a will, I mean--and I told them I wasn\'t going to\r\nhave you worried, and I thought I had stopped it altogether.\'\r\n\r\n\'Stop a woman bent on her duty?  Hein!  But you are a good girl, and\r\nshall come to no loss when we have to make your marriage settlement.\'\r\n\r\n\'You won\'t have to do that, father!\'\r\n\r\n\'Hein!  What do you keep that poor fellow Clarence Fane dangling in\r\nattendance on you for?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t!  I\'m sure I don\'t want him.  I would do anything to keep him\r\nat a distance!\'\r\n\r\n\'How now!  I thought your Grace condescended to him more than to any\r\none else.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t dislike him unless he has _that_ in his head; but as to\r\nmarrying him!  Oh--h--h,\' such a note of horror that elicited a little\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\'So hot against him, are we?  Who is it then?  Not the umbrella fellow?\'\r\n\r\n\'Father! how can you?\' she cried, with a burning flush of indignation.\r\nHe--why--he!
