He has always been a sort of uncle, ever since I was a\r\nlittle girl.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, adopted uncles are very devout when young ladies rush out to\r\nmorning prayers at unearthly hours--\'\r\n\r\n\'Father!\' with her voice trembling, \'I assure you he doesn\'t--I mean he\r\nalways goes to St. Michael\'s, unless he has anything particular to say\r\nto me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes, I understand,\' and Mr. Egremont indulged in a hearty laugh,\r\nwhich almost drove poor Nuttie beside herself.\r\n\r\n\'Indeed--indeed,\' she stammered, in her confusion and suppressed wrath;\r\n\'it is nothing of that sort.  He is a regular old bachelor--he always\r\nwas.\'\r\n\r\n\'At what age do men become old bachelors?  For he seems to me about the\r\nage of poor Clarry, whom you seem to view as a bugbear.\'\r\n\r\n\'I wish you would not think of such things, father; I have not the\r\nslightest intention of leaving you and dear little Wynnie!  Nothing\r\nshould tempt me!\'\r\n\r\n\'Nothing?  Hein!  Then you may as well be on your guard, Miss Egremont,\r\nor we shall have pleadings that you have encouraged them--church and\r\nworld--or both, maybe.  You pious folk take your little diversions and\r\nflirtations just like your poor sisters whom you shake your heads at,\r\nnever guessing how Gregorio and I have looked out at you and your\r\nadopted uncle parading the street.\'\r\n\r\n\'I wish Gregorio would mind his own business, and not put such things\r\nin your head!\' burst out Nuttie.\r\n\r\nAt which Mr. Egremont laughed longer and louder than ever.\r\n\r\nPoor Nuttie!  It was terrible discomfiture, not only for the moment,\r\nbut a notion had been planted in her mind that seemed cruel, almost\r\nprofane, and yet which would not be dismissed, and made her heart leap\r\nwith strange bounds at the wild thought, \'Could it be true?\' then sink\r\nagain with shame at her own presumptuous folly in entertaining such a\r\nthought for a moment.\r\n\r\nYet whenever she actually encountered Mr. Dutton her habitual comfort\r\nand reliance on him revived, and dispelled all the embarrassment which\r\nat other times she expected to feel in his presence.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI.\r\n\r\nSPES NON FRACTA.\r\n\r\n\r\nSummer had quite set in before Mr. Egremont was able to go out for a\r\ndrive, and then he was ordered to Buxton.\r\n\r\nNuttie only once saw her cousins before leaving town, for their little\r\nboy fulfilled the nursery superstition by whooping till May; and all\r\nintercourse was prohibited, till he had ceased for a whole week to\r\nutter a suspicious sound.  Mr. Dutton had insisted on the family\r\nspending a fortnight at Springfield House for change of air, and it was\r\nthere that Nuttie was permitted to see them, though the children were\r\nstill forbidden to meet.\r\n\r\nAnnaple looked very thin, but rattled as merrily as ever.  \'No one\r\ncould guess,\' she said, \'what a delight it was not to know what one was\r\nto have for dinner?\'\r\n\r\n\'To do more than know, I am afraid,\' said Ursula.\r\n\r\n\'Well, next to the delight of knowing nothing at all about it--and even\r\nthat is only good for a holiday--is the delight of seeing a pudding\r\ncome out smooth and comfortable and unbroken from its basin. "Something\r\nattempted, something done," you know.  It is quite as good a work of\r\nart as a water-coloured drawing.\'\r\n\r\n\'Only not quite so permanent.\'\r\n\r\n\'No; it is only one\'s first pudding that one wants to embalm in a glass\r\ncase for being so good as not to leave its better part behind in the\r\nbasin, or to collapse as soon as it is in the dish.\'\r\n\r\n\'Which my puddings always did in the happy days of old, but then I was\r\nalways hunted ignominiously out of the kitchen and told I wasted good\r\nfood,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, and waste is fearful when Mark and Billy have to eat it all the\r\nsame, like the poor cows with spoilt hay.  I wonder whether your old\r\nexperiences recall the joy of finding trustworthy eggs within your\r\nprice.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah, I was not housekeeper.  I only remember being in disgrace for\r\ngrumbling when there was no pudding, because the hens would not lay.\'\r\n\r\n\'Though I heard a woman declaring the other day that there ought to be\r\na machine for them.  Oh, the scenes that I encounter when I am\r\nmarketing!  If I only could describe them for Punch!  I walked home\r\nonce with our porter\'s wife, carrying two most brilliant sticks of\r\nrhubarb, all carmine stalk and gamboge leaf, and expressing a very\r\nnatural opinion that the rhubarb tree must be very showy to look at,\r\nand curious to know in what kind of fruit the medicine grew.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Annaple! do you go yourself in that way?\'\r\n\r\n\'Mark used to go with me, but, poor old fellow, he has ruinous ideas\r\nabout prices and quantities, and besides, now he is so hard worked-up\r\nand down all day--he wants a little more of his bed in the morning.\'\r\n\r\n\'And what do you want?\'\r\n\r\n\'I never was a sleepy creature, and I get back in time to dress the\r\nboy.  I generally find him at high-jinks on his father\'s bed.  It uses\r\nup a little superfluous energy before the dressing.\'\r\n\r\n\'But surely you have a servant now?\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'ve come to the conclusion that a workman\'s wife charing is a better\r\ninstitution.  No. 1, a pet of Miss Nugent\'s, was a nice creature, but\r\nthe London air did for her at once.  No. 2, also from Micklethwayte,\r\ninstantly set up a young man, highly respectable, and ready to marry on\r\nthe spot, as they did, though their united ages don\'t amount to\r\nthirty-nine.  No. 3 was a Cockney, and couldn\'t stay because the\r\nlook-out was so dull; and No. 4 gossiped with her kind when I thought\r\nher safe in the Temple Gardens with Billy, whereby he caught the\r\nwhooping-cough, and as she also took the liberty of wearing my fur\r\ncloak, and was not particular as to accuracy, we parted on short\r\nnotice; and I got this woman to come in every day to scrub, help make\r\nthe bed, etc.  It is much less trouble, and the only fault I have to\r\nfind with her is an absolute incapability of discerning blacks.  I\r\nbelieve she thinks I have a monomania against them.\'\r\n\r\nStill Annaple insisted that she did not work half so hard as her\r\nnieces, Muriel and Janet, in their London season, and that her economy\r\nwas not nearly so trying and difficult as that which Lady Delmar had\r\nbeen practising for years in order to afford them a summer there; nor\r\nwas her anxiety to make both ends meet by any means equal to her\r\nsister\'s in keeping up appearances, and avoiding detrimentals. The two\r\nsisters met occasionally, but Lady Delmar was so compassionate and\r\npatronising that Annaple\'s spirit recoiled in off-hand levity and\r\nrattle, and neither regretted the occupation that prevented them from\r\nseeing much of one another.\r\n\r\n\r\nA year passed by, chiefly spent by Mr. Egremont in the pursuit of\r\ncomparative health, at Buxton, Bagneres, and Biarritz, during which his\r\ndaughter could do little but attend to him and to little Alwyn. The boy\r\nhad been enough left to her and nurse during his father\'s acute illness\r\nto have become more amenable.  He was an affectionate child,\r\ninheriting, with his mother\'s face, her sweetness and docility of\r\nnature, and he was old enough to be a good deal impressed with the fact\r\nthat he had made poor papa so ill by teasing him to stand in the cold.\r\nMr. Egremont was not at rest without a sight of the child every day, if\r\nonly for a moment, and the helplessness and suffering had awed the\r\nlittle fellow a good deal.  It was touching to see him pause when\r\ngalloping about the house when he went past the sick-room, and hush his\r\nmerry voice of his own accord.\r\n\r\nAnd in the journeys, when his father\'s invalided state would have made\r\na fractious or wilful child a serious inconvenience, his good temper\r\nand contentment were invaluable.  He would sit for hours on his\r\nsister\'s lap, listening to whispered oft-told tales, or playing at\r\nimpromptu quiet games; he could go to sleep anywhere, and the wonderful\r\ndiscoveries he made at each new place were the amusement of all his\r\nauditors.  Sister was always his playfellow and companion whenever she\r\ncould be spared from her father, and she had an ever-increasing\r\ninfluence over him which she did her best to raise into principle.\r\n\r\nPerhaps she never had a happier moment than when she heard how he had\r\nput his hands behind him and steadily refused when Gregorio had offered\r\nto regale him at a stall of bonbons forming only a thin crust to\r\nliqueurs, which unfortunately he had already been taught to like.\r\n\r\n\'But I told him sister said I mustn\'t have them,\' said Alwyn.  \'And\r\nthen he made a face and said something in French about you.  I know\r\n\'twas you, for he said "soeur."  What was it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Never mind, Wynnie dear. We had much better never know.  You were\r\nsister\'s own dear steadfast boy, and you shall kiss mother\'s picture.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie had a beautiful coloured photograph of her mother, finished like\r\na miniature, which had been taken at Nice, in the time of Alice\r\nEgremont\'s most complete and matured beauty.  She had taught Alwyn to\r\nkiss and greet it every evening before his prayers, and such a kiss was\r\nhis reward when he had shown any special act of goodness, for which, as\r\nshe told him, \'mother would have been pleased with her little son.\'\r\n\r\nSuch another boon was his one Sunday evening at Biarritz, when she\r\nfound that while she was shut up at dinner with her father he had\r\nvoluntarily gone to church with nurse instead of playing on the beach\r\nwith some other English children.  \'It was all very long and tiresome,\'\r\nhe said, when asked if he liked it.\r\n\r\n\'Then why did you go, old man?  There was no need to drag you there,\'\r\nsaid his father.\r\n\r\n\'She didn\'t drag me,\' said the boy; \'I walked.\'\r\n\r\n\'You need not have walked then, Master Dignity.\'\r\n\r\n\'Poor nursie couldn\'t go without me,\' said Alwyn, \'and sister says\r\nthere\'s a blessing on those that go.\'\r\n\r\n\'A blessing? eh! and what idea does that little head entertain of a\r\nblessing?\' said Mr. Egremont.\r\n\r\nAlwyn lifted his soft brown eyes reverently and said, \'It is something\r\ngood,\' speaking, as he always did, in a baby lisp inimitable here.\r\n\r\n\'Well?\'\r\n\r\n\'And it comes from God.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, what is it?  Can you see it?\'\r\n\r\n\'No\'--he looked in perplexity towards Nuttie, who was in agony all the\r\ntime, lest there should be a scoff that might remain in the child\'s\r\nmind.\r\n\r\n\'Never mind sister.  Can you feel it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes;\' and the little face lighted with such a reality that the\r\nincipient mockery turned into wonder on the next question.\r\n\r\n\'And how does it feel?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, so nice!  It makes Wynnie glad here,\' and he spread his hands over\r\nhis breast; and gave a little caper like a kid for very gladness.\r\n\r\n\'There!\' said Mr. Egremont, leaning back fairly conquered.  \'Any one\r\nmight envy Wynnie!  Goodnight, my boy, blessing and all.  I wonder if\r\none felt like that when one was a little shaver,\' he pursued, as Alwyn\r\nwent off to his bed.\r\n\r\n\'I think I did sometimes,\' said Nuttie, \'but I never was half as good\r\nas Wynnie!\'\r\n\r\n\'What?\' exclaimed her father.  \'You! bred up among the saints.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! but I hadn\'t the same nature.  I never was like--_her_.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well--\'tis very pretty now, and I don\'t know how we could stand a\r\nyoung Turk, but you mustn\'t make a girl of him.\'\r\n\r\n\'There\'s no fear of that,\' said Nuttie.  \'He is full of spirit.  That\r\nold bathing woman calls him "un vrai petit diable d\'Anglais," he is so\r\nventurous.\'\r\n\r\nWhich delighted Mr. Egremont as much as the concession that the boy\'s\r\nfaith was \'pretty\' delighted Ursula.  Indeed, he went a little further,\r\nfor when she came back from her few minutes at Alwyn\'s bedside he\r\nproceeded to tell her of the absolute neglect in which his mother, a\r\nbelle of the Almacks days, had left her nursery.  It was the first time\r\nhe had ever hinted at a shadow of perception that anything in his own\r\nlife had been amiss, and Ursula could not but feel a dreamy, hopeful\r\nwonder whether her sweet little Alwyn could be the destined means of\r\ndoing that in which her mother had failed.  It was at least enough to\r\nquicken those prayers which had been more dutiful than trustful.\r\n\r\nAnd then her hope sank again when she realised that her father\'s days\r\nwere spent between the lull of opiate, followed by a certain serenity,\r\nthen in a period of irritability, each being more or less prolonged,\r\naccording to health, weather, or entertainment, and closed again by the\r\nsedatives in various forms.  It relieved her indeed, but she felt it a\r\nwickedness to be glad of the calm, and she was aware that the habit was\r\nmaking inroads on her father\'s powers.  Between that and his defect of\r\neyesight, he was often much confused, especially about money matters,\r\nand was more and more dependent.\r\n\r\nWould that it had been only upon her, but she was constantly certain\r\nthat Gregorio was taking advantage of his master\'s helplessness, and\r\nkeeping it up by all means in his power.  Yet what could be done? For\r\nthe valet was absolutely necessary to his comfort, and yet she\r\nsometimes thought her father half in dread of him, and afraid to\r\nexpostulate about personal neglects, which became more frequent.\r\nThings, that would have enraged him from others, were only grumbled and\r\nfretted over, when Gregorio caused him real inconvenience by absence or\r\nforgetfulness, and made very insufficient apology.  It seemed like a\r\nbondage; Nuttie thought of her mother\'s efforts, and blamed herself in\r\nvain.\r\n\r\nIt was during this journey that she heard of good Miss Headworth\'s\r\ndeath.  The old lady\'s mind had long failed, and the actual present\r\nloss to Nuttie was not great; but it seemed to close a long account of\r\ngratitude such as she had not thoroughly felt or understood before; and\r\nthe link with Micklethwayte was severed.\r\n\r\nFor Mark and Annaple prevailed on Mrs. Egremont to install Miss Nugent\r\nas governess to Rosalind and Adela.  In that capacity Nuttie hoped to\r\nsee a good deal of her; but of course was again disappointed, for her\r\nfather would not hear of returning to Bridgefield.  It was draughty,\r\nand dull, and desolate, and nothing suited him but London.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXII.\r\n\r\nBLACKS IN THE ASCENDANT.\r\n\r\n  \'Man\'s work ends with set of sun,\r\n  Woman\'s work is never done.\'--Proverb.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was far on in May when Ursula found herself again in the\r\nsitting-room over the warehouse.  Somehow it had not the dainty\r\nwell-cared-for air of erst.  The pretty table ornaments were out of\r\nsight; the glass over the clock was dim, the hands had stopped; some of\r\nAnnaple\'s foes, the blacks, had effected a lodgment on the Parian\r\nfigures; the chintzes showed wear and wash, almost grime; the carpet\'s\r\npattern was worn; a basket full of socks was on the sofa; and on the\r\ntable a dress, once belonging to Annaple\'s trousseau, was laid out,\r\nconverted into its component parts.  The wails of a baby could be heard\r\nin the distance, and the first person to appear was Master William,\r\nsturdy and happy in spite of wofully darned knees to his stockings.\r\n\r\n\'Mother\'s coming, if baby will stop crying,\' he said, \'and lie in her\r\ncradle.\'\r\n\r\n\'Your little sister!  What\'s her name?\'\r\n\r\n\'Jane Christian,\' said the boy, with a much more distinct enunciation\r\nthan Alwyn, though a year older, had yet acquired.  \'She does cry so!\r\nShe won\'t let mother make my new knickies out of her blue gown!\'\r\n\r\nThoughts of the suits that Alwyn was discarding came across Nuttie.\r\nCould they be offered without offence?  She asked, however, \'Do you\r\nremember Alwyn--my Wynnie?\'\r\n\r\n\'Wynnie gave me my horse,\' cried the boy, unstabling a steed which had\r\nseen hard service since the presentation.  \'Where\'s Wynnie?\'\r\n\r\n\'He is at home.  You must come and see him,\' said Nuttie, who had not\r\nbeen allowed to bring him till secure of a clean bill of health. \'But\r\nsee, just outside the door, there\'s something for Billy.\'\r\n\r\nShe had made her servant bring up the parcels to the passage outside,\r\nand Billy was soon hugging a magnificent box of soldiers, wherewith he\r\npranced off to show them to his mother, leaving the doors open, so that\r\nUrsula could more decidedly hear the baby\'s voice, not a healthy\r\nchild\'s lusty cry, but a poor little feeble wail, interspersed with\r\nattempts at consolation.  \'Come, won\'t she go to Emily?  Oh, Billy-boy,\r\nhow splendid!  I hope you thanked Cousin Ursula.  Baby Jenny, now can\'t\r\nyou let any one speak but yourself?  Oh! shall I never teach you that\r\n"Balow, my babe," is not "bellow, my babe."  That\'s better!  Now can\'t\r\nyou let Emily have you, while I go to Cousin Nuttie?\'\r\n\r\n\'Let me come!  Mayn\'t I?\' exclaimed Ursula, invading the room that\r\nserved as kitchen, where Annaple was trying to hush off the child and\r\nmake her over to a little twelve years old maid, who stood in waiting,\r\nhelping Willie meantime to unpack his soldiers, with smothered\r\nexclamations of delight.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Nuttie, how good of you!  Please to excuse the accompaniment.\r\nThere never was such a young lady for self-assertion to make up for\r\nthere being so little of her.\'\r\n\r\nAnd Annaple, very thin and tired looking, held up the child, fearfully\r\nsmall and pinched for four months old, to be kissed by Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Does she always go on like this?\'\r\n\r\n\'\'Cept when she is asleep,\' said Willie.\r\n\r\n\'Poor wee lassie,\' said Annaple; \'there\'s great excuse for her, for the\r\nfood has not yet been invented that suits her ladyship.\'\r\n\r\n\'You must come and consult nurse.\'\r\n\r\n\'And how are you all?  I\'m glad you are at hand, Nuttie!  Is Mr.\r\nEgremont better?\'\r\n\r\n\'As well as ever he is--lame and altogether an invalid,--but he has not\r\nhad such bad attacks of pain lately.\'\r\n\r\n\'And his eyes?\'\r\n\r\n\'About the same.  He can write, and tell one card from another, but he\r\ncan\'t read--or rather it hurts him to do so, and he can\'t bear a strong\r\nlight.  But, Annaple, how are you?  That child is wearing you to a\r\nshadow.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! I\'m quite well--perfectly.  There, I think she is gone off at\r\nlast.  You had better walk her about a little, Emily; she will break\r\nout again if we try to put her in the cradle.\'\r\n\r\nAnd having handed over the child with only a very low murmur, Annaple\r\nleft her combined kitchen and nursery.  She flew at the flowers Nuttie\r\nhad brought like a thirsty person, crying, as she buried her face in\r\nthem, \'Now for beauty!  Now Mark will be refreshed!  Ah! here\'s a\r\npretty pickle for a reception room.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, don\'t put it away!  I could help you; I do so like that kind of\r\nwork.  It is so like old times.\'\r\n\r\n\'It must be put away, thank you, for Mark will be coming in.  And the\r\nsaying about the public washing of garments is specially true of one\'s\r\nown husband.  Ways and means are worrying to the masculine mind.\'\r\n\r\n\'I thought it was too early for Mark?\'\r\n\r\n\'He has an appointment to keep at Charing Cross or thereabouts, so I\r\nmade him promise to come in in time to "put a bit in his head," as our\r\nIrish charwoman says.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then I can take him.  I have the carriage, and I must be at home by\r\nhalf-past twelve.  I wish you would come too, Annaple.  There\'s plenty\r\nof room.  You could show the baby to nurse, and the boys could have a\r\ngood game.  I would send you back in the evening.  Mark could come on\r\nafter his business is done.\'\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, Nuttie, I can\'t to-day--for a whole heap of domestic\r\nreasons; but, if you can get Mark to come, do, it would be so good for\r\nhim.\'\r\n\r\n\'How is Mark?\'\r\n\r\n\'He is well, quite well,\' said Annaple; \'and so good and patient. But\r\nyou see, it does take it out of a man when that doleful little noise\r\nwon\'t stop all night!  We are both acquiring a form of somnambulism,\r\nbut when there\'s real out-of-door business to be done, it is not like\r\nproper sleep.\'\r\n\r\n\'Or when there\'s woman\'s indoor business, I am afraid,\' said Nuttie,\r\nmuch concerned at the extreme thinness of Annaple\'s face and hands, and\r\nthe weary look of her large eyes.\r\n\r\n\'Oh, one makes that up at odd times!\' she answered brightly.  \'One\r\nthing is, this work suits Mark, he feels that he can do it, and he gets\r\non well with the men.  They asked him to join in their club, and he was\r\nso much pleased.  He gets up subjects for them, and I am so glad he has\r\nsuch a pleasure and interest to keep him from missing the society he\r\nwas used to.\'\r\n\r\n\'It must be very good for them too.  Mr. Dutton said he really thought\r\nMark had kept them from going in for a strike.\'\r\n\r\n\'Besides the glory of the thing,\' said Annaple drawing herself up, \'Mr.\r\nDobbs thought so too, and raised us ten pounds; which made us able to\r\nimport that little Bridgefield lassie to hold baby--when--when Miss\r\nJenny will let her.  He has some law copying to do besides, but I don\'t\r\nlike that; it burns the candle at both ends, and he does get bad\r\nheadaches sometimes, and goes on all the same.\'\r\n\r\n\'You must both come and see my Wyn.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! I had never asked after him.  I suppose he is as pretty as ever,\'\r\nsaid Annaple, who secretly thought his beauty too girlish compared with\r\nher sturdy Billy.\r\n\r\n\'Prettier, I think, as he gets more expression.  We can\'t persuade\r\nourselves to cut his hair, and it looks so lovely on his sailor suit.\r\nAnd he is so good.  I could not have believed a child could be so quiet\r\nand considerate on a journey.  You should have seen him standing by my\r\nfather\'s knee in the railway carriage, and amusing him with all that\r\nwas to be seen, and stopping at the least hint that he was chattering\r\ntoo much.\'\r\n\r\n\'Billy is wonderfully helpful.  Ah--\' and Annaple\'s eyes lighted up as\r\nthe step that had music in\'t came up the stair; and as Mark came in,\r\nNuttie thought him grown older, his hair thinner, his shoulders\r\nrounded, and his office coat shabby, but she saw something in his\r\ncountenance there had never been before.  Ever since she had known him\r\nhe had worn a certain air of depression, or perhaps more truly of\r\nfailure and perplexity, which kept before her conscious mind the\r\nDesdichado on Ivanhoe\'s shield, even when he was a gentleman at ease at\r\nthe luxurious Rectory; but there was now not only the settled air of a\r\nman who had found his vocation, but something of the self-respect and\r\neagerness of one who was doing it well, and feeling himself valued.\r\n\r\n\'Is baby--\' he began.  \'Oh, Nuttie!  Are you there?  Mr. Dutton told me\r\nyou were coming.  How is my uncle?\'  And the voice was much brisker\r\nthan in the days of lawn-tennis.\r\n\r\n\'Father, father, look!\' cried the boy.\r\n\r\n\'Why, Billy-boy, you are set up!  Zouaves and chasseurs!  I see where\r\nthey came from.\'\r\n\r\nDuring the mixture of greetings and inquiries, admiration of the\r\nflowers, and the exhibition of Billy\'s treasures, Annaple glided away,\r\nand presently placed before him a tray, daintily benapkinned and set\r\nforth with a little cup of soup.\r\n\r\n\'Baby is really asleep, and Emily as proud as a Hielandman,\' she said.\r\n\'Now eat this, without more ado, for that good Nuttie is going to set\r\nyou down at Charing Cross.\'\r\n\r\n\'This is the way we spoil our husbands, Nuttie,\' said Mark. \'Refections\r\nserved up at every turn.\'\r\n\r\n\'Only bones!  The immortal pot au feu,\' said Annaple.  \'And you are to\r\ngo on after you have interviewed your man of steel, and have tea with\r\nNuttie, and pay your respects to your uncle, like a dutiful nephew.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, that I can\'t, Nannie; I promised Dobbs to go and see a man for\r\nhim, and I must come back as soon as I can after that.\'\r\n\r\nHe looked--as to figure and air--much more like his old self when he\r\nhad changed his coat.  They fed him, almost against his will, with a\r\nfew of the forced strawberries Nuttie had brought.  Billy pressed on\r\nhim wonders from a Paris bonbon box, and Annaple fastened a rose and a\r\npink in his button-hole, and came down to the street door with her boy\r\nto see him off.\r\n\r\n\'What do you think of her?\' was Mark\'s first inquiry.\r\n\r\n\'Think!  As Mr. Dutton said long ago, never was braver lady!\'\r\n\r\n\'Never was there a truer word!  I meant as to her health?  As to\r\ncourage, spirits, and temper, there is no question; I never saw them\r\nfail; but are they not almost too much for the frame?\' he asked\r\nanxiously.\r\n\r\nIt echoed Nuttie\'s fear, but she tried to frame a cheerful answer. \'She\r\nis very thin, but she seems well.\'\r\n\r\n\'She never complains, but I am sure her strength is not what it was.\r\nShe cannot walk out as she did at first.  Indeed, she gets no real rest\r\nday nor night, and there\'s no relieving her!\'\r\n\r\n\'She says you don\'t get much rest either.\'\r\n\r\n\'More than my share,\' said Mark.  \'The poor little thing never sleeps\r\nexcept in someone\'s arms, and if awake, is not content for a moment\r\nexcept in her mother\'s.\'\r\n\r\n\'And that has been going on four months?\'\r\n\r\n\'Three.  Ever since we brought her back from Redcastle.  I have nearly\r\ndetermined to move into some suburb when I get a rise at Michaelmas,\r\nunless she improves.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nurse might suggest something.\'\r\n\r\n\'Or at any rate tell us what to think.  We showed her to a doctor, and\r\nall he could propose was some kind of food, which was no more\r\nsuccessful than the rest.  Did you look at her, Nuttie?  She is a\r\npretty little thing when she is quiet, but she dwindles away--at least\r\nso it seems to me, though Annaple will not see it, and--and if we are\r\nnot permitted to keep the little one, I dread what the effect may be on\r\nher.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie said something about bravery and goodness, thinking in her heart\r\nthat, if the blow fell, it would be better for all than the perpetual\r\nsuffering of the poor little sickly being.\r\n\r\n\'Ah! you don\'t know what her affections are,\' said Mark.  \'You did not\r\nsee her when she lost her mother, and there had been no strain on her\r\npowers then.  However, I\'ve no business to croak.  Many a child gets\r\nover troubles of this kind, and, as Annaple says, little Jenny will be\r\nall the more to us for what we go through with her.\'\r\n\r\nThe carriage stopped, and Nuttie asked him if it would delay him too\r\nlong if she executed a commission about her father\'s glasses.  He had\r\nplenty of time, but she was delayed longer than she expected, and on\r\nher return was surprised to find that he had dropped asleep.\r\n\r\n\'Ah! that\'s what comes of a moment\'s quiet;\' he said, smiling.\r\n\r\n\'Fine quiet in the roar of Ludgate Hill!\'\r\n\r\n\'To a Cockney \'tis as the mill to the miller!  I like the full stir and\r\ntide,\' he added, looking out upon it.  \'I never knew what life was\r\nbefore!\'\r\n\r\n\'I should have thought you never knew what hardness and hard work were.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s just it,\' he answered, smiling.  \'The swing of it is\r\nexhilaration--very different from being a cumberer of the ground.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Mark, all the privations and anxiety!\'\r\n\r\n\'The privation! that\'s nothing.  Indeed I am afraid--yes, I am ashamed\r\nto say--it falls more on my dear wife than myself, but if we can only\r\nwear through a year or two we shall get a further rise, and my poor\r\nAnnaple may get out of this drudgery.  Please God, she and the little\r\none can stand it for a time, and I think she has a spring within her\r\nthat will;\' then, as he saw tears in his cousin\'s eyes, he added,\r\n\'Don\'t be unhappy about it, Nuttie; I have had it in my mind ever so\r\nlong to tell you that the finding you at Micklethwayte was the best\r\nthing that ever happened to me!\'\r\n\r\nYes, so far as character went, Ursula could believe that it had been\r\nso.  He was twice the man he would have been without the incentive to\r\nwork, and the constant exercise of patience and cheerfulness; but her\r\nheart was heavy with apprehension that the weight of the trial might be\r\ntoo heavy.  To her eyes the baby\'s life seemed extremely doubtful, and\r\nAnnaple looked so fragile that the increase of her burthens, any\r\nsaddening of the heart, might destroy her elasticity, and crush her\r\noutright; while even Mark seemed to her to be toiling so close within\r\nthe limits of his powers that a straw might break the camel\'s back!\r\n\r\nShe longed to talk to Mr. Dutton about them, but she found herself\r\ndoomed to a day that perhaps Annaple would have thought more trying\r\nthan her harrowed life.  She was a little later than she had intended,\r\nand her father had been waiting impatiently to have a note read to him,\r\nso he growled at her impatience to run after \'that Scotch girl.\'  And\r\nthe note happened to be of an irritating nature; moreover, the cutlets\r\nat luncheon were said to be akin to indiarubber, and there was the\r\nwrong flavour in the sauce.  Ursula let that cook do what she pleased\r\nwithout remonstrance.\r\n\r\nEven Alwyn did not afford as much satisfaction as usual, for the boy\r\nwas in high spirits and wanted to blow a little trumpet, which was more\r\nthan his father could stand.  He was very good when this was silenced,\r\nbut he then began to rush round the room daring his sister to catch the\r\nwild colt as he went by.  This had likewise to be stopped, with the\r\nmurmur that Ursula spoilt the child.\r\n\r\nShe tried to compose matters by turning out the old toys in the\r\nottoman, but Alwyn had outgrown most of them, and did not care for any\r\nexcept a certain wooden donkey, minus one ear and a leg, which went by\r\nthe name of Sambo, and had absorbed a good deal of his affection.  He\r\nhad with difficulty been consoled for Sambo being left behind, and now\r\nturned over everything with considerable clatter in search of him.\r\nAlas!  Sambo could nowhere be found in the room, and Alwyn dashed off\r\nto inquire of all the household after him.  His father meanwhile\r\ngrowled at the child\'s noise, and went on trying the glasses Nuttie had\r\nbrought, and pronouncing each pair in turn useless, vowing that it was\r\nno use to send her anywhere.\r\n\r\nUpon this, back came Alwyn, terribly distressed and indignant, for he\r\nhad extracted from the housemaid left in charge, who was as cross as\r\nshe was trustworthy, \'What! that old broken thing, Master Egremont? I\r\nthrew it on the fire!  I\'d never have thought a young gentleman of your\r\nage would have cared for such rubbish as that.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are a wicked cruel woman,\' returned Alwyn, with flashing eyes; \'I\r\nshall tell papa and sister of you.\'\r\n\r\nAnd in he flew, sobbing with grief and wrath for the dear Sambo,\r\nfeeling as if it had been a live donkey burnt to death, and hiding his\r\nface on his sister\'s breast for consolation.\r\n\r\n\'Come, come, Wyn,\' said his father, who did not brook interruption;\r\n\'here\'s half a sovereign to go and buy a new donkey.\'\r\n\r\n\'It won\'t be Sambo,\' said Alwyn ruefully.\r\n\r\n\'But you should thank papa,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Thank you, papa,\' he said, with quivering lip, \'but I don\'t want a new\r\none.  Oh Sambo, Sambo! burnt!\' and he climbed on Nuttie\'s lap, hid his\r\nface against her and cried, but her comfortings were broken off by,\r\n\'How can you encourage the child in being so foolish?  Have done, Wyn;\r\ndon\'t be such a baby!  Go out with nurse and buy what you like, but I\r\ncan\'t have crying here.\'\r\n\r\nHe tried to stop in sheer amazement, but the ground swell of sob could\r\nnot be controlled.  Nuttie was going to lead him away, and console him\r\nwith more imaginative sympathy than could be expected from the maids,\r\nbut her father sharply called her back.  He wanted her himself, and\r\nindeed there was no question which was the worse spoilt child.  He\r\nmight idolise Alwyn, but not so as to clash with his own comforts.  The\r\nglasses being unsuccessful, Nuttie proposed to drive back to Ludgate\r\nHill for him to choose for himself, but he would not hear of going into\r\nthe heat of the City, and growled at her for thinking of such a thing.\r\n\r\nThey took an aimless drive instead in the park, and Nuttie was nearly\r\nbaked while the carriage was stopped for her father to have a long talk\r\nover the prospects of the Derby day with one of his most unpleasant\r\nassociates, who stood leaning over the door on the shady side of the\r\ncarriage, no one recking how little protection she derived from her\r\nsmall fringed parasol.\r\n\r\nShe came home tired out, and thankful that her father went to rest in\r\nhis own room.  She climbed to the nursery, thinking to share Alwyn\'s\r\ntea and comfort him, but she found only nurse there.  Nurse had a bad\r\nfoot, and dreaded hot pavement, so she had sent Master Alwyn out with\r\nher subordinate, a country girl, to play in Mr. Dutton\'s garden till it\r\nshould be cool enough to go and make his purchase, and a message had\r\nsince arrived that he was going to drink tea there, and Mr. Dutton\r\nwould take him out.\r\n\r\nHis sister envied him the green shades, and had just done her best to\r\ncool the back drawing-room and rest herself with a book, when Mr. Fane\r\nwas announced.  He talked pleasantly enough, and lingered and lingered,\r\nno doubt intending to be asked to dinner, but she was equally\r\ndetermined to do no such thing.  She had heard enough of races for one\r\nday, she thought, and at last he took his leave, only just before she\r\ndressed for dinner.\r\n\r\n\'I thought Fane was here,\' said Mr. Egremont as he came in; no doubt\r\ntold by Gregorio.\r\n\r\n\'He has been, but he is gone.\'\r\n\r\n\'You didn\'t ask him to stay and dine?\'\r\n\r\n\'I did not know you wished it.\'\r\n\r\n\'You might have known that I should have liked to see him.  I suppose\r\nyou think your sweet self society enough for any man?\'\r\n\r\n\'I am sorry--\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sick of hearing you are sorry!  I believe there\'s nothing you like\r\nso well as doing an ungracious thing to a friend of mine.\'\r\n\r\nNuttie had learnt to hold her tongue on such occasions.\r\n\r\nDinner was nearly over, and her father had been grumbling again at\r\nhaving no one to take a hand at cards with him, when the door opened a\r\nlittle way, and Alwyn\'s pretty glowing face looked in.  He was come to\r\nsay good-night rather later than usual, and he ran up to his sister\r\nwith a little bouquet of yellow banksia and forget-me-nots. \'Mithter\r\nButton\'--so Alwyn called him--\'sent you this.  He said you would like\r\nit, \'cause it came from one that grew at Mittletwait.  And oh, look,\r\nlook!\'\r\n\r\nHe was hugging a little ship, which he proudly exhibited, while his\r\nfather\'s brow had darkened at the message.  \'Did you buy that?\' asked\r\nhis sister.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, Mr. Button went with me, and we sailed it.  We sailed it by the\r\nfountain in Mr. Button\'s garden, And we made a storm!\'\r\n\r\nHe danced about with glee, and Mr. Egremont observed, \'A dear purchase\r\nfor ten shillings.  Did it cost all that, Wyn?\'\r\n\r\n\'They gived me a big silver half-crown, and I gived that to a little\r\nboy what came to speak to Mr. Button, and had his toes through his\r\nboots, and he was so glad.\'\r\n\r\n\'Your money is not for beggars, Wyn.\'\r\n\r\n\'The little boy was not a beggar, papa.  He came with a newspaper to\r\nMr. Button, and he is so good to his poor sick mother,\' said Alwyn.\r\n\'See, see, sister!\' turning the prow of his small vessel towards her,\r\nand showing a word on it in pencil which he required her to spell out.\r\nIt was Ursula.\r\n\r\n\'Oh Wynnie!\' she said, duly flattered, \'did Mr. Dutton do that?\'\r\n\r\n\'He held my hand, and I did!\' cried Alwyn, triumphantly, \'and he will\r\npaint it on Saturday.  Then it will dry all Sunday, and not come off,\r\nso it will be the Ursula for ever and always.\'\r\n\r\nHere nurse claimed her charge; and when the goodnights were over, and a\r\nmurmur recommenced, Nuttie suggested that if Mr. Dutton was at home\r\nperhaps he would come in and make up the game, but she encountered the\r\nold humour.  \'I\'ll tell you what, Ursula, I\'ll not have that umbrella\r\nfellow encouraged about the house, and if that child is to be made the\r\nmedium of communication, I\'ll put a stop to it.\'\r\n\r\nThe words were spoken just as Gregorio had entered the room with a\r\nhandkerchief of his master\'s.  Nuttie, colouring deeply at the insult,\r\nmet his triumphant eyes, bit her lips, and deigned no word of reply.\r\n\r\nAn undefined but very slight odour, that told her of opium smoke,\r\npervaded the stairs that night.  It was the only refuge from\r\nfretfulness; but her heart ached for her father, herself, and most of\r\nall for her little brother.  And was she to be cut off from her only\r\ncounsellor?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIII.\r\n\r\nTHE LOST HEIR.\r\n\r\n  \'Seemed to the boy some comrade gay\r\n  Led him forth to the woods to play.\'--SCOTT.\r\n\r\n\r\nThough it was the Derby day, Mr. Egremont\'s racing days were over, and\r\nhe only took his daughter with him in quest of the spectacles he\r\nwanted.  When they came back, Nuttie mounted to the nursery, but no\r\nlittle brother met her on the stairs, and she found nurse in deep\r\ndispleasure with her subordinate.\r\n\r\n\'I sent him out with Ellen to play in the garden at Springfield, and\r\nswim his ship, where he couldn\'t come to no harm,\' said nurse; \'being\r\nthat my foot is that bad I can\'t walk the length of the street; and\r\nwhat does the girl do but lets that there Gregorio take the dear child\r\nand go--goodness knows where--without her.\'\r\n\r\n\'I\'m sure, ma\'am,\' said the girl crying, \'I would never have done it,\r\nbut Mr. Gregory said as how \'twas his papa\'s wish.\'\r\n\r\n\'What was?\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'That he shouldn\'t never go and play at Mr. Dutton\'s again,\' said Ellen.\r\n\r\n\'I told her she was to take her orders off me, and no one else,\'\r\nreturned nurse, \'except, of course, you, Miss Egremont, as has the\r\nright.\'\r\n\r\n\'Quite so; you should have told Mr. Gregorio so, Ellen.\'\r\n\r\n\'I did, ma\'am, but he said those was Mr. Egremont\'s orders; and he\r\nsaid,\' cried the girl, unable to withstand the pleasure of repeating\r\nsomething disagreeable, \'that Mr. Egremont wouldn\'t have no messengers\r\nbetween you and a low tradesman fellow, as made umbrellas, and wanted\r\nto insinuate himself in here.\'\r\n\r\n\'That\'s quite enough, Ellen; I don\'t want to hear any impertinences.\r\nPerhaps you did not understand his foreign accent.  Did he say where he\r\nwas going?\'\r\n\r\n\'I think he said he\'d take him to the Serpentine to sail his ship,\'\r\nsaid Ellen, disposed to carry on asseverations of the correctness of\r\nher report, but nurse ordered her off the scene, and proceeded, as a\r\nconfidential servant, \'The girl had no call to repeat it; but there\'s\r\nnot a doubt of it he did say something of the sort.  There\'s not one of\r\nus but knows he is dead against Mr. Dutton, because he tried to get\r\nmaster to get to sleep without that nasty opium smoke of his.\'\r\n\r\nThere was bitter feud between nurse and valet, and Nuttie could have\r\nexchanged with her many a lament, but she contented herself with\r\nsaying, \'I wish he would let Master Alwyn alone.  It is high time they\r\nshould come in.\'\r\n\r\n\'The child will be tired to death, and all dirt!  His nice new sailor\r\nsuit too!  Going grubbing about at the Serpentine with no one knows\r\nwho, as isn\'t fit for a young gentleman,\' moaned nurse.\r\n\r\nThis, however, was the worst fear she entertained, and it was with a\r\ncertain malicious satisfaction that she heard her master\'s bell for\r\nGregorio.\r\n\r\nNuttie descended to explain, and whereas the need was not very urgent,\r\nand she looked distressed and angered at the valet, her father received\r\nher complaint with, \'Well, the boy is getting too big to be tied for\r\never to a nursery-maid.  It will do him good to go about with a man.\'\r\n\r\nBut as dressing-time came on, and still neither Gregorio nor Alwyn\r\nappeared, Mr. Egremont became impatient, and declared that the valet\r\nhad no business to keep the child out so long; indeed, he would sooner\r\nhave taken alarm but for Nuttie\'s manifest agony of anxiety, starting\r\nand rushing to listen at every ring at the bell or sound of wheels near\r\nat hand.  At last, at eight o\'clock, there was a peal of the servants\'\r\nbell, and the footman who answered it turned round to the anxious\r\ncrowd: \'Mr. Gregory!  He just asked if the child was come home, and\r\nwent off like lightning.\'\r\n\r\n\'The villain!  He\'s lost him!\' shrieked nurse, with a wild scream. \'Run\r\nafter him, James!  Catch him up!\' suggested the butler at the same\r\nmoment.  \'Make him tell where he saw him last!\'\r\n\r\nJames was not a genius, but the hall boy, an alert young fellow, had\r\nalready dashed down the steps in pursuit, and came up with the valet so\r\nas to delay him till the other servants stood round, and Gregorio\r\nturned back with them, pale, breathless, evidently terribly dismayed\r\nand unwilling to face his master, who stood at the top of the steps,\r\nwhite with alarm and wrath.\r\n\r\n\'Sir,\' cried Gregorio, with a stammering of mixed languages, \'I have\r\nbeen searching everywhere!  I was going to give notice to the police.\r\nJe ferai tout!  Je le trouverai.\'\r\n\r\n\'Where did you lose him?\' demanded Mr. Egremont in a hoarse voice, such\r\nas Nuttie had never heard.\r\n\r\n\'In the Park, near the bridge over the Serpentine.  I was speaking for\r\na few moments to a friend.  Bah!  Il etait parti.  Mais je le\r\ntrouverai.  Parker, he seeks too.  Fear not, sir, I shall find him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Find him, you scoundrel, or never dare to see me again!  I\'ve borne\r\nwith your insolences long, and now you\'ve brought them to a height. Go,\r\nI say, find my boy!\' exclaimed Mr. Egremont, with a fierce oath and\r\npassionate gesture, and Gregorio vanished again.\r\n\r\n\'Bring the carriage--no, call a cab;\' commanded Mr. Egremont, snatching\r\nup his hat.  \'Who is this Parker?\'\r\n\r\nThe servants hesitated, but the butler said he believed the man to be a\r\nfriend of Gregorio\'s employed at one of the clubs.  Nuttie meanwhile\r\nbegging her father not to go without her, flew upstairs to put on her\r\nhat, and coming down at full speed found that Mr. Dutton, passing by\r\nand seeing the open door and the terrified servants on the steps, had\r\nturned in to ask what was the matter, and was hearing in no measured\r\nterms from Mr. Egremont how the child had been taken away from his\r\nnurse and lost in the Park while that scamp Gregorio was chattering to\r\nsome good-for-nothing friend.\r\n\r\nTo Nuttie\'s great relief, Mr. Dutton offered to go with the father to\r\nassist in the search, and the coachman, far too anxious and excited to\r\nlet his master go without him in a cab, contrived to bring up the\r\ncarriage.  Some of the servants were ordered off to the various police\r\noffices.  Poor nurse, who was nearly distracted, started in a hansom on\r\nher own account, persuaded that she should see and recognise traces of\r\nher darling at the scene of his loss, and she almost raced the\r\ncarriage, which was bound for the same spot.\r\n\r\nSluggish natures like Mr. Egremont\'s can sometimes be roused to great\r\nviolence, and then pour forth the long pent-up accumulations kept back\r\nby indolence and indifference.  His only occupation during the rapid\r\ndrive was to vituperate his valet, the curse of his life, he said.  To\r\nhear him talk, it would have seemed as if Gregorio had been the tyrant\r\nwho had kept him in bondage all these years, fully aware of his\r\nfalsehood, peculation, and other rascality, but as unable to break the\r\nyoke as if he had been in truth the slave of anything but his own evil\r\nhabit and helpless acquiescence.\r\n\r\nWould it last if Gregorio made his appearance at that instant with\r\nAlwyn in his hand?  Or even, as Mr. Dutton confidently predicted, a\r\npoliceman might bring the boy home, before many hours were passed. The\r\nchief doubt here was that Alwyn\'s defective pronunciation, which had\r\nbeen rather foolishly encouraged, might make it difficult to understand\r\nhis mode  of saying his own name, or even that of the street, if he\r\nknew it perfectly; but the year he had been absent from London had\r\nprevented him from acquiring the curious ready local instinct of the\r\ntrue town child, and he had been so much guarded and watched that he\r\nwas likely to be utterly at a loss when left alone; and Nuttie was\r\nwretched at the thought of his terror and loneliness, even while Mr.\r\nDutton told her of speedy recoveries of lost children through kind\r\npeople or the police.\r\n\r\nThey found all the officials of the Park already aware and on the\r\nalert, and quite certain of the impossibility of nurse\'s prime dread\r\nthat the boy had fallen into the water unseen by any one and been\r\ndrowned.  She was even ready to look into every bush, in case he had\r\nbeen frightened and hidden himself; and nothing would satisfy her but\r\nto stay making these researches, when her master had decided on\r\nendeavouring to find \'Parker\' at the club, and to ascertain from him\r\nparticulars of time and place.\r\n\r\nHe was found there.  The dinner-hour had brought him back, he being a\r\nman in authority there, very well dressed and deferential, declaring\r\nhimself immensely distressed at the occurrence, and at having accosted\r\nGregorio and attracted his attention.  It was about four o\'clock, he\r\nthought, and he described the exact spot where the little boy had been\r\nsailing his vessel fastened to a string.  They might have been talking\r\ntwenty minutes or half an hour when Gregorio missed his charge, and\r\nsince that time both had been doing all in their power to find him,\r\nuntil half-past seven, when he had to return to his club, and Gregorio\r\nwent to see whether the child had been taken home.\r\n\r\nBy this time Mr. Egremont looked so utterly exhausted, that Mr. Dutton\r\navailed himself of the hope that the boy might be found safe at home to\r\ntake him back; but alas! nothing had been heard there.\r\n\r\nThe poor man was in a restless, unmanageable state of excitement,\r\nalmost as terrifying to his daughter as the distress that occasioned\r\nit.  He swallowed a tumblerful of claret, but would not eat nor go to\r\nbed; and indeed, Gregorio alone having had the personal charge of him,\r\nlatterly sleeping in his dressing-room, none of the other servants knew\r\nwhat to do for him.  Mr. Dutton agreed with her that it would be better\r\nto send for his doctor, as probably he ought to have a sedative, and\r\nneither would take the responsibility of giving it; while he himself\r\ndeclared he neither would nor could rest till he had his boy again.\r\n\r\nThe doctor was dining out, and they had two terrible hours; while Mr.\r\nEgremont paced to the windows; threw himself on the sofa; denounced\r\nGregorio; or, for a change, all the system of police which had made no\r\ndiscovery; and Ursula for letting the boy be so helpless.  Mr. Dutton\r\nsometimes diverted his attention for a few minutes, and hoped he would\r\ndoze, but the least sound brought him to his feet again, and the only\r\ncongenial occupation was the composition of a description of poor\r\nlittle Alwyn\'s person and dress, which set Nuttie crying so\r\nuncontrollably, that she had to run out of the room.\r\n\r\nDr. Brownlow came at last, and was very kind and helpful, taking the\r\ncommand, and insisting that Mr. Egremont should go to bed, and take the\r\ndose which he mixed.  Broadbent, the butler, was to take Gregorio\'s\r\nplace, but he was a ponderous man, without much tact, and unused to the\r\nvalet\'s office.  \'I might just as well have a rhinoceros about me,\'\r\nsaid Mr. Egremont, in a fit of irritation; and it ended, Nuttie hardly\r\nknew how, in Mr. Dutton\'s going upstairs to smooth matters.  He came\r\ndown after a time and said: \'I am not satisfied to leave him alone or\r\nto Broadbent; I have his consent to my sleeping in the dressing-room.\r\nI am just going home to fetch my things.  Let me find you gone when I\r\ncome back.  You will hear no more to-night.  Even if he is found, they\r\nwill keep him till morning.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is of no use; I can\'t sleep.\'\r\n\r\n\'Even if you don\'t, the mere restful position will make you fitter for\r\nthe morrow.  Will you promise me to undress and really go to bed?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes! if you say I must,\' said Nuttie drearily; following an\r\ninstinct of obedience.\r\n\r\n\'And remember,\' he said, \'though I do not say it will be so, this may\r\nbe deliverance from bondage.\'\r\n\r\n\'But what a terrible deliverance!\'\r\n\r\n\'Bonds are not burst without something terrible.  No; don\'t be\r\nfrightened.  Remember there is safekeeping for that sweet little\r\nfellow, wherever he may be.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Mr. Dutton, if I could pray for him; but the turmoil seems to have\r\ndriven away all such things!  My boy, my boy, where is he now? Who has\r\nheard him say his little prayers?\'\r\n\r\n\'His Heavenly Father has; of that we may be secure.  You will feel it\r\nin the quiet of your own room.  Good-night.\'\r\n\r\n\'And I shall know you are praying, better than I can,\' murmured Nuttie,\r\nas she returned his good-night, and crept up to her chamber.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIV.\r\n\r\nFETTERS RENT.\r\n\r\n  \'The gods are just, and of our pleasant sins\r\n  Make whips to scourge us.\'--King Lear.\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was no real sleep for Ursula that short summer night.  She saw\r\nthe early dawn, listened to the distant roll of market-carts, and\r\nwondered when it would be reasonable to be afoot, and ready to hear, if\r\naught there was to hear.  At any hour after seven, surely the finders\r\nwould have mercy and bring the welcome news.  And just before seven she\r\nfell asleep, deeply, soundly, and never woke till past eight, but that\r\nwas just enough to revive the power of hope, and give the sense of a\r\nnew day.  But there was nothing to hear--no news.  She found Mr. Dutton\r\nin the dining-room.  He had had to administer another draught to her\r\nfather, and had left him in a sleep which would probably last for some\r\ntime.  If she would go and sit in the outer room, after her breakfast,\r\nhe would go out to obtain intelligence.\r\n\r\n\'You must have some breakfast,\' she said, ringing the bell, and\r\nwistfully looking over the blinds; then exclaiming: \'Oh, there\'s Mark!\r\nHas he heard anything?\' and out she darted, opening the door before he\r\nrang.  \'Mark! have you found him?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' he said gravely, looking utterly amazed as she clasped her\r\nhands, and seemed ready to fling herself on his neck with joy.  \'I came\r\nbecause it will be a great shock to my uncle.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then it is so!  Nurse was right,\' said Nuttie, turning deadly pale,\r\nand standing as if before a firing platoon.  \'Tell me, Mark, where did\r\nthey find him?\'\r\n\r\n\'At the Faringdon Station.  I was sent for to identify him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Stay,\' said Mr. Dutton, as there was a wild horrified look in Nuttie\'s\r\neyes.  \'Do you mean little Alwyn?\'\r\n\r\n\'Little Alwyn!  No, certainly not.  What of him?\'\r\n\r\n\'Gregorio managed to lose him in the park yesterday,\' put in Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'That accounts for it, then,\' said Mark.  \'No, it was Gregorio himself,\r\npoor man.  He was knocked down by the engine, and killed on the spot,\r\njust by the station, at eleven o\'clock last night.  Our name was found\r\non him, and I was sent for early this morning.  There was no doubt\r\nabout it, so I came on here at once to let my uncle know, little\r\nthinking--\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, it is dreadful!\' cried Nuttie, sinking into a chair.  \'Do you\r\nremember, my father told him never to see his face again unless he\r\nfound Alwyn?\'\r\n\r\nBroadbent came in at the moment with the coffee-pot, and stood\r\nsuspended, as he was told what had happened, Mark adding the detail:\r\n\'He was crossing the line in front of the engine.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, sir,\' said the butler.  \'It is an awful dispensation.  No doubt\r\nhe knew it was all up with him.  You may not be aware, sir, of the\r\nsubject of his conversation in the park.  Mr. Parker had just seen a\r\ntelegram of the result of the Derby, and he had heavy bets on Lady\r\nEdina.  I am afraid, sir, there can be no doubt that he found a\r\nvoluntary grave.\'\r\n\r\n\'We will not talk of that.  We cannot judge,\' said Mark, shuddering. \'I\r\nsaid I would send some one from here to arrange what was to be done\r\nafter the inquest.\'\r\n\r\nBroadbent immediately undertook to go, if his master did not require\r\nhim, and this was thought advisable, as his services were certainly not\r\nacceptable to Mr. Egremont.  Mark had thought himself likely to be\r\ndetained and had provided for his absence, and the awe-stricken trio\r\nwere consulting together over the breakfast-table, eating mechanically,\r\nfrom the very exhaustion of agitation, when the door opened, and Mr.\r\nEgremont in his dressing-gown was among them, exclaiming: \'You are\r\nkeeping it from me.\'  He had been wakened by the whispers and rushes of\r\nthe excited maids, had rung his bell in vain, dressed himself as best\r\nhe could after so many years of dependence, and stumbled downstairs,\r\nwhere, as with his daughter, it was something like a relief to know\r\nthat hope was not extinguished in Alwyn\'s case.  But Mr. Egremont was\r\nin a very trembling, broken condition, and much overcome by his valet\'s\r\nend after so many years of intimate association.  Certainly, if either\r\nof the others had so parted with the man, it would have been a horror\r\nin the recollection, but he did not seem to dwell on it; and, indeed,\r\nattention was distracted by every sound at the door, since each might\r\nbring news of the missing child.\r\n\r\nOne of these tantalising rings proved to be a policeman with poor\r\nGregorio\'s keys, and a demand for an investigation into any papers he\r\nmight have left which would show his state of mind.  Mr. Egremont was\r\nvery much annoyed, declaring that he would have no stranger meddle with\r\nthem, and that he saw no use in such prying.  What difference could it\r\nmake to any living creature?  However, when he found there was no help\r\nfor it, he said he must do it himself. Nuttie offered to help, but was\r\nsharply, strongly refused.  Mark alone might and should help.\r\n\r\nThen Mr. Dutton volunteered to go and explain matters to Mr. Dobbs, so\r\nas to get freedom for Mark for at least the remainder of the day. He\r\nwould call at the police offices and see what was doing in the search,\r\nput forward the advertisements, and obtain that the Serpentine should\r\nbe dragged, for he saw that only that measure would remove one great\r\nterror from these anxious hearts.\r\n\r\n\'And,\' he said to Mark, \'with your permission, I will bring back Mrs.\r\nEgremont and the children if they will do me the honour to become my\r\nguests.  She will be a comfort to Miss Egremont, and then you will be\r\nat hand in the evening.\'\r\n\r\nMark could only be thankful, and presently addressed himself to the\r\ninvestigation, which his uncle insisted should be made in his own\r\npresence, though the opiate kept him for the most part dozing in an\r\narm-chair, only rousing up now and then by some noise at the front\r\ndoor, or putting queries, the replies to which startled him more and\r\nmore, as he grew more wakeful and Mark proceeded.\r\n\r\nAll, except a few unimportant bills and a betting-book, was locked into\r\na dressing-case that had once belonged to Mr. Egremont, and had tricks\r\nof secret drawers that only he could explain.  It was full of papers,\r\nand they were a strange revelation that Mr. Egremont might well wish to\r\nwithhold from his daughter.  They went very far back, and of course did\r\nnot come out in order of chronology, nor would Mark have understood\r\nthem but for exclamations and comments here and there from his uncle.\r\n\r\nEverything seemed to be there,--the old passport and certificate to\r\nGregorio Savelli, when he left his Savoyard home to be a waiter at a\r\nhotel; a few letters in Italian, probably from his parents, which Mark\r\ncould not read, but which soon ceased; the counter-signed character\r\nwith which he had entered General Egremont\'s service; and then came a\r\nnote or two signed A. P. E., which Mr. Egremont regarded with great\r\nannoyance, though they only consisted of such phrases as \'Back on\r\nWednesday.  Find an excuse,\' or in French, \'Envoyez moi la petite\r\nboite!\'  \'Que la porte soit ouverte apres minuit.\'\r\n\r\n\'That was the way,\' groaned Mr. Egremont.  \'The scoundrel! he kept all\r\nthose to be able to show me up to the General if he chose!  I was a\r\nyoung man then, Mark, not the straitlaced lad you\'ve always been. And\r\nthe General!  A bad old dog he was, went far beyond what I ever did,\r\nbut for all that he had no notion of any one going any way but his own,\r\nand wanted to rein me in as tight as if he had been an epitome of all\r\nthe virtues.  And Gregorio seemed a good-natured young fellow then, and\r\nmade things easy for me, though no doubt he meant to have me in his\r\nhands, in case I tried to shake him off.\'\r\n\r\nAnother discovery affected him far more.  It was of a letter in Alice\'s\r\nhandwriting, addressed to Captain Egremont, in the yacht Ninon--poste\r\nrestante, Madeira.  He had never seen it, never known of its existence;\r\nGregorio had gone to inquire for the letters, and had suppressed it.\r\nMr. Egremont had wondered how he had become aware of the marriage.  His\r\nknowledge had from that time been used as a means of enforcing the need\r\nof a good understanding with the heir.  Mr. Egremont was much moved by\r\nthe sight of the letter, and its date, from Dieppe, about six months\r\nafter he had left his young wife there. He made Mark give it to him\r\nunread, handled it tenderly, struggled to read the delicate pointed\r\nwriting to himself, but soon deferred the attempt, observing, \'There,\r\nthere, I can\'t stand it now!  But you see, Mark,\' he added after an\r\ninterval, \'I was not altogether the heartless brute you thought me.\'\r\n\r\nMark, as he told his wife afterwards, could not help thinking of the\r\nold preamble to indictments, \'By the temptation of the devil.\'\r\n\r\nAnd by and by, out of a pocket-book bearing the date of the General\'s\r\ndeath, came a copy of the certificate of the baptism of Ursula Alice,\r\ndaughter of Alwyn Piercefield and Alice Egremont, together with that\r\naddress which Miss Headworth had left at Dieppe to gratify Alice\'s\r\nforlorn idea of a possible rescue, and which Gregorio had asseverated\r\nto be non-existent.\r\n\r\nDoubtless he infinitely preferred his master\'s wandering bachelor life\r\nto the resumption of marriage ties, and thus he had contrived to keep\r\nMr. Egremont from meeting the Houghtons at Florence.  At the same time\r\nthe uncertainty as to Alice\'s fate had prevented any other marriage.\r\nGregorio had taken care that, if Mr. Egremont had been villain enough\r\nto make such an attempt, he should know that his secret could be\r\nbrought to light.\r\n\r\nCompared with all this wickedness, the proofs of fraud and dishonesty\r\nwere entirely unimportant.  Gambling had evidently been a passion with\r\nthe valet, and peculation had followed, and Mark could have traced out\r\nthe full tide before the reinstatement of Mrs. Egremont in her home,\r\nthe gradual ebb during her reign, the diminished restraint under her\r\ndaughter.  The other servants had formerly been implicated, but, except\r\na young groom and footman, Mark thought the present set quite free from\r\nthe taint, and was glad to acquit Broadbent.  But the last telegrams\r\nand the betting-book in the unhappy man\'s pocket confirmed Parker\'s\r\nevidence that of late he had staked almost madly, and had risked sums\r\nfar beyond any means he could raise upon the horse which had failed.\r\nThe bailiff at Bridgefield had, it had long been guessed, played into\r\nhis hands, but to what an extent Mark only now discovered.\r\n\r\nThe result was that what he had learnt in the Park had so astounded him\r\nthat his inattention to the child had not been wonderful.  He had--as\r\nParker testified--sought the little fellow vehemently, and had he been\r\nsuccessful, he might yet have made some effort, trusting to his\r\nmaster\'s toleration; but the loss and reproach had made him an\r\nabsolutely desperate man.  Was it blind flight or self-destruction?\r\nThat he had money about him, having cashed a cheque of his master\'s,\r\nfavoured the first idea, and no one would too curiously inquire whether\r\nMr. Egremont was aware of the amount.\r\n\r\nIt was only too true that, as he had said, Gregorio Savelli had been\r\nthe curse of his life, having become one of the whips left by pleasant\r\nvices, and the breaking of the yoke had been not only at a terrible\r\nprice, but, to a man in his half-blind and invalid condition, the\r\nactual loss of the person on whom he had depended was a privation.  Dr.\r\nBrownlow, however, knew of a good man-servant just set at liberty by\r\nthe death of an invalid master, and promised to send him on trial.\r\n\r\nIt was a day of agitations and disappointments, a sample of many that\r\nwere to follow.  There was not a sound of a bell that did not make\r\nanxious hearts throb.  And oh! how many were spent on vain reports, on\r\nmere calls of sympathy by acquaintance whom the father and sister could\r\nnot see, and on notes of inquiry or condolence that Nuttie had to\r\nanswer.\r\n\r\nAnnaple came and was a great help and support to her.  Poor nurse,\r\noblivious of her bad foot, or perhaps, willing to wreak vengeance on it\r\nas the cause of all the mischief, had insisted on continuing her search\r\nin the morning under all the thorns and rhododendrons where she thought\r\nthe dear lamb might have hidden and cried himself to sleep, and at last\r\nhad been brought home in a cab quite worn out and despairing.  But the\r\nscreaming baby proved to be a much better comforter to her than any\r\namount of reasonable argument.  To soothe it, to understand what ailed\r\nit, to find suitable food for it, was an occupation which made the\r\nsuspense less intolerable.  The very handling of an infant would have\r\nbeen congenial; and a sickly crying one was only too interesting.\r\nWillie was too near her darling\'s age to be a welcome sight, but he was\r\nalready a prime pet with the servants at Springfield; and Annaple,\r\nsecure that her children were in safe and experienced hands, and\r\noverflowing with motherly sympathy for the grievous loss, was ready to\r\ndevote herself to Nuttie, whether by talk, by letter writing, or by\r\nseeing inquiring friends.  She did not expect to be of any use to Mr.\r\nEgremont, who had always held aloof from and disliked \'the giggling\r\nScotch girl,\' but who came drearily wandering at an unexpected time\r\ninto the room where she was sitting with his daughter, and presently\r\nwas involved in their conversation.  Whether it was the absence of the\r\npoor familiar, or that Annaple was no longer a giggling girl, but a\r\nbrave, cheerful wife and mother, it was certain that he found the same\r\ncomfort and support in her presence as did Nuttie.  When fits of\r\nrestless misery and despair pressed hardest upon him, it was soon\r\nperceived that Annaple\'s cheerful tact enabled her to deal with him as\r\nno one else could do.  There was the restraint of courtesy towards her,\r\nsuch as had worn out towards his daughter, and besides her sanguine\r\noptimist spirit never became so depressed as did poor Nuttie\'s.  Mark\r\nwent by day to his work, but came back to dine at his uncle\'s, hear the\r\nreports, and do what he could for him; and meantime Annaple spent the\r\nchief part of the day in aiding Nuttie and Mr. Egremont, while her baby\r\nreally showed signs of improvement in nurse\'s keeping.  And so the days\r\nwent on, while every endeavour was made to trace the child, but with no\r\nresult but bitter disappointment.  Twice, strayed children, younger\r\nthan Alwyn--one even a girl--were brought as the lost boy, and the\r\nadvertisements bore fruit in more than one harassing and heartless\r\ncorrespondence with wretches who professed to be ready to restore the\r\nchild, on promises of absolute secrecy, and sums of money sent\r\nbeforehand, with all sorts of precautions against interference from the\r\npolice.\r\n\r\nThe first of these created great excitement, and the pursuit was\r\ncommitted to Mr. Dutton.  When it proved abortive, Mr. Egremont\'s\r\ndisappointment and anger were great, and he could not be persuaded that\r\nall was not the fault of Mr. Dutton\'s suspicion and precaution in\r\nholding back the money, nor could any one persuade him that it was mere\r\nimposture.  When another ill-written enigmatical letter arrived, he\r\ninsisted that it was from the same quarter, and made Broadbent conduct\r\nthe negotiations, with the result that after considerable sums had been\r\npaid in circuitous fashions, the butler was directed to a railway arch\r\nwhere the child would be deposited, and where he found a drab-coloured\r\nbrat of whom he disposed at the nearest police station, after which he\r\ncame home savagely disgusted.\r\n\r\nNuttie was not much less so at what she felt as a slight to Mr. Dutton\r\nas well as at the failure.  \'When you are doing so much for us.  We\r\ndeserve that you should do nothing more,\' she said with tears shining\r\nin her eyes.\r\n\r\n\'Do not talk in that way,\' he answered.  \'You know my feeling for the\r\ndear little fellow himself, and--\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh yes,\' interrupted Nuttie, \'I do trust to that!  Nobody--not the\r\nmost indifferent person, but must long to save him.  Yes, I know it was\r\ndoing you a wicked injustice to fancy that you could take offence in\r\nthat way at a father in such trouble.  Please forgive me, Mr. Dutton.\'\r\n\r\n\'As if I had anything to forgive.  As if there were anything on earth\r\nthat could come before the endeavour to recover him,\' said Mr. Dutton,\r\ntoo much moved for his usual precision of speech.\r\n\r\n\'Yes; he is _her_ child,\' said Nuttie, with a trembling tearful smile.\r\n\r\n\'_Her_ child!  Yes, and even if he were not, he is _your_ brother,\'\r\nsaid Mr. Dutton; then hastily gathering himself up, as if he had said\r\ntoo much, he rose to take leave, adding as their hands clasped,\r\n\'Remember, as long as I live, you may count upon me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I know, I know!  There\'s nobody like you, but I don\'t know what I\r\nsay in this awful suspense.  If I had only seen him lying white and\r\ncold and peaceful, it would have been far better than to think of him\r\npining and miserable among wicked people, who would try to bring him up\r\nlike themselves.  Mother\'s own little boy!\'\r\n\r\n\'It will not be allowed, it will not be allowed,\' cried Mr. Dutton.\r\n\'God\'s Providence is still over him.\'\r\n\r\n\'And there are prayers, I know--at our church and Mr. Godfrey\'s-and all\r\nours, but oh! it takes a great deal of faith to lean on them.  I wonder\r\nif you would, Annaple, if it were Willy?\'\r\n\r\n\'We will not ask Mrs. Egremont,\' said Mr. Dutton, as Annaple made a\r\ngesture of something like doubt.\r\n\r\n\'It is almost as bad,\' said she, coming up and putting her arm round\r\nNuttie.  \'But indeed, Mr. Dutton, she does trust, only it is very, very\r\nsore, for her,--as it is for us all.\'\r\n\r\n\'You are her great comfort,\' said Mr. Dutton, as he shook hands with\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\'He could hardly help thanking me,\' said Annaple to her husband\r\nafterwards.  \'Mr. Egremont may well call him an adopted uncle.  I\r\nshould say he was a good deal more, poor man.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXV.\r\n\r\nTHE HULL OF THE URSULA.\r\n\r\n\r\nTen days had passed, and Mark and Annaple were thinking that they ought\r\nto return to ordinary life, and leave the bereaved ones to endeavour to\r\nconstruct their life afresh under the dreadful wearing uncertainty of\r\ntheir darling\'s fate.  Still they were detained by urgent entreaties\r\nfrom father and daughter, who both dreaded their departure as\r\nadditional desolation, and as closing the door of hope. And certainly,\r\neven this rest was good for Annaple; and her baby, for whom nurse had\r\ndiscovered a better system, had really not cried more for a whole day\r\nthan \'befitted a rational child,\' said the mother, as she walked back\r\nto Springfield with her husband in the summer night, after dinner, on\r\nthe day that Broadbent\'s negotiations had failed.\r\n\r\n\'Nurse will break her heart at parting with her,\' said Mark.  \'I wish\r\nwe could afford to have her.\'\r\n\r\n\'Afford, indeed!  Her wages are about a quarter of your salary, sir!\r\nAnd after all, \'tis not the nurse that guards the child, as we have\r\nseen only too plainly.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you think he is alive, Nan?\'\r\n\r\n\'I begin to think not.  He is not so young but that he could make\r\nhimself known, and those advertisements are so widely spread.  I am\r\nsure poor Nuttie would be more at rest if she could give up hope.\'\r\n\r\n\'I did not tell you before, Nan, but Dutton was going to-day to look at\r\na poor little unclaimed child\'s body that had been found in the Thames.\r\nHe knew him better than I, so he went.\'\r\n\r\n\'He would have come if--\' said Annaple.\r\n\r\n\'Assuredly.  He meant to fetch nurse if he had any doubt, but\r\nafterwards he was going to his court about his rents.  He always does\r\nthat on Saturday evenings.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton himself opened his door to the pair.\r\n\r\n\'Well,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\n\'Certainly not.  The poor child was evidently much younger, and had red\r\nhair.  But look here,\' and he held out a battered something, black with\r\na white stripe.  Mark understood nothing, but Annaple exclaimed, \'Is it\r\nhis ship?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I could swear to it, for see,\' and he pointed to some grimed,\r\nalmost effaced, but still legible capitals, which, however, scarcely\r\nany one but himself could have read as "Ursula."  \'I guided his hand to\r\nmake those the evening before he was lost,\' said Mr. Dutton.\r\n\r\n\'Dear little man!  And where did you find it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Where I never thought of doing so!  On the bed of a little crippled\r\nboy in the next court to mine.  He is rather a friend of mine, and I\r\nturned in to take him some strawberries.  I found him hugging this.\'\r\n\r\n\'How did he get it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Our "Liz" brought it to him.  Our "Liz" is a very wild specimen, who\r\nhas spent her life in eluding the school board officer till she is too\r\nold for his clutches; but she has a soft spot in her heart for her\r\nlittle brother, and I believe another for Gerard Godfrey.  We must be\r\nvery cautious, and not excite any alarm, or we shall be baffled\r\naltogether.  I am not sure that I did quite prudently in giving little\r\nAlf a fresh boat in exchange for this; but I could not help bringing it\r\nhome.\'\r\n\r\n\'You did not see the girl?\'\r\n\r\n\'No.  Those girls wander long and late on these hot nights, and I do\r\nnot think I could have got anything out of her.  I have been to Gerard\r\nGodfrey, and the next step must be left to him.\'\r\n\r\n\'The next question is whether you will tell those poor things at No.\r\n5,\' said Mark.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton hesitated.  \'I should have no doubt of giving Miss Egremont\r\nthe comfort of knowing that there was a possible clue, but if her\r\nfather insisted on setting on the police, there would be very little\r\nmore hope of success.  I am afraid it will be more prudent to wait till\r\nwe know what Godfrey says.  He hopes to see the girl to-morrow evening\r\nat his mission class, but of course she is a very uncertain attendant\r\nthere.  No, I cannot trust myself.\'\r\n\r\nAnnaple was forced to brook withholding the hope from the fainting\r\nhearts all the ensuing Sunday, which was a specially trying day, as\r\nNuttie pined for her dear little companion with the pictures, stories,\r\nand hymns that he had always enjoyed, and made pretty childish remarks\r\nabout, such as she began to treasure as memorable.\r\n\r\nAs soon as he could, early on Monday morning, Mr. Dutton repaired to\r\nGerard Godfrey\'s lodgings, and found that the young clergyman had\r\nsucceeded in seeing the girl, and had examined her so as not to put the\r\nwild creature on her guard, and make her use the weapons of falsehood\r\ntowards one who had never been looked on as an ally of the police.  It\r\nappeared that she had brought home the ship, or rather its hull, from\r\none of the lowest of lodging houses, where she had employment as\r\nsomething between charwoman and errand girl.  She had found it on what\r\npassed for a bed in its present condition, one morning, when going to\r\nmake the extremely slight arrangements that the terrible lair, which\r\nserved as a common bedroom, underwent, and had secreted it as a prize\r\nfor her little brother.\r\n\r\nAt first she had been stolid, and affected utter ignorance as to how it\r\ngot there, but Mr. Godfrey had entreated her as a friend to try to\r\ndiscover; and had with all his heart made a pathetic description of the\r\ngirl (he durst not say lady) who had always been a mother to her little\r\nbrother, and now had lost him, and was in terrible uncertainty as to\r\nhis fate.  That came home to Lizzie\'s feelings, and she let out what\r\nshe had seen or picked up in the way of gossip,--that the ship had been\r\nleft behind by its owner, whether boy or girl Liz was uncertain, for it\r\nhad long fair hair, wore a petticoat, and had been dosed with gin and\r\nsomething else when carried away.  They said it had made noise enough\r\nwhen brought there by Funny Frank and Julia. They were performing folk,\r\nwho had come in after the Derby day to have a spree, and to pick up\r\nanother kid to do fairies and such like, because the last they had had\r\nhurt his back and had to be left in the workhouse.  Yes, she had heard\r\ntell that they had got the child from Mother Bet, of whom Gerard had a\r\nvague idea as one of the horrible hags, who not only beg themselves,\r\nbut provide outfits for beggars, including infants, to excite\r\ncompassion.  Either she or one of her crew had picked up the child and\r\ndisposed of his clothes; and then finding him too old and intelligent\r\nto be safely used for begging purposes, she had sold or hired him out\r\nto these acrobatic performers, who had gone off into that vague and\r\nunknown region, the country.  Liz had no notion what was their real\r\nname, nor where they would go, only that they attended races and fairs;\r\nand as soon as the actual pleasure of communicating information was\r\nover, she was seized with a panic, implored Mr. Godfrey to make no use\r\nof her information, and explained that the people of the house were\r\nquite capable of killing her, if they suspected her of betraying any of\r\ntheir transactions.  It was impossible to bring any authorities to bear\r\non the quest; and Mr. Dutton held it wisest only to write a note\r\ntelling Mr. Egremont that he had obtained evidence that the child was\r\nliving, and that he was going in pursuit, but thought it safer to say\r\nno more at present.  He gave the note to Mark at his office.  \'I cannot\r\ntrust myself to see your cousin,\' he said.  \'I might be tempted to say\r\nmore than was consistent with Godfrey\'s honour towards his informant.\'\r\n\r\n\'I think you are right,\' said Mark.  \'You had better leave me with only\r\nindefinite knowledge, for I shall be hard pressed.  Do you not go home\r\nfirst?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I go to pack up a few things and fetch Monsieur.  A run in the\r\ncountry will do him good, and he may be a valuable auxiliary.  I shall\r\nfind no one at Springfield at this hour.\'\r\n\r\n\'What is your plan?\'\r\n\r\n\'I shall venture so far as to apply to the police for the names of the\r\nusual attendants at races and fairs, and for some idea of their\r\nordinary rounds.  I have no doubt that these are known at the chief\r\noffices.  For the rest, I must use my eyes.  But tell your cousin that,\r\nwith God\'s blessing, I hope to bring him back to her.\'\r\n\r\n\'He will,\' said Ursula, when Mark gave her the message, and from that\r\nmoment she was calmer.  She did not fret Mark with questions even as\r\nmuch as Annaple did, she tried to prevent her father from raging at the\r\nscant information, and she even endeavoured to employ herself with some\r\nof her ordinary occupations, though all the time she kept up the\r\nceaseless watch.  \'Mr. Dutton would not have said that without good\r\nhope,\' she averred, \'and I trust to him.\'\r\n\r\nYet when four, five, six, eight, days had passed with no tidings, the\r\nheart sickness grew almost more than she could bear, though she still\r\nanswered with spirit when her father again took to abusing the\r\numbrella-fellow for choosing to keep all in his own hands.\r\n\r\nEven Annaple could not help saying to her husband that a precise, prim,\r\nold bachelor was the very last person for a hunt in slums and the like.\r\nThe very sight of him would put the people on their guard. \'And think\r\nof his fine words,\' she added.  \'I wish I could go!  If I started with\r\na shawl over my head, yoked to a barrel-organ, I should have a far\r\nbetter chance than he will.  I declare, Mark, if he does not succeed\r\nwe\'ll do it.  We\'ll hire an organ, whereon you shall play.  Ah! you\r\nshake your head.  A musical education is not required, and I know I\r\nshall do something desperate soon, if that dear little boy is not\r\nfound.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXVI.\r\n\r\nNUTTIE\'S KNIGHT.\r\n\r\n  \'The night came on and the bairnies grat,\r\n  Their minnie aneath the mools heard that.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\'LYNDHURST, 4th July.--Philip Dutton to Miss Egremont.  Found.\r\nWaterloo, 6.15.\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\'I knew he would,\' said Nuttie, with a strange quietness, but as she\r\ntried to read it to her father her voice choked, and she had to hand it\r\nto Annaple.  But for the first time in her life she went up and\r\nvoluntarily kissed her father\'s forehead.  And perhaps it was for the\r\nfirst time in his life that the exclamation broke from him, \'Thank God!\'\r\n\r\nPerhaps it was well that the telegram had not come earlier in the day,\r\nfor Mr. Egremont was very restless, showing himself much shaken in\r\nnerves and spirits before the time for driving to the station, which he\r\ngreatly antedated.
