Nuttie could hardly keep him in the carriage, and\r\nindeed had to persuade him to return thither, when he had once sprung\r\nout on the arrival of a wrong train.\r\n\r\nAnd after all, when the train did come, his blue spectacles were\r\ndirected to the row of doors at the other end, and Nuttie was anxiously\r\ntrying to save him from being jostled, when a voice said \'Here!\' and\r\nclose beside them stood Mr. Dutton, with a little boy by his side who\r\nlooked up in her face and said \'Sister!\'  It was said in a dreamy,\r\nalmost puzzled way, not with the ecstatic joy Nuttie had figured to\r\nherself; and there was something passive in the mode of his hearing his\r\nfather\'s \'My boy, my boy!\'  Instinctively all turned to the harbour of\r\nthe carriage; Mr. Dutton lifted Alwyn in, and as Nuttie received him, a\r\npang shot across her, as she felt how light, how bony the little frame\r\nhad become in these three weeks.\r\n\r\n\'Come in!  Come back with us!  Tell us all!\' said Mr. Egremont, as Mr.\r\nDutton was about to help him in.\r\n\r\n\'My dog,\' said Mr. Dutton, while Alwyn looked up from nestling in\r\nNuttie\'s lap to say, \'Mithter Button come!  And Mothu!\'\r\n\r\n\'We have room for him,\' said Mr. Egremont graciously.  \'Here, poor\r\nfellow.\'\r\n\r\n\'He has the right,\' said Mr. Dutton, \'for he was the real finder.\'\r\n\r\nAnd Monsieur, curly and shiny, occupied with great dignity the back\r\nseat beside his master, while Alwyn, in a silent but dreamy content, as\r\nif he only half understood where he was, rested against his sister\'s\r\nbosom with his hands in his father\'s.\r\n\r\n\'Come, old chap,\' said his father cheerily, \'tell us all about it.\'\r\n\r\nBut Alwyn only shuddered a little, raised his eyelids slightly, and\r\ngave a tiny faint smile.\r\n\r\n\'I think he is very much tired,\' said Mr. Dutton.  \'There was a good\r\ndeal to be done to make him presentable this morning.  You must forgive\r\nme for sacrificing his curls, there was nothing else to be done with\r\nthem.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!\' and Nuttie looked again.  The boy was in a new, rather coarse,\r\nready-made, sailor suit that hung loosely upon his little limbs, his\r\nhair was short, and he was very pale, the delicate rosy flush quite\r\ngone, and with it the round outline of the soft cheek; and there were\r\npurple marks under the languid eyes.  She bent down and kissed him,\r\nsaying, \'Was Mr. Button nurse to you, Wynnie?\'\r\n\r\nHe smiled again and murmured, \'Mr. Button made me boy again.\'\r\n\r\nAfter a question and answer or two as to main facts of place and time\r\nof the discovery, Mr. Dutton told his story.  \'I did not effect much\r\nwith my inquiries after the circuses.  All I heard of were of too\r\nsuperior an order for kidnapping practices.  However, I thought the\r\nonly way would be to haunt fairs and races, and look at their\r\ncamp-followers.  At a place in Hertfordshire I saw a performance\r\nadvertised with several children as fairies, so I went to see it.  I\r\nwas soon satisfied that Alwyn was not there; but it struck me that I\r\nhad known the face of the prime hero, a fine handsome supple fellow,\r\nwho was called in the programme Herr Adalbert Steinfuggen, or some such\r\nname.  Well, it seemed that he knew me, for as I struggled out after a\r\nconsiderable interval, I heard myself accosted, "Mr. Dutton! Sir,\r\nsurely I have the honour of speaking to Mr. Dutton of Micklethwayte?"\r\nI assure you he was the very pink of politeness.  Do you remember, Miss\r\nEgremont, Abel Stone?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Abel Stone!  He was a choir boy at Micklethwayte, I remember! He\r\nwas very handsome, and had a splendid voice; but he was a real monkey\r\nfor mischief, and nobody could manage him but mother.  She was always\r\npleading that he should not be turned out, and at last he ran away.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; he went off with a circus, and there he found his vocation, rose\r\nand throve, married the prima-donna, and is part owner.  He seems very\r\nrespectable, and was so friendly and affectionate that I ventured to\r\nconsult him; when, on hearing whom I was seeking, he became warmly\r\ninterested, and gave me just the information I wanted. He said he had\r\nlittle doubt that Funny Frank was a clown called Brag, with whom he had\r\nhad words some years back for misusing the children. He said he did not\r\nhold with harshness to the little ones in teaching them to do the\r\nfeats, which certainly were wonderful.  If they were frightened, they\r\nwere nervous and met with accidents; but make much of them, and they\r\nthought it all fun, and took a pride and pleasure in their\r\nperformances.  However this Brag, though a clever fellow, could not be\r\nhindered from bullying, and at last he went off with a girl of the\r\ntroupe and set up on their own account.  Stone, or whatever he pleases\r\nto call himself, had met them several times, but he spoke of them with\r\ngreat contempt as "low," and they did not frequent the same places as\r\nhe does.  However, he referred to one of his men, and found that they\r\nhad been at Epsom on the Derby day, and moreover, that there was a\r\nreport of them having lately narrowly escaped being in a scrape about a\r\nchild who had been injured.  There was no scruple as to advising me\r\nwhere to look for them, or as to the best means of detection.  Stone\r\nwas very indignant, and made me understand that all his young people\r\nwere either to the manner born, or willingly hired out by their\r\nparents.  I saw them in private life, and they looked happy and\r\nwell-fed, but that was no guarantee for Funny Frank.  Well, I followed\r\nhim up without success, trying each place Stone had set down for me,\r\ntill I came last night to Lyndhurst, a very pretty place in the New\r\nForest, where there is to be a fair to-morrow, beginning this\r\nafternoon.  Stone advised me to look about before the affair opened,\r\nwhile unpacking and arranging was going on. Well, after all, it was\r\nvery simple.  I strolled out with my dog round the field where the vans\r\nand booths were getting into order. There was what I thought a little\r\ngirl in a faded red petticoat sitting on the steps at the bottom of a\r\nyellow van with her head on her hands.\'\r\n\r\n\'That was me,\' said Alwyn, lighting up.  \'And Mothu came and kissed\r\nFan!\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Mr. Dutton; \'I verily believe we might have missed one\r\nanother, but Monsieur ran up to him and, as I was actually whistling\r\nhim off, I heard a little voice say, "Mothu!  Mothu!" and saw they\r\nwere--well, embracing one another, and then came "Mithter Button,\r\nMithter Button, oh, take me home!\'"\r\n\r\nEager caressing hands were held out to Monsieur, who jumped off the\r\nseat to receive the pats and laudations lavished on his curly round\r\npate, and had to be reduced to order before Mr. Dutton could answer the\r\nquestion whether he had any further difficulty or danger.\r\n\r\n\'I took him up in my arms, and a handsome truculent-looking woman burst\r\nout on me, demanding what I was about with her child.  To which I\r\nanswered that she knew very well he was no such thing.  Her man came\r\nswaggering up, declaring impudently that I had better be off--but I\r\nbelieve he saw that the people who came round would not take his part,\r\nfor he gave in much more easily than I expected.  I explained as loud\r\nas I could that this was a gentleman\'s son who had been stolen from his\r\nnurse in the Park.  The man began to protest that they had found him\r\ndeserted, and taken him with them out of charity, requesting to be paid\r\nfor his keep.  So I thought it better to give them a sovereign at once,\r\nso as to have no further trouble, and get him away as fast as I could.\r\nThe woman came after me, making further demands, but the sight of a\r\npoliceman in the distance turned her back.  I went up to him and\r\nexplained.  I found he knew all about the loss and the reward, and\r\nlooked regretfully at my prize.  We went back to the hotel, where I set\r\nAlwyn to rights as well as I could, sent out for some clothes, such as\r\nthe place would produce, and which at least, as he says, made a boy of\r\nhim again.  I\'m afraid the process was rather trying from such\r\nunaccustomed hands, though he was very good, and he has been asleep\r\nalmost all the way home, and, his senses all as in a dream bound up.\'\r\n\r\nThe heaviness--whether weariness or content, still continued.  Alwyn\r\nseemed to find it too much trouble to talk, and only gave little\r\nsmiles, more like his mother than himself.  He clung quite desperately\r\nto his sister when Mark offered to lift him from the carriage, but\r\nnurse was close behind, and it was good to see the little arms\r\nstretched out, and the head laid on her shoulder, the hand put up to\r\nstroke her cheek, and the lips whispering \'Wyn\'s own nursie.\'  The\r\njubilant greeting and triumphant procession with which he was borne\r\nupstairs seemed almost to oppress him.  He appeared almost as if he was\r\nafraid of wakening from a happy dream, and his lively merriment seemed\r\nall gone; there were only beams of recognition and gladness at \'Wyn\'s\r\nown nursery, Wyn\'s own pretty cup,\' touching it as if to make sure that\r\nit was real, and pleased to see the twisted crusts, his special treat.\r\n\r\nBut he could not eat much of them, and soon laid his head down, as one\r\nweary, with the exhaustion of content; and nurse, who had allowed that\r\nMr. Dutton had, considering all things, done much for the outward\r\nrestoration of the daintiness of her recovered child, was impatient to\r\ngive him the hot bath and night\'s rest that was to bring back the\r\nbright joyous Alwyn.  So Nuttie only lingered for those evening prayers\r\nshe had yearned after so sorely.  When she held his mother\'s picture to\r\nhim to be kissed, he raised his eyes to her and said: \'Will she come to\r\nme at night now?\'\r\n\r\n\'Who, my darling?\'\r\n\r\n\'She, mother dear.\'\r\n\r\n\'Here\'s her picture, dear boy.\'\r\n\r\n\'Not only the picture--she came out of it, when I cried, up on the\r\nnasty-smelling bundle in the van all in the dark.\'\r\n\r\n\'She came?\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, she came, and made it so nice, and hushed me.  I wasn\'t afraid to\r\ngo to by-by when she came.  And she sang.  Sister, can\'t you sing like\r\nthat?\'\r\n\r\n\'Not here, I\'m afraid, dear, dear boy,\' she whispered, holding him so\r\ntight that he gave a little cry of \'It hurts.\'  Then came the prayers,\r\nnot a word forgotten, and the little voice joined in her murmured\r\nthanksgiving for bringing him home.\r\n\r\nShe was much moved and awe-stricken at these words of her little\r\nbrother; but she had to dress in haste for dinner, listening the while\r\nto her maid\'s rejoinings and vituperations of the wretches who had\r\nmaltreated the child.\r\n\r\nWhen she came down she found no one in the drawing-room but Mr. Dutton,\r\nwhom her father had asked to the happiest meal that had perhaps ever\r\nbeen eaten in that house.\r\n\r\nShe went towards him with winged steps in her white dress: \'Oh! Mr.\r\nDutton, we have not said half enough to you, but we never, never can.\'\r\n\r\nHe gave a curious, trembling half smile, as she held out her hands to\r\nhim, and said: \'The joy is great in itself,\' speaking in a very low\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\'Oh! I am so glad that you did it,\' cried Ursula.  \'It would not have\r\nbeen half so sweet to owe it to any one else.\'\r\n\r\n\'Miss Egremont, do you know what you are saying?\' he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t call me Miss Egremont!  You never used to.  Why should you?\'\r\n\r\n\'I have not dared--\' he began.\r\n\r\n\'Dared!  Don\'t you know you always were our own Mr. Dutton--best,\r\nwisest friend of all, and now more than ever.\'\r\n\r\n\'Stay,\' he said, \'I cannot allow you in your fervour to say such things\r\nto me, unaware of the strength of feeling you are stirring within me.\'\r\n\r\n\'You! you!  Mr. Dutton!\' cried Nuttie, with a moment\'s recoil.  \'You\r\ndon\'t mean that you care for _me_.\'\r\n\r\n\'I know it is preposterous--\' he began.\r\n\r\n\'Preposterous!  Yes, that you should care one bit for silly, foolish,\r\nnaughty, self-willed me.  Oh, Mr. Dutton, you can\'t mean it!\'\r\n\r\n\'Indeed, I would have kept silence, and not disturbed you with my\r\npresumption, if--\'\r\n\r\n\'Hush!\' she cried.  \'Why, it makes me so glad and so proud, I don\'t\r\nknow what to do.  I didn\'t think anybody was good enough for\r\nyou--unless it was dear, dear mother--and that it should be me.\'\r\n\r\n\'It is true,\' he said gravely, \'my younger days were spent in a vain\r\ndream of that angel, then when all that was ended, I thought such\r\nthings were not for me; but the old feeling has wakened, it seems to me\r\nin greater force than ever, though I meant to have kept it in control--\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I am glad you didn\'t!  It seems as if the world swam round with\r\nwonder and happiness,\' and she held his hand as if to steady herself,\r\nstarting however as Annaple opened the door saying, \'We\'ve been sending\r\ntelegrams with the good news.\'\r\n\r\nThen an arch light came into her bright eyes, but the others were\r\nbehind her, and she said no more.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXVII.\r\n\r\nFOUND AND TAKEN.\r\n\r\n  \'The angels of the gateway\r\n    Bent softly to the child,\r\n  And stretched glad hands to take him\r\n    To the kingdom undefiled.\'--B. M.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Come up and see him,\' said Nuttie, as the dining-room door was shut.\r\n\'I must feast my eyes on him.\'\r\n\r\nAnnaple replied by throwing an arm round her and looking into her eyes,\r\nkissing her on each cheek, and then, as they reached the landing in the\r\nsummer twilight, waltzing round and round that narrow space with her.\r\n\r\n\'You ridiculous person!\' said Nuttie.  \'Do you mean that you saw!\'\r\n\r\n\'Of course I did; I\'ve seen ever so long--\'\r\n\r\n\'Nonsense!  That\'s impossible--\'\r\n\r\n\'Impossible to owls and bats perhaps, but to nothing else not to see\r\nthat there was one sole and single hero in the world to you, and that\r\nto him there was one single being in the world; and that being the\r\ncase---\'\r\n\r\n\'But, Annaple, you can\'t guess what he has always been to me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! don\'t I know?--a sort of Archbishop of Canterbury and George\r\nHeriot rolled into one.  So much the more reason, my dear, I don\'t know\r\nwhen I\'ve been so glad in my life than that your good times should be\r\ncoming.\'\r\n\r\n\'They are come in knowing this!  It is only too wonderful,\' said\r\nNuttie, as they stood together among the plants in the little\r\nconservatory on the way upstairs.  \'I always thought it insulting to\r\nhim when they teased me about him.\'\r\n\r\n\'They did, did they?\'\r\n\r\n\'My father, incited by poor Gregorio.  Oh, Annaple! don\'t let any one\r\nguess till we know how my father will take it.  What is it, Ellen?\' as\r\nthe nursery-maid appeared on the stairs.\r\n\r\n\'If you please, ma\'am, Mrs. Poole would be glad if you are coming up to\r\nthe nursery.\'\r\n\r\nThey both hastened up and nurse came out to meet them in the day\r\nnursery, making a sign to Ellen to take her place by the cot, and\r\nwithholding the two ladies.  She made them come as far off as possible,\r\nand then said that she was not at all satisfied about Master Alwyn.\r\nThere had been the same drowsiness and disinclination to speak, and\r\nwhen she had undressed and washed him, he had seemed tender all over,\r\nand cried out and moaned as if her touch hurt him, especially on one\r\nside where, she felt convinced, there was some injury; but when she\r\nasked about it his eyes grew frightened and bewildered, and he only\r\ncried in a feeble sort of way, as if sobs gave him pain.\r\n\r\nShe had soothed him, and he had gone into his own bed with the same\r\ngentle languid gladness, but had presently begun moaning, and imploring\r\nin his sleep, wakening with screams and entreaties, \'Oh, I\'ll do it!\r\nI\'ll try!\' and she thought him very feverish.  Would it not be better\r\nthat a doctor should see him?\r\n\r\nNurse was always an alarmist, and Nuttie could not help thinking that\r\nto wake the child to see a stranger to-night would only add to his\r\nterror and distress, while Annaple declared her entire belief that\r\nthough no doubt the poor little fellow had been cruelly knocked about\r\nand bruised, a night\'s rest would probably restore his bright self, and\r\nmake all that was past only like a bad dream.  There was no judging\r\nto-night, and sleep was wonderful reparation to those little beings.\r\n\r\nThen however the moans and murmurs began again, and now the awakening\r\ncry.  They started forward, and as Nuttie came to the cot-side the\r\nchild threw himself into her bosom with, \'Sister!  Sister!  It is\r\nsister!\' but his eyes grew round with terror at sight of Annaple, and\r\nclinging tightly to Nuttie he gasped, \'Send her away! don\'t let her\r\ntouch me!  Fan\'s not here!\'\r\n\r\nTo tell him she was Cousin Annaple, Billy\'s mamma, had no effect; he\r\ndid not seem able to understand, and she could only retire--nurse being\r\nthus convinced that to let him see another stranger to-night would only\r\ndo further harm.  Nuttie and nurse succeeded in reassuring him that he\r\nwas safe at home and with them, and in hushing him off into what they\r\nhoped would be a quiet wholesome sleep in spite of the hot sultry\r\nnight, on which Annaple laid a good deal of the blame of his\r\nrestlessness and feverishness.\r\n\r\nNuttie only came down for a short time before the visitors went away;\r\nand then she wrote a note to Dr. Brownlow, which Mark promised to leave\r\nas he went to the city in the morning, Mr. Egremont, in his present\r\nrelief, pooh-poohing all fears, and backing up Annaple\'s belief in the\r\npowers of \'tired nature\'s soft restorer\'; but Mr. Dutton looked grave\r\nand said that he had remarked the extreme tenderness, but had hoped\r\nthat much was due to his own inexperience in handling little children.\r\nThe parting clasp of the hand had a world of meaning in it, and Nuttie\r\nopenly said that she hoped to tell him after matins at St. Michael\'s\r\nhow the boy was.  But she could not be there.  When she went upstairs\r\nat night the half-delirious terrors had returned, and there was another\r\ndifficult soothing and comforting before the child slept again.  Nurse\r\nfancied the unwonted presence might disturb him, and insisted on her\r\ngoing to her own room.\r\n\r\nWhen she returned in the morning it was to find that since daylight he\r\nhad been more quietly asleep; but there was a worn sunken look about\r\nhis face, and she could not be satisfied to leave him alone while the\r\nnurses stirred about and breakfasted.\r\n\r\nHe awoke smiling and happy; he looked about and said gladly, \'Wyn at\r\nhome!  Wyn\'s own nursery,\' but he did not want to get up; \'Wyn so\r\ntired,\' he said, speaking of himself in the baby form that he had for\r\nseveral months discarded, but he said his pretty \'thank you,\' and took\r\ndelight in breakfasting in his cot, though still in a subdued way, and\r\nshowing great reluctance to move or be touched.\r\n\r\nNuttie was sent for to report of him to his father, who would not hear\r\nfor a moment of anxiety, declaring that the boy would be quite well if\r\nthey let him alone, he only wanted rest, and insisting on following out\r\nhis intention of seeing a police superintendent to demand whether the\r\nkidnapping rascals could not be prosecuted.\r\n\r\nNeither by Nuttie nor nurse could much be extracted from the poor\r\nlittle fellow himself about his adventures.  He could not bear to think\r\nof them, and there was a mist of confusion over his mind, partly from\r\nweakness, partly, they also thought, from the drugged spirits with\r\nwhich he had been more than once dosed.  He dimly remembered missing\r\nGregorio in the park, and that he had tried to find his way home alone,\r\nbut some one, a big boy, he thought, had said he would show him the\r\nway, took hold of his hand, dragged him, he knew not where, into\r\ndreadful dirt and stench, and apparently had silenced him with a blow\r\nbefore stripping him.  But it was all very indistinct, he could not\r\ntell how Mother Bet got hold of him, and the being dressed in the rags\r\nof a girl had somehow loosed his hold of his own identity.  He did not\r\nseem at all certain that the poor little dirty petticoated thing who\r\nhad wakened in a horrible cellar, or in a dark jolting van who had been\r\ndubbed Fan, who had been forced by the stick to dance and twist and\r\ncompelled to drink burning, choking stuff, was the same with Alwyn in\r\nhis sailor suit or in his white cot.\r\n\r\nIt was Dr. Brownlow who at once detected that there had been much of\r\nthis dosing, and drew forth the fact.  It had probably been done\r\nwhenever it was expedient that he should be hidden, or unable to make\r\nany appeal to outsiders.  Alwyn was quite himself by day, and showed no\r\nunreasonable fear or shyness, but he begged not to be touched, and\r\nthough he tried to be good and manly, could not keep from cries and\r\nscreams when the doctor examined him.\r\n\r\nThen it came out.  \'It\'s where he kicked me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Who?\'\r\n\r\n\'That man--master, she said I must call him.  He kicked poor little Fan\r\nwith his great heavy big boots--\'cause Fan would say Wyn\'s prayers.\'\r\n\r\n\'Who was Fan?\' asked the puzzled doctor.\r\n\r\n\'Himself,\' whispered Nuttie.  \'Alas! himself!\'\r\n\r\n\'Wyn was Fan,\' said Alwyn.  \'Fan\'s gone now!\'\r\n\r\n\'And did the man kick poor little Fan,\' repeated the doctor--\'here?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh don\'t--don\'t!  It hurts so.  Master said he would have none of\r\nthat, and he kicked with his big boot.  Oh!  Fan couldn\'t dance one bit\r\nafter that.\'\r\n\r\nHe could not tell how long ago this had been.  He seemed to have lost\r\nall reckoning of days, and probably felt as if ages had past in Funny\r\nFrank\'s van, but Dr. Brownlow thought the injury could not be above two\r\nor three days old, and probably it accounted for there having been no\r\nmore obstructions put in the way of removing the child, since he had\r\nceased to be of use, and the discovery of the injury might have brought\r\nthe perpetrator into trouble.  Indeed, as it was, Mr. Egremont caused\r\nthe police to be written to, demanding the arrest of the man and woman\r\nBrag, but they had already decamped, and were never traced, which was\r\ndecidedly a relief to those who dreaded all that a prosecution would\r\nhave involved.\r\n\r\nAnd Dr. Brownlow became very grave over the injury.  He said it was a\r\nsurgical case, and he should like to have another opinion, enjoining\r\nthat the child should be kept in bed, and as quiet as possible, till he\r\ncould bring his friend in the afternoon, which was no difficult matter,\r\nfor Alwyn seemed to have no desire for anything but rest and the sight\r\nof his friends and his treasures, which were laid beside him to be\r\ngently handled and stroked but not played with.  Mothu and Mithter\r\nButton were among the friends he craved for, but he showed no desire to\r\nsee Billy-boy, and it was thought best to keep that young gentleman\'s\r\nrampant strength at a distance.\r\n\r\nThe chief difficulty was with his father, who declared they were all\r\ncroaking, and that the boy would be as well as ever to-morrow.  He went\r\nand sat by the cot, and talked merrily of the pony that Alwyn was to\r\nride, and the yachting they would have in the summer; and the little\r\nfellow smiled and was pleased, but went to sleep in the midst. Then Mr.\r\nEgremont went out, taking Annaple with him, because Nuttie would not go\r\ntill the doctors\' visit was over, though he declared that they were\r\ncertain not to come till long after her return from the drive.  He\r\nactually went to the dealer\'s, and had pony after pony paraded before\r\nthe carriage, choosing a charming toy Shetland at last, subject to its\r\nbehaviour with the coachman\'s little boy, while Annaple hopefully\r\nagreed with him that Alwyn would be on its back in another week.\r\n\r\nHe still maintained his opinion, outwardly at least, when he was met on\r\nhis return by Nuttie with a pale, almost thunderstruck face.  Dr.\r\nBrownlow had called her from trying to soothe away the fright and\r\nsuffering of the examination, to break to her that both he and his\r\ncolleague thought very seriously of the injury and its consequences,\r\nand deemed it very doubtful whether the poor little fellow could be\r\npulled through.\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont was again angry, declared that she had misunderstood, and\r\nmade the worst of it; that Dr. Brownlow was a conceited young ass; that\r\nhis friend played into his hands; with other amenities of the same\r\nkind, to which she listened with mingled irritation and pity for his\r\nunreasonableness, and even at the sympathy which he found in Annaple\'s\r\nhopeful nature.\r\n\r\nThe young mother never dreaded nor expected what she could not bear to\r\nthink possible, such as the death-warrant of that beautiful child,\r\nwhile Nuttie\'s nature always expected the worst, and indeed had read\r\nthe doom in the doctor\'s eyes and voice rather than in his words.  So\r\nAnnaple backed Mr. Egremont up when he made his daughter write to\r\ndesire Dr. Brownlow to call in the first advice in London; and among\r\nthem they made so sure that this would be effective that they actually\r\nraised Nuttie\'s hopes so as to buoy her through the feverish early\r\nhours of the night when the pain was aggravated, the terrors returned,\r\nthe boy was tormented by his duality with Fan, and the past miseries\r\nwere acted over again.  Even nurse and sister did not suffice, and\r\nMithter Button had to be fetched by Mark before he could feel quite\r\nsecure that he was Alwyn and not Fan.  Indeed, in these light-headed\r\nmoments, a better notion was gained of what he must have endured than\r\nin the day-time, when all seemed put aside or forgotten. After a time\r\nhe became capable of being soothed by hymns, though still asking why\r\nhis sister could not sing like that vision of his mother which had\r\ncomforted him in his previous miseries, and craving for her return.\r\nThen at last he fell quietly asleep, and Nuttie was left with a few\r\nsustaining words and a pressure from Mr. Dutton\'s hand.\r\n\r\nAlas! the new consultation could only ratify the first opinion.  The\r\ninjury need not have been necessarily fatal, though dangerous to any\r\nyoung child, and here it had been aggravated by previous ill-treatment,\r\nand by the doses of spirits that had been forced down, besides which,\r\nAlwyn was naturally delicate, and--though the doctors would not say so\r\nto father or sister--there were hereditary predispositions that gave\r\nhim the less chance of battling through.\r\n\r\nYet Mr. Egremont concluded his purchase of the pony, and insisted that\r\nAlwyn should be carried to the window to see it; and Alwyn\'s smile was\r\nalmost enough to break Nuttie\'s heart, but his head drooped on nurse\'s\r\nshoulder, he hardly lifted his heavy eyelids, and begged for \'by-by\'\r\nagain.  Even Annaple burst into tears at the sight, ran out of the room\r\nwith her sobs, and never augured recovery again, though still she\r\nstrove to cheer and while away the poor father\'s piteous hours by\r\nmaking the most of every sign that the child was happy and not\r\nsuffering much.\r\n\r\nThat he would be viewed as a \'pale placid martyr\' was his sister\'s\r\nchief comfort.  His replies as to the manner of the hurt, as well as\r\nhis light-headed wanderings, had made it more and more evident that the\r\nman Brag\'s brutality had been excited by his persisting in kneeling\r\ndown to say his prayers aloud--the only way he knew how to say them.\r\nIndeed there was a recurring anxiety night and morning to kneel, which\r\nhad to be reasoned away, even when he was too weak to make the attempt,\r\nand was only appeased by \'Sister\' kneeling by his side, holding his\r\nhands, and repeating the little prayers with him. It was of his own\r\naccord that he added: \'And forgive those people, and make them good.\'\r\nAnnaple burst into tears again and almost scolded when she heard of it.\r\n\'Oh dear! oh dear! now I know he won\'t get well!  I\'m glad Billy isn\'t\r\nso horribly good!  Nuttie, Nuttie, don\'t!  You know I don\'t mean it.\r\nOnly I just can\'t bear it.  He is the sweetest little fellow in the\r\nworld!  And oh! the cruelty of it.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Nuttie in her dreary calmness; \'he is too sweet and lovely\r\nand beautiful and good to be anywhere but safe with mother.\'\r\n\r\nFor it was more apparent that they could not keep him.  It did not last\r\nlong; there were a couple of piteous days of restless pain and\r\ndistress, and then came the more fatal lull and absence of suffering, a\r\ndrowsiness in which the little fellow sank gradually away, lying with a\r\nstrange calm beauty on his face, and smiling feebly when he now and\r\nthen lifted his eyes to rest them on sister or nurse.  His father could\r\nnot bear the sight.  It filled him more with angry compassion than with\r\nthe tender reverence and hushed awe with which Ursula watched her\r\ndarling slipping as it were from her hold.  So Mr. Egremont wandered\r\nwretchedly about the lower rooms, while Mark and Annaple tried their\r\nbest for him through the long summer evening, darkening into night.  By\r\nand by Alwyn lifted his hand, turned his head, opened his lips, and\r\nwhispered, \'Hark, sister, she is singing.\' The look of exceeding joy\r\nbeamed more and more over the pinched little face.  \'She\'s come again,\'\r\nhe said; and once more, \'Come to take Wyn to the dear Lord.\'  After\r\nthat there were very few more long breaths before little Alwyn\r\nEgremont\'s spirit was gone to that unseen world, and only the fair\r\nlittle frame left with that wondrous look of delighted recognition on\r\nthe face.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXVIII.\r\n\r\nTHE UMBRELLA MAN.\r\n\r\n\r\nLittle Alwyn was laid to rest beside his mother in a beautiful summer\r\nnoontide.  His father was not in a state to attend the funeral, and was\r\nleft under the care of Annaple, his own choice among those who offered\r\nto stay and minister to him.  It was his own wish that his daughter\r\nshould be to the last with her little brother.  He had even said to her\r\nthat she had been a good sister, and his boy had been very fond of her,\r\nand he would not keep her away on any account.\r\n\r\nAnd, with a man\'s preference for a young and kindly woman, he chose\r\nAnnaple to be with him rather than Mr. Dutton, remembering likewise\r\nthat but for him the boy would have died in some workhouse, unknown and\r\nunclaimed, or among the wretches who had caused his death.  So Nuttie\r\nhad the comfort of Mr. Dutton\'s going down with her, as well as Mark,\r\nand poor broken-down nurse, but not a word referring to the confession\r\nof that happy evening had passed between them during the mournful\r\nfortnight which had since elapsed.\r\n\r\nMay Condamine and her husband had made all as fair and consoling as\r\nthey could.  There were white-robed children to bear the boy from the\r\nchurchyard gate, choristers sang hymns, the grave was lined with moss\r\nand daisies, and white roses decked the little coffin and the mound.\r\nThere was as much of welcome and even of triumph as befitted the\r\ninnocent child, whose death had in it the element of testimony to the\r\ntruth.  And Nuttie felt it, or would feel it by and by, when her spirit\r\nfelt less as if some precious thing had been torn up by the roots--to\r\nbe safe and waiting for her elsewhere, indeed, but that did not solace\r\nthe yearning longing for the merry loving child; nor the aching pity\r\nfor the crushed blighted creature whom she had watched suffering and\r\ndying.  It was far beyond her power as yet to acquiesce in her aunt\'s\r\nconsolation that it was happier for the child himself, than if he was\r\nto grow up to temptation from without, and with an unsound\r\nconstitution, with dangerous hereditary proclivities.  She could\r\nbelieve it in faith, nay, she had already experienced the difficulties\r\nher father had thrown in her way of dealing with him, she tried to be\r\nresigned, but the good sense of the Canoness was too much for her.\r\n\r\nIt was a day of more haste than suited the ideal of such a time, for\r\nMr. Egremont could not be left for a night; so there was only time for\r\na luncheon, with little jerks of talk, and then for an hour spent in\r\nshort private interviews.  Mrs. Egremont obtained from poor Nurse Poole\r\nall the details, and, moreover, her opinion of Mr. Mark\'s baby, in\r\nwhom, it having been born under her auspices, she took a special\r\ninterest.\r\n\r\nNuttie meantime was pacing the shady walk with her dear old friend Miss\r\nNugent, feeling it strange that her heart did not leap up at the bare\r\npresence of one she loved so much, yet conscious of the soothing of her\r\nsympathy.  And Mary, watching her all through, had been struck with the\r\nincreased sweetness and nobleness her countenance had acquired during\r\nthese years of discipline.  More of her mother\'s expression had come\r\nthan could have been thought possible in features of such a different\r\nmould, formed for so much more strength and energy.  They had not met\r\nsince Nuttie had been summoned home to her mother\'s deathbed, and their\r\ntime was chiefly spent on reminiscences alike of the old sorrow and the\r\nnew; but, when the time for parting was nearly come, Mary said\r\naffectionately, \'And you, my dear?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, I am all right,\' said Nuttie, and her eyes shone with a light Mary\r\ndid not at the moment understand; \'you need not be anxious for me\r\n_now_.\'\r\n\r\n\'I suppose that unhappy valet\'s death makes your task easier,\' said\r\nMary.\r\n\r\n\'I think it will,\' said Nuttie.  \'Poor man!  He was--I can\'t help\r\nsaying it--the evil genius of the house.  Dear mother knew it,\r\nstruggled against him, and broke down in the struggle.  It seems so\r\nstrange that what she could not do has been done in such a manner, and\r\nat such a price!  I wonder whether she knew it when she welcomed her\r\nboy!\'\r\n\r\n\'Her influence will aid you still,\' said Mary, \'and you have Mr. Dutton\r\nto help you too.  I was so glad to find he was so near you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, Mr. Dutton!\' exclaimed Ursula, in a strange tone that sent a\r\nthrill through Mary, though she knew not why; but at that moment they\r\nwere interrupted, very inopportunely, by Mr. Bulfinch, who could not go\r\naway without asking Miss Egremont whether she thought her father could\r\nsee him on business if he came up to town the next day.  She thought\r\nthat such an interview would rouse her father and do him good, advising\r\nhim to call on the chance.\r\n\r\nMark\'s tete-a-tete had been with his sister May, to whom he had much to\r\ntell of his wife and her gallant patience and energy, and how curious\r\nit was that now the incubus that had weighed on his uncle\'s household\r\nwas removed, the prejudice had melted away, and he had grown so fond of\r\nher that, next to Ursula, she was his best comforter.\r\n\r\n\'I hope that will lead to more,\' said May.\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t see how,\' said Mark.  The more we rely only on a blessing on\r\nour own exertions the better.\'\r\n\r\n\'Even when Annaple works within an inch of her life?\'\r\n\r\n\'Now that she is on a right tack about the baby, that will be easier.\r\nYes, May, I do feel sometimes that I have brought her down to drudgery\r\nand narrowness and want of variety such as was never meant for her, but\r\nshe will never let me think so.  She says that it is living in\r\nrealities, and that it makes her happier than toiling after society, or\r\nrather after the world, and I do believe it is true!  I\'m sure it is\r\nwith me.\'\r\n\r\n\'But such work as yours, Mark.\'\r\n\r\n\'Nonsense, May; I enjoy it.  I did not when I was in the Greenleaf\r\nfirm, with an undeveloped sense that Goodenough was not to be trusted,\r\nand we were drifting to the bad, yet too green to understand or hinder\r\nit; but this I thoroughly like.  What does one want but honest\r\neffective work, with some power of dealing with and helping those good\r\nfellows, the hands, to see the right and help themselves?\'\r\n\r\nMay sighed.  \'And yet, now that poor child is gone, I feel all the more\r\nhow hard it is that you should be put out of the rights of your name.\'\r\n\r\n\'I never had any rights.  It was the bane of my life to be supposed to\r\nhave them.  Nothing but this could have made a man of me.\'\r\n\r\n\'And don\'t you have regrets for your boy?\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t think I have--provided we can give him an education--such as I\r\nfailed to make proper use of, or Annaple might be luxuriating at Pera\r\nat this moment.\'\r\n\r\n\'Well!\' said May, pausing as she looked up the vista of trees at the\r\ngreat house; \'I can\'t bear it to go out of the old name.\'\r\n\r\n\'Names may be taken!\'\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t mean that there\'s any chance of--Oh! not that horrid Mr.\r\nFane?\'\r\n\r\n\'Certainly not.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh!\' as a trim black figure appeared walking down the open space.\r\n\'That man!\'\r\n\r\n\'I am not authorised to tell any one so, May.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, I understand.  The wretch, he is taking stock of the place\r\nalready!\'\r\n\r\n\'For shame.  May, no one has deserved so well of them.\'\r\n\r\n\'I don\'t care, he got you into that horrid concern.\'\r\n\r\n\'And got me out of it, and found my work for me.  I tell you, May, it\r\nis the best thing that could possibly happen to your parish, or the\r\nestate, or my poor uncle either!  And you will soon come to a better\r\nmind.\'\r\n\r\n\'Never, while he is to get into your place!  Turn back before he comes\r\nwithin hailing distance.\'\r\n\r\nBefore Mark could do anything towards bringing his sister to a better\r\nmind he was seized on by his stepmother to propound a scheme she had\r\nhatched, namely that, as a mutual benefit, Nurse Poole should be\r\nallowed the consolation of bringing her chief comforter, his little\r\ndaughter, down with her on the visit Mrs. Egremont had invited her to\r\npay at Redcastle.  He was very grateful, though doubtful whether\r\nAnnaple would accept the offer, for she was missing her children\'s\r\ncompany, though they were only at Springfield House, and she had been\r\nwith them part of every day.  And, sad as this month had been, it had\r\nbeen such a rest from sheer physical toil that she had gained almost as\r\nmuch by it as the little one.\r\n\r\nThere was a general assembly and coffee-drinking in the verandah,--Mr.\r\nCondamine, Blanche, and her two young sisters were all there,--and May\r\nhad to be duly civil to Mr. Dutton, though he came back with some\r\nwater-lilies that he had fished out of the lake for Nuttie, and she\r\nthought it taking possession.  Then the Londoners set forth for the\r\nstation, and there Mark, having perhaps had a hint from his wife, saw\r\nNuttie and Mr. Dutton safely bestowed by Broadbent in an empty\r\ncarriage, and then discovered a desire to smoke, and left them to\r\nthemselves.\r\n\r\nThey had not been alone together for more than a second since the\r\nevening of Alwyn\'s return, and there was a great shyness between them,\r\nwhich lasted till the first station was past without any irruption of\r\nnewcomers.  Nothing had been said but a few comments on the\r\narrangements and the attendants, but probably both were trying to begin\r\nto speak, and at last it was Ursula who crossed over so that her face\r\ncould not be seen, and said in an odd tone--\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton--\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' and he turned, instantly on the alert.\r\n\r\n\'Did you mean it--what I thought you meant that evening?\'\r\n\r\n\'Can you doubt it?\' he said earnestly.  \'But even then I was surprised\r\ninto the avowal, and I would have held it back if possible, if I had\r\nguessed what was going to happen.\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah! but then I should not have had that drop of comfort through it\r\nall,\' and she laid hold of his hand, which returned the pressure\r\nstrongly, but he sedulously guarded both words and tone as he said:\r\n\r\n\'Listen, Ursula, before you speak again.  How dear you must always be\r\nto me, I cannot tell you, but when I then spoke, it was with the sense\r\nthat on every account, I should meet with strong opposition from your\r\nfather and family.  And now your position is altered, so that the\r\nunsuitability is doubled.  I am not a young man, remember, and my\r\nthoughts must be for you above all, I want you to consider whether, in\r\nthe present state of affairs, you would not do better to look on what\r\nthen passed as unsaid, or only as the ebullition of gratitude towards\r\nyour old friend.  Let me go abroad, and give you full opportunity\r\nfor--for some fresh beginning likely to be fitter for you--\'\r\n\r\n\'Mr. Dutton, how can you say such horrid things?  As if a dukedom would\r\nmake any difference.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' he said, turning towards her.  \'If it is only the old-friend\r\nfeeling, then it is better dropped, but if your heart is in it, child,\r\nthen we go on, come what may.  It is due to you.\'\r\n\r\nShe raised her face towards him now, and he gave a grave kiss to her\r\nforehead.  She drew a long breath, and said after a little pause, \'And\r\nnow I have something to say.  One does think of such things even in\r\nthese sad times, and you can help me.  I am so glad it is you, because\r\nI know you will, and be rejoiced to do so.  You know when Mark found us\r\nout first, dear mother and I always felt that it was a great pity he\r\nshould not have the estate he had been brought up to expect.  I believe\r\ndear mother thought it would have been the right thing for me to marry\r\nhim, but I always did mean to give it back to him, even when I didn\'t\r\nlike him.  Well, then, you know it all seemed settled otherwise, but\r\nnow, it is so lucky you spoke to me while that dear little fellow was\r\nwith us, because now you will help me to persuade my father that it is\r\nthe only satisfactory thing to do to let it go in the male line to Mark\r\nand his Willy.\'\r\n\r\n\'I see!  I see!\' said Mr. Dutton eagerly.  \'It would be an infinite\r\nrelief if it could be carried out.\'\r\n\r\n\'I believe my father would like it,\' said Nuttie.  \'He cares for the\r\nname; and now no one prevents it; he is fond of Mark, and still more of\r\nAnnaple!  And you!  Oh, Mr. Dutton, if he will only take it in the\r\nright way, I think you will make me able to do what it grieved dear\r\nmother never to have brought about for my poor father.\'\r\n\r\n\'My whole self is yours to aid you,\' he said.  \'You know of course that\r\nI could not ask you to detach yourself from one to whom you are so\r\nnecessary.  If he will permit us, we will watch over him together as\r\ndoing her work.\'\r\n\r\n\'Thank you,\' was all Nuttie\'s lips could utter, though her hand said\r\nmuch more.\r\n\r\nAnd before they reached London they had arranged something of a plan of\r\naction for propitiating Mr. Egremont, and bringing the future prospects\r\nto be available so as to save Annaple from being worked to death in the\r\nmeantime.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIX.\r\n\r\nANNAPLE\'S AMBITION FALLEN.\r\n\r\n\r\n\'Well, how did you get on, Annaple?\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! very well, poor old man, on the whole, though it made one pity him\r\ndoubly that he chose to make as if he forgot everything, and you were\r\nall gone on a picnic, taking me out for a long drive in the\r\nafternoon--where we were least likely to meet any one--that I will say\r\nfor him.\'\r\n\r\n\'Forgetting is not the best for him.\'\r\n\r\n\'As if he could forget!  But he was very nice and friendly, and put on\r\nhis best, most courteous self.  I think he looks on me rather as a\r\nprotector from the solemn Mr. Edsall.\'\r\n\r\n\'Surely Edsall treats him well.  He was excellently recommended.  You\r\nknow I saw his master\'s daughter.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! only _too_ well.  He takes the management of him as if he were\r\nthree years old, or a lunatic.  He simply _will_ not be offended any\r\nmore than if he had to do with a baby.\'\r\n\r\n\'What should offend him?\'\r\n\r\n\'That Mr. Egremont greatly resents being allowed nothing but by what\r\nEdsall calls medical sanction.  He is too blind, you know, to venture\r\nto pour out anything for himself, and besides, Edsall has all the drugs\r\nunder lock and key, and is coolness itself about any amount of\r\nobjurgations, such as I fancy go on sometimes.\'\r\n\r\n\'Do you think he will stand it?\'\r\n\r\n\'Who?  Your uncle?  Yes, I think he will.  This man really makes him\r\nmore comfortable than poor Gregorio did.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes; Nuttie said she was sure that there was neglect, if not bullying\r\nlatterly.  But he must miss Gregorio terribly.  They had been together\r\nfor at least five-and-twenty or thirty years, and had plenty of gossip\r\ntogether.\'\r\n\r\n\'Whereas the present paternal despotism and appalling dignity and\r\ngravity will keep him more dependent on his right congeners.\'\r\n\r\n\'If they are of the right sort, that\'s all.\'\r\n\r\n\'He has been making me read him a whole heap of letters; indeed, as you\r\nknow, I have been doing that all along, when he could not get Nuttie.\r\nThere were some from Mr. Bulfinch.  Do you know that bailiff of his\r\nmust be next door to a swindler?\'\r\n\r\n\'Bulfinch is coming up to see him to-morrow.\'\r\n\r\n\'And, Mark, do you know, he has been putting out feelers as if to\r\ndiscover whether we would do--what he asked us to do five years ago.\'\r\n\r\n\'Would you?\'\r\n\r\n\'If it were not for the children, and--and sometimes the extreme pinch,\r\nI should say it was more like _life_ to work yourself up as a City\r\nman,\' said Annaple.  \'If you were the Squire, with all his\r\nopportunities, it would be a different thing, but there\'s no outlet\r\nthere, and I have often admired the wisdom of the Apocryphal saying,\r\n"Make not thyself an underling to a foolish man."\'\r\n\r\n\'Well, it is lucky you think so, Nannie, for though Dutton is certainly\r\nnot a foolish man, he will not want an underling.  And what do you say\r\nto my mother\'s proposal of having poor Poole to stay at Redcastle, and\r\nborrowing baby to comfort her till she goes out again.\r\n\r\n\'I hate it,\' said Annaple energetically.  \'It is very horrid, but it is\r\nawfully good of the Canoness; and I suppose we shall have to let it\r\ncome to pass, and miss all that most charming time of babyhood which is\r\ncoming.  But most likely it will quite set the little woman up, and be\r\na real kindness to poor Poole.\'\r\n\r\n\'If we could only keep her for good.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes, and then our children would not be half so much our own.  I do\r\nwant to be away with them in our own quarters.  I wonder when Nuttie\r\ncan spare us, but I should like to see her through the great crisis\r\nwith her father.\'\r\n\r\nThat crisis was to involve more than Annaple in the least expected.\r\nNuttie found that the momentous confession could not possibly take\r\nplace before the interview with Mr. Bulfinch, at which her presence was\r\nneeded to help her father with his papers.  The principal concern was\r\nto show the full enormity of the bailiff, and decide upon the steps to\r\nbe taken, the solicitor being anxious for a prosecution, while a\r\ncertain tenderness for poor Gregorio\'s memory, or perhaps for the\r\nexposure of his own carelessness, made Mr. Egremont reluctant. There\r\nwas also a proposal, brought forward with much diffidence from Mr.\r\nCondamine\'s mother, to rent Bridgefield House, but on this, as well as\r\nrespecting a successor to the bailiff, Mr. Egremont was to give his\r\nanswer the next day, when Mr. Bulfinch would call again.\r\n\r\nNuttie was thankful for the business that had filled up the hour after\r\nluncheon, when Alwyn used to play in the drawing-room and delight his\r\nfather; but she was feeling desperate to have the crisis over, and\r\nresolved to speak when she went out driving with him.  It was he,\r\nhowever, who began.  \'I sounded Mark\'s wife yesterday, Ursula.  She is\r\na nice little thing enough, and a good wife in her way.\'\r\n\r\n\'A very good wife.\'\r\n\r\n\'Except when she persuaded him to turn up his nose at the agency. D\'ye\r\nthink he would take it now, since he has tasted the sweets of his\r\numbrella business?\' then, as Nuttie paused, taken by surprise; \'Five\r\nhundred a year and the Home Farm would be better than, what is it, a\r\nhundred and fifty and a floor over a warehouse!  I don\'t like to see\r\nold Will\'s son wearing himself out there, and the lad is a good honest\r\nlad, with business habits, who would do justice to you after I am gone.\'\r\n\r\n\'Father,\' said Nuttie, trembling with the effort, \'I want you to do\r\nsomething better than that.  I want you to let Mark take the agency\r\nwith a view to himself--not me.  Let him be as he would have been if he\r\nhad never hunted us up at Micklethwayte, and put me in his place.\'\r\n\r\n\'Eh!\' said Mr. Egremont.  \'It is not entailed--worse luck; if it had\r\nbeen, I should not have been bound to dance attendance at the heels of\r\nsuch an old sinner as the General.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, but it ought to go to the heir male, and keep in the old name.\r\nThink--there have been Egremonts at Bridgefield for four hundred years!\'\r\n\r\n\'Very pretty talk, but how will it be with you, Miss.  We shall have\r\nFane, and I don\'t know how many more, coming after the scent of\r\nBridgefield now,\' he said with a heavy sigh, ending with a bitter \'Hang\r\nthem all!\'\r\n\r\n\'And welcome,\' said Nuttie, answering the thought rather than the\r\nwords.  \'Father, I wanted to tell you--\'\r\n\r\n\'You don\'t mean that any one has been after you at such a time as\r\nthis!\' he cried.\r\n\r\n\'It was before--I mean it was the evening when we were all so glad,\r\nbefore we began to be afraid.\'\r\n\r\n\'The umbrella man!  By Jove!\'\r\n\r\n\'And now,\' went on Nuttie, in spite of the explosion, \'he would hardly\r\nhave ventured to go on with it but for this--I mean,\' as her father\r\ngave a little laugh of his unpleasant sort, \'he said it would be the\r\ngreatest possible relief, and make it all right for the property to go\r\nto the heir male.\'\r\n\r\n\'Hein!  You think so, do you?  See how it will be when I come to talk\r\nto him!  A shrewd fellow like that who got out of the Micklethwayte\r\nconcern just in time.  Catch him giving up a place like that, though he\r\nmay humbug you.\'\r\n\r\n\'Then you will see him, father?\'\r\n\r\n\'If you turn him in on me, I can\'t help it.  Bless me! umbrellas\r\neverywhere!  And here you mean to turn me over to the mercies of that\r\nsolemn idiot, Edsall.  I should have been better off with poor\r\nGregorio.\'\r\n\r\n\'No, father; Mr. Dutton would not take me from you.  We would both try\r\nall we could to make you comfortable.\'\r\n\r\n\'Convert the old reprobate?  Is that his dodge?\'\r\n\r\n\'Don\'t, father,\' for the sneering tone returned.\r\n\r\n\'Come now,\' he added in a much more fatherly manner, for her voice had\r\nstruck him.  \'You don\'t mean that a well-looking girl like you, who\r\ncould have her pick of all the swells in town, can really be smitten\r\nwith a priggish old retired umbrella-monger like that.  Why, he might\r\nbe your father.\'\r\n\r\n\'He has been getting younger ever since I knew him,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'Well.  He plays as good a game of whist as any man in England,\'\r\nmuttered Mr. Egremont, leaving his daughter in actual doubt whether he\r\nmeant this as a recommendation, or as expressing a distrust of him, as\r\none likely to play his cards to the best advantage.  She had to remain\r\nin doubt, for they overtook Clarence Fane, who came and spoke to them\r\nin a very friendly and solicitous manner, and showed himself willing to\r\naccept a lift in the carriage.  Mr. Egremont, willing to escape from\r\nperplexities as well as to endeavour to drive away if possible the\r\noppression of his grief, invited him in, and he had some gossip to\r\nimpart, which at first seemed to amuse the hearer after this time of\r\nseclusion, but the sick and sore heart soon wearied of it, and long\r\nbefore the drive was over, Mr. Egremont was as much bored as his\r\ndaughter had been from the first.\r\n\r\nWhen Mr. Fane got out, he paused a moment to hold Ursula\'s hand in a\r\ntender manner, while he told her that he had not ventured to intrude\r\n(he had left a card of inquiry every day), but that if ever he could be\r\nof the least use in amusing Mr. Egremont, he was at her service, and\r\nwould give up any engagement.\r\n\r\n\'Hein! my fine fellow!  No doubt you would!\' said Mr. Egremont, when\r\nhis daughter had uttered her cold thanks, and they had driven on.  \'I\r\nsee your little game, but it is soon to begin it.  We may as well let\r\nthem know that she is booked before the running begins.\'\r\n\r\nIt was a remarkable intimation of his acceptance of her engagement, but\r\nUrsula was contented to take it as such, and be thankful.\r\n\r\nMr. Dutton had his interview as soon as Mr. Egremont had rested after\r\nhis drive, and the result was satisfactory.\r\n\r\nNo doubt much was due to the Egremont indolence and want of energy,\r\nwhich always preferred to let things take their course.  And now that\r\nGregorio was no longer present to amuse, and take all trouble off his\r\nhands, Mr. Egremont could hardly have borne to part with his daughter;\r\nand, despite of umbrellas and religion, was not sorry to have a\r\nperfectly trustworthy son-in-law in the house, able to play at cards\r\nwith him, manage his household, and obviate all trouble about suitors\r\nfor the heiress.  Moreover, his better feelings were stirred by\r\ngratitude on his poor little son\'s account, and he knew very well that\r\na more brilliant match for his daughter would not have secured for his\r\nold age the care and attention he could rely upon here.  He was obliged\r\nlikewise to believe in the disinterestedness, which disclaimed all\r\ndesire for the estate, as involving cares and duties for which there\r\nhad been no training; and he was actually glad to keep the property in\r\nthe direct line.  The old liking for Mark, and sense of the hardship of\r\nhis exclusion, revived, strengthened now by regard for Annaple;\r\ntogether with the present relief from care obtained by making him\r\nmanager of the estate.\r\n\r\nWhen once brought to a point, Mr. Egremont was always sudden and\r\nimpetuous, chiefly for the sake of having it over and being unmolested\r\nand at rest again.  So that very evening, while Nuttie only ventured on\r\nsharing with Annaple the glad tidings that Mr. Dutton was accepted, and\r\nin his marvellous goodness, undertook to make his home with her father.\r\nMark was almost stunned by the news, confirmed to him by Mr. Dutton as\r\nwell as his uncle, that he was to be acknowledged as heir of\r\nBridgefield Egremont, and in the meantime manage the estate with an\r\nincome suitable to an oldest son.\r\n\r\nPresently he came upstairs by himself, and beckoned to Nuttie, rather\r\nto the alarm of his wife.\r\n\r\n\'Ursula,\' he said, and took both her hands, \'I cannot have you do this\r\nfor me.\'\r\n\r\n\'Can\'t you, Mark?  You can\'t prevent it, you see.  And don\'t you know\r\nit is the beginning of all my happiness?\'\r\n\r\n\'But indeed, I cannot feel it right.  It is a strained sense of\r\njustice.  Come and tell her so, Nannie.\'\r\n\r\n\'What?\' said Annaple coming forward.\r\n\r\nThey both paused a moment, then Nuttie said, \'Only that the estate\r\nought to go in the male line.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh, is that all?\' said Annaple, \'I was afraid Mr. Egremont had a fit!\'\r\n\r\n\'Ah!  Don\'t you see what it means,\' said Mark.  \'They want it to be as\r\nif there were an entail--to begin treating me as an eldest son at once.\r\nIt is Ursula\'s doing, putting herself out of the succession.\'\r\n\r\n\'I always hated being an heiress,\' said Nuttie.\r\n\r\n\'It would be more dreadful than ever now.  Annaple, do be sensible!\r\nDon\'t you see it is the only right thing to do?\'\r\n\r\n\'Billy!\' was the one word Annaple said.\r\n\r\n\'Yes, Billy and Jenny and all,\' said Nuttie, \'before you\'ve all died of\r\nyour horrid place--Oh! you haven\'t heard that part of it.  Of course\r\nMark will have to go down to Bridgefield and look after the place, and\r\nlive like a gentleman.\'\r\n\r\n\'Eight hundred a year,\' murmured Mark, \'and the house at the Home Farm.\'\r\n\r\n\'Oh! dear,\' gasped Annaple, \'I wanted you to be Lord Mayor, and now\r\nyou\'ll only be a stupid old country squire.  No, no, Nuttie,\r\nit\'s--it\'s--it\'s the sort of thing that one only laughs at because\r\notherwise one would have to do the other thing!\'\r\n\r\nAnd she gripped Nuttie tight round the waist, and laid her head on her\r\nshoulder, shaking with a few little sobs, which might be one thing or\r\nthe other.\r\n\r\n\'It will save her youth, perhaps her life,\' whispered Mark, lifting\r\nNuttie\'s hand to his lips for a moment, and then vanishing, while\r\nAnnaple recovered enough to say, \'I\'m tougher than that, sir.  But\r\nlittle Jenny!  Oh, Nuttie, I believe it has come in time.  I\'ve known\r\nall along that one straw more might break the camel\'s back.  We\'ve been\r\nvery happy, but I am glad it is over before Mark got worn down before\r\nhis time.  Grinding is very wholesome, but one may have too much; and I\r\nhaven\'t Mark\'s scruples, Nuttie dear, for I do think the place is more\r\nin his line than yours or Mr. Dutton\'s.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Ursula, \'you see he was always happy there, and I never\r\nwas.\'\r\n\r\nThe next thing was for Mr. Dutton and Ursula to keep Mr. Egremont up to\r\nthe point of making his long deferred will; nor did they find this so\r\ndifficult as they expected, for having once made up his mind, he wished\r\nto have the matter concluded, and he gave his instructions to Bulfinch\r\nthe next day.  Of course Mark had to give full notice to his employers;\r\nbut the allowance was to begin at once, so that Annaple only went back\r\nto the warehouse to pack up, since she was to occupy No. 5, while Mr.\r\nEgremont and his daughter were going under Mr. Dutton\'s escort to the\r\nbaths in Dauphine, an entirely new resort, free from the associations\r\nhe dreaded, for he could not yet bear the sight of little Willy--the\r\nrival \'boy of Egremont.\'  But the will was safely signed before he\r\nwent, to the great relief of Nuttie, who, according to the experience\r\nof fiction, could hardly believe his life safe till what she called\r\njustice had been done.\r\n\r\nAfter all Mr. Egremont became so dependent on Mr. Dutton, during this\r\njourney, that he did not like the separation at its close, and pressed\r\non the marriage even sooner than either of the lovers felt quite\r\nreverent towards the recent sorrow.  He insisted on Bulfinch having the\r\nsettlements ready for them on their return, and only let them wait long\r\nenough to keep their residence, before there was a very quiet wedding\r\nin their parish church, with the cousins for bridesmaids.  Then Mark\r\nand Annaple took care of Mr. Egremont for the fortnight while Mr.\r\nDutton showed his wife his old haunts in France, returning to\r\nSpringfield House, where there was plenty of room for Mr. Egremont to\r\nmake his home with them.\r\n\r\nSaid Annaple to Miss Nugent, \'I never saw Nuttie so youthful and\r\nbright.  She is more like a girl than I ever saw her since the first.\'\r\n\r\n\'Yes,\' said Mary, \'she has some one to rest on now.\'\r\n\r\nMr. Egremont lived between three and four years, more contented and\r\npeaceful than he had ever been, though frequently suffering, and\r\nsometimes giving way to temper and impatience.  But Mr. Dutton\r\nunderstood how to manage on these occasions, and without giving up his\r\nown extensive usefulness, could give him such care, attention, and\r\namusement as beguiled his discomforts, and made his daughter\'s task an\r\neasier one.\r\n\r\nHow far the sluggish enfeebled nature was capable of a touch of better\r\nthings, or whether his low spirits were repentance, no one could judge.\r\nAt any rate sneers had ended, and when he was laid beside his wife and\r\nboy at Bridgefield, Ursula stood by the grave with a far more tender\r\nand hopeful feeling than she could have thought possible when he had\r\nrent her away from her old home. She looked up at her husband and said,\r\n\'Is not her work done?\'\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE END.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of the Project Gutenberg EBook of Nuttie\'s Father, by Charlotte M.
